  
 
 
 
 
Walter’s Deal 
A STORY BY PHILIPP LEHNER 
 
2 
 
 
© 2016 Philipp Lehner 
 
Edited by Auri. 
 
Proofread by Khimitsu, Michael and Nine. 
 
This is a work of fiction. Names, characters, businesses, places, events and 
incidents are either the products of the author’s imagination or used in a 
fictitious manner. Any resemblance to actual persons, living or dead, or 
actual events is purely coincidental. 
 
www.muddasheep.com 
 
 
 
 
 
3 
 
 
 
A young, lean man was standing in the basement of his home, calculating 
and conceptualizing. As usual, the house of his parents was empty, except 
for him: Walter, the scientist. That’s what he liked to call himself. It wasn’t 
official or anything like that. It wasn’t a hobby either; it was something that 
had taken over his life without him choosing to do so. It just happened to 
be the one thing he was interested in. The only thing he was actually good 
at. 
And he was about to prove it. To whom? Walter didn’t know. To the 
people of the school he had dropped out of? To the whole town? To his 
country? Or could it be - to the entire world? 
In any case, the rat in its cage would believe in his abilities soon. 
Hopefully. 
Walter took off his obligatory white coat and threw it on the back of a 
nearby chair. It was getting hot due to the running machines, or because of 
his nervousness. A lot had been building up to this very moment. Too 
often he had argued with his parents, too many nights he had spent 
immersed in his project, forgetting to sleep and eat. 
It was true that his parents gave him free reign to ruin his own life 
however he wanted, but that doesn’t mean his father wouldn’t want his 
son to inherit and run the Trimond car company sometime soon. 
His mother didn’t care that much, saying her own father had been very 
similar. Indeed, Walter’s grandfather had left a bunch of notes which 
actually helped him to foster his belief that he was on his destined track.  
And he would stay on that road. His parents were notorious 
globetrotters, so, most of the time, his father’s criticism would barely reach 
him. 
It was already past midnight when all these pesky thoughts crossed 
Walter’s mind. This time of the night had proven to be his most successful. 
Usually, Walter let his body sleep from around eleven o’clock until late in 

 
4 
the afternoon. On Sundays he’d follow his long time tradition of not 
sleeping at all, health be damned. 
As Walter was fiddling around on his current brainchild - the machine 
of his dreams – his fingers slipped into the abyss of the huge machinery 
and he got jolted by a shock. The screwdriver in his left hand was thrown 
over his left shoulder into the array of books resting on an old shelf behind 
him. 
There he was, sitting on the ground, shaking his head, trying to get his 
hair straight again. After coming back to his senses, he immediately 
stopped caring about his hair, stood up, picked up the screwdriver and 
went back to work. There wasn’t even time for cursing. Ever since his fifth 
birthday Walter had been sure that if he could challenge time to a duel, 
he’d win hands down. 
There. It was done. Once again his mind went over the list of 
components needed and checked if they were properly connected.  
Yep, everything seemed to be in order. 
He marched into a rather dark corner of the basement, opened one of 
the cages and grabbed the rat. It protested loudly and even bit Walter ’s 
worker hands, but he didn’t care to notice. The rat was put into a glass 
booth which had wires all over the surface, running into the apparatus 
next to it. Walter had designed the booth for a different purpose, so the rat 
appeared way too small for it. 
There was an identical booth on the opposite side of the room, also 
decorated with black wires, making it look like a frozen black oil fountain.  
Walter wiped his forehead, closed the glass booth, and the rat’s calls for 
help were muted. 
Maybe check the wires again, Walter thought. So he did. Like a hunter 
following the tracks of a deer, he traced every wire forth and back. 
Everything looked fine. Walter wanted to move forward with the 
experiment, but the images of what had happened last time jumped into 
his mind, urging him to thoroughly inspect the machinery one more time. 
No doubt about it, everything was in order, precisely as the plan in his 
head instructed. 
With a swift move he flipped the blue switch on the apparatus. Sparks 
appeared everywhere, the lights went out. The machinery started 
humming a tune, just like a soprano from a choir. The sparks’ sounds 
resembled high-hats of a drum set. 
While Walter couldn’t hear the rat’s complaints anymore, he certainly 
noticed that its mouth was shut and it was nervously looking around. 
Walter knew what was coming as the machinery heightened its tone and 
 
5 
he closed his eyes firmly. 
An almost painful white light filled the room for an instant. And then - 
silence. 
The light went back to normal. The machinery’s concert was over. 
Walter slowly opened his eyes. This was it. Either his mind would receive 
a satisfying visual, or not. He considered keeping his eyelids closed; he 
wouldn’t want to cope with yet another disappointment. But his biological 
cameras were cold and relentless. With uncompromising determination 
they took a glance at the booth containing the rat. Or, shall we say, the 
booth in which the rat had been sitting in. Walter jumped to the booth and 
pressed his hands and nose against the warm glass. For years his eyes had 
been doing a fine job, but now he just couldn’t believe them. 
The rat was gone. 
Then again, this hadn’t been the entire plan. Walter hastily stood up 
and ran towards the opposite side of the room. 
Behind the glass of the other booth, the rat had already resumed its 
complaining. 
The scientist smiled. 
 
6 
 
 
 
The morning sun was shining through the window, greeting Xave, who 
was half asleep in his bed, eyes shut. For a moment, he felt two decades 
younger, expecting his mother to carry a warm smile into his room, 
reminding him to get up for school. 
“Xave, you’re going to be late,” she had used to say in her beautiful 
voice, making it a bit easier to set the first foot on the cold floor. 
He slowly opened his eyes, embraced reality and took a glance at the 
red digits beside his bed. 
It was precisely seven in the morning. 
Xave moaned, yawned and stretched his middle-aged body, and then, 
just like the last dozen years, he set his right foot on the unwelcoming 
laminated ground, and, after taking a deep breath, the left one too. Finally, 
he stood up. 
A few minutes and a quick shower later he was sitting in the kitchen of 
his old apartment, his short black hair air-drying, his right hand resting 
beside his cup of coffee, his left one holding the newspaper. 
Xave always thought of two types of people reading the news. The first 
being the consumers, the casual crowd, looking for shocking and violent 
news to fuel their gossip talk at work. The second type, the one he thinks 
himself to be a part of, were the seekers; people who can use information 
and make something out of it. 
“See a need, fill a need,” is what Xave’s father had continually preached 
when sitting on Xave’s bed in the evening. 
“See a need, fill a need. See a need, fill a need,” Xave’s thoughts would 
sing in a constant rhythm to drive his eyes, judging each headline carefully 
and questioning its value. 
There were the usual killings, the usual politics - in both of which he 
saw several needs, but none he was interested in filling - and the usual 
trash talk. Arguing neighbors, dogs with weird patterns shaved into their 
fur, newly born rare animals in zoos, ghost sightings, latest gibberish about 
celebrities (who was married to whom for how long before their 

 
7 
umpteenth breakup), and an interview with some scientist about-  
Whatever. 
Xave folded the newspaper, put it aside, downed his coffee and then 
rested for a while, looking outside one of the kitchen windows. The early 
summer sun was still trying to cheer him up, and the ceiling fan was 
putting all effort into blowing a calming breeze down his back. 
Everything Xave had just read formed a mass of information in his 
mind, which he bent and turned to look at and through it in all angles, 
while ripping out parts that wouldn’t fit into the puzzle he was trying to 
solve. Only emptiness remained after the process. 
Xave was about to doze off, when the window got smashed in. 
He jumped up, trying to get hold of the situation. A whirling ball of 
white feathers was bouncing around in his kitchen, chirping loudly, 
panicking. Xave’s heart pushed itself to new limits. His kitchen turned into 
a pool of chaos. And he would not approve of chaos that wasn’t of his own 
creation. 
Xave quickly ran into the living room, grabbed the holster off the 
couch, ran back into the kitchen, pulled out the gun and shot at the raging 
pigeon. 
In the next split second, the second window behind the bird caught the 
bullet and burst into pieces, the bird jerked upwards in a deadly refle x, 
steering towards the rotating blades of the ceiling fan. 
An explosion of blood and feathers followed, painting the whole 
kitchen with abstract forms. Xave froze for a minute, while torn plumage 
was gracefully being brought down by gravity. Just then he realized that 
he could’ve simply grabbed the bird and thrown it back out the window, 
which would’ve prevented the mess he was standing in. Then again, it had 
been too long since the last use of his gun. He felt invigorated, even though 
his ears did not agree with the sudden noise onslaught and rang alarm. 
Everything that had been resting on the table was now lying on the 
floor in odd positions, covered by a thin layer of feathers and blood.  
Like an angel, shot down from the sky, Xave thought. 
He holstered the gun and brought it back to the living room, putting it 
next to his coat, which was always lying on the couch at the same spot. The 
kitchen’s mess sought Xave’s attention, so he had to go back and fill the 
kitchen’s need.  
With a small plastic shovel he collected the shards of his broken cup 
and some of the feathers, which refused to let go off the ground due to the 
sticky blood.  
 
8 
Then he picked up the newspaper. The see-a-need-fill-a-need-thing. The 
thing full of celebrities, grotesquely detailed accidents, worthless 
hyperbole- 
And an interview with a scientist. 
He slowly skimmed through the pages. Because, naturally, he didn ’t 
have anything else to do. Standing in coffee, blood and feathers, he read 
the interview with a scientist called Walter Trimond about the newly 
discovered technology of, as the newspaper called it, beaming. 
 
9 
 
 
 
The Inventor of Beaming 
 
An interview with Walter Trimond, by Madeleine Long. 
 
As I arrive at the simple, yet elegant house, I get excited. Am I really 
going to meet the man who claims to have succeeded in what other 
scientists deem impossible? 
I knock on the door, as there is no sign of a bell. I hear footsteps 
approaching, and then the door gets opened and a man with short brown 
hair and glasses looks at me. After telling him that I’m here for the 
interview, he lets me in, takes off my coat and offers me a cup of coffee. 
The house seems to be nicely decorated, with a lot of attention to detail, 
most obviously the work of a woman. Is Mr. Trimond married? Or was he? 
We sit down on a couch in the living room, which for some reason doesn’t 
seem to be used quite often. Walter folds his arms; he seems anxious, but 
underneath that layer I see genius. 
 
Madeleine Long: How are you today? 
Walter Trimond: I’m fine, thank you. A bit nervous though. And tired. 
How about you? 
Madeleine: Nervous, yes. I can’t believe I’m sitting opposite to a man 
who has invented beaming. 
Walter: Well, I certainly wouldn’t call it beaming. 
Madeleine: Why not? Most people who have watched Star Trek know 
that it’s a synonym for teleportation. 
Walter: The only appropriate use of the word “beaming” finds itself in 
trivial literature. My invention is much more than just a gimmick of a 
television series. 
Madeleine: So, you don’t like Star Trek? 
Walter: Star Trek is just an idea, far from reality. 
Madeleine: Have you given your invention a different name then?  

 
10 
Walter: No. Well, in my journals I simply refer to it as “travelling”. But 
it has no mass appeal to it, I’m afraid. 
Madeleine: Can you tell me how “travelling” works? 
Walter: I could ask you how interviewing works and I’m sure there are 
dozens of books written about it. So, you should understand why I can ’t 
just summarize my years of work in a few sentences. 
Madeleine: I wasn’t talking about the technical background. I’d like to 
know how, say, a tourist would use it. 
Walter: Oh. Well, you step into the one container, somebody presses a 
button, and a few seconds later you appear in the container on the other 
side. 
Madeleine: Wow. 
Walter: Yes, breathtaking, isn’t it? 
Madeleine: Can you show me? 
Walter: Not right now, it needs a lot of power, and I’ve already used it 
too often this month. I get hefty electricity bills, believe me. 
Madeleine: How can you pay for it? I don’t think you have time left for 
a regular job, right? 
Walter: My father owned a car factory and I’m an only-child. 
Madeleine: Owned? You mean, he retired? 
Walter: (sighs) Yes, you could say that. 
Madeleine: What about your mother? 
Walter: My mother is a retired psychology teacher. 
Madeleine: What are your parents doing now? 
Walter: I’d rather not talk about it. You’re here for my invention, 
remember? 
Madeleine: Right. How did you get the idea which led you to invent 
beaming? 
Walter: Well, like most ideas, I stumbled upon it by accident. I saw a 
documentation of physicists trying to “copy” and “move” electrons over 
short distances, a small step towards moving entire organic bodies. I 
picked up the trail and here I am. 
Madeleine: So, basically you started from scratch and now you’re ahead 
of others who’ve worked on it for several years before you? 
Walter: You could say that. 
Madeleine: What did you use to test your invention? 
Walter: I started with vegetational items, small leaves, branches, and 
then mice and rats. I’ve also experimented with other things, like a CD 
player, clothes, and such. 
Madeleine: And I suppose it didn’t always succeed, right? 
 
11 
Walter: Correct. Only a month ago I got my first positive result.  
Madeleine: What happened to the animals? 
Walter: Well, most of them just disappeared. They weren’t coming out 
on the other end with dislocated body parts or something horrible like 
that. Mostly. 
Madeleine: Mostly? 
Walter: I’d rather not talk about that either. 
Madeleine: Have you ever tried it yourself? 
Walter: Not yet. I want to make sure it works, so I need a couple more 
tests with animals, possibly apes. 
Madeleine: And you really can’t demonstrate your invention now? 
Walter: I would like to, but I can’t. I’ve prepared a video though. 
[He hands me a video tape, which I watch afterwards. It shows Walter’s 
basement, just like you would imagine a scientist’s cave, and a rat getting 
beamed from a huge glass container to another. There were no apparent 
film cuts or other traces of editing, so we can’t really tell if it is fake or not.] 
Madeleine: Thank you. So, what are you going to do with your 
invention now? Such a discovery should be shared with the public, don ’t 
you think? 
Walter: That is definitely something I’m thinking about, yes. It is a huge 
responsibility though. I still need to figure out all the negative effects 
travelling could have. 
Madeleine: The only side effect I can think of is that cars would lose 
their main purpose. 
Walter: Yes, cars are one of the many means of transportation that 
would become obsolete over time, but not immediately. 
Madeleine: I’ll let you get back to it then, so you can finish your 
contraption within the next five years! I expect to be able to beam myself to 
any beach in the world after work every day. 
Walter: I’ll see what I can do. 
Madeleine: Thanks for the interview, and thanks for the tape. I’m 
excited. 
Walter: Thank you as well, I hope you’ll enjoy it. 
 
With those words I get my coat and the tape and leave. Can you 
imagine a world with beaming? You better start now, because it’s certainly 
becoming reality. 
 
 
 
12 
 
 
 
Walter shook his head at the newspaper, held up by his sweaty hands. A 
small section showed an interview with him, next to a series of images 
captured from the video tape. As he read through everything he had told 
the interviewer lady one week ago, all he could do was wonder about his 
own arrogance. He couldn’t remember being so full of himself while 
answering those questions. Sure, he had been awfully nervous, not only 
because it was the first interview of his life, but also because the woman’s 
choice of clothes hadn’t been of the shy and reserved kind. But, damn. It 
wouldn’t have hurt if he had just been a tiny bit more casual. 
He put the newspaper away, stood up and started circling through the 
living room, drawing an invisible eight on the floor. 
The newspaper wasn’t a very popular one, and most of the articles 
were probably made up. Walter didn’t expect any responses, at least no 
serious ones. The idea of giving away a prepared videotape instead of a 
live demonstration and the risk of failing machines had been a very smart 
move, Walter kept telling himself. But what if it had reduced his credibility 
to zero just because he hadn’t shown anything except what’s on the 
cassette? Most people would probably just read the interview, giggle and 
turn the page, hoping that in maybe forty or fifty years they’d see some 
mad scientist reveal a true beaming machine. 
There was that word again. Beaming. Ugh. 
Walter shivered. Oh, yes. He should probably expect a lot of attention 
from Star Trek fans now, since that reporter had actually decided to put 
that word in the headline: The Inventor of Beaming. 
Just when Walter was about to take the newspaper and throw it into the 
trash, the phone rang. 
Walter turned his left wrist. Eight o’clock sharp, the watch said. The 
rising sun outside seemed to nod in confirmation. 
He picked up and answered: “Hello?” 
“Good morning, Walter Trimond,” the male voice on the other end 
greeted. 

 
13 
“Good morning,” Walter replied. “Who am I speaking to?” 
“My name is Xave Stern. I would like to talk to you about the interview 
in the Future Today newspaper,” the voice calmly explained. 
“Go ahead,” Walter said, clearing his throat afterwards. 
“Is it real?” Xave asked, with a tone Walter couldn’t quite figure out. It 
was a bit spiteful, a bit defensive, and even somehow hopeful. 
“If by ‘it’ you mean the travelling machine I have invented, then yes, 
yes it is,” Walter proudly affirmed. There was that big-headed tone again. 
He could’ve slapped himself. 
“Good,” the voice said, obviously satisfied. “I’ll be there in two hours.” 
“What? You can’t just invite yourself, Mister Stern. Please remind 
yourself of your manners, which you were hopefully taught in your earlier 
years.” 
Well handled, Walter. 
“Oh, I can invite myself, Walter. We’re business partners now.” 
“Business partners?” 
“That’s right. Let’s talk face to face. Don’t let anybody else buy your 
invention in the meantime, got that?” 
Walter swallowed. And he thought he had been cocky. 
“Mister Stern,” Walter began. “At the moment I have no intention to 
sell my patent. Speaking of which, such a thing doesn’t even exist yet. I...” 
“Don’t worry, I will arrange all that.” 
A rustling sound. Probably paper, Walter thought. 
The voice continued. “You won’t have to deal with any of these things 
anymore. If your invention is real, that is. It is real, correct?” 
“As I already said, yes, it is real. As real as the voice you hear at the 
moment,” Walter said with a slight tone of anger. 
“Alright. See you later,” Xave said and hung up. 
Walter stared at the receiver. He had expected a few things, but not of 
that size and scale. This was going in a direction Walter wasn’t ready to 
take a turn into. In fact Walter hadn’t been ready to show his invention to 
the world at all, but his subconsciousness had decided against him and 
had told him to call the press. 
He put the receiver back and grabbed the newspaper again. The 
Inventor of Beaming. The Inventor of Travelling. The Inventor of... It didn ’t 
sound any better, no matter which words were inserted. He didn ’t like the 
word ‘inventor’, his ears screamed in agony at the sound of ‘beaming’. He 
had a profound aversion towards any title of that sort. It should’ve simply 
been Walter Trimond, not this of anything. Dammit. 
The phone rang again. “Hello?” 
 
14 
“Is this Walter Trimond?” a calm male voice asked. 
“Yes,” Walter said. 
“Good morning, I’m Stephen Mannor, CEO of Jupiter Logistics.” The 
voice took a small break, as if underlining the importance of its owner. 
“We heard about your invention and we would be interested in 
purchasing all rights to use your new technology exclusively. We thought 
about giving you two hundred thousand dollars, taxes included.” 
Walter’s eyes widened and he started to feel dizzy. The voice continued 
relentlessly, like an unstoppable train. 
“Nobody else will ever be able to use a technology similar to yours. We 
would name it whatever you prefer, and you shall be available to us in 
case our engineers have any troubles in the future. If you have received 
other offers already, name us their price and you can add their amount to 
our offered two hundred thousand dollars. From this day on you are a rich 
man, Walter Trimond. Congratulations.” 
Walter felt uneasy, weak and about to throw up. The voice seemed to 
strangle and slowly suffocate him. The cold and soulless tone sent a 
paralyzing wave through his body. 
“It’s not real,” Walter mumbled. 
“I’m sorry, what?” the CEO said. 
“The machine, it’s not real. The interview was a publicity stunt,” Walter 
said, trying to cover up the shaking voice. 
“Oh,” Stephen said. “I’m sure you...” 
Walter hung up. His breath went fast; his heart was pumping blood 
through his veins with a thousand beats per minute. What on earth had he 
gotten himself into? Two hundred thousand dollars. Walter definitely 
wasn’t poor, but the possibility that such an offer would be thrown at him 
for his simple - in comparison to other projects out there - invention was 
beyond even his understanding. It’s just a teleporting machine! Couldn’t 
be that ground-breaking now, could it? 
Walter received another three phone calls that tried to purchase his 
child, before he ripped the phone off the table, threw it through the house 
and sat down on the couch, where he remained seated, staring at the 
newspaper on the ground for the next two hours. 
 
15 
 
 
 
That was it, Xave kept telling himself while driving to the scientist’s 
address, which he had found in the phone book. From time to time he ’d 
throw a quick glance at the map on the passenger seat, hastily unfolded 
and held down by his right hand. 
He replayed a sentence from the interview in his head: “I expect to be 
able to beam myself to any beach in the world after work every day.”  
There it is. The need. The absolute need of every working human being 
on the planet. The ability to go anywhere instantly to relax after work. Not 
only that. It would open up so many new possibilities, completely 
overloading Xave’s mind. But he didn’t have the time to sort them out 
right now. His brain was concentrating on the road, and figuring out how 
to do business with a scientist. His father would have said: “Wear a suit, a 
red tie, shiny shoes and put gel in your hair. Appear as intelligent as 
possible, so that you’re regarded as part of the scientist’s circle.” 
Xave pulled on his tie’s knot to loosen it a bit and put his hand back on 
the map. Suit - check. Tie - check. Shiny shoes - well, no, but they were 
black. It would do. Gel - not necessary. He had washed his hair after the 
pigeon blood bath; it was still a bit wet. It would dry, but never mind.  
Another intersection and there should be the simple house Madelei ne 
mentioned in the interview. 
Boy, was he nervous. 
Don’t be nervous, Xave. Don’t be. There’s nothing to it. It’s just usual 
business. 
There it was. The small, but beautiful house, perfect for one family, 
with a proud oak tree guarding it and casting enough of a shadow to 
darken most of the house. A small crowd of reporters and business men 
and women had already gathered on the front lawn. Xave parked the car 
away from the house, in case the scientist took a coincidental look outside 
the window, see Xave’s old red car and get the wrong impression. 

 
16 
One last check in the driving mirror, a big grin to see if Xave’s teeth 
looked clean and healthy, a quick stroke through the hair, and one last self -
assuring nod. 
You can do this, Xave. 
He got out of the car, and forgot to lock it. While walking toward the 
house, he shrugged twice and moved his neck around quickly to make 
sure the collar sat perfectly centered. 
You’re a winner, Xave. This time you’re a winner. 
“I’m standing here in front of the scientist’s house,” a female reporter 
with a black dress blared into the microphone, showing her face to a huge 
camera, held by an even bigger man. “This is the place where a new age 
shall begin, if it is indeed true that Walter Trimond invented beaming.”  
The business people, surprisingly dressed almost exactly like Xave 
(even the women wore red ties), made it very hard for Xave to get to the 
door. 
“Who do you think you are? I got here before you,” a man with a 
flushed face hissed at Xave. 
“Yeah, but do you have an appointment?” Xave shouted back. 
“No, nobody does,” the man replied. “That guy doesn’t pick up his 
phone.” 
Well, I do, Xave thought. There was no way he would be let in by Walter 
with that crowd in front of his door, ready to tear everything apart. But 
there had to be a way. 
 
Walter was rooted in his chair, the same chair in the basement that had 
given his body comfort for the past twenty years. The distant noise of 
people shouting and talking nonsense terrified him. This wasn’t what he 
had hoped to achieve by letting the press know about his new technology. 
He regretted the moment he had dialled that cursed number; he called 
himself a fool over and over, hiding his face behind both his hands. 
A rat suddenly ran past him. The first being to ever have travelled and 
lived to tell about it. In rat language. 
“Oh, great, how did you get out of there,” Walter sighed. As he was 
about to stand up and catch the rat, he heard a knock coming from one of 
the cellar windows. 
Walter looked up at the sound’s origin. There was the face of a smiling 
man next to a sheet of paper pushed against the glass, showing a name in 
apparently hastily drawn black letters: Xave Stern. 
Walter hesitated, but then rose from his chair and opened the window.  
 
 
17 
Yes, Xave thought. One step closer to victory. 
“What do you want?” the scientist asked out of the basement. 
Xave was kneeling on the grass, hoping that it wouldn’t leave a huge 
mud stain on his expensive trousers. 
“Don’t you remember? I’m your new business partner,” Xave 
explained, grinning and wrapping himself in confidence, in hope it would 
convince the scientist. 
Xave saw Walter sighing and looking at the ground, thinking hard. “I ’ll 
meet you at the backdoor,” he finally said. 
“Great, thanks!” Xave stood up and walked to the back of the house. It 
was a miracle no reporter or other business person had been there yet.  
Walter opened the door and Xave entered, shaking hands with the 
scientist. 
“It’s an honor to meet you,” Xave beamed. 
“Likewise, I guess,” Walter said. 
A typical scientist, Xave thought. Full of himself, cocky, bigheaded, arrogant. 
He’ll be yours in no time, Xave. 
Walter locked the door. 
Small talk, yes or no, yes or no, Xave thought. Do scientists like small talk? 
No, I guess they don’t. 
“Let’s cut the small talk and get to the point,” Xave began, closely 
following the reactions on the scientist’s face. No frowning, no boredom. 
So far, so good. “I’m here to make you a rich man.” 
“Me, or rather yourself?” Walter asked. 
Whoops. 
“I’ll be honest with you, Walter. I can call you Walter, right?” 
“I guess,” Walter replied. 
Xave noticed a slight annoyance and corrected his course. “Walter,” he 
began. “I have a lot of experience. My father was a businessman, just like 
myself. His father was a carpenter, and his father’s father was a fisherman. 
You see, my family knows how to sell stuff.” 
“Stuff, uh-huh,” Walter mumbled. 
“Yes, for three generations we just sold stuff. Now’s my chance to sell 
something meaningful.” Xave balled up his right fist and held it in front of 
him. 
There it was. Walter’s forehead formed a huge canyon. Xave’s father 
had called it ‘The Frown’. A sign of complete failure. 
“I understand completely,” Walter said. “You see a huge opportunity to 
cash in on my work, so you can retire and live on a remote island for the 
rest of your life. Correct?” 
 
18 
Xave was desperate. “Yes, you got me.” 
The Frown disappeared. In its stead the left side of the scientist ’s mouth 
got raised. 
Alright, Xave. Complete honesty is your weapon of choice. 
Xave put his hands in his pockets. Casual. 
“Of course, I’m in for the money. I mean, ‘businessman’ is not just a 
decorative title. But most of the money would flow in your pockets. I ’d just 
be the middle man. I’d manage everything out there. And believe me, it 
gets really ugly on the battlefield. You’ll want to stay inside, working on 
your experiments. I can give you that freedom.” 
A short pause. Xave’s father had used pauses for two reasons: one, to 
take a deep breath and invite fresh oxygen into your brain; and two, to let 
the spoken words sink into your opponent’s mind. Xave took one hand out 
of the pocket to support his words with fitting gestures, and started 
walking around a bit, trying to appear visionary, confident. “We need to 
start small. First of all, we make sure your technology works fine. Then we 
need to polish it up for mass-appeal. Nobody wants to -” Beam? Teleport? 
What was the word again? Quick, Xave, quick. Ah, yes. “- travel around using 
machines that look like they were made back in the sixties.” 
Walter shrugged. “Why not? I love the sixties.” 
“Yes, there are a lot of people who still do, including myself.” A quick 
healthy lie. “But to attract the maximum amount of people, we need to be 
up to date on current styles.” Xave turned around on his heels. “And 
then...” He clapped. “We’re going to connect the first two cities.” 
Walter nodded repeatedly, staring on the ground, probably imagining 
the future Xave had laid out before him. 
He’s considering it, Xave thought. Yes! 
But with a sudden wave of the hand Walter dismissed the presented 
future. 
“Well, I think I should listen to other offers before making a decision,” 
the scientist concluded. 
Damn it. Xave went through his options. What to do? Ah, yes. He 
quickly walked to the window next to the front door. 
“Are you sure you would trust them more than me?” Xave asked and 
gestured Walter to look outside. 
The scientist came closer and gazed at the business people standing 
outside, yelling at each other. A separate crowd of Star Trek fans had 
arrived, wearing “Beam me up, Scotty!” t-shirts, standing behind the 
female reporter and waving into the camera. 
 
19 
“You should also consider the fact that other scientists will try to invent 
their own travelling machines as quickly as possible, throwing you off the 
throne sooner or later - if you don’t act now,” Xave said, thinking whether 
that had inflicted too much pressure. 
Still looking at the chaos outside, Walter sighed. 
Xave reached out. The final nudge. Come on, Walter. 
The scientist snapped out of it, turned and shook Xave’s hand. “Deal.” 
 
20 
 
 
 
Walter felt uneasy. He had probably made the biggest decision of his life a 
few hours ago. From now on, there would be no turning back. The train 
was rolling, the tracks couldn’t be rearranged. 
Sitting in his second favorite chair in the living room, he was listening 
to the crowd outside, which was creating a noise that tried to draw him 
into a state of trance, like a siren. 
The spell got broken as Xave barged in through the front door, using 
Walter’s key. 
“I’m back,” Xave announced. 
“So I noticed,” Walter said, lingering in his chair like a lazy old cat. 
“Any news about the health of my lawn?” 
“Well, I called the police. Two of those guys are standing in front of 
your house, making sure nobody steps on your property anymore.”  
Walter’s mood brightened up a bit. 
“I’ve got my war chest,” Xave presented the black suitcase, “containing 
all the necessary documents.” 
Bureaucracy. Only religion was worse to a scientist. 
“I can see from the look on your face that you don’t like paperwork,” 
Xave said. 
“Most definitely not,” Walter replied. “But I guess it is a necessity of 
our society. Each paper represents one of the many patches that hold 
humanity together.” 
Xave let out a quick laugh. “Good one. But anyway. Let’s do this later. 
First, you’ll have to show me your machine and tell me how it works, so I 
understand what I’m going to sell.” 
“Sounds reasonable,” Walter mumbled and forced himself out of the 
chair. “Follow me, please.” 
This was the moment he had been afraid of for a long time. Nobody 
other than himself had ever seen the travelling machine in reality. With his 
luck, it would stop functioning right at the start of the presentation.  

 
21 
“I’m sure it won’t work now,” Walter said to make an eventual 
upcoming disappointment less painful for the man in the suit. 
Now that Walter thought about it, Xave’s tie had a terrible color. But he 
kept his mouth shut about it. 
“Yes, don’t worry, I’m sure it will work eventually,” Xave assured, 
following Walter down the stairs into the basement. 
Walter turned on the light and both men stood still for a second, as if 
paying respect to the room with a moment of silence. The size of the 
machinery suggested it would make quite a sound when turned on and 
even the walls seemed to remember and reflect that noise. 
“So, this is where the magic happens,” Xave said, astonished. 
“Not magic, Xave, science,” Walter corrected the businessman with a 
raised finger. “I haven’t caught the rat yet, so be careful and watch your 
step.” 
“Rat?” 
“Yes, the rat I used for the first successful travelling test,” Walter 
added. “It somehow broke out of its cage today.” 
Walter knew that he should prepare himself for baseless criticism. The 
mental wall was up and Walter hoped it was thick enough not to let 
anything through and break his spirit. He went to the main machinery and 
turned it on. The familiar basic humming started. Soon all other 
instruments would follow the lead. 
“And these are the... travelling chambers?” Xave asked, pointing at the 
two glass booths with his hands. 
“Yes, you could say so,” Walter said over his left shoulder while 
monitoring the displays. 
“I guess this thing uses quite an amount of energy, right?” 
“Correct.” 
“Hm,” Xave said. 
“I will try to reduce the needed energy, of course. This is only a 
prototype, as you’re surely aware of,” Walter said. 
“Yes, yes.” 
Walter heard how Xave impatiently tapped on the ground with one 
foot. 
“So, how does this thing work?” Xave asked. 
The scientist thought about giving Xave an explanation he surely 
wouldn’t understand only to intimidate him, but then decided against it. 
While fiddling around with the controls and looking at everything twice to 
make sure it would work, he began describing the process. 
 
22 
“First of all,” he cleared his throat, “the subject that shall be sent to the 
target destination steps into the first - to use your words - travelling 
chamber.” 
“Uh-huh,” Xave said. 
“I have designed this machine to be activated by a single button, once 
everything has been set. As the button is pressed, a perfect copy of the 
subject’s organic data - specifically the position and momentum of all 
atoms - is created and saved, while the subject itself is being split into all 
its atoms by a bundled beam of light. Keep in mind that the number of 
atoms in an average human body is a seven followed by twenty -seven 
zeroes.” 
“Wow.” 
“That’s what the glass sphere is for, by the way. It helps bundling the 
light, coming from outside of it.” 
“I see.” 
“The thick black cable connecting the two travelling chambers is used 
for sending the atoms across, so that the subject can be assembled again. 
You have to understand that it would be impossible to create matter out of 
nothing.” 
“Makes sense, yeah.” Xave folded his arms. 
“It might sound plausible to prepare a stash of common atoms like 
oxygen, carbon and hydrogen, but it gets borderline impossible for atoms 
like uranium and beryllium.” 
“Uh-huh.” 
“Using the saved data the subject is recreated with its own atoms, using 
another beam, controlling the air in the target glass sphere with heat, to 
arrange the atoms in the right position.” 
“Okay.” 
Walter turned around. “Bear in mind that I’m basically circumventing 
the Heisenberg principle here, but I guess you’ve never heard of that. 
Anyway, that’s the gist of it.” 
“So, as far as I understand, the subject is dead for a few seconds?” Xave 
asked with raised eyebrows. 
Walter knew this would come. The whole lecture on ethics. 
“If you call a creature’s collection of atoms ‘alive’, then by that 
definition, it would still remain in the same state during the journey.”  
“You’re saying it can see itself getting sucked through the cable?” 
“No, I think it just suddenly wakes up in the target chamber. Just like a 
narcosis. The cable is cold to slow down any sort of decay.” Walter leaned 
on his machine. 
 
23 
“Slow down? But then how would you make long distance travelling 
possible?” 
“That is what I need to figure out.” 
“I see,” Xave said, looking at the ground. 
“I want to use a few more rats for testing, then I need an ape,” Walter 
explained. “I was hoping maybe you can help me in that matter.”  
“Well, how did you get the rats? Can’t you get an ape the same way?” 
“I’m afraid not, the pet shop in this area doesn’t sell any apes. I’m not 
even sure if it’s legal.” 
“Okay, I will take care of that,” Xave said. 
Walter thanked him. Maybe the deal hadn’t been such a bad idea at all. 
He started walking around in the basement, looking for the rat. 
“What are you doing?” Xave wanted to know, still filled with 
impatience. 
“I need my test subject,” Walter answered. 
“Don’t you have other rats?” 
“No, that was my last one,” Walter said and stopped looking. “It’s 
gone. I’m sorry, I can’t demonstrate my machine without a test subject. 
Well, I could use your wristwatch or other items, but I guess that’s not 
what you’re here for, is it?” 
“You’re right,” Xave said. “I’ll get your ape then.” 
“What? Now?” Walter blinked. 
“Yes. The sooner the better.” 
“But I can’t assure the ape’s safety,” Walter exclaimed. 
“I’m sure you’ve had enough of testing with rats. We’re in business 
now, and we’re going to be serious about it,” Xave said and left the 
basement. 
Walter scratched his chin. The humming sound of his machine seemed 
to crave a new test subject and support Xave’s actions. Like a chanting 
group of mayans preparing their human sacrifice. 
He shivered. 
 
24 
 
 
 
How to get an ape? Xave was driving through town in large circles, 
thinking of a possible solution. Normally in situations like these he’d 
simply visit the library and look for a book on how to deal with angry 
customers, or what to invest money in. There wasn’t a book on how to get 
an ape. A zoo displays apes. But Xave didn’t feel like a thief. Not that sort 
of thief stealing animals, anyway. Weren’t apes sometimes used in circus 
attractions? Maybe he could buy one. Circus people seemed to be 
“underground” enough for it to work. Besides, how did they get their apes 
in the first place? Xave thought of flying to Africa. Now that was 
ridiculous. Xave in Africa... What would his father do? His father…  
Xave shook his head. His father simply wouldn’t deal with apes. He’d 
get a human. Humans can be bribed into doing anything. And it’s 
probably cheaper than purchasing an ape, or flying to Africa. Xave knew 
exactly where he’d get a human willing to be a laboratory rat. For free.  
Thanks, father. I can always count on you. 
Xave turned right, heading back to Walter’s house, which was still 
patrolled by two policemen. A bunch of Star Trek fans were still gathered 
on the sidewalk in front of the scientist’s property, keeping their slogans 
high up on self-made wooden signs. 
Again, Xave parked his car a few meters away, and walked up to the 
Star Trek people. He counted seven of them; two women, five men. They 
all were dressed regularly, only one of the men wore plastic Spock ears.  
“Hey there,” Xave greeted them casually, receiving seven different 
versions of “Hey” in response. “How’s it going?” 
“We’re doing okay,” one of the women said. 
“Yeah, just fine,” a man said. 
“Everything is under control,” Spock reported. 
“That’s good to hear,” Xave continued. “You know, today is a really 
wonderful day. Don’t you agree?” 
“Yes, absolutely,” the same woman from before replied. 
“Indeed,” Spock said with a dry voice and a raised eyebrow. 

 
25 
“But you know what, for one of you this will be the best day ever,” 
Xave said with his salesman voice. “Because we have decided to let one of 
you get an inside look at the biggest invention of the century.” 
The little crowd jumped in excitement (all except Spock, naturally), the 
women screamed. Then everybody suggested he or she would be best 
suited. 
And while the others simply exclaimed their desires, Spock held a 
speech about his importance and overall qualification, but of course, Xave 
couldn’t really hear him. 
“Okay, calm down everyone,” Xave pleaded, holding up both hands. 
The crowd was silent. Fourteen eyes eagerly and impatiently stared at 
what Xave’s mouth had to announce. “I have here a black marker.” He 
took it out of his right pocket. “Every one of you will write a number 
between one and fifty on the palm of your hands. When we’re all done, I 
will call out a number, and the one closest to my number will come with 
me. Understood?” 
The crowd nodded with glee. All except Spock. He simply approved 
with his presence. Xave thought he was a damn good impersonator.  
“Alright,” Xave said, handing the black marker to one of the women. 
She turned away from the rest, looked into the sky, probably hoping to 
receive the number that would win, and then she carefully wrote the 
number down on the inside of her left hand, closing it and giving the 
marker to the next in the crowd. 
The policemen questioningly stared at Xave. He waved back, notifyin g 
them of the situation’s harmless intentions. 
Every one of the seven people had a closed left hand, and the marker 
went back to Xave. 
“Good,” Xave said, taking a deep breath. “My number is - one! Now, 
please show me your hands.” 
Spock had 50, the two women had 13 and 41, the other four men 
showed a 30, another 30, a 12 and a 37. One of the men was the winner; he 
exclaimed his joy with a loud “Yes”. Spock remained stiff and silent. The 
other fans moaned in disappointment. 
“Alright, come here. What’s your name?” Xave asked, holding his right 
arm around the short man of the guessed age of twenty. 
“Jeremy,” the man answered. 
Xave turned to the rest of the crowd, thanked them for their 
participation and wished them a good day. With Jeremy close behind him, 
Xave went back into the house. 
 
26 
“Walter, I’m back,” Xave announced, closing and locking the door after 
Jeremy. “Walter?” No response from the scientist. 
Xave turned to the winner. “The scientist is probably in the basement.” 
He smiled and noticed Jeremy was shivering with anxiety. “Follow me.” 
They both went down the wooden stairs and caught Walter working on 
one of the travelling chambers, with an extra magnifying pair of glasses on 
his nose. 
“Hello, Walter,” Xave said. 
“Ah, Xave, did you get me the ape already?” Walter asked, then looked 
up and took off his glasses. “Oh, I didn’t expect another visitor.” 
Xave laughed. “Well, he certainly isn’t an ape, right, Jeremy?” 
Jeremy just stood in awe and examined the basement’s machinery with 
an open mouth. 
“Walter, this is Jeremy, one of your greatest fans.” 
“I-I’m very honored to meet you,” Jeremy stuttered, holding out his 
hand like a robot. 
With a troubled face Walter shook the man’s hand. “What is this all 
about, Xave?” 
“Don’t you remember your request to find one person to be the first 
human traveller?” Xave explained. 
“What?” Jeremy jumped so high Xave was afraid he would hit his head 
on the ceiling. “This is too much, I-I’m just a humble guy, it’s truly not my 
destiny to be a part of something this big,” Jeremy said, eyes wide open, 
smiling from ear to ear. 
“What? I have-” Walter began. 
Xave knew Walter wouldn’t like this. “Walter, hold on a minute, I’ll be 
right back,” Xave said, ran up the stairs into the living room, and opened 
his suitcase lying on the old sofa. His fingers flipped through various 
documents. 
Where is it, where is it, come on, Xave. 
“Organisation is everything, son,” the voice of his father echoed from 
side to side in Xave’s head. “Organisation is everything, son. Organisation 
is-” 
“Got it!” Xave left out a sigh of relief, and quickly made his way back 
into the basement. 
“Before you can take part in this historic moment, you need to sign this 
paper, no strings attached,” Xave said, smiling persuasively, handing the 
document and a pen to Jeremy. The man quickly took both items without 
the slightest hesitation, clicked the pen and signed the paper. He returned 
the writing tool and the signed sheet to Xave. 
 
27 
“What’s it say?” Jeremy asked. 
Xave had trouble hiding his grin. Oh boy, you sure have a lot to learn in 
life. “It just states that we’re not responsible for anything happening to 
you.” 
“Oh,” Jeremy said and shrugged. 
 
Walter looked at the two men at the entrance to his basement and tried 
to cope with the situation. Going from a rat directly to a human in a chain 
of experiments resembled amateur work, mildly put. The life of the person 
named Jeremy was now in his hands. He had been able to handle the 
responsibility of the deaths of a dozen rats, but ... How could he ever get 
over killing a human? Walter couldn’t believe Xave seemed to take this all 
ever so lightly, just like a... a game. 
Life isn’t a game, Walter thought. Even, or rather especially as a scientist, 
life has a certain amount of value. Life is precious. And deep inside he kept 
apologizing to every animal and insect he had ever killed in his life, even 
though it had always been important for some kind of scientific progress. 
Why couldn’t Xave have simply brought an ape? No, it had to be a human. 
And knowing Xave a little already, Jeremy surely had been brought in 
under false assumptions. 
“So, Walter, are you ready to send the first human travelling?” Xave 
asked, still smiling. 
“I can’t believe I’m going to be beamed,” Jeremy mumbled, staring at 
the glass booths. 
Beaming. Walter stepped closer to Jeremy. “Beaming? My dear friend, 
beaming has nothing to do with my invention. Not in the least. Beaming is 
merely a trivial idea of some guy, trying to write a science fiction novel. 
Beaming is not a reality, while this,” he walked to the controls an d hit it 
twice, “this is solid, this is real. Based on an idea, yes, but you can see it, 
you can feel it, you can step into it.” 
Jeremy blinked. Xave smiled. 
“So, now that that’s out of the way, I guess Jeremy can ‘step into it’, 
right?” the salesman asked. 
Walter breathed deeply, calmed down and finally gave in. He looked 
down on his machinery and asked about its feelings, if it was ready to take 
a rather serious test. The machine looked confident. Or at least Walter 
imagined and hoped it would be. Walter nodded. “So be it,” he said, and 
opened the glass chamber designed to resemble the start of the travel.  
Jeremy hesitated. “Am I going to be alright?” 
Xave patted Jeremy’s back. “Of course!” 
 
28 
“Jeremy, I won’t force you to travel, but you should be aware of the 
significance of your role. A simple step into this glass booth paves the way 
for millions of other people,” Walter said in his serious voice. “I won ’t let 
you die.” 
Jeremy seemed satisfied, and slowly put his body inside the sphere.  
Walter trembled even more than Jeremy. This can’t turn out well, Walter 
thought to himself. This just has to fail. 
The scientist firmly closed the glass sphere, quickly turned to the 
controls, pushing several buttons in a strict and relevant order. The 
machine started to sing. The drums and trumpets marched along a few 
moments later. 
Through the sides of his eyes, Walter noticed Jeremy looking nervously 
from Xave to Walter. Jeremy’s mouth moved, but the sphere cut off his 
voice. Xave made a gesture to Jeremy that it was impossible for anybody 
outside the sphere to hear him. Thus, Jeremy gave up, closing his eyes.  
Walter pushed another array of buttons, looked at Jeremy, and quickly 
hit the blue button. 
Light filled the room, a loud ear-penetrating sound rode through the 
air, the floor vibrating with excitement. Indifferent, Walter stood by his 
controls and stared at the feedback of his instruments, while chaos 
unfolded around him. 
Everything working as usual, so far. 
The light disappeared, along with the noise. The room was now filled 
with the smell of burnt flesh. 
Walter waited a moment, expecting the worst, and slowly looked to the 
left where Jeremy had just been standing in the glass sphere. It was empty. 
No trace left of him. No loose body parts, no blood. He quickly forced 
himself to look to the right. 
A morphed creature, barely resembling a human being - that was what 
Walter had anticipated. But instead, he looked into the healthy eyes of a 
complete and intact Jeremy. 
With large steps Walter went to the destination booth, opened it and 
shook Jeremy’s hand. 
“Congratulations,” Walter said. 
Jeremy stumbled out of the sphere, fell on his knees and vomited on the 
ground. 
 
29 
 
 
 
Again, Walter rested in his second favorite chair in the living room, his 
arms stretched out. Xave was outside, talking to policemen about the noise 
of the travelling machine. Next to Walter was a young man lying on the 
couch in front of the TV. Jeremy had passed out after his stomach had 
decided to empty itself. The salesman and the scientist had carried him 
upstairs. Walter still felt the pain in his back from the weight of the 
unconscious body. 
This hadn’t been a good day up until then. Why had Xave insisted on 
trying it with a human prior to proper testing with an ape? 
He sighed and looked at Jeremy, still expecting the whole body to just 
fall apart, like the first few rats did. He remembered the unbearable high 
pitched shrieks of the little animals; whole body parts had started falling 
off of their trembling bodies, they had coughed up blood and shaken 
violently, until finally their hearts had given up. 
The news flash on the TV interrupted Walter’s gory thoughts. A 
woman with an emotionless face told a story about a scientist in Taresville 
claiming to have invented instant travelling. According to a recent 
interview the scientist’s only proof of said invention was a video, recorded 
in the man’s basement. 
Walter sat upright. He was on the news. 
Xave returned. “Anything wrong?” he said. 
Walter filtered out those words and kept staring at the woman still 
talking about a certain invention of him. 
“Today, Scientist Dr. Halver responded with a public statement that the 
video must have been faked,” the woman read off the teleprompter. A 
video clip was shown of Dr. Halver giving a press conference. 

 
30 
“After careful observation of the footage of Mr. Trimond’s teleportation 
device,” Dr. Halver said, “I am now certain that what we’re seeing is 
actually one of the oldest camera tricks since the invention of filming. 
Please watch the short video with me. Here you can see the scientist 
talking about the importance of his project, and how it works in theory - 
which by the way is absolute nonsense - and now he walks to his 
machinery, pressing buttons, obviously in random order. Now he puts a 
rat in a huge glass container, goes back to the console, and here - right 
there, a bright light, that’s exactly when the camera was stopped to put the 
rat out of the container. The light goes away, the rat is gone. The scientist 
now approaches the camera, turning it to the other glass container, 
showing us a happy little rat. And that’s it.” 
Walter shook his head. “What is wrong with people nowadays?”  
Xave shrugged. “Nothing to worry about. Envy is just one of the many 
things you’ll have to face on your way up. Apparently that man has 
nothing better to do than putting himself above others who have clearly 
beaten him. Poor old frustrated bastard.” 
Walter looked at Xave with raised eyebrows. 
“We have better things to do,” Xave continued, “than watching him act 
superior in front of the world.” He turned off the TV. “How’s Jeremy?” 
“Still sleeping, I suppose,” Walter answered, taking a quick glance at 
the Star Trek fan. 
Xave went to Jeremy and checked if he was breathing. 
“Seems fine, no scars, no blood,” Xave noted. “Then again, I’m a man of 
economics, not a man of Trekkies.” He walked away from Jeremy. “Can 
you continue improving your machinery while I watch over our little 
patient here?” 
“I guess,” Walter mumbled through his dry lips. Just then he felt weak, 
a bit out of place; everything around him went too fast, dragging him 
along with it on the ground. He slowly rose from his chair and walked 
down the stairs, taking each step like an old man with too much time, 
carrying his whole past on his shoulders. 
Improving my little machinery, Walter thought. Like it was some kind of 
toy. A childish tower construction, only built to be destroyed afterwards, 
watching earth’s gravity do its magic. He should be happy now. A human 
just travelled from one spot to another using his invention. What kept him 
from celebrating his success? 
The path ahead, he answered himself. There was darkness in front of 
him. He didn’t know where his next step would take him, yet he was 
forced to move forward. 
 
31 
Walter arrived at his mechanical child and put his hands on it, trying to 
sense if it felt alright. There was still a slight stench of burnt flesh in the air, 
but Walter was afraid of opening the window, fearing for the safety of his 
child. 
He rolled up his sleeves and said: “Let’s do this.” 
 
Xave slapped Jeremy a second time. “Wake up, kid!” 
No response. The rising and sinking chest was the only sign of life.  
If Jeremy wouldn’t wake up soon he’d have to call a doctor, only 
resulting in more negative rumors about Walter’s invention. With the 
situation already bad enough, Xave was glad that Walter had gone to the 
press immediately. Now the government or any shady organisations 
couldn’t try to steal the technology and keep it locked away for whatever 
reasons. The public knew about it now, even if it was just one of those 
headlines people would read in magazines while waiting for the dentist to 
call their names. 
Jeremy breathed in deeply and exhaled; Xave saw his patient opening 
his eyes. 
“How are you?” Xave inquired. 
“Where am I?” Jeremy asked the obligatory question. 
“Well, you had the privilege of being the first traveller, then you spread 
your last meal on the ground - causing the scientist some additional work - 
and passed out. We had to carry you up here and you’ve been out for a 
few hours.” Xave explained. 
Jeremy moaned and touched his head. “I’ve got a terrible headache.” 
“I suppose that’s because you were split into your atoms and put 
together afterwards. I guess it comes naturally.” 
“I ... I saw a bright light.” Jeremy began slowly, still holding his head, 
keeping his eyes closed. “Then absolute darkness. I felt pain, but it was 
distant, it hurt, but the pain didn’t fully materialize.” 
“Uh-huh,” Xave said. “Listen, you can’t talk to anybody about this until 
I allow you to do so, alright? This must be handled with care.” 
Jeremy weakly nodded twice. 
“Do I have your word?” Xave pushed. 
“Yes,” Jeremy assured. 
“Thanks, kid,” Xave said, patting Jeremy’s left shoulder. 
 
32 
 
 
 
“Today a young man named Jeremy called the press to tell the world 
that he was the first human traveller,” the news lady on the screen read. 
“Yesterday’s report of Dr. Halver’s opinion on what seems to be one of the 
most important inventions of the human race falls in direct contradiction 
with Jeremy’s experience.” 
Xave grinned at the TV, which showed Jeremy surrounded by a large 
group of reporters, covering his face in multi-colored microphones in 
various shapes and sizes. The camera shook wildly. 
“It was awesome, absolutely amazing. I’m sure y’all can’t wait to try it 
too. I heard it’s not gonna be long before everybody can use it,” Jeremy 
said loudly, breathing quickly, with a broad smile and bright eyes.  
“That kid has got some guts,” Xave mumbled to himself. He never 
expected Jeremy to break yesterday’s promise so fast. 
Time to check on the scientist, Xave thought. 
His black shoes led him down the stairs into the basement, filled with 
humming sounds of various frequencies. Walter was kneeling behind the 
console. 
“Ah, Xave,” Walter greeted the salesman and stood up, moving his 
glasses to the top of his head. “How are you doing?” 
“I’m pretty fine, actually,” Xave said. “The kid from yesterday spilt 
everything. Now Dr. Valve or whatever is out of the ring, I guess.” He 
scratched the back of his head and frowned. 
“People rather believe a twenty-year-old’s babble than a man with a 
title wearing a suit?” Walter took a few steps towards Xave. 
“It’s a TV world, Walt, that kid probably seems a lot more passionate to 
the audience than that old frustrated bastard,” Xave said. “Youth in itself is 
a big plus.” 
Walter shrugged. 
“Any improvements I should know of?” Xave asked. 

 
33 
The scientist eagerly rubbed his hands. “Oh, yes. Long story short, I 
found a way to cut down the required power for one travel. It ’s still far 
from efficient, but I’m getting there.” He yawned. “After one or two hours 
of sleep, I assume.” 
“What about that light? Do you think it can be...” Xave hesitated, 
drawing circles with his right hand. “You know, less painful to the eye?”  
“Ah, well,” Walter began. “I never thought this would become an issue, 
but I will try to dim the blinding results of the splitting procedure. But to 
tell you the truth, the bright light would spare the audience from seeing 
the traveller’s insides at that exact moment.” 
Xave nodded. “Okay,” he said. “When do you think it will be ready for 
the public? You know, with less energy consumption, increased range, and 
lower chance of becoming blind. Because I’m sure after that little 
information leak this morning people are going to be really up for a little 
travelling themselves, and we should use that opportunity.” 
“In all honesty?” Walter asked. 
“Sure, hit me.” 
“Probably a year.” 
“One year?” Xave exclaimed. “Why exactly did you go to the press 
with your invention when it wouldn’t be ready to use for another 
eternity?” 
“I just wanted the world to know that I did it first, that is all. I haven’t 
thought of it becoming big like this in such a short period of time.” Walter 
replied. 
“I need it in one month, Walter,” Xave said with a serious face. Time to 
get a little more pressure on the man, he thought. 
“What exactly do you need in one month? A killing machine?”  
Uh-oh, the scientist’s angry with me now. 
“I need one of those machines that sent the first human from that dusty 
corner to the other one on the opposite side right in this freaking room. I 
need it to be power efficient, I want it to look sleek, attractive, and I want it 
to send people from Taresville to Monoura. And I want people not losing 
their eyesight when cheering on travellers. If anyone can do this, it ’s you, 
Walt.” Xave pointed at the scientist. 
Xave felt the harsh pile of words he had just released looming over 
Walter’s head. The scientist shook his head and looked at his feet. 
“Well, what do I have to lose? Only one life left in that body of mine 
anyway,” he said and patted his chest. 
“Good man,” Xave said. “I’ll be back later.” 
 
34 
Outside of the house, Xave fought his way through a throng of 
reporters, who were shooting a variety of questions at him. 
“No comment,” Xave said without even listening to the words 
swarming him. 
Additional policemen were overlooking the scene from the corners of 
the front lawn. Xave sure was glad they were still sticking around. 
Otherwise the situation probably would’ve been ugly. 
After dodging a group of Trekkies storming the scene, Xave finally 
arrived at his car. 
Today was another milestone, he thought. He threw himself into his 
vehicle and looked into the driving mirror to check his tie, hair, eyebrows 
and nose hair. 
A winner. That’s what you are, Xave. 
The car drove away with shrieking tires. 
Alright. You got all the facts in your head, Xave. You will see the man, do a 
quick analysis, and adjust your behavior accordingly. You won’t sell anything to 
somebody who doesn’t want your product. Thus your product must wake a sudden 
desire in your target’s head. You know how to push the right buttons. You’ve done 
it before. Sure, it’s a lot bigger this time around, but everybody’s just a child 
anyway. Look at that man, look past the suit he’ll probably be wearing, see the 
young boy with eager eyes, playful, childish. And well, you still have plan B, 
right? 
Xave’s eyes met themselves in the rear-view mirror. 
Right? 
His left hand slowly touched the little bulge on his chest, barely visible 
through his suit. 
Xave’s eyes looked at the traffic again. 
 
35 
 
 
 
Lost deep in his thoughts, subconsciously counting the white stripes in 
front of his car on the highway, he was driving towards the village of 
Monoura, the stage of his plan. He did have a plan, at least he thought so. 
Having a plan always makes one appear more confident, even i f it’s sewn 
together on the go. 
A loud shot ripped his mind back into reality. Xave’s head darted back 
and forth, left and right, trying to find the cause for that sudden 
interruption. Only then he realized that his car wasn’t driving as smoothly 
as before, rather as if it were bouncing over several small rubber balls. 
“A tire,” he mumbled to himself. “Goddamn it.” Hearing his own voice 
made him feel a bit calmer. 
His car was already at a very low speed, as he saw a guy standing and 
waving next to the outmost lane. He had intended to stop there anyway to 
check the damage and parked a few meters after the person he had just 
seen. 
He got out, minding the fast moving traffic right next to him, and 
checked the tires. He cursed again as he saw a saggy, black mass right 
where a round shape used to be. He turned away, only to face it again, 
kicking it once. The car creaked. 
“Hello there,” a voice greeted Xave. 
He checked its source and saw a man in blue suspenders, the same man 
who had waved at him before. Right before Xave could reply, the man 
knelt down in front of the tire. 
“Do you have a spare one in the trunk? I’m a mechanic, I could fix it for 
you if you can’t do it yourself,” the man offered. 
“Sure, that would be great,” Xave accepted. His spirits lifted a bit . 
Maybe he would still be able to make the deal today. 
They shook hands. “Xave,” the salesman said. 
“Rick,” the mechanic replied and began his work by opening the trunk 
and pulling out the replacement tire. 

 
36 
“I’m a bit amazed by how quick you are to help me out,” Xave said 
after a while of awkward silence. He took a glance at Rick’s vehicle, a small 
van, which had seen better days. “You seem to be having a problem of 
your own.” 
Rick cleared his throat and looked up, while removing the old tire from 
Xave’s car, which, in its lifted pose, looked like a dog, marking a tree. “My 
problem seems to have caused yours.” He had to squint at Xave, for the 
sun blinded him. 
“How so?” Xave asked. 
A few more jabs and turns and Xave’s car was ready to roll again. Rick 
stood up, wiping his hands on his jeans, which were already filled with 
black spots. 
“I tried to warn you by waving, but I guess I was too far away for you 
to notice.” Rick wiped his forehead. “My car lost a part of its exhaust pipe 
and you seem to have run right over it. At least that seemed to have sent it 
flying off the road, so nobody else is gonna have a blown tire.” 
Xave frowned, but said nothing. Rick held on to the broken tire under 
his arm. “I’m gonna take care of that for you,” he explained. 
“Thanks,” Xave said shortly. 
“If you ever need any repairs or anything, I’ll give you a good price.” 
Rick handed Xave a card. “Good day.” 
The mechanic went back to his van, stuck the tire in the back and 
disappeared below to check on his problem. 
“Good day,” Xave shouted. He looked at the card in his right hand. 
Rick Lorand. Might come in handy, he thought. 
He shook his head, inhaling deeply, as if trying to wake up from a 
dream, put the card into one of his suit’s pockets and drove on. 
 
37 
 
 
 
In the afternoon Xave arrived at Monoura, a town so small it was barely 
justified being called something larger than a human settlement. He 
probably had a single shot at it, but he could always try other locations. 
Still, it’d be better if it worked the first time. 
And it will work the first time, he reassured himself, while getting out of 
the car. 
A tall white house towered in front of him, surrounded by a luxurious 
garden, guarded by two men in suits. Surely they had noticed Xave 
already. 
The salesman approached the house and was stopped by a raised hand 
of the guard on the right. Supported by the neck of a bull, he slowly 
moved his head to stare Xave to death. “Name?” 
“Xave Stern,” Xave said, offering his hand. The gesture was ignored 
and the hand floated awkwardly between them, before it retreated. 
The guard folded his arms in front of his chest. “You got no 
appointment, do you?” the guard thundered. 
“If you’re required to come up with appointments, please do, I don’t 
have all day.” Xave risked a lot by being so straightforward, but he forced 
himself to feel confident, believing that it would show and intimidate. Or 
at least make a minor impression. 
“What is your intent?” the guard asked, his deep voice vibrating in 
Xave’s ears. 
The other guard shifted his weight and continued to stare into the 
distance, making a tough face. 
“I have an offer the mayor won’t be able to refuse,” Xave explained, as 
sinister as he could. “This is a historical moment he wouldn’t want to pass 
up.” 
“Do your rugs smell of autumn leaves?” the guard asked, probably 
intended as sarcasm, but he didn’t look like he was joking. 

 
38 
“I’m not selling rugs, I’m offering a bright future for a bargain price,” 
Xave replied, removing every trace of a smile from his expression to keep 
up with the staring contest. 
Both guards looked at each other, then the talkative one faced Xave 
again. “The mayor is not in.” 
“I shall wait while you give him a call,” Xave said and tried to move 
past the gorillas. 
A wall out of flesh and bones blocked his way. The guards were calm as 
a lake, their cold eyes killing everything in sight. 
Xave gave up. “I’ll wait in the car then.” 
The mayor arrived half an hour later in a black limousine. Xave jumped 
out of his vehicle and shot words at the small, round man with the hat.  
“Mayor of Monoura, I presume?” Xave began. 
“Yes,” the mayor said, the guards already behind him. 
“Mr. Mayor, this man wanted to speak to you in an urgent matter, he 
states that he is offering a bright future for a bargain price,” one of the 
guards said. 
The mayor scoffed and looked at Xave. Seeing no trace of jest in the 
salesman’s figure, the round face lost its demeaning expression and turned 
into a visage of curiosity. 
“Very well, come on in.” The mayor went into the house, taking each of 
the three steps slowly with only his left foot reaching for the next. Xave 
followed inside, trying not to mind the overpowering presence of the two 
muscle packs. 
“Take a seat,” the politician offered once they were inside his office, 
without signalling any chair or other possible sitting location. 
Xave decided to stand and begin right away, before the mayor could sit 
down. The sun was already setting, as he noticed through the one wide 
window, which was framed by dark brown draperies. 
“What would you say if I offered you a way to transport people 
instantly from here to Taresville?” Xave shot right for the bullseye. 
Startled, the mayor turned around, looking at Xave as if somebody had 
pinched his butt. His forehead featured a meaty frown with three sweaty 
lanes. 
“What?” the mayor said with a high voice. 
“Imagine,” Xave started, walking to his target person and holding an 
arm around his shoulder. The confused mayor played along, the two 
guards tensed up. 
 
39 
“Imagine a device the size of that cupboard over there.” Xave pointed a 
thumb at said furniture in the corner. “Picture yourself stepping into it, 
and a mere second later, stepping out of another cupboard in, say,” Xave 
paused, took a quick look around and saw photographs of various holiday 
locations. “Venezia,” he finished. 
Xave noticed that he had the mayor’s attention. 
Now to reel him in. 
“Imagine the people of your town being the first to experience instant 
travelling, look at how their lives would improve.” Xave’s free hand 
painted an imaginary picture into the air, followed by the mayor ’s 
intrigued gaze. “How does that sound?” 
The mayor blinked, coughed briefly and said: “It sure sounds nice, but 
if such a device existed, why wouldn’t there be more about it in the news?” 
“Nobody believes it except a small handful of people.” Xave looked at 
the mayor as if to say, people like you. 
“Who says I believe it?” the mayor rebelled, marched to his throne-like 
chair and sank into it, almost crushing it. “You’re telling me beaming 
exists? Here? Today? And nobody knows about it because nobody believes 
it?” 
I didn’t say beaming, Xave thought. “Exactly.” 
The mayor laughed heartily. “You’ve been watching too much 
television.” He waved at him in a dismissing manner. 
Xave remained silent, but kept a knowing smirk on his mouth.  
The round man’s laugh ebbed away into a giggle, then into a drawn out 
humming sound. 
He must be realizing that I’m not joking around, Xave noted, keeping his 
calm posture. 
“Show me,” the mayor gnarled, annoyed at the fact that he was both in 
disbelief and dying of curiosity. He rose from his chair like an avalanche 
from a mountain, and led Xave outside. 
 
40 
 
 
 
Walter turned on the light for the basement. 
Nightfall again, he thought. And still so much to do. 
He dragged his feet to a large table where he kept a part of his 
schematics and plans, and leaned against it with both hands flat on the 
surface. 
Sighing deeply, he stood upright again, squeezing the nasal bone 
behind his glasses, closing his eyes for a short while. 
There was a commotion outside. 
Walter looked at the basement windows, straightened the spectacles, 
went upstairs and opened the front door. 
A limousine parked rudely on his lawn, driving through a throng of 
cheering people, who got held back by brave uniformed men. The motor 
stopped. 
Walter’s heart leaped. What now? 
The driver quickly exited the noble vehicle to assist his passengers. 
The black automobile spilled out four people: two examples of 
successful muscle growth therapy; a short, well fed chap with a hat, and - 
“Xave!” Walter shouted to drown out the noise of the chattering people.  
“What is the meaning of this? Haven’t we had enough publicity?” 
A man wearing an antennae cap escaped the clutches of the police and 
ran up to the scientist, showering him with questions and eager spit.  
Walter shielded himself from the wordy and watery attack using both 
of his hands, until one of the officers stepped between them and dragged 
the naughty sheep back to its herd. 
Xave went back inside the house, gesturing the other three figures and 
Walter to follow him. Walter closed the door behind them and took a deep, 
exhausted breath. 
“Walter, this is the mayor of Monoura, Petar Toringen, and his two 
guards,” Xave introduced. “Mr. Toringen - Walter Trimond, the scientist I 
told you about.” 
They all shook hands one after another. 

 
41 
“It is an honor to have you in my house,” Walter said humbly. “May I 
offer you something to drink? I fear I only have tap water, but-” 
“Just show me the beaming device,” the mayor demanded bluntly, 
hiding his hands behind his back. 
“Very well.” Walter, startled by the direct inquiry, turned to the 
staircase down the basement. “Don’t hit your heads on the way down,” he 
muttered without looking. 
“This is it?” Petar said in disbelief as he laid eyes on the two glass 
booths. He chuckled heavily; one of the guards snorted, and shook his 
head. 
Walter said nothing. He looked at Xave and wondered what was going 
on in his head. 
Apparently, Xave knew what was coming. 
“You,” Petar said to the giggling bear in a suit. “Try it.” 
“Me?” the guard said. 
“Sure, nothing’s gonna happen,” the mayor assured him. “That or 
you’re the first person to be beamed.” 
Walter bit his lip. Neither would he be the first, nor would he be 
beamed. That last word made him grimace in disgust. 
“What’s wrong?” the mayor addressed Walter. 
The scientist put on a smiling face and offered the guinea bull to step 
inside one of the cells. “Careful,” the scientist warned him and moved 
some black wires out of the way. 
As Walter had closed the glass booth, the mayor grabbed Xave by his 
collar and said: “He better not die.” 
The guard in the beaming cell looked terrified all of a sudden. 
“Not to worry,” Xave stuttered, freeing himself from the grasp. He 
straightened his collar, and nodded at Walter. 
Elaborately, the scientist slid his glasses in his coat’s chest pocket and 
put on a pair of safety goggles. He played the array of buttons like a 
pianist, strict in technique, beautiful in its purpose. The machinery awoke 
to his commands, marching along, rising in speed and tone. A short pause, 
to let the build-up sink in - then Walter firmly nudged the blue button. 
Blazing white light filled the room. Both the mayor and the other guard 
exclaimed in shock; Xave coolly shielded his eyes with one hand. 
The brightness level returned to normal and everyone except Xave and 
Walter rubbed their eyes and tried to adjust their vision to see the results. 
The mayor clapped once and pressed his hands together. “My God,” he 
blurted out. 
 
42 
Walter opened the glass booth, which had been empty a moment ago, 
and the short distance traveller took a wobbly step forward, forcing 
himself not to succumb to his obvious bad feelings. Another step, though, 
and the bodyguard threw up on the ground. Annoyed, the scientist 
removed his goggles, shook his head and put his hands on his hips.  
The mayor turned to Xave and almost stumbled upon his own words. 
“I have to- When can we- How much do you-” Once again, just to make 
sure, he took a good gander at his guard, making sure he was in one piece. 
Then, like shooting an arrow, he stuck out his hand with wide open eyes 
towards the salesman. 
Xave shook it. 
 
43 
 
 
 
The next morning, Walter helped the postman wrestle away the drowsy, 
yet passionate people who apparently had decided to set up camp. The 
three policemen, who were assisting Walter’s attempt of accepting his 
mail, seemed to be new faces; probably a different shift. 
In a matter of seconds, Walter, now holding quite a few letters and 
postcards, went back inside and closed the door, leaving the others to their 
own fate. 
He let himself fall on his thinking chair and went through the mail. 
Appreciation, congratulations, admiration, death threats, love letters, 
small monetary and some questionable donations, a photography of a thin 
man in his forties holding the interview with Walter next to his grinning 
face, and - 
Wait, death threats? 
Humbug. He balled up the mysterious paper and threw it in a corner. 
Ah, there was something of interest. A postcard from his parents.  
 
My dear Walter, 
having a good time here in Tahiti, 
not sure where we’ll go next, but we’ll 
know by the time you read this! 
Hope you are all right! 
Love and hugs, 
Mom and Dad 
 
Walter recognized his mother’s handwriting. She always took care of 
crafting these cards. 
Tahiti. 

 
44 
He stood up, mindlessly letting the rest of the letters and cards drop to 
the floor like dead leaves, and walked around the chair to a wall that had 
been constructed in the center of the room as a sort of separation. On the 
side facing the window there were maybe forty postcards pinned to a cork 
surface board. Walter searched for a good spot, nabbed a red pin and 
placed the newly acquired card. For a moment he stared at the photo of 
Tahiti. Beach, sea, people in swimwear, palm trees, blue sky. 
Soon, he contemplated, he would be able to simply visit his parents. No 
matter where they were. In a matter of seconds. 
He smiled and tapped the postcard twice, as if telling his parents to 
wait a minute, he would be right there. Inhaling and exhaling deeply, he 
drew his thoughts back to where he needed them and went down into the 
basement. 
 
45 
 
 
 
Both windows were still broken, but Xave had glued a broad towel in front 
of them with brown tape. It would do for now, since it was Summer. He ’d 
find an actual solution at another time. 
At least the remains of the bird, the feathers, the blood and the glass 
shards were gone. Xave had stayed up late the night before to clean up the 
mess. 
That was reason number two why he had overslept. 
The actual reason: He was simply exhausted. Not from scrubbing the 
floor, but from this whole endeavour of trying to bring Walter’s invention 
to the masses. 
He had done well so far, he thought, getting the mayor of Monoura to 
accept an agreement before even trying to reach the mayoress of Taresville, 
a town with a much stronger population than the mere five thousand 
people of Monoura. 
Xave had explained to his new partner that Taresville’s leading 
politicians hadn’t been informed about the plan yet. Petar, Monoura’s 
mayor, had said that he would take care of that. 
Xave sipped his cold coffee, made a disgusted face and poured the rest 
of the cup’s contents down the drain. 
He stuck three fingers into the left pocket of his shirt, where the bulge 
had given away something hidden, and produced a gold watch. It wasn ’t 
ticking. 
A slight squeeze and the lid sprung to the side, revealing the actual 
clock, its hands pointing at Roman numbers. Three minutes past eight. 
Inside the lid a small round picture showed a couple, the man wearing an 
expensive tuxedo, the woman a long and wide dress, her décolleté 
generous, yet elegant, surrounded by a thin line of animal fur. A discreet 
flower arrangement held up her hair into a neat knot. 
For a minute Xave examined the object in his hand in silence.  
“Plan B,” he whispered to himself. 
“Always think of a backup”, his father would say.
