46 
Fortunately, now was not the time. He closed the watch carefully, 
drawing out the motion so that there were two audible clicks instead of 
one, and put it back into his pocket, where it formed the familiar bulge 
again; a constant reminder. 
The phone rang. 
Xave cleared his throat and picked up. “This is Xave Stern. State your 
business.” 
“Xave,” Walter spoke through the speaker with a muffled tone. “I need 
help.” 
“What happened?” Xave felt anxiety rising. 
“I can’t do the changes alone in time, I need a second hand, someone 
who’s good with the handiwork.” 
“Ah,” Xave exhaled and calmed down. There was no danger. “Another 
scientist then?” 
“No, scientists are weak,” Walter generalized. “I need someone who 
can lift heavy materials, move them around, install cables, replace parts, 
you know?” 
Xave felt there was something Walter was trying to convey, so he 
waited. 
“Someone to do the dirty work,” Walter finally revealed. 
“I was just about to look for a larger place, too,” Xave said. “An 
abandoned storeroom, or a factory or something. Your basement is already 
too small with more than one person standing in it.” 
“Sounds good.” 
“But, I’ll have to ask the mayor for some money or find another sponsor 
first.” Xave scratched his unshaven chin. 
“No need, I’ll take care of the money issue,” the distant voice of the 
scientist assured. 
Xave had actually hoped for that. He was never really sure where all 
the stuff in the basement came from, or why Walter was living alone in a 
house suited for a family with two, maybe three children. Now the 
scientist had confirmed Xave’s suspicions about Walter’s wealthy family. 
“Good,” Xave said. “I’ll get back to you when I have found you an 
assistant and a new place.” 
“Thanks.” 
Xave was about to say goodbye, when Walter said: “I got a death threat 
letter.” 
So, there was danger! Xave inhaled, straightened his back and rested his 
free hand on his hip. “What did you do with it?” 
 
47 
“I threw it away. But maybe I should pay more attention to it,” Walter 
thought aloud. 
“I’ll look into it when I meet you again. Thanks for telling me. Stay 
away from the windows.” 
“Stay away from the windows?” Walter asked in a high tone. “It’s not 
like I’m getting targeted by snipers now, am I?” 
“Just to be on the safe side, we’ll assume the worst. For now, just 
concentrate on the improvements, please. I’ll be there soon.” Xave hung 
up, rushed to the door and left his apartment. 
The rooms lay silent for a while, dust sailed through shy sunrays and 
the towel in front of the windows waved gently. 
One minute passed, then Xave stormed back into his apartment, 
grabbed his gun off the kitchen table and was on his way again, slamming 
the door shut behind him with a loud bang and locking it twice. 
Always have a backup plan, he reminded himself. 
 
48 
 
 
 
It was a small workshop that employed Rick. Only two cars could be 
worked on at the same time, the others had to be parked outside, awaiting 
their turn. Business was good, given the small size of the company. There 
were only three employees and the owner, who didn’t actually touch any 
of the cars, but rather stayed behind the scenes and was rarely seen. 
Every day was usually a rough day. They all did good work, fast, 
efficient, and with nothing to complain about, and customers kept 
recommending them and spreading the word. 
Rick was about to replace the windshield wipers on a red Volksw agen, 
when he saw a rundown red Opel Omega A approach the workshop and 
stop right in front of him. He froze in his motion, holding a loose damaged 
wiper in his right hand and the two replacements in his other one, while 
staring at the guest. 
A man in a suit exited the car. “Rick!” he greeted from afar with a 
raised hand. When he reached Rick, the raised hand was offered to shake 
his. 
Rick sprang back to life, tucked the two new wipers under his arm and 
grabbed the salesman’s hand. “You,” he said. “From the highway 
yesterday, right? Xave, if I remember correctly.” 
“A fine memory,” Xave smiled, showing his teeth. 
Rick hadn’t expected a cashing in of that generous offer of a reduced 
price so soon, not when he was swamped with work already. 
He realized his hands were dirty and apologized to Xave. 
“Ah, never mind that,” Xave said. 
“What can I do you for?” Rick asked, wiped his hand on his jeans, put 
the wipers on the hood of the beetle, and nodded in the direction of the 
Opel. “Anything that needs fixing?” 
“No, but I do need your help,” Xave answered mysteriously. 
“If it’s not your car, I’m afraid I can’t offer any other skills,” Rick said, 
wiping his nose with the back of his hand, sniffing loudly. 

 
49 
“My business partner, a scientist by the name of Walter Trimond, is 
working on a very important machine and needs a hand that is used to 
working with complicated devices,” Xave elaborated. “And I immediately 
thought of you.” 
Rick looked perplexed. “What sort of machine?” 
“You may have heard about it. It sends people to other locations in a 
matter of mere seconds.” 
Rick had a hard time judging the man’s character, and thought it best to 
decline and not risk anything. 
“That sounds wonderful, but as you can see, I already have a job and 
I’m quite good at it.” Rick turned his body slightly away from Xave, as if 
trying to end the conversation. 
Xave took one step closer, making Rick feel awkward. What could he 
do to shake that guy off? 
“Listen,” Xave began. “What are you earning right now?” 
Rick hesitated. He saw a bulge through the salesman’s chest pocket, 
wondering what it was. “One and a half grand, plus tips,” he said, not 
mentioning his work on the side. 
 “We’ll pay you three times that,” Xave offered. 
Rick frowned. 
“This is a one-time chance, friend,” the man continued. “You helped 
me, so I’ll try to help you. You’re married with children, right?” 
Rick looked at his wedding ring and nodded. 
“Good, here’s what you’re going to do. Talk to your wife and kids-” 
“Only one son,” Rick interfered. 
“Talk to your wife and son. I’ll be back tomorrow and ask you again, 
but only once. Sound fair?” 
There seemed to be no harm in that, Rick thought. At least the man 
would go away. For now. 
“Sure, I’ll do that.” 
A triumphant and satisfied expression formed on Xave’s face. “Good 
man. See you tomorrow!” The salesman turned around, waved goodbye 
without looking back, sat down in his car and left. 
“Who was that?” Jamey asked, appearing from behind the beetle. Rick’s 
co-worker, a short guy with horn-rimmed glasses, had apparently 
eavesdropped on the discussion. 
“Not sure,” Rick said, returning attention to the wipers. He still had to 
process everything. 
“Well, do you take the job?” Jamey pushed his glasses up with his 
index finger and seemed excited. 
 
50 
“Gonna have to ask the wife, see what she thinks about it,” he 
mumbled over his shoulder, trying to concentrate on attaching the new 
wipers. 
“Well,” Jamey said, shrugged and returned to the car he was working 
on. “He wouldn’t have to ask me twice.” 
Rick looked at him thoughtfully. 
 
51 
 
 
 
Kristella’s gaze wandered off, away from her desk with piles of delivery 
notes on top, over to the window to her right, offering a view across the 
city. 
She sighed and continued touch-typing away on the keyboard with 
impressive speed, the broad ring on her right hand rhythmically clicking 
against the keys. 
Her task was to enter all physical shipment data of the past three years 
into this new thing called “computer”, which was basically a gray box with 
a slightly larger gray monitor on top of it. The flickering screen usually 
gave her a headache by the end of the day, and it refused to show any 
colors other than black, white and more depressing shades of gray – when 
even her TV at home could do better. 
Being a young apprentice, she didn’t have much of a reason to 
complain. She got a job in an office, exactly what she had wanted, but after 
three months of staring into a fuzzy box she felt kind of stupid for not 
pursuing her parents’ idea of a good job - waitress, for example. At least 
something where she’d be dealing with people and not be stowed away 
like a dusty carton with old memories. 
Yes, she was working alone in her tiny room and her sole responsibility 
was to work through the towers of paper that decorated and surrounded 
her desk, some stacks leaning dangerously against a wall, on the brink of 
collapsing. 
Typing came naturally to her, that’s why she got the job. She was fast. 
But wouldn’t she rather be amongst people all day, with the chance of 
meeting, you know, that special someone? 
All the dating options in there were a small statue of a silver unicorn on 
her desk - a long-time lucky charm of hers - and moths. Not quite the 
bright future she had hoped to look forward to. 
She took a break and stood up, stretching. It was hot outside and even 
more so inside. The air felt heavy on the lungs, opening the window didn’t 
help much, but she did so anyway. 

 
52 
Summer definitely wasn’t her favourite season. 
Walking around the desk, she caught sight of the small heap of mail she 
had been given at the entrance of the skyscraper earlier this morning. Her 
boss had given her the extra duty of handling all incoming letters. What 
she found were mostly invoices she had to prepare for payment, orders she 
had to forward, various ads for office utilities, business clothes, and the 
latest craze in electronic devices - and finally, this day’s newspaper. 
It wasn’t a well-respected paper, but her boss had a subscription for it 
anyway. 
She sat down with it at her table, fell back in her chair, and started 
skimming through the headlines, enjoying the break. 
Buried in excessively attention grabbing articles there was a story that 
snatched her interest. She slowly leaned forward and read the details. 
Apparently, a scientist seemed to have found a way to teleport people, 
transporting them from one spot to another almost instantly. The paper 
made him look like a fool and the tone of the chosen words were rather 
degrading. Clearly, this article was written to entertain the reader by 
underlining the stupidity of others. 
Kristella dismissed it and turned a few pages. 
Then a thought struck her mind and she looked at the city’s skyline 
again. The sun was resting high above, heating up the buildings as if 
trying to burn them to ashes. 
What if it were true? 
She returned to the page about the scientist’s invention and read the 
column word by word this time. 
“A man by the name Walter Trimond, residing in Taresville, has 
allegedly found what so many professional, highly skilled and trained 
scientists have failed to discover: A contraption that is able to beam people 
around. Sure, and while he’s having a streak of genius, why not find a cure 
for cancer, or a way to travel at light speed, or hell, why not go all out and 
come forward with a time machine? Beaming is so last year. Besides, 
Walter gets angry when you call it ‘beaming’, according to an interview we 
did in a previous issue. He prefers the term ‘travelling’. Maybe that’s 
because he actually didn’t find a way to beam people. Maybe he simply 
knocks them out, drags them to their destination and splashes water into 
their faces to wake them up. If you decide to ‘beam’ around on your next 
holiday trip using Walter’s device, be aware of side effects like losing your 
wallet or having a new haircut on the other end. Walter’s surely working 
on a fix, right?” 
Taresville, Kristella thought. He basically lives right around the corner! 
 
53 
She could phone the paper staff, persuasively ask for the address and 
be there in half an hour after work and see for herself. 
She shrugged and a grin appeared on her face. 
Why not? 
 
54 
 
 
 
In the evening, Rick returned to his apartment on the second floor of a five 
story high residential building and was greeted by the smell of vegetable 
soup. 
“Dad?” his son Bernand asked from the living room. 
“Yes, it’s me,” Rick answered and closed the door. 
He left his brown jacket on a hook and his shoes in a small cupboard in 
the small entrance area and went around the corner to greet his child, 
sitting on the couch in front of the TV, playing on the video game system 
he had been given last Christmas; something called a “Nintendo”, Rick 
vaguely recalled. 
“How are you, kid?” Rick asked, standing next to him, watching the 
tiny hands fiddle with the rectangular controller. 
“I can’t see!” he responded, craning his neck to the left, almost falling to 
the side. 
His wife Roanne appeared next to Rick and kissed him on his bearded 
cheek. “You look tired,” she noted warmly. 
“I am. Lots of new clients today, they said that a bunch of other 
workshops were closed today for vacation. What a coincidence, right?” 
Rick told her. 
Roanne smiled, but her expression seemed strained. 
“You’re having a hard time too, haven’t you?” Rick noticed. 
“Just feeling a bit dizzy, that’s all,” Roanne said. “Let’s eat.” 
A few moments later they were all sitting in the kitchen at the dining 
table, slurping the hearty soup. 
“A man in a suit came up to me today,” Rick began, digging his spoon 
deep under the vegetables and noodles. “It was the same guy I helped out 
on the highway yesterday.” 
“What did he want from you?” Roanne asked without losing focus on 
her meal. 
“He offered me a job with three times the pay.” 

 
55 
Roanne let her spoon hit the plate and stared at him, swallowing a 
chewed carrot. “What kind of job? Do you have to kill somebody?” 
“No,” Rick chuckled, trying to lessen the tension. “It’s a science project. 
I think it’s supposed to beam people around, you know, like in that science 
fiction series. Star Hike?” 
“Cool!” Bernand yelled out with a sparkle in his eyes, almost spilling 
his soup. 
“Shh,” Roanne said with a finger held in front of her lips. “The 
neighbors are asleep!” 
“That’s so awesome, Dad,” Bernand whispered with glee. 
Rick blinked. “You think?” 
Roanne shook her head. “It sounds pretty dangerous, if you ask me.”  
“You’re right,” Rick said. He hadn’t made up his mind yet, so he was 
pretty quick to change sides. 
“But he’s going to be famous! My friends are gonna be so jealous!” 
Bernand almost burst with excitement. 
A grin forced itself on Rick’s mouth. “It does pay a lot,” he argued. 
Roanne shrugged. “I guess so. We could get a bigger apartment. 
Bernand’s not getting smaller.” 
“I could try it for a week and get another mechanic job if it doesn’t 
work out,” Rick offered. 
Everyone had paused eating by now. Roanne leaned forward and 
rested her elbows on the table. “But what if something happens?”  
“To be honest, my current job is probably a lot more dangerous,” Rick 
said, fuelled by his kid’s enchanted face. “Cars could crush me every day, 
there are lots of sharp tools lying around, I lift dozens of heavy objects, 
every spark could send me flying.” 
Roanne thought about it for a few seconds, inhaling deeply. “One 
week,” she said in a worried voice. “If there’s even one tiny scratch on you, 
you’ll find something else.” 
“Promise.” 
“Yes!” Bernand yelled, throwing both arms in the air as if he had won 
the world. 
His mother reprimanded him again, and they finished their lukewarm 
supper. 
 
56 
 
 
 
Kristella had called five different people of the paper to reach one man 
without moral conscience and get the address. 
“Everything for a beautiful voice like yours,” he had flirted. 
She had wanted to slap that guy through the receiver, but that didn’t 
matter anymore. 
She was sitting in a bus in the direction of her destination, and exited 
after ten or fifteen minutes. Looking around, she oriented herself and 
headed straight into the correct street. She was counting down the 
numbers on the passing houses, when she noticed a small crowd in the 
distance. 
This must be it, she thought, and walked faster. 
In the dim light of the setting sun, there were two police cars parked 
right in front of the fence, a silver van of a radio station on the opposite 
side, and people in alien costumes, holding signs such as “Beam me up, 
Walter!” and “We believe!” 
Policemen were restricting the area, probably making sure nobody 
would approach the house without permission. Kristella only spotted 
three, so there had to be others patrolling around the building. 
Disappointed, she realized she probably wouldn’t see the scientist. On 
the other hand, what would she say to him? It was bound to be awkward 
anyhow. Testing the new way to travel sounded promising in her mind, 
though. 
She fought her way through the aliens and cyborgs and tipped a cop on 
his shoulder. 
“Please don’t touch me, I represent the law,” the uniformed man said 
sarcastically. He lowered his head and looked her up and down. “But 
there’s probably a paragraph with you as an exception.” 
Kristella groaned inside. Well, maybe there was a way in after all.  
“I really have to see Walter Trimond, it’s very urgent,” Kristella 
pleaded, playing with her hair. 

 
57 
“I’m sorry, I can’t let anyone through, not even a beauty like you,” the 
cop said like a sleazy snail. 
Beauty always comes with the added flavour of having to deal with jerks like 
you, she wanted to reply. 
Instead she pouted. “Oh, I see.” 
“If she gets to see him, we’re going in too,” said a green man next to 
Kristella. 
“Yeah, me too,” a blocky silver robot said, his voice sounding male and 
damp. 
“See? No exceptions,” the policeman insisted. 
A loud diesel engine noise seemed to come closer and a moment later, 
Kristella saw a truck turning into the street. It drove past the house, halted 
and went into reverse with flashing white lights and a high, regular 
beeping sound. 
To everyone’s surprise, the driver didn’t seem to care much for the 
bystanders, as the truck forced its way backwards towards the scientist’s 
house. Chaos broke out, some wanted to evade to the right, others to the 
left, bumping into each other, causing more panic. 
Seeing no other way out, Kristella snuck past the dumbstruck 
policeman, who was trying to deal with the situation by calling out to the 
extra-terrestrials to remain calm and to the driver to look out. 
Eventually, a path cleared and the truck slowly but surely pushed its 
rear tires through the lawn and stopped a meter before the entry door. 
Kristella quickly hid behind a rain barrel, crossing her fingers in hope 
of not getting discovered. She imagined an old man being the judge at 
court and declaring: “Well, darling, seems like you’ve been trespassing, 
we’ll have to hang you by your sweet little neck. Unless you marry me, of 
course.” 
She shuddered. 
Four policemen closed off the entrance to the area behind the truck, and 
some costumed fans reluctantly returned back to their idle occupation.  
The door on the driver’s side of the truck opened, and after someone 
had jumped out it was closed again. Kristella could only see the right side 
of the truck, which had a giant advertisement of a furniture store printed 
on it. 
Then somebody opened the house’s door. 
A high male voice called out a name and said: “You are truly a 
blessing.” 
“Enough with the flattery, Walter,” another voice said, probably that of 
the driver. “Let’s pack up.” 
 
58 
Someone opened the back of the truck and Kristella heard a dozen 
more footsteps. 
“I’ve brought help,” the second voice said. “They’re from the furniture 
store on the other end of the city. I hired these guys along with the truck I 
rented from the same company.” 
Kristella remembered last year when her company had to move to the 
new building and saw the vivid images of a similar truck in her mind. 
She heard everyone going in, then the higher voice – Walter? - asked: 
“What about the other helping hand I asked you for?” 
The door closed behind them and Kristella only heard an 
indistinguishable mumble in reply. 
Her heart drummed an aggressive beat and she quickly went through 
her options. 
First: Wait until everyone is gone and then try to simply walk away.  
Second: Knock on the door and offer help. 
Third... 
She sprang to her feet, dashed across the grass and jumped into the 
open truck. The storage space appeared a lot roomier than from outside. It 
measured approximately three meters in height and four or five meters in 
length. And to her relief, it wasn’t completely empty. There were three 
tables piled on top of each other, fixated to the wall with a strong rope, and 
two cupboards standing next to each other at the end of the trailer, secured 
the same way as the tables. 
Acting without thinking, she slipped under the desks and crouched 
down as close to the wall as she could. 
She giggled as she realized her stupidity; she was acting like a child! 
But - oh, how exciting it was! It reeked of adventure, not of dusty office 
interiors. 
The door to the house opened and heavy footsteps approached the 
truck. 
“Careful with that!” she heard Walter say. 
Two other male voices moaned and groaned under pressure.  
Something heavy got loaded into the trailer and Kristella rocked along.  
Fifteen minutes later, she was surrounded by gadgets and laboratory 
tools. Nobody had seen her, even though a pair of legs had come 
dangerously close. 
The helping men jumped into the trailer, probably trying to find 
enough room for each other, and the door was closed shut. 
“Let us through now, please,” the driver yelled, probably to warn the 
police. 
 
59 
Two doors opened and closed, the motor started and the truck drove 
away. 
 
60 
 
 
 
The warehouse, located at the eastern end of Taresville, lay spookily in the 
moonlight. There was a rusty sign up front, hanging at a funny angle, 
reading “Familia” in black letters against a supposedly yellow 
background. Crickets happily sang their songs, cherishing the fact that the 
noise of the city was too distant to disturb their rituals. 
Xave’s truck thundered through the silent night, waking up even the 
deafest living thing. 
“We’re here,” he said to Walter, who was sitting on the passenger seat, 
grabbing onto the handle above the door on his side. 
“You drive like the devil,” Walter said, jumping out of the truck. “I 
hope my equipment isn’t damaged.” 
Xave smirked and got out as well. 
They both opened one side of the trailer’s door and the four workers 
started unloading Walter’s stuff. 
Xave and Walter went to the entrance of the warehouse. 
“Here is your key,” Xave said and handed Walter a small, shimmering 
item. “I have the second one. There is no third yet, but I’ll let copies be 
made whenever we need more.” 
“How did you get this so quickly? And how much did all this cost?” 
Walter gestured at the building, the truck and the workers. 
“You said money isn’t a problem, and we were running out of time, 
consider this until you see the actual figures.” Xave unlocked the large gate 
and pushed the left half of it inward. 
As he went in, he produced and turned on a flashlight. Walter 
followed. 
“Oh, and I got a tip for this unused warehouse from the mayor’s office. 
I had to talk to them about the connection to Monoura anyway.” Xave 
paused, touched the wall next to the entrance in search for the light switch, 
found it, and hit the knob with his fist. 

 
61 
A dozen fluorescent light tubes sprang to life, illuminating a vast empty 
space, the lights reflected by the floor. Apart from the windows close to the 
ceiling, there was nothing else. 
Walter was taken aback for a moment. 
Xave clicked off his flashlight. “The company who previously owned 
this place went bankrupt last month and nobody had bothered to buy it 
yet, so they were eager to let it go,” Xave finished explaining. 
Walter had stopped listening and was already running to the center of 
the hall, which took him a good five or six seconds, then he stretched out 
his arms and turned around, facing Xave with a broad smile. “This is 
fantastic!” 
His content expression quickly faded as he saw blue and red lights 
through the front windows of the building. 
Xave went outside and saw five cops standing next to two police cars, 
one of them talking to a female news reporter, the other one prohibiting 
anyone else to approach the warehouse. Six fans had apparently followed 
the truck as well and were in the process of setting up camp anew.  
“Just as I thought,” Xave mumbled to himself. 
The workers began carrying various inventions, gadgets, tools and 
furnishings into the building, and Walter took it into his own hands to 
guide them. 
One of the policemen tapped Xave on his shoulder. “Can I have a word 
with you?” 
“About what?” Xave asked, facing the uniformed man. 
“We can’t protect you forever, you should hire security guards,” the 
officer explained. 
Xave nodded. “Definitely, but can you guys just stay for one more 
night? The mayor would be glad.” 
Noticing that this was becoming bigger than estimated, the cop 
refrained from trying to get home earlier. “Will do,” he said, straightened 
his hat and returned to warding off the mob. 
Good boy, Xave thought. 
As he was about to join Walter, he noticed a woman climbing out of the 
trailer. Xave realized that all workers were inside right now and he leapt 
forward, grabbing her by the arm. 
“Who are you? What were you doing in there?” he asked firmly. 
The woman turned around - and Xave was stunned. He let go of her 
immediately. 
 
62 
She laughed unabashed. “I did the most hilarious thing,” she said. “I 
went along for the ride as a stowaway.” She put both of her hands in front 
of her mouth, as if she had formed the naughtiest word in the universe.  
Xave chuckled. His mind ran through various reasons for her being 
here, but every thought ended in the fact that the woman in front of him 
looked stunning. 
“I hope you’re not a reporter, I’d hate to throw you out,” Xave said. 
“No need to worry,” Kristella reassured him. “I’m a fan, I come in 
peace.” 
“How did you get in here?” the officer from before barked at the 
woman. 
Xave reacted quickly. “It’s fine, she’s with me.” 
The cop formed a sour face, as if to say, “Whatever” and walked away. 
“My name is Xave. And you are?” Xave inquired gently. 
“Kristella.” 
They shook hands. 
The workers returned with bare hands, climbed into the trailer and 
heaved a cupboard together. 
“I’ll introduce you to Walter,” Xave said and led the way. Kristella 
followed him with a flushed face. 
“Walter!” Xave yelled into the warehouse as he and Kristella entered. 
His voice echoed off the walls. 
The scientist was inspecting one of the glass booths, probably trying to 
find any damages. The other booth was still in the truck. 
He rose to his feet when he saw Xave and Kristella approaching him.  
“Walter, this is Kristella, your biggest fan,” Xave said jokingly, waving 
at Kristella with his hand. “Kristella, meet Walter, the mad scientist 
everyone’s talking about.” 
They greeted each other awkwardly, not shaking hands. 
“She was actually hiding in the truck all the way here,” Xave continued. 
Walter frowned in disbelief. “Did you steal anything?” he asked, eyeing 
Kristella suspiciously. 
“Of course not,” Kristella defended herself. “Where would I even put 
that thing?” She threw a glance at the glass booth. 
“You could have stolen plans or memorized blueprints.” Walter folded 
his arms. 
“I don’t have any pockets, and I’m not a genius like you,” Kristella fired 
back. 
“Come on, Walter, give her a break,” Xave intervened. 
 
63 
Walter fumed, but soon the anger lifted from his eyes and he 
apologized. “I’m sorry, it’s been quite an achievement to get this far in my 
young life and I won’t let anyone spoil that for me.” 
That’s about the worst apology ever, Xave thought, and buried his face in 
his right hand. 
The four workers brought in a cumbersome black cupboard, banging 
the door with one of its corners. 
“Right over there, please!” Walter gestured in a direction. “Thank you!”  
“So, is it true then?” Kristella asked, trying to ignore Walter’s 
accusations. “Can you beam people?” 
Walter hesitated and bit his lip. Xave had to force himself to look away 
and not laugh. 
“Yes,” the scientist said. 
“Can I try it?” 
“Oh, you know, why not? We’re in the middle of setting everything up 
right now, but if you stick around, I’m sure I’ll have it up and running 
again in, ah, five or six hours.” A dark cloud formed above Walter’s head. 
“You’re grumpy like an old man, you know that?” Kristella said. 
“Okay, that’s enough,” Xave once again jumped between the two. 
“Kristella,” he said as sweet as he could. “Why don’t you give me your 
number and I’ll call you when you can try it out?” 
“You know what, this was a bad idea after all,” she said and turned 
around to leave. 
Xave darted a sharp look at Walter. He shrugged and continued 
examining his creation. 
Just as Kristella reached the gate, Xave held her back. “I see that you 
long for fresh experiences in your life, this is a great opportunity, and I’d 
love to have you on board for this journey,” Xave blabbered in his best 
business talk. 
Kristella seemed to consider simply walking off for a moment, then she 
said: “Kristella Attenburg. Look me up in the phone book.” 
She smiled, walked outside and began talking to a policeman, probably 
asking him to take her home. 
Xave tore off his gaze from her attractive body, evaded the marching 
workers who were on their way to the truck once more, and went back to 
Walter, who was kneeling next to the booth. 
“Anything broken?” he asked the scientist. 
“No signs of any loose parts, at least,” Walter replied. “I’ll have to open 
it up later and I expect to find a mess of torn wires and scattered resistors, 
thanks to your careful driving style.” 
 
64 
Xave raised an eyebrow. “Walter, my friend, look at me.” 
Furious, the scientist turned around and their eyes met. 
After a few seconds, Xave won the staring contest. Walter sighed. “I’m 
sorry. This is just going a bit too fast for me.” 
“Well, you started it,” Xave said. 
“So I did.” Walter looked down on the ground at the reflection of the 
lights. “Thanks for helping me out.” 
“No worries.” Xave had personal gain in mind by aiding Walter on his 
way to fame and success, but he kind of started to like the wiz not only 
because of his ability to print money in the near future. His father 
would’ve warned him not to get attached to business partners though, and 
he pushed those feelings aside for the moment. 
Kristella appeared in the gate. “Guys? I need a ride home. The cops are 
too busy and I don’t have enough money for a taxi.” 
Walter seemed indifferent, but Xave looked at her with surprise. He 
figured out a plan. “Once the truck is fully unloaded I’ll have to drive the 
workers home and return the truck anyway, so you can come with me,” 
Xave offered. 
“That would be great,” Kristella said loudly. “I’ll wait outside.” 
Two workers carried the second booth inside, almost hitting Kristella 
over her head with it. 
“Careful with that!” Walter yelled. 
 
65 
 
 
 
“How can I trust you, Xave?” Walter asked the salesman. 
They were sitting at a table in a separate area of the warehouse, 
surrounded by drawing boards. 
Walter knew he had to tread carefully by posing such questions. “I 
don’t want to be rude, you’ve been very trustworthy ever since you 
stepped in to help me out.” 
Xave crossed his legs, his left arm resting on the table, his right arm 
dangling from his shoulder. His suit featured gray stripes today, Walter 
noticed. 
Why isn’t he saying anything? Walter thought, panicking. “I’m sorry, I 
shouldn’t have asked.” 
“No, no, it’s fine,” Xave finally replied. “We’ve only known each other 
for a week.” 
Their voices echoed off the walls, even though the warehouse was now 
packed with all of Walter’s basement equipment and new furniture. He 
couldn’t believe that already two days had passed since they had moved 
his stuff in here. 
Walter waited for more. 
“I’ll be honest with you, my life has and always will be about money,” 
Xave begun. “I wasn’t exactly successful until I met you, but I think we’re 
off to something great here.” 
Xave placed both feet on the ground and leaned forward. “We’re in the 
same boat,” he said. “If I carve a leak in it, I’ll drown as well.” 
Walter let the words resonate in his head, just like the walls of the hall 
absorbed them. 
“Will you ever turn on me?” Walter asked in a sinister tone. 
“I might be cold and quick to react, but I assure you I will never find a 
reason to hurt our business relationship. And if I do, I’ll try to look for a 
way around it,” Xave said. 
“That’s not very convincing.” 
Xave sighed. “I’ll give you access to my bank account.” 

 
66 
“You might have more than just one.” 
“I’ll make you a copy of my car keys.” Xave sounded annoyed and 
slightly desperate. 
Walter still wasn’t sure. This man could turn his life around anytime, 
just as he already did. “I don’t drive,” he said. 
“I’ll dance around naked in town and write your name on my chest.”  
“You’ll get arrested, and then you won’t be able to help me,” Walter 
said dryly. 
“Okay.” Xave banged his fist on the table. “Here.” He stood, and pulled 
a golden watch out of the chest pocket of his shirt. “This was given to me 
by my father, who is dead now. The clock’s not working, and on the left 
side it shows an old image of my father and my mother, who, by the way, 
is also dead. And despite all the money I will ever make with you as a 
business partner, this is always going to be the greatest treasure I have 
ever had the honor of calling my property.” 
Walter could see that he had pushed over the boundary. He was 
shocked at his own stubbornness. “Ah, that’s alright.” He leaned back and 
folded his arms. “I’m sorry I doubted you.” 
Xave left the golden watch dangling on his index finger for a second. 
Then he put it back into its place and took a seat on a swivel chair.  
“Do you trust me?” Walter asked. 
“I trust that you could build a weapon that could kill me in my sleep 
without you even being close to me, so the simple fact that I have survived 
a week in your vicinity makes me believe in your sweet affection towards 
me.” 
Walter felt ashamed. “That’s actually a pretty good idea,” he said. 
“Being in love with me?” Xave punched Walter on his shoulder. 
“No, the weapon you were talking about,” Walter said, grinning from 
ear to ear. 
Xave’s face lost any expression of kidding around. “Are we good?” 
Walter shuffled his feet, recollecting his senses. “Yes, we’re good.” 
Xave fell back in his chair, relaxing a bit. “By the way, I talked to  the 
mechanic yesterday. He’s in. He said he’ll talk to his boss, but I’ll probably 
have to do that for him to speed things up. I’ll try to bring him in 
tomorrow.” 
“Sounds good,” Walter said enthusiastically. 
I really shouldn’t have doubted this man, he thought. 
 
67 
 
 
 
Kristella took a sip of her coffee and stared at the fluid, jumping up at the 
sides, trying to escape. The flickering screen in front of her refused to show 
anything new. 
Beep, beep. 
There it was again, the outcry of the gray box, even though she wasn’t 
doing anything to it. She was having a hard time understanding the 
apparent improvement on efficiency this thing should bring along.  
She looked up from her mug. What was it trying to say to her? She 
didn’t speak beeperish. 
A look at the pile of paper that should’ve been digitalized today 
brought her down even more. Tired, she rose from her seat like a 
marionette, pulled upwards by invisible threads, and slurped to the tilted-
open window, enjoying a little air of freedom. 
She recalled the night Xave had brought her home. He had tried to get 
to know her better by going through various topics one after another. He 
was charming at least, not a brute like so many others have been before 
him. Still - he was a salesman; finding ways to people’s hearts was his 
bread and butter. 
She had been waiting for him to call ever since. It had definitely felt like 
a dream, her jumping into the truck like a careless child, hiding from the 
workers, then coming out to meet Xave and Walter. It had been the most 
surreal experience in her life. Nothing had ever felt so exciting, so terrific, 
so full of life, and yet so like a dream at the same time. 
There was fog hanging amidst the city walls, shutting out the sun like a 
thick spider web. The sky was painted pure white; a rather unusual 
Summer day. 
Kristella turned her head back to what seemed to be her final resting 
place. A grave she was sitting in before she was even dead, buried under 
endless heaps of paper. 
That’s it, she thought. Enough of this. 

 
68 
She tried telling the computer to shut down with various key strokes 
she thought the machine should understand. Then she leaned over and 
pulled the plug. 
As she was about to leave the office, the phone rang. 
The door she had just opened swung to the side uncontrolled, banging 
against the hat stand. Kristella’s eyes were focused on the black little thing 
on the table, which was trying to get somebody’s attention every three 
seconds. 
She let it ring twice, three times - jumped towards it and grabbed the 
receiver. “Hello?” 
“Kristella? This is Xave,” a familiar voice said. 
“I was just about to go and meet you guys,” Kristella answered.  
“Good, you’re hired.” 
Hired? Oh, right, he was trying to score with his charm. “Quit it,” she 
said, smiling. 
“Yes, you should quit whatever job you’re having right now,” Xave 
insisted. “And then get over here. We need another assistant. You’re 
working in an office right now, correct?” 
Kristella became nervous. “Yes, but-” 
“Don’t worry, you’ll get three times more than what you’re being paid 
right now. Just tell us your current wage and we’ll triple it.” 
She laughed in return, but choked a bit on the anxiety stuck in her 
throat. “I... I’m not sure I’m cut out for the tasks you have in mind for me.” 
“You’ve already shown courage, that’s enough,” Xave assured her. 
Kristella closed her eyes for a moment. She imagined herself standing at 
a crossroads, the left way leading back to her current life, always in a loop, 
the right one exiting the circle into the unknown. 
Determined, she looked out the window. “I’ll be there in an hour,” she 
agreed. 
 
69 
 
 
 
Kristella stepped out of the taxi and handed the driver one hundred and 
fifty Schilling. “Keep the change,” she said, hoping that the money would 
return to her through a different source soon. 
The taxi drove off, leaving a dirt cloud behind. Kristella turned around 
and faced the building. 
Only two days had passed and so much had changed, she realized.  
A meter away from the walls there was a black steel fence embracing 
the building, reaching two meters into the air. Security cameras panned 
left and right atop each corner of the fence, and two additional in front of 
the entrance gate. The fence had an additional door that needed to be 
opened, and there was a button next to it and a small speaker below it.  
Astonished, she walked forward and pressed the button, resulting in a 
ringing tone inside the house. The loudspeaker clicked. 
“Yes?” Walter’s voice asked. “Oh, it’s you.” 
Kristella’s mouth had formed a word to reveal her identity, but instead 
she smiled awkwardly and pushed the door of the fence inward as she 
heard an electrical buzz. 
Walter opened one half of the gate for her without saying anything, and 
as soon as she had walked past him, he closed it again. 
Xave approached Kristella. “Good to see you,” he greeted her and 
shook her hand. 
“This place has changed a lot,” she noted, looking around. 

 
70 
The warehouse had been turned into a full-blown laboratory. There 
were six sections now, divided by waist-level wooden walls as thick as an 
arm. The first section on the right had a thick round oriental rug in the 
center and two sofas placed on it, this area seemed to be used for resting, 
sleeping, hanging out, and brainstorming. Adjacent to that area was the 
kitchen, obviously for preparing food, eating, drinking, and, judging by 
the neatly stacked decks, for playing cards. The third section in the far 
right corner of the warehouse appeared to be used for storage as it had 
random objects standing around in no particular order. Opposite to that, 
Kristella saw two glass booths - must be the beaming booths - and hundreds 
of cables on the ground, which were connected to a console with a 
ridiculous amount of buttons. The second area from her left had a desk, 
cupboards and one of those computers she hated so much. And the sixth 
area, the one she was actually standing in, had two desks and eight TVs 
stacked in two rows, and another console with knobs and labels hooked up 
to them. The TVs showed monochrome images from the security cameras 
outside, flickering from time to time. 
“We’re finally able to get other stuff done, now that we’ve moved in 
completely,” Xave said. 
“You both live here?” Kristella asked. 
“I still sleep in my apartment. As for Walter, well...” He looked at the 
scientist, who scratched his head. 
“I can’t really go back to my house right now anyway,” Walter justified 
himself. 
Kristella took another quick look around, trying to find a bathroom or 
at least a shower. 
Xave seemed to have read her mind and said: “The bathroom is down 
the stairs in the basement and yes, there’s even a shower.” 
“Not bad,” she said. “Say, what happened to your fans outside?” 
Walter seemed to be impatient and folded his arms. 
“We bought this place, or rather, he did,” Xave nodded at Walter. 
“They were standing on private property and we threatened to sue the m 
all. So, either they’re gone, or they’re camping half a mile away 
somewhere.” 
“Ah,” Kristella said.  
There was a pause. A feeling of uncertainty overcame Kristella. This 
was becoming more surreal with every passing second. 
“Well, why don’t you have a seat over there while Walter and I 
continue our work?” Xave said, showing his teeth. 
“I... I don’t understand,” Kristella said. “Am I just here to sit around?”  
 
71 
For a moment she thought she had simply been hired for her looks, 
something to stare at in case they were bored. 
“Oh, you’ll be reading for a bit,” Xave said. He went to the hangout 
zone, as Kristella had labelled it in her head, grabbed a stack of paper, 
returned and handed it to her. “This is all you need to know about 
Walter’s invention. Travelling, beaming, transporting, teleporting, 
jumping, sliding - whatever you want to call it. It all means the same thing 
- a new way to reach destinations in the blink of an eye.” 
Kristella held the bleached pile of dead wood in her hands. “Travelling 
- By Walter Trimond”, the first page read. 
“And you’re going to tell the world all about it,” Xave said 
encouragingly, patting Kristella’s shoulder. 
 
72 
 
 
 
One day later, when the sun was hovering straight above and roasting the 
earth, two pitch black limousines parked in front of the heavily secured 
warehouse. Each of them spawned three bodyguards in tight suits, one 
more muscular than the other. The mayor of Monoura got out of the first 
car, casually dressed in gray trousers, a white shirt with thin gray stripes 
and brown suspenders. 
A few moments later, the mayoress of Taresville rose from the second 
classy automobile. Her red hair was drawn back into a tight knot. Her 
dress, black and elegant, reached down to her ankles, its V-shaped 
décolleté emphasizing the small, sparkling jewel resting on her chest. 
She waited for Petar to press the button on the intercom that was 
affixed next to the gate and slightly raised her chin towards the nearest 
security camera. 
“Please come in,” Walter’s voice resounded from the speaker. 
Everyone heard a distorted humming and Petar pushed open the small 
door that was built into the massive gate. Two bodyguards squeezed past 
him, scanning their surroundings. 
Petar went in, followed by Isela. 
The other bodyguards waited outside like alert watchdogs. 
Walter opened the left part of the inner gate and welcomed them. “It’s 
an honor to have you here,” he said, shaking hands with both mayors.  
Walter entered the warehouse, followed by Isela, Petar and two 
gorillas. The scientist closed the gate. 
Xave greeted them. “Good day to you, Mrs. Mantorry. Good day to you 
as well, Mr. Toringen. I assume you bring positive news?” 
“We have a deal,” Isela proclaimed. “But right now it’s merely in verbal 
form. We would like to change that.” 
“Exactly,” the mayor of Monoura approved, proudly sticking out his 
belly. 
“Please, don’t touch that,” Walter said to one of the bodyguards who 
was checking out the glass booth in the back area. 

 
73 
“I’m sorry,” he said in a low voice. 
“Shall we sit?” Xave offered, and gestured at the two spacious sofas. 
“There’s no time,” Isela declined, raising her hand. “We must be on our 
way soon. Other cities have caught wind of your invention and the 
rumours have ignited a bit of a contest.” 
“Luckily, we’ve been in talk with you first,” Petar added. 
The bell rang and Walter went to operate the intercom. “Yes? Oh, it ’s 
you again.” 
Unhurriedly, he made his way to the gate and opened it using his 
weight. Kristella walked in - and was taken aback by who was standing in 
front of her. 
“Kristella, glad you are here,” Xave said warmly. He introduced her to 
the mayors. 
“A pleasure to meet you,” Kristella greeted them, intimidated. 
Isela looked at her silver wristwatch, stressing the fact that her time was 
limited. 
“She can be a witness,” Xave said. “I’ll prepare the document.” He 
hurried to the desk that was home to one of the computers, fetched a pen 
and several sheets of paper from the leftmost drawer and started writing.  
As the gate clanked shut, Walter rejoined with the mayors and Kristella.  
“May I see it?” Isela asked him, her green eyes filled with curiosity. 
“Naturally,” Walter said, and led the way to the two glass booths. 
The mayors followed immediately, while Kristella hesitated for a 
moment before tagging along. This was her working place, after all . 
Isela looked the two apparatuses up and down and sneered. “How 
unremarkable,” she said, then her expression lifted. “Yet impressive.”  
“I can assure you that it works, Mrs. Mantorry,” Petar said. “I’ve seen it 
with my own eyes. And one of my bodyguards can attest that it’s the real 
thing - he was the one being teleported.” He waved at the bodyguard near 
the gate, who simply nodded once. 
“Travelled,” Walter said, smiling with a strain. 
“Travelled?” Isela asked. 
“Yes, I prefer that term.” 
“One cannot be travelled,” Isela corrected him. “But I admire your 
choice of humble words for something that will change the world.”  
Walter looked embarrassed and laughed sheepishly. “I don’t foresee 
such an optimistic future for my invention.” 
“You’re quite near-sighted then,” Isela said. She touched the glass 
booth at the top, where it was shaped like a dome. “May I see it in action 
as well?” 
 
74 
Walter sharply inhaled through his teeth. “I’m afraid that’s not possible 
right now, I’m currently recalibrating the instruments, exchanging cords 
and improving the overall design. There’s still a lot of energy being used.” 
He pointed at the loose parts scattered around the device. 
“We’ll let you know the instant it works again,” Kristella added to the 
discussion. “You have my word.” 
The two mayors and Walter turned around to face her. 
“Very well,” Isela said, showing no emotion. 
“Here it is,” Xave said, marching towards the group with pen and 
paper in hand. 
Without warning, Isela snatched the document and the writing utensil 
and skimmed through the handwritten text. 
Xave stuck his hands into his trouser pockets and waited patiently. 
Walter looked at him nervously, while Kristella was squinting and trying 
to read the document from afar. 
 
“This document represents the willingness of all parties 
involved to exclusively connect the city of Taresville to the village 
of Monoura using the invention, an instant travelling device, of 
Walter Trimond. 
 
Until that connection has been placed, no other cities, villages 
or other locations shall be permitted to use said invention. All 
involved parties agree that after the exclusive initial connection 
has been made, this document and its goal and intention are 
nullified. 
  
We - meaning Mrs Isela Mantorry, mayoress of Taresville, Mr 
Petar Toringen, mayor of Monoura, Mr Walter Trimond, inventor 
of the instant travelling device, Mr Xave Stern, salesman, and Ms 
Kristella Attenburg, contract witness - hereby approve of the terms 
stated above.” 
 
Isela showed the document to Petar without letting go of it. After a  few 
seconds they both nodded and she pressed the paper against the glass of 
Walter’s invention. She signed at the bottom with a graceful squiggle and 
gave the pen to Petar, who scribbled his name as well. 
 
75 
Walter was next in line, drawing his full name between the two 
mayors’, then Xave took hold of the pen and, while Isela was still pinning 
the sheet against the transparent, cold material, he autographed it and 
gestured at Kristella to come. 
She was the last to spell her name as curly as possible, having a hard 
time finding a free spot beneath all the presumptuous graffitis. 
“We need photocopies,” Isela commanded. 
Xave received the document from Kristella, ran it through the machine 
next to the computer desk, and handed a copy to every participant.  
Isela shook her head. “I will take the original and treasure it,” she said, 
sticking out her copy. 
“Fine with me,” Xave said, and exchanged the genuine contract with 
her copy.  
“I’m glad we settled this in writing,” Isela said, satisfied. “If you will 
excuse us now, we’ll have to parade the news to our competitors, now that 
it’s official.” 
“Certainly.” Xave nodded deeply. 
“Thank you,” Walter said. “For your trust.” 
“I’m convinced you’ll be worth the investment,” the mayoress of 
Taresville said. 
“I’m quite sure of that as well,” Petar added, his double chin wobbling 
with excitement. 
“You’ll be hearing from me,” Kristella said, shaking hands with both of 
them. 
“Looking forward to it,” Isela replied. 
The mayors made their way to the exit, followed by Walter and the two  
bodyguards. The scientist showed them out. 
Kristella had to sit down. “I feel dizzy,” she said, exhausted. 
“You’re doing great,” Xave tried to cheer her up. 
Walter walked past them, taking a deep breath and letting it out with a 
sigh. “Back to work then,” he said. 
 
76 
 
 
 
“Rick should be here soon,” Xave said to Kristella, who was sitting on the 
sofa, reading through Walter’s notes about travelling. 
“The mechanic, right?” Kristella asked. 
She looked tired, Xave thought. But no less beautiful. 
“Yes, he’ll assist Walter. I’m going to drive around and see where we 
can put these things.” He gestured at the glass booths at the other end of 
the warehouse. “I probably won’t be back until nightfall.” 
“Alright, take care,” Kristella said. “Oh one more thing, when am I 
getting paid? Taking a cab twice every day is kind of putting a hole into 
my wallet.” 
“Ah, of course, Walter?” He went to see the scientist, who was buried 
somewhere in the storage area, looking for something. “Walter, can you 
hear me?” 
Walter crawled out from underneath a large metal plate, possibly from 
an airplane, almost hitting his head. “Very funny. Of course I can hear you. 
What’s up?” 
“She needs money for the cab,” Xave said bluntly. 
“Sure, I’ll give her some.” Walter kept looking around, disinterested. 
There was something about the way he acted that Xave didn’t like. 
With a lowered voice, he asked: “Is there something I need to know?”  
That got Walter’s attention. His eyes fixated on Xave. 
“No, why?” 
“You’re behaving differently, and I have a feeling that it’s because of 
her.” 
There was a flash of guilt on Walter’s face, affirming Xave’s 
assumption. But it was neither hate, nor any other malevolent look in his 
eyes - rather, something else. Shyness? 
Walter tried hard to deny it. “I don’t know what you’re talking about.” 
Xave gave up for now, but he made a mental note to come back to that 
topic later on. “Fine - don’t forget to give her money, please. I’ll take a look 
around for a fitting place for your travelling instruments.” 

 
77 
Travelling instruments - Xave knew Walter liked to compare his 
creations to music. 
“Sure,” the scientist said. 
Xave left the warehouse through the gate and fence contraption and 
entered his car around the corner. 
On the way back into the city, Xave went through various scenes of th e 
past few days in his head. It all had happened so fast that he needed to sort 
through all the important events. 
He looked at the dashboard of his car, patting it with his right hand. It 
was a good car, probably fifteen years old by now. It wouldn’t hold out 
much longer. 
Then, with a strange feeling, he realized that he probably wouldn ’t need 
it much longer. Maybe. 
The idea of stepping into Walter’s device terrified him a little, and deep 
down inside he prayed he’d always have his car to move around. 
He wondered if people felt the same when trains were introduced to 
the public a few centuries ago and if others would share his uncertain 
feelings. 
With a shrug, he shook off his doubts. 
When the traffic lights turned red in front of him, he used the time to 
look at a map of Taresville. 
Between miles and miles of house blocks and streets, there were a few 
areas where the density loosened up a bit. Several parks put green holes 
into the otherwise uninterrupted city web. 
An obvious choice would be right in front of the city hall, he realized. 
Somebody honked twice and Xave drove on, waving his hand upwards 
to whoever was behind him. 
Several turns later, he arrived at the city hall and spent another fifteen 
minutes circling around in heavy traffic to find a free parking space. 
After locking up his car, he went to the Roman-style building. It was 
surrounded by five-meter tall pillars and crowned by a triangle shaped 
roof with a Latin phrase carved into the front, reading “vox populi vox 
dei”, which probably meant, “instant travelling is going to make you rich.” 
There was also a statue of some smart-ass sitting in front of it on a 
pedestal. Xave scoffed at it, but he admitted to himself that he was actually 
envious. 
He stood in front of the petrified giant and turned around. There was a 
wide place in front of the city hall, plastered with white marble stones. 
Adjacent to that was the broad street, jam-packed with cars and buses, and 
across the street, there was a park with lush vegetation. 
 
78 
Xave put his hands on his hips, pulling back his jacket. 
It was perfect. 
 
79 
 
 
 
“How do I look?” Rick asked his son and his wife, standing in the entrance 
area of their apartment. 
He looked just the same as one hour before; blue jeans, gray sneakers, 
suspenders, gray pullover. Only now he had taken a shower before work, 
instead of afterwards, so his hair fell differently. 
“Nervous,” his Roanne said, holding her son in front of her by his 
shoulders. 
“Can I come with you?” Bernand asked. 
“Not today, but I’ll see what I can do,” Rick said, putting a blue cap on 
his head. 
“Yay!” his child exclaimed, dragging out the “y” at the end for a few 
seconds. 
Rick chuckled. “You be good to your mommy while I’m gone.” He 
kissed his wife on her cheek. “See you later.” 
“Take care,” she said, waving. 
Rick went out, down into the parking garage, which was used by 
everyone living in the block, sat down in his Ford Escort Mark IV and 
drove away. 
It was afternoon already. Xave had rung up Rick’s boss in the early 
morning, which had caused quite a storm. Rick hadn’t had to quit; he had 
been fired on the spot and basically thrown off the premises. 
He hadn’t told that to his wife. She knew about his decision to try the 
new job, so there was no harm in not telling and thus not worrying her.  
Besides, a certain confidence had grown inside Rick that the new job 
was going to work out. He’d make sure of that. 
He was supposed to meet with Xave, but traffic increased and finally 
jammed. He was late. 
When he arrived at the warehouse, which, to his surprise, looked more 
like a prison with the strange fence and all the security cameras, he noticed 
that Xave’s car wasn’t there. 
He might be here without his car, Rick noted. 

 
80 
He parked around the corner, stepped outside and pushed the button 
next to the door in the fence. 
“Yes?” a male voice answered after a short while. 
“Rick Lorand, I’m here for the job,” the mechanic said, unsure about 
what that job would entail. 
It was almost dark now and Rick felt a chill running down his spine. 
Probably just a gust of wind, he tried to calm himself. 
The door buzzed and Rick went inside, where a man in a white coat 
opened the gate for him. 
“My name is Walter Trimond, I’m the scientist working on this project,” 
he said to Rick. 
They shook hands. 
“Pleasure,” Rick said, noticing that he probably almost broke the 
scientist’s fragile hand by squeezing it too hard. 
They went inside and the gate slammed shut. 
“This is Kristella, our spokesperson and general assistant,” the scientist 
said without looking at her. 
She stood up from the sofa and Rick - again - shook hands, this time 
trying not to inflict any major damage. 
“Hello,” she said. “I’m new, by the way. Second day.” 
“Ah, alright,” Rick responded. 
The scientist had already gone far ahead and Rick hurried after him.  
Scratching his head, Walter began talking about his machine. Rick 
nodded in regular intervals without understanding anything. He shifted 
his weight from one foot to another, pretending to be absolutely aware of 
the input he was receiving. 
“Any questions?” Walter asked. 
Rick looked at the two person-sized glass pillars with round roofs. His 
painted-on smile fell apart and turned sour. “Uh,” he said. “Look, just tell 
me what I need to do, like, which cable goes where, and I’ll do that.” 
Walter appeared to be slightly angry about the fact that all his godly 
words had sailed past Rick’s ears without leaving an impression - yet, 
somehow he seemed to appreciate the simplicity of Rick’s compromise. 
“Sounds good,” the scientist said. “We still need to drastically decrease 
the energy use and the white light that it’s emitting when someone is about 
to be travelled.” 
Be travelled? Rick chose to disregard that. 
 
81 
“And,” Walter continued. “We have to test it with a longer distance. 
Physical distance shouldn’t matter, we’ll just utilize a longer cable. It’s not 
that flexible, so we still have to position one booth here in this corner and 
the other one on the left side of the gate. I need you to unhook the left glass 
booth and carry it over there.” 
“I’ll get right to it,” Rick said, rolling up his sleeves. 
Walter seemed pleased. “In the meantime I’m going to order more 
cables and recalculate the energy consumption in my formulae.” 
Rick felt that the scientist had only said that to make a mental note.  
“Sounds good,” he noted and knelt down to the bottom of the left glass 
booth. 
There were a dozen thick black cables stuck around the circular bottom 
metal plate. 
Rick hesitated. “Do I just... cut them off?” 
Walter, who had already taken a few steps towards the area with the 
desk and the telephone, turned around and frowned. Then he seemed to 
realize something and said: “Oh - yes, I think there’s no other way right 
now.” 
“Fine with me,” Rick said, stood up and went over to a cupboard full 
with tools. 
So far so good, he thought as he grabbed a cable cutter. 
 
82 
 
 
 
Around midnight, Xave returned to the warehouse. 
“I’ve got great news,” he announced, tossing his jacket onto the sofa, 
and startling Kristella from her sleep. 
Drowsily, she sat up and rubbed her eyes. 
Xave adored her disheveled look, but refrained from commenting on it. 
“I’m glad you made it,” Xave said to Rick, who was sitting in front of 
the stack of TVs, which displayed night vision mode images of the cameras 
outside. 
The mechanic stood up and shook hands with Xave. “Thanks for 
having me,” he said. “Although I’m still not sure I fit in.” 
“Don’t worry, this is a completely new field,” Xave reassured him. 
“Walter will throw all kinds of tasks at you, and soon you’ll find a spot 
that suits you well.” 
Xave hadn’t had a particular job in mind when he had hired Rick, but 
his ability to repair cars would surely translate well to similar processes.  
As for Kristella - her attractiveness, innocence and wit would prove 
beneficial for presenting instant travelling to the public. As obvious as his 
reasoning was to him, he hadn’t bothered telling anyone about it. 
His mother for instance would have scolded him severely for reducing 
another human being - especially a woman - to an object, just to promote a 
product. 
His father, on the other hand, certainly would have approved. "A smart 
move, son," the voice of his father resounded in his head. 
“Walter!” Xave called. 
The scientist answered from a section of the warehouse where Xave 
hadn’t expected him to be. “Yes, yes. I’m coming.” 
When Rick, Kristella and Walter were gathered around him, he 
produced the street map he had used on his trip to find the perfect location 
for the first instant travelling station. There was a zig-zagged red line 
drawn on it, which connected Taresville and Monoura. 

 
83 
“I’ve met with Isela and then talked to Petar on the phone about it,” he 
began. “The first travelling booth will be placed here.” He tapped on a spot 
in the middle of Taresville. 
Walter examined the location closer. “At the city hall?” he asked, 
puzzled. 
“Exactly,” Xave said, flashing a grin. 
For a moment, Walter scowled at the map, then his face lit up. “That’s 
actually a good idea.” 
Despite agreeing, Xave resisted the urge to brag, and continued: “The 
second booth will be placed here, in the central public place of Monoura. 
The surrounding shops will definitely profit when travellers come 
streaming in from Taresville.” 
Rick and Kristella listened silently, Xave noticed, wondering whether 
they had a hard time wrapping their minds around instant transportation. 
At least Kristella seemed excited. 
Walter suddenly looked terrified. “That’s... a lot of cable.” 
“About forty kilometers, yes,” Xave stated. 
“What?” Walter’s mouth fell open. 
“Isela and Petar agreed that they will help us out in any way possible, 
and they said that they will fund your project with fifty million Schilling,” 
Xave revealed. “I handed them your bank account information, it should 
be transferred in a few days.” 
“It’s not a money issue,” Walter said coolly. “You know that.” 
“I do, Walter.” Xave leaned against the back of the sofa Kristella was 
sitting on. “But it won’t hurt if the mayors feel that they’re investing in 
something valuable.” 
Rick and Kristella were even quieter now, Xave realized. 
He looked at them one after another. “I can trust you both to keep 
everything said within these walls to yourselves, right?” 
“Of course,” Rick answered. 
“Absolutely,” Kristella promised. “My mom already thinks I’m a 
lunatic for throwing my old job over board, so the less she knows the 
better.” 
“Awesome,” Xave said. “Now - Walter, my friend, when do you think 
this can be done?” He tapped three times on the line between Taresville 
and Monoura. 
“First, I need to test if it even works from here to there,” Walter said, 
pointing from one corner of the warehouse to the other. “And then,” he 
paused, rubbing the back of his neck. “We probably need more men for 
laying out the cable. And it’s not only one thick cable, it’s four or five thick 
 
84 
ones bundled together. And who’s going to make sure nobody drills holes 
into any of them?” 
“Isela suggested sending twenty policemen who patrol along the 
route,” Xave said. “From what I’ve seen, your cables are shielded quite 
well, right? So, a little kid can’t just come and cut them with a butter 
knife.” 
“Hm,” Walter said, holding his chin. “That would do, I suppose.” 
The doorbell rang, causing everyone to turn their heads in the direction 
of the entrance with an alarmed expression. 
“Has anyone ordered pizza?” Xave asked, looking into the eyes of the 
others, one by one. 
“Not me,” Walter answered defensively, frowning and raising his 
hands. 
“That’s a shame, maybe we should,” Xave suggested, and approached 
the intercom. 
The TV showed a policeman waving at the camera just outside the gate. 
His eyes were strangely highlighted by the green night vision.  
“Police? At this hour?” Xave wondered. 
Walter and Rick joined him to form their own opinions on the visitor.  
“Something bad must’ve happened,” Rick guessed. 
Kristella sighed and retreated to the sofa. “Let’s find out, shall we?” 
Xave went to the door, opened it and nodded at Walter, who let the 
policeman in through the outer gate with a buzz. 
The uniformed man walked up to Xave and stopped in the door in 
front of him. 
“I’m sorry for the disturbance,” the policeman said, took off his hat and 
held it in front of his belt buckle. “I was sent here to check if everything’s 
fine.” 
Xave hadn’t been informed about any late night inspection. “We’re a bit 
tired, but I’m certain there’s nothing the law can do about that,” he joked. 
The policeman let out a strained laugh. “I know something you can do 
for me, though.” 
Before Xave could reply, the policeman pushed forward with both 
hands, sending Xave stumbling backwards. The door slammed shut; the 
policeman dropped his hat to the ground, drew his gun, and aimed at 
Xave. 
“I need details, notes and blueprints of your invention; files, papers, 
notebooks - everything you have, and you better be quick,” the policeman 
demanded. 
 
85 
Instinctively, Rick, Walter and Xave slightly held up their hands; 
Kristella froze. 
“There are no complete notes,” Walter said nervously. “Most of it is still 
in my head.” 
“What’s this, then?” the intruder said, pointing the gun at the sheets of 
paper on the table in front of Kristella. 
“Instant travelling trivia, but nothing too technical,” Walter explained, 
shivering. “You can have it, of course.” 
Trying not to make his gaze too obvious, Xave examined the stranger 
and noticed that the uniform had no name tag and didn’t seem to fit; it was 
slightly too large and hung loosely around the shoes and off the arms.  
“Oh, I can have it? Very kind,” the intruder said sarcastically, taking 
one step forward. He pointed aggressively at Rick and Xave. “You two, sit 
down next to her, slowly, and keep your hands up.” 
They did what they were told to do. As soon as they were seated, the 
demands continued. 
“You,” the policeman addressed Walter. “You have one minute to 
throw those papers together and hand them over. And I don’t care if they 
aren’t complete, I’m not walking out of here empty-handed.” 
“Just do it, Walter,” Xave said. 
The scientist nodded twice, beads of sweat appearing on his temples, 
and headed into the back of the warehouse. 
“Hey! You better not try anything,” the fake policeman yelled. “Or I ’ll 
gladly start shooting your minions one by one.” 
Kristella leaned forward. “Say, don’t I know you?” 
Irritated, the man waved his gun around and stared at Kristella. 
“Let it go, Kristella,” Xave intervened. No need to unnecessarily enrage 
him. 
“I’ve seen you outside,” Kristella continued, still keeping her hands in 
clear sight. “You were one of the fans.” 
“Kristella!” Xave snapped at her, fearing for her life. 
Walter returned, tripping over his own feet, carrying a handful of 
documents and detailed drawings. 
“Slowly!” the policeman yelled, reinforcing his voice by stabbing the air 
with the barrel of his gun. “Grab the manual from the desk and bring it all 
over. And you, Miss Blabbermouth, you’re coming with me.” 
“Come on, man, leave her alone,” Rick tried to reason with the attacker. 
“It’s alright,” Kristella said, standing up. 
 
86 
While Walter was struggling to collect every piece of paper and put it 
on top of the stack in his hands, Kristella carefully made her way around 
the table, never losing eye contact with the attacker. 
“Nothing’s going to be alright, if you don’t keep your mouth shut,” he 
said, a strand of sweaty hair sticking to his forehead, making him look like 
a lunatic. 
Walter’s face was a grimace of pain and humiliation; he finished 
stacking up all the information he could gather and offered the bundle to 
him. 
“You take them,” the policeman commanded Kristella, pointing the 
gun at her head. He waited until she had picked up the papers from 
Walter. 
How important are these documents? Xave’s thoughts rushed through his 
mind. What would happen if somebody else gained this information? Would they 
be able to replicate Walter’s machines and compete against us? 
“We’re leaving, come on,” the policeman barked, shoving Walter away 
and sending him tumbling against a desk. He violently grabbed Kristella 
by the arm and forced her to turn her back towards him. 
She looked at Xave and their eyes met. 
He watched in terror as they moved backwards, Kristella closely 
hugging the documents, her arm still in the firm grip of the ruffian. They 
reached the door and the policeman pushed against it with his back. 
Should I risk a shot? Xave’s face darkened. Would it be worth ... wounding 
her? 
A fast moving shadow tore him back to reality: Kristella stepped hard 
on the attacker’s foot, drilling her heel into the soft leather of his shoe. The 
intruder yelped and lost his balance. As he stumbled backwards, he 
dragged Kristella with him, and she let herself fall against his body with 
full force, sending the sheets flying through the air. 
The invader cursed, hit the door with the back of his head and finally 
released Kristella’s arm. With nothing to hold onto, she fell to the floor and 
rolled to the side, leaving the surprised uniformed man leaning against the 
door without any cover. 
Now. 
Xave unfroze, jumped up from the sofa, pulled his gun out of the back 
of his trousers and fired two shots at the policeman. 
Blood burst out of the intruder’s shoulder and chest. His body opened 
the door on its way down and lay still, the dark night outside embracing it.  
 
87 
There was smoke in the air; nothing moved except for Walter’s design 
documents rustling with the wind like autumn leaves. Xave only heard the 
familiar ringing noise in his ears, and for a moment, nobody moved.  
Kristella looked up. “Is he dead?” 
Xave forced his stiff legs to approach the body, and checked the man’s 
pulse on his neck. “Not yet.” 
“I hope you guys realize that I might require therapy after pulling a 
stunt like this,” Kristella noted, standing up, dusting off her dress.  
“I’m sorry for dragging you into this,” Xave replied. 
And for weighing your safety against a handful of documents... 
He looked down at the man’s face. “Let’s call the actual police. And the 
mayor.” 
“May I use the phone afterwards? I need to call my wife and tell her 
that I’m okay, before she and Bernand see anything in the news about all 
this,” Rick said. 
“Of course,” Walter replied, absentmindedly raising one hand to the 
ear. 
Xave felt tired and sat down on the sofa, still holding the small gun in 
his hands. 
“You’re a pretty good shot,” Kristella noted, taking a seat next to him, 
while examining the grazes on her elbows. 
“My father taught me,” Xave explained, staring at the barrel of the 
weapon. 
He taught me a lot, his mind continued. 
“By the way,” Rick interrupted. “I quit.” 
Everyone looked at him. There were no objections. 
 
88 
 
 
 
Kristella’s dream about a green hill with rabbits staring at her ended 
abruptly when a loud metal sound attacked her ears. She sat upright, 
trying to figure out her surroundings through the blurry vision.  
Still in the warehouse, she realized. Walter’s probably working on something. 
There was an orange blanket covering her body, which was good, because 
otherwise she probably would’ve frozen to death. 
Her eyes caught the spot where the man had been shot down by Xave.  
She rubbed her eyes and the images of the previous night rushed back 
into her mind. 
Walter had fetched a first aid kit, and Rick had pressed gauze on the 
wounds to stop the bleeding until a paramedic had taken over upon 
arrival. 
The police had joined them shortly thereafter, asking a few quick 
questions to everyone involved, while a bunch of serious people were 
moving out the bad guy on a stretcher. 
Finally, the mayors Isela Mantorry and Petar Toringen had been called 
and informed about the whole situation. 
There wasn’t much else she remembered. She had to have fallen asleep 
immediately. 
Xave wasn’t here. “Is Xave out?” she called to Walter. 
“Yes,” Walter’s voice echoed off the walls. 
Kristella saw that all documents had been collected and neatly placed 
on the other sofa next to hers. 
Her heart raced as she remembered how she had attacked the intruder. 
How could she have done such a thing? What if it hadn’t worked? 
She buried her face in her hands. 
Walter appeared out of nowhere next to her. “I prepared breakfast. 
Even though it’s noon already.” 
Kristella looked at the plate with ham and eggs being offered to her. 
With a deep growl, her stomach approved of it. 
“Thank you.” 

 
89 
She accepted plate and fork and started eating. 
“And I thank you for saving my creation yesterday,” he said stiffly. 
“And all of our lives, of course.” 
Kristella simply nodded. 
“You’re a good cook,” she lied, just to change the topic. At least the 
burnt bacon and the almost cold egg filled her belly. 
Walter scoffed. “I know I’m not, but I appreciate the effort for eating it 
nevertheless.” 
Why’s he still standing there, she thought. This is getting awkward. 
“Xave will be back soon,” Walter broke the silence. “He’s having 
another talk with both mayors. I gave him the idea of placing the cable 
underground through the sewers. Even if that happened only partially, it 
would still decrease the occurrences of random vandalism.”  
Oh yes, Kristella remembered. We’re going to insta-shoot people from one 
town to the other. 
“You know,” she said after swallowing the last bite. “It’s still kind of 
hard to believe this actually works.” She gestured at the glass booth 
standing next to the gate. Thick cables were already connecting it to the 
other booth on the opposite side of the warehouse. 
“Well, you’re here; I suppose a part of you already does.” Walter put 
his hands behind his back and smirked. 
What a smart-ass, she thought. Then again, he was probably right. Why 
else would she be here? The simple idea of walking from Paris to New 
York in the same time it took her to go from her living room to the 
bathroom intrigued her. And here was a man claiming that it was possible. 
A not very handsome, but somewhat likeable man. At least for his 
ambitions. 
“I’ll have to call my mother and tell her that I’m alright.” Kristella put 
her feet on the ground. 
“She already called, I spoke to her and told her everything.” 
“Oh, what did she say?” 
“She was furious. In her opinion, you should have held on to your 
previous occupation.” 
Not in this lifetime, she thought. It’s either shoving a mad man with a 
pistol away with her butt to save lives or hacking meaningless stuff into a 
stupid machine for eight hours a day. And the latter didn’t sound quite as 
adventurous. Maybe if there had been a risk of getting sucked out of that 
super small window. But other than that, surely nobody on this planet 
would want to kill her over boring delivery papers, and even if so, she ’d 
question that person’s priorities before kicking the bucket. 
 
90 
“She lectured me that I should have paid a taxi to bring you back to her, 
instead of letting you sleep here,” Walter continued. “And before hanging 
up, she called me a perv.” 
“I guess I’ll let her cool down before I ring her up.” She tried to comb 
her hair back with her fingers. “What will you do now that Rick is gone?”  
“I’m not sure,” Walter replied. “At least he was here long enough to 
carry the glass booth and connect the cables.” 
Kristella was out of topics, so she kept staring at the shiny magic glass 
box that held the promise of a rather interesting future. 
“Well - if you need anything, I’ll be around,” Walter said charmingly, 
took the empty plate out of Kristella’s hands, turned left on his heel and 
walked away. 
“Thanks,” she said before he was out of earshot. 
She stood up, stretched and went towards the stairs leading down into 
the basement. 
Let’s see if the shower works. 
 
91 
 
 
 
Rick was sitting on a large, cheap, yet comfy chair - one out of two 
standing in front of the TV in the living room - and searching the unfolded 
newspaper for a new job. 
There were a few workshops within half an hour of driving that looked 
promising. He had to be careful and avoid workshops of the same brand 
that he had quit; word surely had spread among the company. 
Or not. Maybe he gave himself too much credit for thinking anyone 
would care about a highly replaceable mechanic like him. 
He toyed with the idea of changing the field entirely and repair 
elevators or simply open up a private company that fixed common 
household problems, like clogged drains, broken lawnmowers - stuff his 
neighbours might need. 
Looking away from the paper in his hands, he noticed that everything 
in the living room was bleak. The walls were white, there was a small TV 
resting atop a rack, filled with nothing but a VCR, an extra VHS cassette 
they used to record shows or movies every now and then, and Bernand ’s 
video games console. There was no rug, just a plain wooden floor. 
And even with so few items around, it still felt packed. It definitely 
wasn’t the biggest apartment. 
The job Xave had given him probably would have changed a few things 
for the better. Then again, he wanted to come home and see his family and 
not die of some freak accident. 
Bernand arrived at home through the apartment door. “Dad? You’re 
here?” 
“Yes, I’m here,” Rick answered. He folded the paper and waited for his 
kid to sloppily take off his shoes and come inside the room. 
Bernand came running towards him and grabbed his arm. “Where were 
you last night?” 
“Didn’t your mother tell you in the morning?” 
“She said you had to stay late, and that you quit! Did you really quit?” 
His face was showing a mixture of sadness, irritation and anger. 

 
92 
Rick patted his back. “It’s okay, son. Yes, there were things happening 
that forced me to work way past midnight and I simply had to quit a job 
that doesn’t let me watch you grow up.” 
“How can you do this to me?” Bernand yelled, almost crying. 
“Doing what?” 
“I told everyone at school that you’re working with the scientist who 
was in the news, and now they’ll think I made it all up!” he whined. 
Rick hadn’t thought about that. And why would he? That was a silly 
reason to be angry. “Just tell them the truth,” he said, knowing that it 
wouldn’t do anything good, except for giving him time to think of 
something else.. 
“I can’t do that, I’d be the biggest loser and everybody would pick on 
me!” Bernand broke out in tears and darted off into his room, slamming 
the door shut behind him. 
“Oh, come on, Bernand!” Ricked called after him. He sighed, threw the 
paper on the ground and leaned his forehead against his hand. 
He had only been trying to do the right thing, and this was his reward?  
He got up, walked to Bernand’s room and knocked. 
“Go away!” Bernand bawled. 
Against Rick’s better judgement, he turned the knob and slowly went 
in. 
Bernand’s room was about two meters in width and three in length, 
containing a bed, a closet and a little desk. The child was lying face down 
on his bed. 
“Leave me alone!” he screamed into the pillow.
