“You have to understand, Bernand, I’m trying to be there for you. The 
job was way too risky and I could’ve been killed every day. There was a 
man yesterday who threatened to kill everyone with a pistol - and he was 
shot twice.” 
Bernand looked at his father with a red face, sniffling twice. 
“I could be dead right now,” Rick added. “Would you want that?” 
To his surprise, Bernand seemed to hesitate. He didn’t understand a 
word, Rick thought. 
“I’ll be in the living room,” Rick said. It was probably best to leave him 
to himself for a while. 
Bernand didn’t sleep that night and kept causing a ruckus. Objects flew 
around in his room, hitting the wall or the desk. He even attacked Roanne 
when she tried to calm him down. 
“I hate you, both of you!” he kept yelling. 
Rick had enough. “Stop it or I’ll break your video game,” he threatened. 
 
93 
“I don’t care about that stupid piece of shit,” Bernand cursed and threw 
himself into bed. 
“You don’t talk like that to your father, understand?” 
“You’re not my father!” 
That broke Rick’s heart. 
Roanne was standing behind him. “He doesn’t mean it,” she mumbled, 
trying to calm him down with an embrace. 
Rick pushed her arm away and left the room, followed by his wife. 
“How can he be so stubborn about such a trivial thing? It’s just a job, 
for god’s sake!” 
“You heard him, he bragged to all his friends about it, and he has a 
hard time finding friends in the first place,” Roanne tried to explain.  
It’s all about him, Rick’s mind started rolling. Ever since he was born, all 
attention had to go his way, and for what? Cheap food, tiny-ass apartment, no sex, 
no money, no contact with friends because they all hate kids, no dog, no nothing.  
“God fucking damn it,” Rick swore, driving his hands through his hair 
with a torn expression on his face. 
“Don’t talk like that, he can hear you,” Roanne warned him. 
“I’m tired, it’s 4 a.m. in the morning, the neighbours are probably 
gonna knock any second and all you care about are the words I throw 
around,” Rick attacked her. 
She blinked. “You got no reason to blame me now, Rick. I’m not the one 
screaming and keeping everyone awake. I have to get up early too, you 
know?” 
“For that half-time job at the restaurant that doesn’t pay shit, yeah, I 
know.” Rick started walking in circles. 
“Stop it!” Roanne raised her voice. She grabbed his arms, forced him to 
stand still and stared deeply in his eyes. 
“You know what?” Rick said. “I’ll keep the job. As long as he’s happy 
everything’s fine, right?” 
“You’re no good to us when you’re dead,” Roanne argued. 
“I’m no good at all, apparently.” Rick pushed her aside and marched to 
Bernand’s door. “You hear that? I’ll keep working for that freaking 
scientist.” 
The bawling in the dark room turned to sobbing almost immediately 
and then stopped altogether. 
Rick went to bed. 
 
94 
 
 
 
Walter closed the lid at the bottom of the glass booth and put the 
screwdriver in one of his coat’s big pockets. 
He admired his work for a second, correcting the alignment of his 
glasses. 
Let’s give it a try. 
“Kristella,” he exclaimed. “I’m doing another test run.” 
“Sure thing,” Kristella’s voice resounded from the other end of the 
warehouse. 
Walter took his cup of coffee, placed it in the center of the open booth 
and closed the glass afterwards. Two large steps to the right and he was 
standing in front of the console, ready to begin. 
First, the woodwinds. He flipped two yellow buttons. Trumpets. His 
left hand raised, as if holding a baton, he pushed another three knobs.  
The ground began to vibrate and a humming was audible. 
Percussion. 
With his foot Walter kicked a hand-sized button on the floor, which 
was connected to the console by three thin cables. 
Then, Walter turned two regulators to the far right: Strings. 
The noise turned into a shriek, the earth trembled - and then, silence. 
The coffee cup was gone. 
“Kristella!” he yelled. 
“The cup’s here!” she replied. “And it’s hot!” 
Walter threw his right fist into his left hand. I’ve done it, he thought. The 
light was basically gone, it was super energy efficient, and it even 
functioned perfectly over a greater distance - however, there was still a lot 
of potential. 
Now to test it on an insect, or a cat, or maybe a dog. 
He could test it himself, too, although he had never stepped inside his 
own machine. 
For a moment he tried to think of a reason as to why he was scared to 
be his own test subject. Wasn’t he confident enough in his creation? 

 
95 
Maybe all those dead rats back in his basement had rendered him 
slightly suspicious. 
Kristella approached him, holding his cup of coffee with only two 
fingers, barely touching it. “Here,” she said, placing it on the waist-level 
high wooden wall that separated this area from the one with the desk.  
Walter came closer to it and sniffed it. “Smells like coffee,” he analysed. 
Should he take a sip? Maybe it would impress Kristella. 
He picked up the cup, ignored the hot material, and tasted it with his 
tongue. “Tastes like coffee,” he concluded. 
“Now what?” Kristella said, amused. “When can you get back to 
inventing laser pistols?” 
“One thing at a time,” Walter replied, trying too hard to make it sound 
like a joke. 
Maybe he wouldn’t have to conduct another test; instead he could skip 
ahead and try to increase the distance once again. Luckily, new bundles of 
cables had arrived that morning, just three hours earlier, which were still 
lying next to the gate. He would simply have to connect all cables into 
longer ones, assuming they would bend enough, and run another test with 
ten times the range. 
Somebody rang the bell and Walter hurried to answer it. In one of the 
TVs he saw Rick standing in front of the fence, looking at the camera. 
Walter let him in. 
“What brings you here?” Walter asked without pretext. 
Rick stopped in the open door. “I was hoping you guys wouldn’t 
remember what I said. You know, something with the words ‘I’ and ‘quit’ 
thrown together.” 
Great, now I won’t have to carry cables around all day by myself, Walter 
thought. 
“I’ve no recollection whatsoever of what you’re talking about,” Walter 
lied, gestured Rick to come in and closed the gate behind him. 
Kristella arrived in the entrance area and greeted the mechanic as well. 
“Glad to see you again,” she said. 
“My son wouldn’t let me off the job,” Rick said, jokingly. “He’s the 
worst boss I’ve ever had.” 
“Ah, well, let’s get right to it then!” Walter said, clapping once. 
 
96 
 
 
 
Not long after Rick’s Return, the connection proved successful over a 
longer distance, and Walter, confident that it would work over forty 
kilometers, signalled the start of the construction. 
It took the self-proclaimed scientist, Rick and a hired crew of forty 
people ten days to lay the bundled cables from Taresville’s city hall to 
Monoura’s village center. Coated by a sturdy, black cable sheath and as 
thick as a child’s body, they ran mostly underneath the surface to lessen 
their impact on the environment. 
Due to unforeseen problems with the ground in a certain location, they 
were forced to cross the outskirts of another city, Whells, and its mayor 
wasn’t very happy about it. To avoid further delays, Xave, Petar and Isela 
made another contract with him, stating that Whells would be the third 
location to be connected to Walter’s new transportation system. 
At least twenty-five police officers were doing their best at keeping an 
eye on the cable at all times, even underground. There were several 
incidents where they either had to shoo away dogs, cats, or birds. Once, 
protesters even tried to cut through the black cables, but the police caught 
them red-handed. 
On the fourth day, an as of yet unidentified person managed to sneak 
by and throw a small explosive device through a manhole into the sewers, 
near the border of Whells. Three of those ten days an additional five men 
were paid to clean up the hole, and to fix destroyed landlines, power 
connections and the broken part of the brand new instant travelling cable.  
Walter’s bank account was leaking money, like a patient succumbing to 
a bloodletting. The mayors were still very supportive, but Isela had been 
doubtful from the beginning, and with every passing day it seemed harder 
to keep her convinced of the project. 

 
97 
The interest of the media had grown exponentially over time. In front of 
Walter’s warehouse there were always at least twenty people, five news 
vans, another bunch of random cars, and fans chilling out in their camps a 
few yards further away. Even Jeremy, the first human traveller, was still 
around, shouting praise through a megaphone during the day, when he 
was allowed to do so. Usually, there were three or four half-naked young 
women cheering him on, as if he were some kind of divine being. 
Whenever Walter walked outside to check on the long cable himself, 
Rick was always by his side to assist him as much as he could. 
Kristella had taken over public relations; she talked on the phone, met 
with people who wanted to know more about the machine that bore the 
promise of having a job and a house thousands of kilometers apart from 
each other, stepped in for TV and radio interviews, and tagged along with 
Xave for business talks. 
On day ten, both glass booths were set up in Taresville and Monoura, 
respectively, hidden inside small blue tents. Walter worked half a day on 
each tent, making sure they were properly connected, and ran tests with 
three generic items, a spider, and a rabbit. 
The rabbit was the only subject which Rick sent back from the other 
end, and Walter’s confidence had finally outgrown his doubt. More or less. 
On the day before the first official travelling from Taresville to 
Monoura, Walter, Xave, Kristella and Rick met the president of t heir 
country Lostarichi. They had dinner together with Petar and Isela in the 
finest restaurant of Taresville. A throng of constantly flashing cameras had 
awaited them at the entrance, much to the dismay of the bodyguards.  
From the first moment on, the president had had the word “doubt” 
written across his frown, but he forced himself to show goodwill and smile 
broadly. 
“Mr. Trimond,” the president said in an important voice, probably 
expecting to be recited for centuries. “Tomorrow you and your team are 
going to reveal a whole new way of transportation to the people all around 
the globe, right here in Taresville.” 
Walter was shaking, but trying hard to conceal it. “Yes, Mr President.”  
He barely touched his food. Xave, on the other hand, had finished a 
while ago. 
“Are you aware that this can - and will - introduce us all to a new era?” 
the president continued, his smile embedded in his face as if carved in 
stone. 
 
98 
“If you put it this way - no, I’m afraid I haven’t thought about that and I 
will cancel the unveiling and take it back to the drawing board to consider 
the aftereffects,” Walter’s words spilled out of him without realizing; a 
bodyguard chuckled behind him. 
The president nodded awkwardly. “Who will be the first to 
demonstrate that it actually works?” he asked. 
Kristella, quickly swallowing a piece of meat, replied: “That falls under 
my jurisdiction.” 
“Your jurisdiction?” the president repeated. “Shouldn’t the inventor do 
the honours?” 
Walter twitched. 
“I shall be the first official insta-travelling woman,” she continued 
without hesitation. “But there were two other humans before me, who 
should not be ignored.” 
The president looked stung, but only for a split-second before 
recovering. “A toast to the new era!” he shouted, raising his beer jug.  
“Cheers!” A dozen mugs clashed. 
Walter felt as if experiencing a dream, or rather a nightmare, and a part 
of him wished he could vanish like his test objects. 
The next day, all of that was forgotten when Kristella, Walter, Xave, 
Petar and Isela were standing in a triangle formation in front of the city 
hall of Taresville, with Kristella at the tip, behind a lectern, speaking into a 
microphone and addressing an audience of roughly two hundred people.  
The glass booth was to Kristella’s left, secured by Petar’s bodyguards. 
The cable bundle began at the bottom of it like a spider embracing its prey, 
and continued off into the sewers three meters away, where it received 
additional protection from two policemen. Walter knew that there were 
men in the canalization as well, overlooking every nook and cranny, up 
until the destination point in Monoura. 
It still boggled his mind, but there it was: the glass booth he had built in 
his basement, being stared at by awed onlookers. 
“As the president said yesterday,” Kristella’s confident voice boomed 
through two speakers on the sides of the city hall square. “We ’re 
witnessing the dawn of a new era. This will be the time when borders of 
regions all over the world start to blur, when international cultures mix 
even more than they already do.” 
Petar and Isela were dressed exquisitely; Petar had chosen a fine 
Stresemann, Isela wore a black dress that was one of a kind, a large yet 
elegant hat, adorned with a  feather, and dark gloves, that reached beyond 
the elbows. 
 
99 
Xave appeared in his classy suit. Walter’s appearance hadn’t changed 
much; white coat, jeans, random shirt, glasses. He had shaved and combed 
his hair, though. 
They all were nervous, yet differently skilled in hiding it. 
Someone in the audience shouted: “Beam us up, Walter!” 
Walter’s face turned red from anxiety. For a moment, Xave was worried 
that the scientist would collapse and tumble off the stage. Or maybe he’d 
lose his last bit of sanity, tear his shirt apart and start biting people.  
“Ladies and gentlemen, I give you - Walter Trimond,” Kristella 
introduced the scientist. 
She stepped aside, clapping. Half the audience applauded, only 
Walter’s personal fan club cheered, with Jeremy standing tall among them 
somewhere in the corner of Walter’s eyes. 
Walter approached the stand and tapped the microphone once. A 
shrieking noise pierced the eardrums of all people present, causing the 
majority to groan in discomfort. 
Walter’s mind drove two-hundred kilometers per hour through 
Wonderland, picking up man-sized mushrooms on his windshield. 
“I-” Walter stopped his racing thoughts and mentally slapped himself.  
“I thank you all for coming and believing in my invention.” He paused to 
swallow. Sweat pearls ran down his back and dropped from his forehead. 
He wanted to continue, opening his mouth to form words, but lost them. 
While clearing his throat, he produced a cheat sheet out of his left pocket.  
“Walter!” Xave whispered fiercely. “Get your shit together!” 
Walter looked back at Xave, threw away the small paper and stared at 
the audience again. “I have no doubt that what you are going to see here, 
today, will change the way you think,” he finally went on with a firm 
voice. “After this quick demonstration, the device will be useable by each 
and every one of you, but to keep it stable, a slight pause of ten minutes is 
mandatory.” Speaking a lot quicker, he added: “Naturally, these 
limitations will be eradicated in the near future.” 
To his right, a TV with a camera sitting on it showed Rick standing next 
to the other glass booth in Monoura. There weren’t as many people there, 
but Rick seemed stressed nevertheless. 
“Can you hear me, Rick?” Walter said. The camera atop the tube, facing 
the scientist and the glass booth behind him, conveyed both Walter’s voice 
and picture. 
“Loud and clear,” Rick’s voice came out of the TV’s loudspeakers. 
“Very well,” Walter said to the audience. “Kristella, would you kindly 
enter the glass booth?” 
 
100 
“Certainly,” she said, carefully stepping towards and into the booth, 
trying not to fall and embarrass herself. Her heart felt like a hammer, 
beating against her rib cage. 
Walter took the microphone off the stand, controlling its cable like a 
snake, went to the booth and closed it behind Kristella. She was now 
breathing air through three tiny holes on the bottom of the cell.  
Taking a deep breath himself, Walter approached the console next to 
the transparent device with the tense woman inside, rested the 
microphone on an even surface, and played the buttons like a keyboard 
player. 
The audience was silent now, expecting the female body to turn into 
little pieces any second now. 
“Go, Walter!” Jeremy screamed, his girls squealing along. 
Isela felt terribly out of place, Xave sensed by looking at her, as if 
standing on the stage of a rock concert aimed at a much younger audience.  
Taking in the crowd in front of him, a chilling sensation ran down his 
spine. He consciously felt the gun in his back pocket; always ready, just in 
case. 
Walter hacked away at his instrument. Steam pushed itself through 
vents on the back of the console, and a soothing hum started to rise. 
Rick kept staring at the camera, squinting to fight the sun’s rays, and 
wind blowing through his hair. Clouds were shrouding Walter’s spectacle 
in Taresville, fortunately; nobody had to watch the scientist through 
squeezed eyelids. 
The hum became louder and higher, and two supporting voices began 
to sing along one octave below. 
Kristella’s smile faltered a little and she pressed her hands against the 
glass, her breath appearing on the translucent surface. 
Petar was tapping with his foot, impatiently. Isela looked at her watch. 
Flipping buttons and turning knobs, Walter shook his butt to a rhythm 
only he was hearing. He hit a round pedal on the ground with his foot and 
punched another vital part of the console - and just as the spiralling noise 
became unbearable, Kristella turned to dust and was sucked into the cable. 
Walter’s machine shut down. 
The audience gasped in awe. 
There was no impressive light show, no thunder, no earthquake. Just a 
woman suddenly gone, like a vampire getting staked and its ash carried 
away by the wind. 
A policeman backed off from the slightly twitching cable bundle, as if 
scared by an anaconda. The mayor’s bodyguards tried hard to appear 
 
101 
indifferent. The one who had been sent through the travelling device 
before corrected his collar. 
A low rumble inside the cables raced through the sewers and got cut off 
in the far distance. 
Kristella was gone. 
His hair in a mess, Walter stared at the TV, along with all the other 
present eyes. 
Xave held his breath. 
On the screen, Rick turned around and looked at the booth behind him, 
taking two steps backwards, so that the camera could see more clearly.  
The cable shot Kristella’s dust with a damped bang inside the booth 
and a distant buzz resonated. For a millisecond the TV was flashing her 
skull, her muscular structure, half her eyeballs, the insides of her brain, 
and parts of her bare breasts, legs and belly, all covered in a rotating and 
pulsating red and blue and white particle storm. In the blink of an eye, the 
remaining molecules stuck to her face - and her body was fully restored, 
clothed in her elegant black pantsuit with a wide cowlneck top. She 
opened her eyes - and smiled. 
The audience appeared bewildered. Had the quick horrifying image 
been too disturbing? Walter’s mind ran back in time and told his past self 
to not remove the blinding light, just to make sure this reaction wouldn ’t 
happen. 
But then - they all cheered, laughed, some even cried. They applauded, 
like a wave crashing against a cliff. Xave ran towards Walter, wrapped his 
arm around him, almost stumbled, then shook his hand, looked at the 
audience and raised both of his fists. 
Walter waved shily. 
“She’s fine,” Rick said warmly in the TV, holding Kristella steady. A 
pleased choir of the audience was audible behind him. 
The police had to hold back several people trying to storm the booth.  
“I knew you could do it, Walter!” Jeremy cried. 
Isela looked stunned. She hadn’t seen Kristella being rebuilt on screen, 
but she was now a firm believer in Walter’s technology. Petar had a certain 
know-it-all expression on his round face. 
A gunshot ripped through the air and hit the booth, leaving a crack in 
the glass. 
 
102 
The audience’s cheer turned into a bone-chilling scream. Like a bunch 
of ants, they tried to escape the place, running into each other. Half of 
them had ducked, as had Walter, Xave and everyone else onstage. Two of 
Petar’s bodyguards jumped in Walter’s direction, while the other two 
looked around like aware watchdogs. 
Another shot echoed through the streets. 
The booth burst into pieces. Instinctively, Walter raised his arms to 
shield his face from the shattering glass. Xave coolly pulled him away, 
towards the bodyguards. 
The policemen talked frantically over their radios, pointing at a possible 
source of the shots in one of the high buildings a few hundred meters 
away. 
Sirens began to whirl, while the voices of the leaving audience faded 
away. 
“What’s going on over there?” Rick in the TV said with a worried face, 
still holding Kristella in his arms. “Walter? Xave? Say something, god 
damn it!” 
Buried below the two towering meat shields, Walter and Xave didn ’t 
move an inch. 
“Are you alright, Walter?” Xave said. 
“I think so,” the scientist replied, holding his head with his hands, and 
wishing himself away to a lonesome island. 
“Get off the stage and stay down,” someone yelled at them. 
The other two bodyguards had run towards Petar and Isela and helped 
them escape with a limousine, followed by Walter, Xave and their own 
two guards, leaving the place empty, apart from a dozen scattered 
policemen. 
The audience had produced a field of random garbage; papers, plastic 
drinking cups, confetti, and a bunch of balloons stuck in the trees of the 
park across the blocked off street. 
The camera in Monoura showed Kristella bending forward and 
vomiting off screen. 
 
103 
 
 
 
The president of Lostarichi himself had ordered the police to protect 
Walter, Xave, Kristella and Rick and bring them into one of the finest 
hotels in Taresville, a building eleven stories high and graceful in its 
appearance. 
Two days had passed since the worldwide revealing of Walter’s tech 
and the destruction of a vital part of it by a fairly skilled shooter. The 
investigations had revealed the improbability that the sniper had been 
aiming at Walter. It had been their goal to destruct the booth, warning 
Walter against moving forward. 
Walter had recovered the crumpled paper with the death threat from 
his house and given it to the investigators. They had said there might be a 
connection to the letter, the other robbery attempt at the warehouse (that 
man had already been sent to prison – and he hadn’t talked yet) and the 
shooting in front of the city hall. 
Someone definitely didn’t approve of Walter’s achievements. 
Xave was lying on a rather expensive double bed in the suite at the 
highest level of the hotel, his hands behind his head, looking at the ceiling. 
Next to him, Walter was sitting at a small table, writing and drawing on 
one paper, throwing it away, getting another, scribbling something new.  
“There has to be a way to get rid of that darn sickening feeling,” Walter 
mumbled. 
“I don’t know,” Xave said, even though he knew Walter hadn’t been 
talking to him. “It’s a nice business move. Vomiting people need to eat and 
drink more, so you can always pair a booth with a hot dog stand right next 
to it. Maybe even make the throwing up part of the action, giving everyone 
a free barf bag before travelling. You know, like in planes.” 
Walter scratched his head with the pen in his hand. “I’m sorry, what?” 
he asked, without looking at Xave. 
“I said, you can do it,” Xave lied, grinning. 
There was a knock on the door. 
“Come on in,” Xave called. 

 
104 
Kristella entered and closed the door behind her. 
The suite was really more or less one gigantic room, with a sleeping 
area, a living space featuring a modern TV, a kitchen with a curved bar, 
and two doors leading to bathroom and WC, respectively. 
“Any news?” Kristella asked. She went over to the leather couch in 
front of the TV and let herself fall onto it. 
“Walter is working on the puking issue and I’m dreaming of Rome,” 
Xave said, while getting up and walking to the bar. 
“And the cops? When are they letting us out of here?” Kristella seemed 
bored, her feet dangling from the left side of the sofa. 
“Never.” Xave poured himself a drink. “Unless, of course, they find the 
one,” he swallowed all of the golden content of his glass at once, 
“whodunit.” 
“You’re enjoying this way too much,” Kristella noted. 
“A free suite in one of the most expensive establishments of the hotel 
business? You’re right, how could I even dare to try and savour this 
situation!” 
“Blasphemy!” Walter shouted out of nowhere, holding up both his 
hands. 
“What?” Xave asked. 
“I just realized that if I had actually believed in an old idea of mine, it 
would’ve helped me a great deal from the start and prevented most of this 
mess,” Walter explained. 
Kristella buried her face in her hands. 
“And you’re calling yourself a blasphemer for not believing in 
yourself?” The salesman waved at him dismissively, turned around and 
didn’t expect any response. 
“I just like to use that word whenever I know I was wrong before, just 
because I was too stubborn to accept a fact.” 
“Blashphemy!” Kristella yelled, pushing her fist into the air. 
“Infidel!” Xave cried out. 
“Burn him! Throw him into a lake! Tie up his hands! In that order!” 
Kristella shouted. 
“Bite me,” Walter muttered and bent his head, focusing on his papers.  
“Where’s Rick?” Xave asked Kristella. 
“Down in the lobby talking on the phone with his family, I think he’s 
pretty torn about the circumstances,” she said. 
“What do you mean?” 
 
105 
“Well, on the one hand, his life just got a lot more exciting, but on the 
flipside, well, his life may be just a tad too exhilarating. His boy adores 
him, though.” 
Xave sat down next to her, resting his feet on the broad, but short table 
in front of them. His butt pressed several random buttons on a remote, 
which he hadn’t seen - and the TV sprang to life with a sizzle. 
A news woman appeared, sitting behind an empty gray desk, with

event. 
“-vestigations are continuing regarding the incident of the shooting in 
front of Taresville’s city hall, where a scientist named Walter Trimond has 
revealed a pioneering invention, allowing human beings to beam from one 
location to the other within an extremely small amount of time.” 
“It’s called travelling, for heaven’s sake,” Walter exclaimed, throwing

picked up another pen. 
Xave and Kristella looked at each other, amused. 
The screen displayed various theories about the location of the sniper in 
cheap graphics. 
“We could watch a movie,” Kristella suggested. “I hear they have a 
room packed full with tapes to borrow.” 
Xave continued to stare at the TV. 
“Why are you so calm, anyway?” she asked him. “Shouldn’t you be 
doing damage control or something?” 
“Oh, I’m thinking. My hands are pretty much tied though. I’m not a 
perfect salesman on the phone, I need to be face-to-face with people, and 
since I’m not allowed to leave, my business is handicapped.” Xave 
swapped positions of his feet. “Besides - the president and several mayors, 
including Petar and Isela, invited us to dinner tonight. It’s a private one, no 
press allowed.” 
“... until then, we will continue coming up with possible scenarios for 
Mr. Trimond’s invention,” the man on the screen concluded the topic. 
“Stay tuned for a report of a company which has just made public that they 
are also working on a beaming technology that will compete with the 
Trimond business. 
Walter’s pen fell out of his hand. Xave and Kristella sat upright. 
“What?” Walter gasped. “It can’t be!” 
 
106 
Xave stood up, walked towards Walter and put a hand on his shoulder. 
“Don’t be alarmed, you’ve simply started a race and you’re still ahead. It’s 
probably just a cheap imitation of some sort, or a quick cash-in, trying to 
sell stuff while it’s on everyone’s mind.” 
Walter breathed heavily. “They stole it.” 
“Don’t come up with unnecessary accusations,” Xave said. “How could 
they have stolen it? The basement of your house is completely empty; 
there’s no trace left of your baby. Your warehouse is safe, secured by the 
police around the clock.” 
Then it dawned on Xave. There could’ve been a leak. A bribery. A 
conspiracy. Now that Walter was out of the warehouse, it was basically 
free to be raided. 
They could’ve stolen it. 
“Aha! I see it in your eyes! You’re thinking what I’m thinking!” Walter

Xave shook his head, trying to forget all about it. “It’s not true until we 
find hard facts.” 
“It is a mighty convenient coincidence though, don’t you think?” 
Walter asked, sarcastically. “Stepping into the spotlight with basically my

“Let’s wait and see what the president and the mayors have to say

the news makes me believe that it could be some fake news item from that 
one network, trying to get attention. Surely someone would ’ve called us 
about it by now.” 
The phone rang. 
“Oh, that is just great,” Walter said, rose from his seat and kicked the 
bed. 
Kristella blinked. She hadn’t seen Walter in such a state before. 
Xave answered the phone. “Yes?” A long pause. “Oh, certainly. Did 
they show their passports to you? Are they real?” A mumble on the other 
end. “Very well, send them up.” 
Fuming, Walter asked: “What’s going on? Who’s coming up?” 
Xave began to smile. “Your parents.” 
 
107 
 
 
 
“How is my little boy?” Walter’s mother said with a mouth formed to a

Kristella thought he looked cute, and chuckled behind her hand.

Both were in their late fifties, yet they showed showed no sign of aging

“You know we love to travel, son,” Tom cut in. “Now that you’re

Walter seemed to have something on his mind, something he wanted to

His eyes met with Kristella’s and he took the opportunity to change the

Kristella was already on her way from the sofa, and greeted them

“Yes, I’ve seen you on TV,” Marlinda said through her wrinkled mouth, 
holding out a crooked finger in Kristella’s direction. 

 
108 
“Yep, that was me, making a speech, jumping to another town, and

Marlinda focused on her child again. “What will happen now? Do you

“The president has invited us to dinner,” Walter said. “We’ll figure

“The president?” Tom exclaimed, his jaw dropping.

“You really made it,” Tom mumbled in awe. 
“Isn’t he something,” Marlinda said, squeezing Walter’s left cheek. 
“I’m sure they wouldn’t mind if you tagged along,” Xave mentioned,

“The parents of the currently most important man in Lostarichi?” She 
scoffed. “Oh, he definitely wouldn’t mind! I even believe he already knows 
you’re here. Eyes and ears everywhere, you know?” 
“So, when’s dinner?” Tom clapped his hands. 
Walter checked his wristwatch on his left arm. “In four hours and 
thirteen minutes.” 
Tom looked around the room and noticed the TV. “Let’s see if Walter is 
on!” 
Five hours later, they were seated at a round dining table with gold and 
silver glimmering everywhere and each person’s plate surrounded by 
several knives, forks and spoons. 
The circle started with the president, followed by Petar, Xave, Walter ’s 
parents, the mayor of Whells named Sam Steward, the mayor of Illsburg 
called Clause Florentia, the mayoress of Vowkinn referred to as Tyra 
Omand, and finally Isela, Kristella, Walter, and back to the president . 
After they all had eaten delicious soup and warmed up to each other 
with small talk, the president stood up to speak and rang his glass three 
times with a small spoon. 
Everyone went silent. 
“Friends and colleagues, thank you for coming,” he said casually. “Let ’s 
begin our discussion.” 
 
109 
They were in a large private space of the hotel’s restaurant, behind 
closed doors. Every person had more than enough room to not feel penned 
in. All eyes were on the head of the state. 
He sat down again, put away his spoon and folded his hands above his 
plate, shaping a pyramid with his held up arms. “We still have no clue 
about the identity of the shooter, and we think it best to lay low for a 
month.” 
Xave interrupted his speech. “If I may - that won’t be necessary.” 
Kristella frowned at him. The mayors looked bemused. 
Tyra of Vowkinn raised her hand. “Let the president finish, please.” She 
wore a thick layer of make-up on her dark skin, her hair straight and long, 
hanging down to her lap, her fingernails artificially increased in length and 
pure white. 
“The youth of today may seem impatient, but this man is part of 
Walter’s success,” Whells’ mayor Sam noted. “We - the president and us 
mayors - haven’t been able to come up with a solution and I’m sure that 
you, Mr. President, were just leading up to let him speak anyway.” 
The president nodded, and gestured at Xave. 
Xave bowed and continued. “As I said, it won’t be required to keep 
ourselves hidden. In fact, I’m absolutely certain that now is the best time to 
continue. Nobody would expect it.” 
“So, what you’re saying is, Walter should simply repair the destroyed 
booth and let the public use it?” the president asked. 
“It is much grander than that,” Xave went on. “Mrs. Mantorry-” 
“Isela, if you will,” the mayoress said. 
The salesman swallowed. “Isela and-” He pointed at Petar and 
hesitated. The mayor nodded. “Isela and Petar have an exclusivity contract 
with us which would have to be annulled.” 
“What do you have in mind, Xave?” Isela asked, her nose held 
upwards. 
Clause, mayor of Illsburg, lit a cigar and produced a biting cloud.  
“We connect all the cities of the mayors present at this table,” Xave said 
in a firm tone. 
No reaction. At least, not at first. 
Kristella noticed how Walter’s parents felt as out of place as she did, 
trying to blend in as much as possible. They looked around nervously 
under the pressure of the awkward silence. 
“Splendid idea,” Clause said, exhaling smoke along with his words. 
“They can’t appear on all places at once.” 
 
110 
“Yet it would mean we would have to increase security and spread it 
out to several different locations, all at the same time,” the president 
pondered. 
“Not to mention all the cables that have to be protected,” Walter

travelling booths. When do you think we should do this, Xave?”  
“Why is that a problem?” the president asked. “Increase your 
manpower and work on all four simultaneously.” 
Kristella’s food was cold. A waiter came in and began removing the 
plates. Only Kristella and Walter’s parents noticed him. 
“Too risky, I can’t trust anyone yet with my plans,” Walter declined. 
“There already seems to be a competitor with stolen blueprints.” 
“I’ve heard about that,” Sam said, resting his arms on his round belly. 
“A company announced that they have discovered a similar technology.”  
“Call them and buy them up, if you have to,” the president said. “If it is

“It could be true, though,” Walter admitted. “I can’t be the only one 
coming up with that technology.” 
“Hm,” the president said. 
“I’ll deal with that - you’re right, Mr. President,” Xave said. “I’ll try to

Rick opened the door. “I apologize for being late,” he said loudly. A

Rick opened his mouth twice before he was able to pronounce words.

A calculator chattered in Walter’s mind, his eyes fixated on a point on
