698 days later 
 
On a rainy afternoon in early autumn, the train was rolling through the 
woods, ten cars long, unstoppable and yet somehow graceful.  
Leonard was staring at the raindrops on the restaurant car window to 
his right, making their way across the glass like worms through wood.  
The seat opposite to him was empty. He was travelling alone. 
The waiter brought a cup of steaming coffee. “14.60 Schilling, please.” 
Leonard opened his wallet, which he had already produced in advance, 
and gave him a twenty Schilling note and a five Schilling coin. “Keep the 
change,” he said in his low voice, looking at him through his shoulder -long 
hair. 
The waiter disappeared and Leonard slid the wallet back into the front 
left pocket of his jeans. 
He knew better than to use the pockets in the back or in his coat. Those 
were the easier targets. 
The clock, placed above the door at the end of the car, clicked and 
revealed to Leonard that there would be another two hours before he 
reached his destination: the city called Taresville, where, according to the 
news, a new travelling method had been developed, mainly by one man. 
Leonard had needed to leave the previous town anyway, for he never 
stayed for more than a week. Two days were already a bit of a risk in most 
cases. 
He picked up the small silver spoon, looked at it, let the lights shimmer 
in its surface. Cheap material, he thought, immersed it in his coffee and 
stirred the black fluid, hitting the inner sides of the cup in regular 
intervals. 
A couple sat down at the table behind him, the woman with her back to 
Leonard’s back, the man opposite to her. 
Leonard knew they were a couple the instant he saw them. Even if they 
didn’t stick together like glue, it was easy to see once you knew what to 
look for. He sipped his coffee and promptly burned his upper lip. 

 
113 
“I’m so excited,” she said to her husband. 
They were both around his age, definitely not much older than thirty 
years. Their wedding had probably occurred five years earlier, Leonard 
figured out just by listening to their voices. In love, but already a little b it 
too used to each other. Slightly bored, even. 
“Don’t get your hopes up, darling, I expect that it’s not being operated 
by the time we arrive,” the husband said. “But we’ll be there first thing in 
the morning.” 
“They don’t let people travel over night?” she asked in disbelief. 
“Didn’t you read the article you showed me?” 
“Well,” she said, giggling. “I was too thrilled and just wanted to see it 
for myself.” 
Leonard rolled his eyes. He was still wearing his brown coat, although 
now, especially with the coffee warming his body from within, it was 
getting hot. It felt good though compared to all the nights he had had to 
spend outside in his life. 
“The article said that they are only operating the travelling booths from 
nine in the morning to seven in the evening,” the husband recalled. “And 
it’s five o’clock, and we still have a good two hours ahead of us.” 
The wife sighed. “Awww. Alright then. But we could still go there and 
just have a look at it before we check in at our hotel.” 
“Okay, let’s do that.” 
“How far can we travel with those things anyway?” she asked. 
Leonard listened carefully. He had been wondering that as well. 
The wife’s husband cleared his throat and answered: “The article said 
that there are six different stations available right now, all of w hich connect 
to six different towns and villages all over Lostarichi, the furthest distance 
being, like, two hundred kilometers.” 
“Two hundred kilometers? How many kilometers are we currently 
covering?” she wondered. 
“I’d say, about the same. Maybe three hundred,” he guessed. 
“And it’s taking us close to four hours, right?” 
“Right.” 
“Did the article say something about the speed? Is it really instant?” 
Her voice sounded more like that of a happy little girl, Leonard thought.  
“Almost, I think,” he said, clearly not being able to remember. 
“Imagine that,” she said. “Not having to sit around in trains or cars for 
hours and hours. Just go wherever you want whenever you want.”  
“No more plane crashes, no more ships sinking,” he pondered. 
 
114 
Except for those cargo transports, Leonard thought. Nobody was going to 
send a container through an instant travelling booth anytime soon. Then 
again, what was keeping them from building larger installations?  
“A whole new world,” she said, dreamy. 
The waiter appeared and took their orders. 
For the rest of the journey Leonard kept listening to their ideas of how 
the new technology could improve their lives and what those “mad 
scientists” would come up next. Outside the window, the trees and the flat 
landscape got replaced by signs of civilization, larger and higher buildings, 
and cars and buses filling broader and more convoluted streets. And 
finally, dim sunlight got pushed back by artificial illumination. 
The train came to a halt in the Taresville train station. Leonard stood up 
and bumped into the woman who had been sitting behind him for two 
hours. 
“Oh,” she exclaimed. “Sorry!” 
“My apologies, Ma’am,” Leonard said. 
They exited the train, first Leonard, then the couple. He wished them a 
nice evening, just to be polite. 
Leonard walked through the station, a giant building, housing several 
shops, filled with a throng of people. There were signs everywhere, 
humans of all shapes, forms, and sizes, and the constant noise of dozens of 
voices talking at the same time. 
His hands in his coat pockets, Leonard went down the stairs and 
outside of the station. For a moment, he stood still and deeply breathed in 
cold city air, forming white clouds as he exhaled. 
“Greetings, Taresville,” he mumbled. “Long time no see.” 
In fact, he couldn’t clearly recall the last time he had been here. The 
eventful years in-between had carelessly overwritten his old memories. 
But the layout of several public places was ingrained in his mind well 
enough. 
He looked left and right, searching for a bus station. The couple was 
standing ten meters away from him, looking at a map. 
They did have the same destination in mind, he remembered. Simply 
tagging along should lead him there. 
He walked towards them, pretending to look for something. To his 
surprise, the woman turned around and started talking to him. 
“Oh hello, we met in the restaurant car. I’m sorry I bumped into you 
you,” she said, giggling. 
Her husband made a worried face, he had probably not expected her to 
talk to Leonard either. 
 
115 
“Can I help you?” Leonard asked, playing dumb. 
“We would like to have a look at the instant travelling booths and I was 
wondering if you knew the way,” she explained. 
Her husband is suspicious of me, Leonard thought. Oh well. “I don ’t 
know my way around this city,” Leonard explained. “I’d be interested in 
tagging along though.” 
“Sure,” the man said. “We believe we have to take that bus over there.” 
He pointed at a bus station over his shoulder. 
“Let’s try that then,” Leonard agreed. 
They walked towards a curve in the road and a sign showing two bus 
numbers. They asked an old woman for directions and took the second bus 
that arrived. 
The bus was crammed with people and Leonard heard another five or 
six persons mentioning the incredible new technology, assuring them that 
they were on the right track. 
Half an hour later, after the bus had finally made its way through the 
slow evening traffic jam, they arrived at Taresville’s city hall and got off. 
“Thanks, I’d probably still be sitting around at the train station, waiting 
for a miracle,” Leonard tried to jest. 
“No worries,” the woman said. 
They waved goodbye and parted ways. Of course, they would still be 
standing next to each other for a few minutes, Leonard realized, but at 
least he wouldn’t have to deal with them anymore. 
Keep social contacts to a minimum, he reminded himself. 
Too bad the woman hadn’t carried any purse on her when their bodies 
had met in the train. On the other hand, if Leonard had stolen something 
from her, he would have been the prime suspect and then getting to this 
place would have been more time consuming than necessary. 
So in a way, he had stolen their time instead of their money. An 
acceptable compromise. 
Two lights beamed at the city hall’s pillars in front of Leonard, and the 
place before the building was wide and made out of marble stone; it felt 
expensive. 
According to the news, the first official unveiling of the technology had 
happened right where he was standing, with the, as the paper had called it, 
“glass booth” being placed right at the top of the stairs leading in to the city 
hall and the crowd reaching way behind him, even standing on the street.  
Leonard couldn’t see any booth or machine or anything. Just the city 
hall and a bunch of people staring. But staring at what? 
 
116 
His eyes followed the gaze of the others, and he turned around. There it 
was. 
Across the street, there was a white, sterile building, which Leonard 
thought was new, but the park surrounding it had been there long before. 
Large bold and blue letters had been placed above the inviting entrance, 
forming the word “INSTANT”. 
A line of tourists reached far to the right along the street. There was a 
low hum, spiralling upwards, and ending immediately thereafter. The line 
moved forward slightly and was stuck again. 
Apparently, they were still operating. Leonard saw that the couple 
from the train had already figured that out; they were waiting at the far 
end of the line. 
He wasn’t here to try it out yet. He simply wanted to see if it worked.  
The cars, buses and motorcycles on the street between Leonard and the 
building stopped again and he zig-zagged through the vehicles to the 
other end. Without hesitating, he simply walked past the line - ignoring 
comments, gasps and moans directed at him -  and entered the structure. 
He had never seen anything like it before. 
It wasn’t a grand room like the daunting train station. Instead, Leonard 
realized, it was just about the size of a regular family’s house, maybe just a 
tad broader. There were five glass containers - or booths - standing in the 
center, and another five right behind them, facing the opposite side. Each 
booth had a man or woman in a spacey blue outfit standing next to it, and 
each and every one of those persons looked muscular and well trained, yet 
friendly and charming. 
Above every booth there were signs of the destinations they were 
connected to. Leonard read the five he saw on this side: Whells, Monoura, 
Glothermines, Mienz, Vowkinn. 
A boy was standing in the closed glass booth to Mienz, his mother in 
front of it, eyeing it suspiciously. 
“Don’t worry, Ma’am,” the woman in blue, responsible for this booth, 
calmed her down. “It is perfectly safe. Your husband is already on the 
other side, as you can see.” 
Leonard noticed very small TVs standing on the ground next to every 
booth, showing the other end of that connection. A man waved at the 
camera. 
The woman in blue pressed a button, a hum built up to a crescendo - 
and the child simply disappeared. 
The mother seemed terrified and looked at the small screen with both 
hands held in front of her mouth. 
 
117 
Her son appeared on the other end and the father high-fived him. Then 
they both cheered and encouraged the mother. They said something, but 
the TV didn’t have sound. 
The mother stepped in, looking at the inside of the booth, now 
trembling slightly with fear. 
“Are you alright?” the woman dressed in blue asked slowly and overly 
friendly. 
The mother appeared to think it over, and then nodded a few times in a 
row. 
The female travel assistant closed the booth’s glass lid, hovered her 
hand above the same button that had already sent the boy away, then, at 
the same time as she pressed it, the mother screamed: “No!” 
Then she was gone. 
Leonard couldn’t believe his eyes. Next to Mienz, the closed booth 
labelled Vowkinn started to sizzle and the bulky blue man next to it 
towered in front of it, shaking his head at the person next in line. 
An old man appeared out of thin air inside the booth. The guard 
opened the lid and helped the man step out. 
So they work both ways, Leonard assumed. 
“This is astonishing,” the old man said in a shaky voice. “To think that I 
lived long enough to experience this...” 
“Let the man outside,” the guard yelled, pushing away a few random 
people to help get the old man through, while the Mienz guard kept an eye 
on Vowkinn. 
“Sir, you’re not in the line,” another man in futuristic blue clothes said 
to Leonard. That man must be around to take care of other things, Leonard 
thought. His dark skin made the blue suit stand out especially well.  
“I just wanted to see it work, I’ll be on my way now,” he apologized. “I 
have a few questions though before I leave. How much does a ticket cost?”  
“No tickets; free of charge,” the guard said. “It’s all provided by the 
state of Lostarichi, which is tax money basically, in addition to funds 
provided by the Trimond company and several sponsors.” He gestured at 
the ad banners hanging from the ceiling, which, ironically, Leonard hadn ’t 
noticed yet. 
Two hums were audible. Another person gone, another one appeared.  
“I see,” Leonard said and created a mental note: Free. “Are you working 
around the clock here?” 
The guard looked distraught and impatient. “They’ve changed our 
working schedules into several shifts today, actually. We’re now open 
twenty-four seven.” 
 
118 
Leonard nodded. Open at night, he added to his list. “Thanks.” 
He found his way out through the crowd, and buttoned his coat. 
Quite impressive, his mind spoke. There must be a way to make use of this. 
But first - he needed a room. Subtracting the money for coffee, train and 
bus, he still had roughly 400 Schilling in his wallet. Sleeping in the near 
park was out of the question with all the security around, but it should be 
enough money for a half decent bed and four walls to keep him safe.  
He walked past the queue of people, which now bent around the corner 
at the next crossroads, and set out to look for a hotel. 
The next day couldn’t come early enough. 
 
119 
 
 
 
“Hello, Walter,” Xave said, while entering Walter’s suite. 
They had bought the hotel almost two years ago, slowly transforming 
the building into the Trimond company headquarters over time. Several 
rooms had been turned into offices, with walls removed and new ones 
erected. The kitchen had been kept intact, providing food for everyone 
working for the company, mainly preparing small meals that could be 
eaten quickly by technicians and security guards. The warehouse had been 
sold again and Walter’s parents had encouraged him to even sell their 
house; the house he had grown up in. Never would he have been able to 
go there again anyhow, without stalkers, fans or press people assaulting  
him. 
Xave was living in the same building as well, in a suite one floor below 
Walter’s. His old apartment was nothing to be sentimental about, so he 
had been able to leave it without remorse. 
“Xave,” Walter greeted his business partner, keeping his eyes on the 
plans on his desk. He was sitting on a broad leather chair, leaning forward 
and looking concentrated. 
Just as I like to see him, Xave thought. “The new cable failure detection 
you’ve let your team install seems to work,” he began. “The connection to 
Illsburg reported an error.” 
“What?” Walter looked up. He rose from his seat and went over to 
another desk in the corner of his suite next to the double bed. There were a 
dozen TVs stacked in three rows, some showing various scenes of security 
cameras, others showing maps of Lostarichi with various icons and signs. 
He loves stacking those tubes, Xave noted. 
“Dang,” Walter said. “Why did I miss that? It should be making a-” 
The scientist turned up the volume of the screen showing a map and a 
yellow exclamation mark on the line from Taresville to Illsburg. The 
speakers beeped loudly. 
“A sound,” Walter finished and shook his head. 
Xave laughed and Walter had to chuckle as well. 

 
120 
“As brilliant as you are, my friend, sometimes you just have to forget 
the simple things to show me that you’re still a human being,” Xave said. 
“I’ll send Rick and his team to have a look at it,” Walter suggested. 
“Good call.” Xave sat down at the bottom of the bed. “I already told 
Isela to cut down on security. The automatic system will save everyone a 
lot of time and money.” 
Walter didn’t respond to that; he returned to his desk. 
“Anything else?” he asked Xave, as if he were trying to say, “I’m busy.” 
“What are you working on?” Xave asked. 
“A crazy idea I had when I slipped in the bathroom in the morning. 
Wireless transmission.” 
“Wireless!” Xave said in awe. “How’s that supposed to work?” 
“I’m not sure yet, but I’ll certainly find a solution.” Walter wrote 
something with a pen. 
Xave started bouncing up and down on the bed. “By the way, people 
still love it. Keeping it free and making it available all the time was a smart 
move.” 
“It was your idea, of course it was a smart move,” Walter said 
sarcastically, but not in an attacking manner. 
“Oh right,” Xave said, grinning. 
“How’s Kristella?” Walter asked, picking up a protractor and drawing a 
circle in-between sketches and scattered notes. 
“She’s talking to mayors and politicians and preparing to expand to 
other cities. I’m getting her the contacts, making the first steps and selling 
them your idea.” 
“Doesn’t it sell itself? I thought people like it.” 
“They do, but the majority still needs that little push to fully accept it. 
And for many, a little push means a leap of faith. The international media 
is more or less embracing instant travelling, but they’re all careful about it, 
just in case we crash and burn.” 
“Will we crash and burn?” the scientist asked. 
Xave had expected another one of his dubious questions. “At the 
moment, we’re swimming in money and support. Even the competing 
company that we fought against a year or two ago was no issue when we 
discovered that they were basically just spewing hot air to get attention, 
with no tech to support their claims. Remember that?” 
“I sure do, still waiting for the next one to come out of hiding to smash 
our success.” 
“I love your pessimism, Walter,” Xave said sympathetically. “It always 
motivates me to prove you wrong.” 
 
121 
“I know,” Walter admitted. Then he looked at Xave. “Please, stop the 
Xaveman Bounce.” 
“Why is your bed bouncier than mine, anyway?” Xave said and sat still. 
Walter looked at his calculations again. Or random drawings. Or whatever 
they were, Xave thought. 
“It stops me from going to sleep. Whenever I try to fall into my bed, it 
spits me back out immediately,” Walter joked. 
“I’m impressed,” Xave said. “That was a fairly decent attempt at what 
we mortals call ‘humor’.” 
“You mortals simply haven’t got the divine ear to catch all the jokes I 
make all day,” Walter said with an exaggerated noble tone. 
Xave looked around and remembered what the suite had looked like 
after the shooting incident at the city hall. 
“Still no sign of the shooter,” Xave wondered. “I hope that son of a 
bitch never returns. When was the last time you left this building?”  
Walter scratched his head, but still kept looking at his most interesting 
papers. “Not sure, the kitchen staff feeds me well. And I still have to build 
new booths in the fifth and sixth floor. Not much sense in leaving this 
place. Even my parents are already god-knows-where.” 
Xave knew better than to pursue the “parents” subject. “Which reminds 
me,” he said. “When can we finally outsource the booth production? There 
are factories all over the globe that could help us out. Don’t you think 
we’re ready?” 
“I can’t give out my plans to anyone else, Xave,” Walter said firmly. 
“You know that.” 
“I know, yes, but the technology is already out there, people know it ’s 
your invention. We already have several patents for it. Nothing’s gonna 
happen.” 
Walter sighed and let go of his writing tools. He looked through the 
window behind him. 
“If you want the world to use your technology as a replacement for 
cars, trains, planes, ships and all other means of transportations - you can’t 
sit around on your plans and keep them to yourself. You’re already a 
footnote in the history books of the future, now I believe it’s time to get off 
your ass and make them write a full chapter about you.” 
“You’re trying to sell my own invention to me,” Walter remarked.  
“Yes, I believe I am,” Xave said, smiling. 
“What if I like to sit on my plans?” 
“A bird can’t sit on its eggs forever, one day those eggs hatch, the 
chicks grow up and have a life of their own.” 
 
122 
Walter sighed. “You and your stupid analogies.” 
Xave knew it was pointless for now. But he will agree soon, he thought. 
“Well - I’ll talk to Rick about the cable failure now and tell him to check 
it out,” Xave said and stood up. 
“Alright, thanks.” Walter picked up his pencil again. 
Xave was almost through the door, when Walter said: “Fine, you win. 
I’ll prepare the plans and documentations for mass production.” 
Surprised and relieved, Xave looked at him, holding the door handle 
with his right hand. “No worries, Walter. This is a good choice.” 
“Don’t make me regret it,” the scientist replied. 
 
123 
 
 
 
Rick hit the beeping alarm clock, which continued to illuminate the 
bedroom with its green digits, showing 5:00 in tall intimidating numbers. 
The annoying repetitive sound died off immediately; he sat up, placing his 
feet on the ground. 
He looked over his shoulder and watched Roanne for a second, just to 
see if she was merely asleep and still breathing. 
The blanket covering her slender body up to her shoulder was moving 
up and down very slowly. 
Sometimes he had these weird feelings that she might die in her sleep 
and he was thankful for every time her breathing proved his fears wrong. 
He got up and walked into the bathroom. 
Living in their new house at the border of Monoura, they had quickly 
grown accustomed to having lots of space. The bathroom, for instance, 
consisted of a bathtub, a separate luxurious shower, two sinks and broad 
mirrors. 
While Rick was brushing his teeth, he went over Walter’s instructions 
that had been given to him the night before over the phone. 
It all boiled down to one issue: the connection between Illsburg and 
Taresville was down and it couldn’t be fixed remotely. The error detection 
system was good enough to locate the approximate location of the failure, 
and Walter had given him the coordinates. 
Walter’s cables allowed for a safe transmission, even if one or two of 
the bundles had been cut. Every week or two, Rick had to repair at least 
one of them. But rarely was a line malfunctioning completely. 
He spat out white foam, put away the toothbrush and flushed his 
mouth and face with water. He didn’t take a shower. Where he had to go, 
cleanliness was irrelevant. 
Bernand appeared behind him in his blue pyjamas, and father and son 
looked at each other in the mirror. 
“Can I come with you?” the boy said, rubbing his right eye. 
“Not today, son,” Rick answered. “I’m going into the sewers.” 

 
124 
“But I want to,” he persisted, yawning. 
“Look, kid,” Rick turned around and crouched down in front of him, 
matching his son’s eye level. “You can’t even see anything yet through 
those tired eyes. Besides, there’s nothing to do for you there. And I’m not 
going to let you travel unless it’s working again. Ask your mom if she 
wants to travel to Taresville and buy you a burger after school.” 
“Can’t I skip school?” 
“We already had that discussion,” Rick said and stood up. “School is 
important, and unless you’re feeling sick, you’ve got to be there.” 
“I don’t feel so good,” Bernand said, his voice slowing down, and his 
tongue sticking out of the side of his mouth. 
Rick grabbed Bernand and flung him in the air, almost hitting the 
ceiling with Bernand’s head - and tickled him. Bernand squealed; Roanne 
woke up. 
“I oughta call the police on you two noisemakers,” she complained. 
Rick put Bernand down again. “If I fix this cable, I promise you, I ’ll let 
you travel to Illsburg as soon as possible. Cool?” 
“Cool,” Bernand said and ran back to his room. 
Roanne looked at her husband, her hair pointing in all directions. “Why 
are you up so early, anyway?” 
“I’ve got a cable to fix before the onslaught of the travelling tourists,” 
Rick said. “Can you drive him to school?” 
They still owned the Ford Escort. Rick had vowed to keep it alive as 
long as possible; he simply loved that car. 
“Sure,” Roanne said, even though she meant the opposite. 
Rick kissed her on the forehead. “See you soon, sweetie.” 
“Yeah, yeah,” she said playfully and fell back asleep. 
Rick left the house with his heavy tool bag and began walking towards 
the center of Monoura. 
The autumn sun was rising effortlessly in the distance as he passed a 
park with a sparkling fountain and ducks swimming in it. After that, a 
bakery teased his nose and stomach, but he had to resist - there was not 
enough time. Besides, he never ate anything before investigating a cable in 
the sewers; it was counterproductive. 
The traffic on the main street leading straight through Monoura was 
light; there were very few people awake at this hour. 
He approached the central place, an area plastered with granite stones 
and surrounded by weeping willows and small statues of important 
political figures. There it was, a small black building with the blue let ters 
“INSTANT” written above the entrance. 
 
125 
It was kind of hard to imagine that approximately two years ago 
Kristella had been sent here through the prototype of those glass booths, 
and while Walter and Xave had been shot at on the other end, Kristella had 
thrown up right in front of him and the cameras. 
Such a memory was hard to forget. 
Rick greeted the woman in the blue suit, who was guarding the booth 
inside the building. “Good morning, Hannah.” 
“Good morning, Rick,” she said. Hannah was about 40 years old, but 
the tight blue suit revealed a body that ignored that fact. 
Rick scolded himself for thinking like that when his wife was lying in 
bed at home, with at least three times the attractiveness. 
But, that didn’t stop his brain from doing so. 
“Lots of travellers today?” he asked, just to get other thoughts in his 
head. 
“Not yet, but wait another two hours and you won’t recognize this 
place anymore,” she said. 
“There’s an error I have to fix on the Illsburg route, do you know 
anything about it?” 
“It’s not working, that’s all I’ve been told.” 
“Fair enough,” Rick said, and entered the glass booth. 
“You ready?” she asked, holding the front lid. 
“Sure,” Rick said. 
He still remembered the first time he travelled, he had been utterly 
nervous, and he had had to put his hands into his pockets to hide the fact 
that they had been shaking. Then, he had heard a short burst of static, and 
seen a light - and had woken up on the other end, feeling slightly dizzy, 
but without the need to throw up. Walter had asked him a hundred times, 
but no, Rick hadn’t felt like puking. Walter had been pleased. 
“Here we go,” Hannah said. 
She closed the lid and pushed a button - the one that ignited the mass 
changer, Rick remembered. 
Hannah smiled and waved him goodbye, as she was trained to do. 
Rick’s right corner of his mouth curled into a smirk, the noise of the 
turbines at the bottom of the booth increased as they spun faster and faster 
- then Rick saw the light again and heard absolutely nothing. 
Sometimes he would make out damp scratches, as if he were 200 
kilometers below sea level, listening to a giant crab digging a hole in the 
mud. 
It was a peculiar feeling; floating, but heavily drawn downwards, blind, 
but seeing bright light, deaf, but hearing muffled clunks and sizzling.  
 
126 
Then, his senses kicked back in and a colorful image was shot at his 
eyes again - he had reached Taresville, and there was a man in a blue outfit 
opening the glass booth and offering a hand to help Rick step out.  
Rick pressed his eyelids together, then spread them wide open, grabbed 
the man’s arm and crawled outside. 
“Thanks,” Rick said. 
“You’re welcome, Rick,” the man responded. Rick was known amongst 
most company personnel, even though he often couldn’t return the favor 
of remembering names. 
“How are you feeling?” the man asked. 
“Dizzy, but it will wear off in a few seconds,” Rick replied. 
“Hey, Rick,” another blue space man standing in front of the Vowkinn 
booth greeted him. 
Rick waved. “Do you guys know anything about Illsburg?” 
“I do,” a woman said, appearing from behind the Whells booth. She 
had a Walkman attached to her belt and an ear plug in her left ear. “I ’m 
responsible for Illsburg.” 
“What’s your name again?” Rick asked unabashedly, knowing he had 
heard it before. 
“Rae,” the woman in blue replied. 
“Ah, right. Tell me about Illsburg, Rae.” 
The tool bag he had tucked under his arm grew heavy and he shifted it 
to prevent it from slipping from his grip. 
Behind him, a traveller - likely a businessman - stepped into the 
building, positioned himself in the booth to Glothermines and was sent 
away. 
“The last one I sent there was an old granny,” Rae explained. “She 
definitely arrived on the other side, but after that, it just stopped working. 
Jayne had to pay her a taxi back to Taresville.” 
“An old woman, you say, do you think she had any, you know, 
artificial limbs, joints, or anything like that?” 
“I don’t think so, why do you ask? Doesn’t it work with everything?” 
“Yes,” Rick said, thinking for a moment. “In theory, at least.” 
There were ten booths in total, standing in two rows, back to back; Rick 
had appeared from one facing the entrance area. The broken Illsburg line 
was connected to one of the booths on the hind row. Rick went around to 
it, placed his tool bag in front with a loud clank and examined the booth 
from afar, hands on the hips. 
Maybe he wouldn’t have to check out the sewers after all. There 
could’ve simply been something wrong with this machine. 
 
127 
Rae had followed him and Rick gestured at her. “Make sure nobody 
gets back here, just say we’re having technical difficulties.” 
He just wanted some privacy. There was nothing he had hated more in 
his old job than customers watching him while he had crawled under their 
cars. 
“Other people could arrive on those four at any time,” Rae argued.  
“Just lock these lines for now, please,” Rick said. 
“Alright.” Rae went to the mini consoles next to the other four and 
pressed a certain combination on each of them. Nobody was able to use 
these routes now. 
“Did you make sure they weren’t in use?” Rick asked. 
“Of course, I’m not stupid,” she said. 
“Sorry.” Rick bent down to his tool bag, grabbed a screwdriver and 
faced the booth. “Let’s have a look then, shall we?” he mumbled to 
himself. 
Half an hour later, Rick had placed various objects around him in a neat 
manner; tiny screws, long screws, cogs, cables, small motors, rubber bands, 
fans, plastic grids and pieces of electronics with chips on them. 
Ever since he and his team of ten people had built half of the booths, he 
knew the plans like the back of his hand, and sometimes he wondered if he 
were able to build one blindfolded. 
He took a cogwheel between thumb and index finger and held it 
against the neon light. Nothing. 
Every vital part was in best shape; not new, but good enough for at 
least another year or so. 
He sighed and as he started to put everything back together - he 
noticed something odd. 
One of the black plastic boxes that contained a large fan for cooling felt 
as if something inside shifted weights when held to different sides.  
He stuck two fingers through the grid, past the blades - and touched 
something. 
It was soft, fleshy - furry, hairy. 
He produced a smaller screwdriver and painstakingly opened the filter 
box. 
Once the grid was removed, he turned the box on its head - and a small 
dead rat fell into Rick’s lap. 
“Ugh!” He jumped to his feet, and the miniature corpse landed on the 
ground between screws and bolts. 
On the other side, people were already lining up again to travel, the 
booths humming and buzzing, the crowd chattering and cheering.  
 
128 
He had to show this to Walter, even if it meant nothing in the end. He 
looked around for a bag or any other type of container, but couldn ’t find 
anything - except for one of his gloves. 
Oh well, he thought. I’ll get a new one. 
The dead fur ball fell into the glove. Rick folded it carefully across a 
horizontal line of the palm, and stuck it in his jacket. 
It took him almost another hour to put everything back together. He 
ran a quick test to see if it worked. 
Nothing. 
The rat hadn’t caused the machine to malfunction. 
Off to the sewers then, Rick thought with dismay, and wiped sweat off 
his forehead. 
“I couldn’t repair the damn thing,” Rick said, while heading for the 
door with his tool bag. “I’m gonna check it out from below.” 
“Alright, Rick,” Rae said, while closing the lid for another traveller. 
“Take care!” 
“Come back in one piece,” another blue guard called after him. 
Rick pushed through the waiting line and went around the INSTANT 
building. 
Illsburg lay directly next to Monoura, maybe two kilometers apart, but 
Xave had told him that the error had appeared closer to Taresville’s main 
station. And as usual in such cases, instead of going through the next ten 
sewer entrances, he decided to bite the bullet and walk until he saw the 
problem. 
It was a good hour before midday, so still plenty of time. 
The manhole had been put right behind the INSTANT structure, for 
easier maintenance. Rick pulled out a crowbar from his tool bag, stuck his 
crowbar sideways into one of the holes, twisted it, and pulled it upwards 
with all his strength. The round stone cover moved. Rick dragged it to the 
side and replaced the crowbar in his bag. 
He climbed down. 
For a quick moment on the ladder, he thought the rat was actually alive 
and running around in the glove in his pocket, but he knew that the jacket 
moved on its own and calmed himself down. 
Behind the ladder he recognized the good old giant box where all the 
travelling cables were captured into one huge mass of black rubber and 
wires, exploding at the top, seemingly digging their own holes upwards 
through the cement walls, to the INSTANT building. 
The air already smelled slightly rotten and humid. Rick sniffed. 
 
129 
“Let there be light,” he mumbled as his foot stepped on firm ground, 
just to hear his own voice. 
He punched a light switch, and a long hallway stretched out in front of 
him, with lights placed on the ceiling every ten meters. 
At least, he wasn’t standing in sewage. Yet. 
All cables were placed in the lower right corner of the corridor, as thick 
and massive as three stacked walruses. 
While Rick was following them for about a minute, he produced a map 
of the sewers out of his bag, with the complete layout of Trimond ’s net. A 
crossroads broke up the cables. They went in three different directions.  
He was now directly below the park, according to the plan. Illsburg 
was the same direction as Monoura - so, West, and currently he was facing 
North-East. 
His finger traced the route to Illsburg from his current standpoint.  
“Let’s go left, then,” he said, his voice echoing back and forth. 
A breeze carried more of that foul stench to his nose. 
He turned around, as if somebody had watched him. Fortunately, the 
hallway to the East was empty, aside from the thinner bunch of cables.  
His heart didn’t agree, and decided to beat a wee bit faster, just in case. 
Fifteen meters further, the hallway abruptly led into an old tunnel with 
dark walls and water pouring in from various pipes. 
The light of the modern hallway ended about five meters in the 
distance, which was also where the tunnel bent down slightly and water 
filled the floor. 
“Here we go,” Rick muttered. He grabbed a bulky flashlight from his 
bag, turned it on, and lit the way. 
Five steps later, his feet were wet. 
The smell became unbearable. 
One of those days when I wished I hadn’t quit my old job, he thought. 
At least his son was growing up in a house - a luxury his own parents 
hadn’t been able to provide. 
While slowly moving forward, he kept checking the INSTANT cables, 
which were affixed to the walls, just above water level. 
Splashing noise all around him, he began to constantly feel followed, 
even though his rational mind kept slapping his imagination for being so 
stupid. 
 
130 
The tunnel split in two, luckily the cable still ran in only one direction, 
so Rick kept hugging the left wall, still checking the thick black coating for 
any damages. Every time he had to focus on the cable, the darkness 
embraced him even more, and he had to quickly look forward and 
backward again before moving on. 
The air became thicker, and if that weren’t enough, several drops of 
unknown liquid landed on Rick’s skin every now and then. 
He jumped - a loud motor sound drowned the splashing, and was gone 
as fast as it had occurred. 
Probably a truck driving past a manhole, Rick thought, pointing his 
flashlight back and forth. 
“Man the fuck up,” he cursed at himself. His grip around the flashlight 
tightened and he put more weight into his steps to make him feel stronger, 
which resulted in louder splashes. 
Then, he hit a dead end. 
It looked as if a giant had decided to put his foot down, not paying 
attention to the tunnel below. There was rubble everywhere, loose stones 
and bricks, pieces of decayed wood and broken rusty metal pipes.  
His flashlight shone at the Trimond cable. 
One of the broken metal pipes had forced itself through the coating 
near the collapsed wall. The pipe was stuck deep inside, like a knife in an 
eggplant. 
He could have tried to remove it, but the collapsed wall made that 
difficult. 
“I’ll need help with that,” Rick mumbled, pointing his flashlight across 
the damaged stones. 
He turned around and retraced the cable back to the exit. 
 
131 
 
 
 
“How about it?” Isela asked, her eyebrows raised. 
The mayoress of Taresville was seated on a white chair which had an 
artistic curve in the back. Her legs, covered by a touch of brown stockings, 
extended elegantly below the coffee table, on which two cups rested, each 
containing steaming black fluid. 
Opposite to her, Kristella, in a dress that easily competed with Isela’s, 
tried to appear as cool as ice, when in fact, she was as nervous as a gazelle 
trapped in the lion’s den. 
“Well,” Kristella finally said after thinking it through for a few seconds. 
“You certainly have shown us immense support in the past years. I see no 
reason to object.” 
They were seated outside of a café in a large pedestrian area full of 
shops of all kind. The surrounding tables were occupied, mostly by 
tourists and people conducting business in their obligatory outfits. Waiters 
continually dashed through the tight alleys between human backs, 
returning empty trays into the café and reappearing with beverages and 
snacks. 
Kristella noticed a suspicious middle-aged man in a suit sitting alone at 
a table behind Isela, occasionally eyeing the mayoress over the edge of his 
newspaper. 
“Please don’t mind him,” Isela said, following Kristella’s gaze. “He’s 
just here to protect me.” 
Isela turned back around and drank from her coffee. Light wrinkles 
around her mouth shamelessly revealed that her body was slowly aging. 
She replaced her cup and continued talking business. 
“I assume we’ve reached an agreement: Taresville shall be the first 
town to connect outside of Lostarichi,” Isela concluded with what seemed 
to be an attempt at an honest smile. “I’ve brought the necessary 
documents.” 
She handed Kristella six sheets of paper stuck together by a clip . 

 
132 
“I’ll show them to Walter and Xave and have them sign it,” Kristella 
said. 
“No worries, darling,” Isela said, throwing her hair back. 
Darling? Kristella tried to suppress her irritation; shook off the 
comment as a common way of talking to women almost half her age. 
Kristella emptied her cup. 
“What does it feel like?” Isela asked. 
“What exactly?” 
“Travelling.” 
Kristella was surprised. “Have you never tried it?” 
Isela shook her head. 
“Hm,” Kristella said, looking at the table to try and remember the exact 
feeling. “Well, it stings a little. Like, needles all over your skin. For a really 
short moment you feel... torn apart.” 
“I see,” Isela said. 
“There’s a noise that somehow resonates from inside your head, it 
resembles that of a large waterfall. And then - there’s light. Not the light 
you see when you look into the sun, it is more of a, uh, impression of being 
engulfed as a whole.” 
Isela listened closely, leaning forward and resting her chin on her right 
hand. 
“You can almost touch it. But - it’s not warm, in fact it’s cold as ice. All 
of those feelings last for merely five seconds or less, but in that state you 
are lying in the arms of eternity.” 
“Is that a text you’ve memorized?” Isela interrupted her. 
“I’m sorry if it sounds like that,” Kristella said. “I’ve had to try and 
explain the feeling to a lot of people, especially the press, and I’ve found 
better words every time I described it.” 
Isela was satisfied with that. “What then?” 
A waiter took away Kristella’s empty cup and asked if she wished for 
anything else. She shook her head. 
“Nothing,” Kristella continued. 
“Nothing?” 
“You wake up at the other end. It used to be slower, as in, you took part 
in the rebuilding process. It didn’t hurt, it was more like a...” She hesitated. 
“A bad dream.” 
“How come?” 
“As if you were floating above fire. That final process has been sped up 
a lot by Walter and Rick, so it’s almost gone. People don’t actually see you 
getting reconstructed anymore.” 
 
133 
Isela also drank the last drop of her cup. Then she said: “Do you think I 
should be the first to travel outside of Lostarichi into another country?”  
“By all means, Mrs. Mantorry,” Kristella supported the idea. “What 
would stand against it?” 
Isela wanted to say something, looked to her side, and ran her index 
finger across the round edge of her empty cup. “I fear death.” 
“You don’t trust the machinery?” 
“Now I do more than before.” 
“I’ve travelled at least once or twice a week for the past two years,” 
Kristella said. “I’m still in one piece, as you can see.” 
As if trying to find a loose molecule, Isela looked her up and down. 
Kristella felt that the mayoress didn’t only look for missing body puzzle 
pieces, but also to examine her dress closer. 
Isela changed the topic. “I’ll meet up with two presidents and see which 
one is more open to the idea of a temporary exclusivity deal. My assistant 
will call you when I know more.” 
“Wonderful,” Kristella said, noticing that the discussion was about to 
end. “Thank you.” 
They shook hands, although for Kristella it was more like holding her 
hand with a slight squeeze. They rose from their seats and went away in 
opposite directions. 
 
134 
 
 
 
Leonard’s fingers reached deep into his coat pockets - and only retrieved 
crumbs, dust and dirt. 
His talent of acquiring money with ease had resulted in the habit of 
living extravagantly - especially concerning exquisite food. In turn, he 
often found himself with nothing to his name. 
He sighed at his empty hands. 
It’s time. 
While strolling through the rural residential area, dodging playing 
children and evading a whole group of preschool toddlers with their neon 
colored hats and their anxious caretakers, he thought hard about possible 
advantages of travelling. There were certain benefits; he just wasn’t able to 
see them yet. 
Using the booths to flee from a group of followers was not an option. 
They would take him far away, but in a predictable direction, and in an 
environment full of people. 
Well, not at night. But there were still security guards in silly violet 
science fiction costumes. Or were they blue? He couldn’t care less. 
He crossed a street and someone honked at him. 
Standing in line would be an opportunity. Right before his turn, he 
could rob something valuable from the person in front of him, then travel 
to a different destination, and leave them to figure out why the wallet 
hadn’t been brought along. 
It was the system’s fault, people might say. It leaves wallets out of the 
rebuilding process and materializes on some other location where already 
heaps of wallets had gathered. It’s like a big conspiracy of the crazy 
scientist. 
Or - they could realize it was him and tell the guards on the other side 
to pin him down. 
It was no good. 
He kicked a tiny stone and it bounced off the back of a parking car. 
Nobody saw it. 

 
135 
The fact that all stations showed video footage of the other side made it 
difficult to get rid of the security guards on one end before trying to use 
their guarded booth as an exit. Besides, he couldn’t press the button 
himself. Somebody had to do it from outside. 
Would he really have to continue stealing the normal way? 
He wasn’t the working type. Stealing was his calling. He had perfected 
it a long time ago, yet he always tried to find a new challenge. It kept him 
on his toes. 
“Excuse me,” he said to a young woman, waiting for a bus. 
“Oh!” she exclaimed, and grabbed her black fur hat, trying to keep it on 
her head, as Leonard hurriedly walked between her and a pillar of the 
small bus station. 
Her handbag, hanging from her right shoulder, had been open and 
Leonard - in a swift, elegant motion - had retrieved a purse, while 
deliberately touching the left shoulder. 
He kept marching around the corner of a high-rise building, down a 
few stairs, back up another, and hid in the next park. 
There was a couple strolling past the high trees, an old woman on her - 
assumedly - daily excursion, and a jogger resting on a bench. 
Leonard slowed down to a casual walk and opened the purse, 
pretending it was the property of his - imaginary - wife. 
It helped believing in his own lies - that way people wouldn’t get 
suspicious. 
Twenty Schilling, a worthless ring - probably a luck charm, a photo of a 
teenage girl - maybe the woman ten years ago, and a bottle cap - a 
souvenir of a holiday or a special night. 
He made a content face, as if to say, thank god, all of his wife ’s things 
were still in there - and put it away. 
For all his skills, he kept pulling unnecessary risky stunts like this. 
Twenty Schilling. Definitely not much. 
At least he had made sure to make her stumble a bit, so she couldn ’t 
have gotten a good look at him. 
But anyway. 
The park ended and he took a left turn to the city hall and the 
INSTANT building. 
Yeah, why not, he thought. 
The line of people was fairly short. Leonard queued up. 
Ten minutes later he saw himself inside the building, with only three 
people in front of him. They were asked their destination and sent in the 
correct direction. 
 
136 
Leonard’s turn. 
“Good day, where do you want to travel to this instant?” the woman in 
the blue suit asked, a happy smile plastered on her face. 
“Any place where I don’t have to wait,” Leonard said, as politely as 
possible. He wasn’t much of a charmer when he didn’t concentrate. 
She turned around, checked for vacancies, and faced Leonard again.  
“Vowkinn just departed,” she said. 
Skipping the chit-chat, Leonard went past her. 
Another guard greeted him, a man this time, probably Leonard’s age. 
“Please step inside the glass booth,” he said to Leonard. 
Leonard, now getting a bit nervous by the sight of the alien looking 
machine in front of him, shoved an unsure foot inside, followed by the rest 
of his body. 
He turned around and looked at the guard again. 
“Remain calm at all times, alright?” the guard asked. 
“I’m perfectly calm,” Leonard said. Get me the hell out of here before that 
woman comes in here and calls the cops on my butt, he thought. 
It was a low probability that the woman would walk all the way and 
identify him, but worse things had already happened. 
Like that one time in the fitness studio, when Leonard had- 
The man in the blue space suit closed the glass door, which was pretty 
thin for having to be air tight, Leonard realized. 
“Ready?” the man asked. 
Before Leonard could answer - the button was pressed. 
He was gone. 
His body felt light, almost like air. Like a cloud. He saw nothing at first, 
then... white nothingness. The voices of the travellers faded away, and in 
their stead, a high pitched tone soared through Leonard’s ear drums. 
He tried to move his legs, but there were none attached. He tried to 
look down, but neither was there something to look at, nor was there even 
something to see with. 
He was there, but somewhere else at the same time. 
For at least two hours, he floated, staring at whiteness, a blank shape he 
had never witnessed before. 
There was a black spot that attracted his attention. If he had any sense 
of direction, he would have said the speck was below him. 
A shadow maybe? 
An exit? 
For another few hours, he kept existing, or thinking about existing. A 
blurry line appeared, but then he wasn’t sure, since he didn’t have eyes. 
 
137 
He woke up. 
A hairy male hand stretched out to him. 
“Are you alright, Mister?” a distorted voice said. 
The face looking at Leonard came into focus. 
Leonard disregarded the offered hand and stepped outside of the 
booth. 
Another question was thrown at him. “Are you okay?” 
Of course, he was okay, why wouldn’t he be? Leonard’s face turned 
into an annoyed grimace. 
He began to fully sense his surroundings again. 
Vowkinn. The heart of Lostarichi. A city so grand, it had the status of a 
state. He checked the clock of a distant church tower: Only a minute or so 
had passed since his departure in Taresville. 
People were staring at him and he left the place with the travelling 
booth behind. There wasn’t a big station like in Taresville, rather a tent-
shaped house, but with the same INSTANT logo sitting atop the entrance. 
He had to stop for a second, feel for his forehead and close his eyes. 
He opened them again and looked back at the glass booth. 
What a strange way to travel, he pondered. 
 
138 
 
 
 
“A postcard for you, Walter,” the waiter of the Trimond company 
restaurant said. He placed the card neatly next to Walter’s right hand, 
which was resting there while Walter’s left transported soup with a spoon 
to the scientist’s mouth. 
Walter looked at it. “Thank you,” he said, and the waiter walked away.  
He had told every person to call him by his first name; he couldn’t 
handle all the “Sir this” and “Sir that”. 
His right hand picked up the card, showing mountains, green at the 
bottom, gray in the middle, white at the top. 
He slurped another spoon full of warm spicy water with noodles and 
turned the card around. 
 
“Son, 
sitting in a terrific hotel room in Bellrine. 
Going hiking again in a few minutes. 
Keep up the good work. 
Dad and Mom” 
 
A card written in his father’s handwriting, how... 
Nice? Interesting? 
He put his spoon down and sent out his tongue to chase a noodle stuck 
between his teeth. 
Again, he turned the postcard and imagined himself being there with 
his parents. He had chosen a path for himself. Not their fault. No point in 
being jealous. Or... hateful. 
He was sitting alone in a wide room, surrounded by lots of empty 
tables and chairs. The atmosphere was quite enchanting, with lights placed 
on the walls, a chandelier hanging from the ceiling three meters above 
ground, and piano music caressing Walter’s eardrums. 
He put the postcard back, parallel to the edge of the table. 
Would he rather want to be there? With them? 

 
139 
Or here? 
It was past seven in the evening, but there were no windows showing 
the setting sun. At least, Walter imagined it would be dusk by now. 
He finished his soup quickly and the waiter brought a plate with 
schnitzel, potatoes, rice, two slices of lemon, and a cucumber salad in a 
separate bowl. 
Walter again thanked him, and the man in a black suit disappeared in 
the kitchen. 
For a while he stared at his neatly arranged food, the smell luring his 
senses. Yet he was already full. 
Rick barged into the restaurant. 
“Hello Walter,” he said while walking towards the scientist. 
“Hey,” Walter said in return, and started eating. Can’t show them that I 
don’t have an appetite, he thought. 
Rick sat down opposite to him. “How are you doing? That looks damn 
good,” he said, nodding at the meal. 
“You want some?” Walter said, his cheek harbouring half a potato. 
“I’m good, my wife is waiting for me at home with dinner,” Rick  
declined. 
“The warning was still on the screen an hour ago,” Walter said. “Is 
there a problem in the sewers?” 
He tried not to sound like a dissatisfied king scolding his incompetent 
servant. 
“I actually came here to talk to you about that,” Rick replied. “I 
followed the cables for about half an hour, then I saw a caved in wall, and 
a pipe stuck in the wire.” 
“A pipe?” Walter asked, shoving a piece of breaded meat into his 
mouth. Hunger had finally set in. 
“Yes, it probably used to be inside the wall, a water line or something 
like that, and somehow it must have escaped the wall when it crumbled 
and pierced through the coating of our cable.” 
“Do you really think that’s possible?” It sounded highly unlikely to 
Walter’s ears. I’m sure someone’s behind this... 
“I think so,” Rick said, but Walter saw that he had planted an idea in 
his head. “Now that I think about it, it could’ve been damaged 
deliberately,” Rick admitted. 
“Call the police to check it out, maybe they can find something, 
fingerprints, signs of vandalism, anything that points to an unnatural 
cause for the cave-in.” Walter put a fork full of cucumber salad between 
his teeth and chewed it loudly. 
 
140 
“I’ll do that. Can I use the contact we got from Isela?” 
“Absolutely,” Walter said. 
“Anyway, I need help with the repairs, but I suppose I’ll have to wait

Illsburg line.” Walter ate the last bite of his meat and continued to munch 
away on the last potato and the second rice hill on his plate.

Rick seemed to falter a bit under the sudden weight of responsibility. 
But Walter didn’t want to deal with that, while he had ideas for wireless 
travelling in his mind. 
“I’ll do that, thanks for your trust,” Rick said. 
“No need to thank me, I know having more responsibility is more of a

“I can handle it.” Rick folded his arms and looked optimistic.

Something in Rick’s voice made Walter stop eating and look the

“Before going down into the sewers, I inspected the glass booth in 
Taresville,” Rick said. “I took it apart and, well, it was in best condition. 
Just like my team and I had pieced it together back then.” 
“Ah yes, our teams. Too bad they were only given to us for a year,” 
Walter digressed. “We could always hire more people if you’re 
overwhelmed by the maintenance, you know?” 
“I know, yes. I will get back to you on that later, when I have to repair 
the cable. Now, the insides of the booth were fine - but I found a dead rat.” 
A dead rat? 
Walter recalled all the early tests in his basement. It hadn’t been pretty. 
To Walter’s surprise, Rick produced a folded worker’s glove. 
“I’ve brought it with me,” Rick said, holding the glove in his right 
hand. “You’re eating right now, so I’ll just leave it here on the ground.” 
Walter shook his head. “Don’t worry, it takes a lot to steal my appetite. 
Besides, I’m done anyway.” He licked his lips, shoved the plate with the 
empty bowl on it to his left and stretched out his hand. 
Rick gave him the glove. 
Walter opened it and glanced inside, but it was too dark. 
 
141 
“I’ll take a look at it in my lab.” He had no real laboratory, but what

“In one of the cooling boxes. Maybe it got in there somehow.” 
“Wasn’t this the glass booth that was originally standing in Monoura?” 
Walter asked.

the unveiling in Taresville. And since the booth in Taresville had been

“We used the one in Monoura in Illsburg,” Walter concluded. 
“Exactly,” Rick said. 
Walter’s thoughts ran wild: It’s definitely the booth I have constructed 
myself in my basement. There were no rats in the warehouse, not even for testing 
purposes. 
Was it the rat that had survived being travelled? 
One cannot be travelled, he heard Isela’s voice in his head. 
He had to examine it closely later and figure out the cause of its death. 
And - if it was indeed the basement rat - why it hadn’t decomposed. 
Although it did emit a terrible stench. 
“What’s up?” Rick asked, and Walter’s mind train grinded to a halt 
with a shriek. 
“I,” Walter said and began his sentence anew. “Thanks for bringing it to

“Don’t mention it,” Rick said. “I’ve actually seen rats caught in cars, 
too. Even cats tend to do that. So, it’s probably no big deal. Besides - it 
wasn’t the rat’s fault that the Illsburg line didn’t work.” 
“Yes, you may be right,” Walter said, staring at the empty bowl next to 
him. 
Rick checked the watch on his arm. “Anyway, I’ve got to run. I’ll take 
the instant back to Monoura.” 
That brought a distorted smile on Walter’s face. 
He’s using it every day, Walter realized. 
“I’ll see or hear you tomorrow,” Rick said, stood up and wished Walter 
a good night, while leaving the restaurant. 
A good night it should be, Walter thought.

Xave parked his car underground, right below the Trimond hotel. There 
were three floors of parking space, enough for forty cars; most of the lots 
were empty, now that the hotel had been transformed into Walter ’s 
headquarters. 
It felt weird, yet strangely exciting to Xave. 
No more of that rundown apartment. Food on the table whenever he 
wanted. Hot shower every time, not whenever it decided to work. Fresh 
clothes. 
He sure had come far. His father would have been proud. 
He got out of his car, locked it up and went to the elevator. 
There he was again. His father. Why did it mean so much to impress his 
deceased old man? 
And why would he never try to imagine his mother’s feedback? 
Wouldn’t she have been proud as well? 
Maybe he hadn’t known her long enough, he realized. There were only 
a handful of images of her in his head, and half of them weren’t memories, 
but photos and a video he hadn’t seen in a long time. 
He entered the elevator when it arrived a few seconds after having 
pressed the button. His finger hovered over the console to his right. 
The restaurant sounded tempting, although he had already eaten 
dinner with the CEO of the Leimer production facilities. Maybe he should 
take a look at the lobby, but at eleven minutes to midnight, only the janitor 
and the receptionist would be around. He wouldn’t know what to talk 
about with them anyway. 
He pressed the button next to the number 10. 
I’ll just go to bed, he thought. 
A repetitive melody accompanied Xave, until the elevator elegantly 
stopped at the tenth floor. 
He stepped out - and Walter startled him. 
“Xave,” Walter said. He looked distressed, although he tried hard to 
contain his excitement. 

 
143 
“What’s wrong, Walter? You look shaken,” Xave said, touching 
Walter’s shoulder. For a moment, the scientist looked like he ’d seen a 
ghost. 
“Come with me,” Walter said simply and went back into the elevator. 
Xave followed him and Walter ordered the elevator to escort them to 
floor 11; the highest storey. 
The elevator doors closed, the mechanical box levitated upwards, a 
high beep signalled their arrival, the doors opened and they got out.  
“Why won’t you just tell me what’s wrong?” Xave persisted. 
“I’ll just show you,” Walter said. 
Walter opened the door to his suite and both went in. 
The room was a disaster, although that wasn’t new. Walter had a 
tendency to spread chaos wherever he lived. There were books scattered 
around, the TV’s view was half-blocked by a weird device that looked like 
a mix between a dishwasher and a radio station of the 60s, the curtains 
shut out the outside world, and Walter’s bed was full of newspapers, both 
large and small, sometimes rumpled and clean, others torn and even 
stacked. 
At the end of the room, right below a window next to the bed, there 
was a desk lamp aimed at the surface it was standing on, showing a 
grotesque scenery involving what seemed to be a rat lying on a sterile ti n 
foil and two metal sticks protruding from its open body. There was no 
blood. 
“What is this, Walter? Are you trying to reanimate rats?” Xave said, 
trying to bring Walter down a notch. 
But the scientist didn’t even hear him. “Look,” he said, pointing at the 
animal cadaver. 
Xave stepped closer and examined the dead rat. 
He expected to see miniature organs in the cleft spread open by the 
metal sticks. But instead of a perfectly well preserved biology example, he 
saw a sickening knot that consisted of organs, tightly tied together. Worst 
of all, they looked fresh, but somehow wrong. 
“I don’t understand, Walter, help me out here,” Xave said. 
“What do you see?” Walter said, cryptically. 
“I see a poor little thing with its insides knit together in a weird 
unsettling way,” Xave observed. 
“That wasn’t me,” Walter said. 
“What do you mean?” 
“I didn’t do that. Rick found this rat inside a cooling box of the glass 
booth that I built in my old house’s basement.” 
 
144 
“You mean this rat’s been in that booth for two years?” Xave felt a 
strange sensation crawling up his back. 
“This is the first rat that survived my travelling experiments,” Walter 
explained. “It went missing afterwards.” 
“Why do you think this is your rat?” 
“I remember what it looked like.” 
“Rats all look the same to me,” Xave said and looked at the opened up 
animal with a tilted head. 
“They don’t, I’ve had more than a dozen and could always tell them 
apart.” Walter’s eyes fixated on Xave’s. “It is my rat!” 
“What does it mean, then? That we’re all going to die because our 
innards snuggle up?” 
“I’ve refined the algorithm twice before Kristella demonstrated it to the

anything.” 
Xave stared at the tiny ball of guts. For a moment, he felt relieved that 
he had never tried it himself. 
“You have never travelled, have you?” Walter asked and Xave felt 
caught. 
The salesman shook his head without meeting Walter’s gaze. 
“Neither have I,” Walter admitted. 
They looked at each other. 
“How can that be?” Xave asked in disbelief. “Don’t tell me you don’t

Walter stumbled over a few words before another full sentence formed

green numbers. I’ve been thinking about this for the past few hours. 
Maybe there is an error that only my subconsciousness knows about.”

“So, you’ve made a mistake?” Xave glared at the scientist. This was 
bad. 
Walter hesitated. Then, his eyes opened wide and Xave felt a cold chill 
when the scientist mumbled: “Maybe it was my destiny to do that. Maybe 
I’m the devil.” 
“To kill a few people?” 
“To kill everyone,” Walter muttered. 
“We can still pull the plug on the project, Walter,” Xave tried to calm

145 
The watch in Xave’s breast pocket pinched his skin. Never give up 
without a fight, Xave’s father would have said with a raised finger. A

“No, Walter,” Xave said with a firm voice. “We’ll figure this out. Run

Walter vigorously shook his head. “No, Xave, this has to stop, I can’t let

Walter blinked, but Xave felt sure the scientist was on the verge of 
going crazy. 
“This is difficult, Walter,” Xave continued. “I know that, but please, just 
try. Don’t give up yet. I really believe in your creation. It can and will 
change the world. For the better.” 
Walter shot a bitter look at the rat. “But why hasn’t it decomposed? It 
can’t look like this if it has been dead for two years.” 
“You figure it out, Walter,” Xave said and stabbed a finger into Walter ’s 
shoulder. “If there’s one man that can do this, it’s you.” 
Walter sighed deeply. Xave felt as if a demon had finally let go of the 
scientist’s body. 
“You’re right,” Walter said. “Maybe I just need some sleep.” 
“Definitely,” Xave agreed. “You’re a good man. Don’t forget that.” 
“I’ll try.”

Leonard needed money. 
For two days he had been surviving on stolen food and sleeping on a 
bench in a subway station, trying to look like a passenger who had simply 
fallen asleep waiting. 
But he knew that there was another way. 
He had gotten ahold of an old black hat to hide his unwashed hair, 
which had already begun to reflect the neon lights underground. He had 
obtained chewing gum from a shop that freshened up his breath, and had 
washed his face in public restrooms. 
Large crowds were usually his primary target audience for grand 
acquisitions. Vowkinn was heavily populated, a perfect place for men like 
him. 
Yet there was not only opportunity, there was also risk. 
The more people around, the higher the chance of someone watching 
you steal someone’s properties. He normally preferred smaller actions, but 
he craved a more dignified place with a roof above his head. 
He waited until early morning, resting on a bench in front of subway 
tracks. 
Finally, people started gathering. Leonard opened his eyes and hid his 
face behind the newspaper, which he had found in the trash. His hat 
provided enough status and a certain air of authority to hide his otherwise 
lousy appearance. 
People didn’t notice him at all. 
There were children with sleepy eyes headed for school, stressed men 
and women going to work, elderly people on an adventure to visit their 
doctors or on the hunt for groceries. The hectic surroundings would ’ve 
made it hard for Leonard to concentrate, if his mind hadn’t been a fortress 
of silence, trained to sustain enormous pressure; an accumulated skill 
Leonard was secretly proud of. 
The subway arrived, a long gray worm appearing out of the black hole, 
with a round nose and a dozen cars. 

 
147 
The doors opened. 
Leonard knew exactly what was going to happen: The passengers 
would be trying to get out, while the others would be nervous and slowly 
but steadily push themselves into the car. 
He folded the newspaper, put it aside and stood up. 
Through the opened door in front of him the following persons exited: 
a woman with a short skirt, expensive necklace and watch; a man dressed 
in regular blue jeans and a green shirt; another woman with her baby 
buggy; and two young boys, all facing a dozen other human beings who 
impatiently stood aside. 
Leonard walked in. 
He gently moved through the scenery, hidden from all eyes, barely 
touching anyone or anything. Like a shadow, he appeared to the right, 
then vanished and resurfaced inside the subway car, amidst all the other 
passengers. 
While five or six people were still trying to get out, others had already 
started to ignore them and formed a line streaming inward. 
The car filled up and Leonard was now trapped between four backs, 
two of them male with broad shoulders. 
The doors closed after the exchange of people was complete, and the 
subway drove off. 
Leonard’s pockets felt heavier already, but he wasn’t done yet. It would 
take approximately one or two minutes before they reached the next 
station. Leonard tried to relax. Nobody in here had been his victim yet; 
only the ones that had left the car earlier. And they were at least two miles  
away already. 
“Tickets, please,” a low man’s voice resounded to Leonard’s right. 
Now there was something he hadn’t thought of: An employee of the 
train staff persistent enough to squeeze through dozens of bodies and 
check their tickets. 
Leonard’s poker face showed slight discomfort. 
“Tickets, please,” the voice continued, like a stampede roaring towards 
Leonard. 
He could see the man now. He wore a dark red uniform and a hat of 
the same color with a golden chain hanging from left to right. 
Their eyes met, but there were still another five people between them. 
Leonard waited for the inspector to check another ticket for validity - 
then he ducked slightly and pushed through the two male backs to his left.  
 
148 
“Excuse me, that’s my exit,” Leonard said, trying to veil his tension 
with a layer of charm, while he dug through the slightly annoyed human 
bodies. 
“Watch it,” a woman said, as Leonard stepped on her toes. 
A speaker in the ceiling announced the next station’s name. 
Leonard was now right in front of the sliding doors and saw the softly 
illuminated black rocks of the tunnel through the glass. 
“Tickets, please - thank you,” the inspector repeated. 
The subway slowly stopped and the doors slid apart. Leonard walked 
outside with wide steps, through the waiting crowd in front of him. 
He marched for a few seconds and slowed down a bit, catching his 
breath. His pockets were now filled to the rim with random valuables, 
including a wallet, a bracelet, a watch, concert tickets, a sealed condom, a 
bottle cap, and a Swiss Army knife. 
The subway drove away, and so did his victims. 
Leonard was safe. 
A hand touched his shoulder. 
“I believe you have something from me,” a male voice said. 
Leonard turned around. 
A man, roughly twenty-five years old, was standing there with a broad 
grin, his hair neatly combed to the side, his white shirt beaming of 
cleanliness, his brown trousers wide, but not unfitting, his shoes 
shimmering in a blue hue. 
“What are you talking about? I’m late for work and I don’t have time 
for your childish games,” Leonard said, playing the thundering boss of a 
worldwide company. And I’m using the subway to get to work like a regular 
employee… Well, maybe the company’s credo involved saving the planet. 
Leonard turned on his heel, and made for the stairs, leading up and 
outside. 
Yet again, the hand held him back. 
“You’re good,” the young lad said. “What do you think about me?” 
“What the devil are you blabbering about?” Leonard fumed. Then he 
looked at the man’s palm, showing Leonard the Swiss Army knife. 
How did that get out of his pocket? It must have fallen out. Somehow. 
“Are you threatening me with your knife?” Leonard asked, choosing 
the safe route of trusting nobody. “I’ll be on my way now. Maybe you 
should return home and rethink your actions. And let your mother spank 
some sense into you.” 
“You stole the knife from me,” the guy insisted, with a cheerful tone. 
“We could use someone like you.” 
 
149 
“What do you mean, use someone like me? I have got to get to the 
office, now let go of me.” 
“You can’t fool me, I’m the same as you. You’re alone too, right? Did 
you lose your parents?” 
Leonard held his breath. In a split second, long forgotten memories 
crawled up the cliffs from deep below. He shook his head and kicked them 
back down again, only to get injured by their claws. 
“I didn’t lose my parents, you idiot,” Leonard shot back, but with less 
force now. 
“Whatever you say,” the man replied and put the knife away. “So, are 
you coming or not?” 
“Coming? I told you, I...” 
“To our hideout. We’re only a small group of six and we definitely 
manage, but with you tagging along, we could live like kings! Or 
landlords, at least.” 
Leonard’s mind constructed a hundred complex excuses, while his 
mouth simply said: “Fine.” 
 
150 
 
 
 
It took them over half an hour to reach the hideout using two different 
buses. They hadn’t talked, except for Leonard inquiring about the 
estimated time of arrival every now and then, and about his companion ’s 
name, which turned out to be Mardo. 
They were standing in front of a building, which looked like all the 
others surrounding them, except that the windows weren’t intact and the 
door looked like an improvisation, a bundle of wooden beams held 
together by a thick chain and a heavy lock. 
The district they were in felt average, Leonard noticed. Certainly not

forsaken building. 
“Is that the entrance?” Leonard pointed at the provisory door. 
“No, that’s our fridge,” Mardo said. 
“What?”

“A distraction? What for?” 
“For those trying to find shelter. They see that there’s no coming in

“That doesn’t make a lot of sense to me,” Leonard said. 
“The entrance is over here.” Mardo walked to the right side of the 
building into an alley, a tight hallway between the two snuggling 
buildings. 
Leonard followed him hesitantly. He could be walking into an ambush.  
Mardo dug his arms into a hill of black waste bags, the stench of which 
was attracting hundreds of flies. Leonard’s nose didn’t really care, it was 
used to much worse. 
The stranger kneeling in front of Leonard gasped as his hands found 
something to hold on to. 
Mardo opened a small metal door with a dozen plastic bags stuck to it.  
“Is this your secret entrance?” Leonard scoffed. 

 
151 
“Yes, make sure nobody sees you, then follow me,” Mardo said and

Leonard looked around. Except for an old crone across the street, he 
saw nobody. Against his better judgement, he walked towards the square 
hole and let himself fall into it. 
Although, he didn’t fall far. He was standing, but his upper body still 
showed out of the hole. 
“Come down here,” Mardo said, dragging Leonard by his collar down 
a small set of stairs into the dark, closing the door after him. 
Mardo held up a burning candle on a plate; it was the only light source 
now. 
“Follow me,” he said. 
Leonard waited for Mardo to walk past him like a ducked geezer and

The air was thick and the stench had found its way inside. The walls

web. 
“No,” Mardo said.

on the metal grid and pushed it aside with a loud shriek. 
“Well, well. Look who’s coming home,” a woman said. 
“Hey, sis,” Mardo greeted the person when he was out of Leonard’s

Leonard crawled out as well and stretched his back. 
“Who’s that?” This time it was a masculine voice. Leonard felt the 
sharp blade of a knife against his throat. 
Only now he could make out the whole room. It was rather small, the 
walls consisted of the same bricks as the tunnel, only the floor was made

spiralling upwards to the second floor. Two round tables were standing in

persons sitting at each of them, playing what appeared to be poker or

with books, wine, food and other stuff. Mardo was standing next to one of 
the tables and the man keeping Leonard at bay still towered behind him.  
“Calm down, I’m with Mardo,” Leonard said, slightly raising both of 
his hands. 
 
152 
“Yes, he’s with me, I caught him stealing my knife. And I barely noticed

against Leonard’s unshaven skin.

Verayne gave him a playful wink. 
 
153 
“I saw him stealing various items from several different people on the

Leonard didn’t know how to respond. All his life he had told himself 
that everything he did to survive was a bad thing. Of course, killing had 
never been an option, but forcing items into his possession from other

And now, he was receiving praise for it? The world had turned upside

A job. Leonard had never thought about his actions as an occupation.

It represented his means to an end, something he was goo d at, but he

A small leech that probably didn’t hurt that much, but a leech none the

They all mumbled something to each other in excitement.

“Hear, hear,” Timony said and raised his cup of beer.

Lonely he was, he thought. But - by choice. Being alone had a certain 
benefit. 
It was warm in here though, and Leonard’s stomach could hardly resist

Besides, he could always find a way to escape and carry some more
