The cemetery in Taresville always reminded Xave of the day his mother 
had died. 
It had been snowing; his mother, dressed in comfortable gray clothes 
and sitting next to him on his bed, had been reading a book to six-year-old 
Xave in her beautiful, soothing voice. 
“The woman said to the boy: ‘Are you blind?’ The boy kept staring at 
the numbers above the woman’s head, which - ironically - only he could 
see.” 
Then - she had looked at him strangely. Her eyes filled with a certain 
kind of sadness, emptiness and fear. She had held onto him, touched his 
cheek and his little hands. 
She had stopped breathing and fallen off the edge of the bed. 
The doctors would diagnose a thrombosis later on. 
Xave never brought flowers. His mother hadn’t seemed to care much 
for them, and his father had never believed in them either. It would 
honour him more if Xave brought a million Schilling every visit and buried 
it with his corpse. Then his father would say: “I’m proud of you. Now go 
get the next million.” 
The two stones of his parents rested next to each other, their graves 
topped with nothing but brown earth. Xave used to water it, but the rain 
usually took care of it. His parents were simple beings, and yet they had 
instilled more ambition into his mind than he could handle. 
“Why do you never bring flowers?” Kristella’s voice cut through Xave’s 
thoughts. 
He turned around and saw her approaching. “How did you know I was 
here?” 
“You told me once that you visited your parents’ grave every Friday, 
for your mother had passed away on a Friday and your father had stuck to 
the same tradition ever since,” Kristella recalled. 
“I don’t remember telling you,” Xave said. 
“You don’t remember a lot of things,” Kristella said. She walked 

 
156 
around him and knelt down to the two graves, touching the earth. 
“Wouldn’t they love it if something blossomed here?” 
“They never liked flowers. I think my mother even was allergic to 
them.” 
“How can a woman not like the sight of blooming nature?” Kristella 
asked, standing up again. 
“My mother wasn’t part of that stereotype,” Xave replied. Neither was 
his father, he thought. 
“I’m sorry,” Kristella said. 
“Don’t be, I suppose it does look a bit bleak,” Xave admitted. “I wish I 
could erect a palace right on top of them. That would show how dear they 
were to me.” 
“Well, you’ve got the money. Buy the cemetery and start building!” 
Xave had to smile at the thought of plastering away all those other 
worthless final resting places and dropping a giant golden palace on top of 
his parents’ remains, just to show everyone how great they had been. 
“You know,” he said. “I do all of this for them, especially for my 
father.” 
“I know,” Kristella said. “I’ve heard you say that a few times. Your 
father was a businessman just like you.” 
“Exactly, well, not like me. He was better.” 
“You’ve made it into history books,” Kristella pointed out. “Your father 
may have done some great things in his time - like bringing you into this 
world - but this, this whole deal with instant travelling, I think even your 
father would proudly admit that his son has outgrown him.”  
“I don’t want to think like that,” Xave said, staring at the earth covering 
his father. “Life shouldn’t be a race to the top. Too much risk of missing 
things.” 
“Maybe so,” Kristella said. 
A minute passed, as they both watched the two graves in silence, 
completely forgetting about the other dozen graves surrounding them.  
The humid spring air brought sweat pearls to Xave’s forehead and he 
wiped them away with his sleeve. He checked his watch. 
“I have an appointment with another possible factory partner,” he 
mumbled. 
The last one still hadn’t returned his call. Xave wasn’t surprised after 
the suspicious looks on the CEO’s face all evening long. 
“Why did you come here?” Xave asked. “Couldn’t you just have waited 
back at the hotel?” 
“It’s a nice day. Besides, I have something important to tell you,” 
 
157 
Kristella said. 
“Spit it out, then.” 
“Isela’s assistant called me an hour ago. The exclusivity deal with 
Taresville and Bellrine has been agreed upon by all sides.” 
Xave looked at her, then back to his parents. “What’s that, father? Yes, 
you’re right. She’s awesome,” he said and grinned at Kristella. 
“Stop it,” Kristella said shyly. 
“Have you told Walter already?” Xave asked. 
“No, he wouldn’t answer the door, I kept knocking for maybe five 
minutes and everyone at the hotel assured me that Walter was in his 
room.” 
“Hm,” Xave said. 
He’d have to talk to him again about the dead rat. It was bringing a lot 
of concern to his scientist friend. 
Friend... When did business become a private matter? 
“How are you feeling?” Xave asked for reasons he would rather not 
share at the moment. 
“Oh, I’m fine, extremely excited about the new agreement,” Kristella 
replied. “Slowly but steadily, we’ll take over the world! And then I can 
finally send my mother to Ireland for breakfast and a walk along the 
shores, and have her back for dinner.” 
“Take over the world?” Xave repeated. “You make it sound like  an 
invasion.” 
“You taught me to think in battle plans, war strategy and missions,” 
Kristella defended herself, poking his shoulder and pouting. 
“Maybe it’s time you developed your own salesman jargon,” Xave 
suggested with a hint of jest. 
“I like taking over the world, what’s wrong about it? It’s not like we’re 
doing anything bad,” Kristella said. 
Xave bit his lip. He decided to ignore that and waved his parents good 
bye. “I’ll see you next Friday,” he mumbled. 
They left the cemetery behind and walked to Xave’s car. 
“Should I drop you off somewhere?” Xave asked Kristella over the car’s 
roof while unlocking the doors. 
“Let’s go talk to Walter,” she said, opened the door and sat down 
inside. 
Xave looked around. A bus passed his car with a large advertisement 
printed on it in blue letters: “Travel LIGHT - Travel INSTANT” 
“Sure,” he muttered to himself, and sat down behind the steering 
wheel. 
 
158 
 
 
 
“We’re ready to go,” Kristella said to Walter, who was sitting at his table,

Walter turned around. 
He looked devastated, Kristella realized a little shocked. His eyes were

“Perfectly fine,” Walter replied. “Late night research. You’ve been there 
with me before, remember?” 

 
159 
She did remember a few nights on the sofa in the warehouse. Walter

Xave answered: “Isela has provided us with some laborers before, ask

At almost exactly 5 a.m., an apparently drunk figure ascended the few 
stairs in front of the Trimond company. With one arm held tightly against 
the belly, the man banged on the glass of the entrance door. After the fifth 
time, he slid down to the ground as if defeated. 
The interior of Walter’s hotel was dimly lit and there were only a 
handful of security guards doing their rounds. One had seen the man out 
front and was already on his way. He unlocked the front door, opened it 
and the man, whose upper half had been leaning against the glass, 
basically landed in front of his feet. 
“Get your drunken ass off the stairs,” the security guard said in a deep 
calm voice. 
“Walter,” the man gasped. “I need to speak... to Walter.” 
“And who might you be?” the guard asked, expecting no convincing 
reply. 
“Tell him...” the man said, trying to stand up by pushing himself away 
from the ground with his left arm only. “Tell him, it’s Jeremy.” 
 
Walter, of course, was not asleep when his telephone rang. 
He was sitting at his desk next to his bed. The rat was still covered, and 
there were several sheets of paper scattered in front of him, all containing 
as many words as possible, all covering different theories of the rat’s 
failure to succumb to one of nature’s firmest rules. 
The phone rang again. 
Walter stood up, walked to the other side of the bed and grabbed the 
receiver of the annoying white plastic communication device. 
“Yes?” 
“Mr. Trimond, there’s a man in the lobby who wants to see you. He 
seems to be drunk, or in pain, or both. His name’s Jeremy,” a male voice 
said. 
Sometimes Walter wondered how security could work with so many 
people he didn’t even know personally. Whom could he really trust? 

 
162 
“Jeremy?” Walter repeated. 
“Yes, that’s who he claims to be. Wasn’t Jeremy your first test subject?” 
The guard had done his homework, Walter thought. Or - he was just

anyway. 
“Does he have any passport or other means of assuring us he’s telling

“Alright,” Walter agreed and hung up. 
He took a quick glance around his stuffy room, shrugged, and went

Xave was probably sound asleep in his suite and not much help right

Walter pulled back his hair, which had grown quite a bit the last two 
years, and rubbed his eyes. It actually felt good, being outside his room for 
a minute, stretching his arms and legs. 
Down the hall, the elevator’s bell announced the arrival of the guests

“Jeremy - what are you doing here?” Walter said, crouching down in

“Walter,” Jeremy said, panting. “It hurts so bad. I didn’t know where 
else to go.” 
“You didn’t try going to a doctor or a hospital?” Walter asked, checking 
Jeremy’s eyes by opening them up wide with two fingers each.  
 
163 
Jeremy giggled, followed by another gasp. He pushed Walter aside and

Probably - or quite possibly.

“Well?” Jeremy asked, his mouth askew, his eyes pleading.

A theory that involves Jeremy’s death in a few hours and a rat lying on

A rat that just won’t disappear.

Jeremy said, each word strained and half-whispered. He dug his hands

Still alive.

“Well, duh,” she said. “I broke up with him! He was almost as boring 
as you! But definitely not as sexy!” 
“Shut up already!” Patrigg shouted at the group behind Trina. 
Somebody must have already noticed the noise they made and he was sure 
he saw the lights go on in one of the houses across the street. 
They all calmed down. Trina grinned and kissed Patrigg on his cheek.  
“Yeah, yeah, now get going.” Patrigg waved them all towards the booth 
standing behind him, the INSTANT logo atop of it lighting up the 
surrounding area in a bright blue. 
He took a step back, let the adolescent couple walk towards the 
travelling device and picked up the receiver of a phone which was 
attached to the right side of the base part of the construction. 
“Glothermines, this is Mienz,” Patrigg said. “I’ve got another couple 
coming up.” He paused and listened. “Yeah, I know. - Yeah, we talked 
about it last time. - Come on, dude.” He paused again. “Yeah, I owe you 
another round.” He casually kicked a little stone on the ground off into the 
dark, where it landed in the grass. “Yeah, five in total. Call me if there’s 
trouble.” He hung up. 
The boy went in first, his hair a - probably purposely - mess of black 
dye, then he struck out his hand to his loved one. It was an awkward 
gesture, as if he was copying a scene from a movie involving a couple. His 
girlfriend stepped up to him and squeezed into the glass booth with him. 
Their clothes pressed tightly against the inside glass. Their faces showed 
excitement and repressed fear. 
 
166 
The other three teenagers, including Trina, were standing a meter 
away, clapping and cheering at them. 
Patrigg sighed and closed the translucent lid of the booth. He had to 
lean against it a bit to lock it up, the girl’s right arm now stuck between her 
body and the material of the contraption. The skin was distorted, and it 
was so tight both of the teens inside seemed to be having a harder time 
breathing now. They tried to cover up their anxiety and started kissing. It 
all looked like a vial grotesquely filled with human parts, with only the 
heads being able to move by themselves. 
Patrigg shook his head disapprovingly and pressed a button on the 
console next to the booth. A short bright light and an ear-stroking hum 
appeared - and the couple disappeared. 
Trina and her friends applauded and drank from their bottles. 
“Oooh,” Trina mourned. “I know exactly how they’re feeling right 
now.” She closed her eyes and threw her arms up high, the bottle in her 
left hand. “They’re like one being, their hearts beat in unison, their minds 
think the same thoughts, they’re floating, they’re above the world.” 
The other girl said: “You’re making me jealous.” 
“Try it!” Trina encouraged them. “It even lasts a few hours. This 
amazing... feeling,” she said, tilting her head. 
Patrigg buried his face in his palm. 
“With you?” the girl asked, pointing at Trina. 
“Why not with me?” the boy suggested with a broad smile. 
“You’re not my boyfriend!” she said, laughing. 
“Not yet, anyway!” he said. 
“Oh, you,” Trina said, boxing the boy’s left shoulder. 
“Come on, Angee, let’s do it!” Trina said to the girl. 
“Yay!” Angee said and jumped. 
The phone rang and Patrigg - relieved to have a distraction - answered: 
“Yeah?” 
As he looked at Trina with the receiver held to his right ear, the 
expression in his face changed disturbingly. 
Trina sensed the sinister air around Patrigg’s eyes. Her smile froze. 
 
167 
 
 
 
A mere four hours later, Kristella was standing in the entrance hall of the 
INSTANT headquarters with a dozen microphones shoved in her face, 
held by equally as many reporters, yelling questions that only arrived at 
Kristella’s ears as one long distorted blaring noise. 
“Please,” she interrupted the chaos surrounding her with raised hands. 
“I can’t understand what you are saying!” 
The flood of gibberish didn’t stop, though, so Kristella sighed and 
started to concentrate on one female reporter and try to hear the words her 
mouth was forming. 
“Ms. Attenburg, what is Mr. Trimond’s comment on the incident of last 
night?” the reporter seemed to say according to Kristella’s best guess. Even 
if it actually differed - she was sure everybody’s question kind of 
resembled it. So she just went with it. 
“Mr. Trimond was deeply shocked when he heard about the incident. 
He wanted me to reiterate that our booths are designed to function with 
one person only, and we will take extra effort to reinforce that rule 
amongst our staff,” Kristella said. She almost couldn’t hear her own voice, 
and the repeated flashlights and the light of the cameras pointed at her 
made her feel a little dizzy. 
She was used to a lot, but this was a new level. 
“Will Mr. Trimond make certain that the parents of the deceased 
teenagers receive adequate compensation for pain and suffering?” was 
another question Kristella’s ears - probably - filtered out of the storm of 
words. 
Well, will they? Kristella wasn’t sure. In fact, she hadn’t talked to Walter 
at all yet since the deaths of the teenagers. Everything she said now would 
be held true later on. She decided to tread carefully. 
“Mr. Trimond hasn’t made any announcement yet on the topic of 
financial support for the affected families,” she answered.  
A few voices calmed down now. Kristella was on the right track to 
answer the most pressing questions. 

 
168 
But it was far from over. 
“Is it true that Mr. Trimond wants to study the molten remains of the 
teenagers?” a male reporter asked. 
That sounded a little like Walter, Kristella thought bewildered, trying 
not to show her surprise. In fact, it would probably yield a bunch of 
valuable results that could help improve the overall functionality.  
But it was still gross and inhuman. 
“I think it’s safe to say that Mr. Trimond – as a scientist – would be 
interested in inspecting the remains. Nothing like this has ever happened 
before, as you all know,” Kristella said. “However, both families have the 
last word on that topic.” She sighed inside, thinking of the question well 
answered. 
She wondered whether the multi-coloured microphones would transfer 
a whole lot of static noise because they touched each other constantly.  
A woman’s voice rose above the others: “Why can’t Mr. Trimond be 
here for a minute and show himself?” 
Ah, an easy one, Kristella thought. “As I said before, this tragedy has 
deeply shocked Mr. Trimond and he is already working on preventing this 
problem from ever occurring again.” 
Three questions in three different voices shot back. 
“How was this even possible before?” 
“Did Mr. Trimond not think ahead?” 
“What other problems may occur that we don’t know about yet?” 
Here comes the pain. Kristella thought quickly and came to a 
conclusion. “Mr. Trimond has covered hundreds of probable errors. We 
have an engineering staff that is assigned to keeping instant travelling as 
secure as possible. Don’t forget - we are all human beings. Of course, this 
does not excuse the death of such young people, but remember - there’s no 
regulation in place that keeps people from stepping in front of trains 
either. There’s always the unforeseen.” 
“But what happens when a pregnant woman uses the device?” 
someone retorted. 
Kristella’s mind broke into a few little pieces. Had this already 
happened in the past two years? No reports of such an accident had come 
up, but still. Had Walter been the cause of miscarriages? 
“The INSTANT guards have always had instructions to not let 
pregnant women use our devices,” Kristella lied and made a mental note 
to let Walter know about it. 
 
169 
“Didn’t your guards already have instructions to only let people travel 
one at a time?” a reporter asked, dashing forward with his red 
microphone, as if it were a dagger. 
True, Kristella thought. But she wouldn’t get herself cornered. “Again, 
one cannot foresee a man stepping in front of a train. A knife company 
cannot think about people trying to commit suicide or else we would all 
have problems cutting our steaks.” Nobody laughed or even chuckled, but 
the room went a little quieter. “Also, Mr. Patrigg Trakos - the guard who 
let the two now deceased teenagers go into the booth together - has been 
arrested for involuntary manslaughter and will not return to his post, no

to update our recruitment plans and regulations to make sure our guards 
follow their instructions in the future.” 
“Does Walter have any regrets now that his invention has killed two 
people?” a female reporter’s high voice battled its way through the crowd. 
How should I know? Kristella had almost said that out loud. 
Thankfully her mouth knew better. “One million people were killed in car 
accidents last year. Yet, no car company has stopped producing the safest 
cars they possibly can. Instant travelling has been in business for two 
years, and so far we have had two casualties. And for the record, currently 
we are successfully sending ten thousand people to their desired locations 
every day.” 
That ought to shut them up, Kristella thought triumphantly. 
“When will Walter implement the new safety features?” 
Kristella thought about how much time Walter would need and how 
much time the public would accept. Before she found a reasonable answer, 
she blurted out: “Tonight, if possible. Until then all instant travelling 
stations remain out of order.” 
“Why have there been no pictures of the victims released to the 
public?” a man with twice the amount of chin asked. 
Kristella hadn’t seen anything either. Only her imagination had come 
up with vague horrifying sketches. “To respect the wish of the families of 
both sides - and out of common respect for the deceased themselves.” And 
probably because the public would freak out and never insta-travel again. 
One centimeter closer and one of the green microphones would pick 
Kristella’s nose. If she moved back a little, the swarm of hungry electronic 
hydra heads would follow her face for the same distance. 
The lights and all the people made the room feel hotter than usual; 
Kristella started to yearn for some fresh air. 
 
170 
“Has Walter made a deal with the devil?” a man with a long brown

“No comment,” Kristella answered. That was probably a sign that they 
had run out of serious questions. Time to wrap this up. “You are all invited 
to another press conference at eight p.m. right here, same place.” 
She turned around and bumped into another microphone hanging 
beside her right ear. She held a hand to where it had hit her, dodged 
another two demanding devices and escaped from the choir of voices that 
began to rise again behind her back. Two guards stepped between her and 
the following pack of reporters, and Kristella was able to escape into an 
elevator. 
She pressed the number of Walter’s floor and the doors closed, finally 
silencing the roaring accumulation of random inquiries. 
Kristella sighed with relief, blowing a strand of hair out of her face. 
Next time she saw Xave, she would check his head for horns. Just in 
case. 
 
171

172 
Walter seemed pretty nervous and shaken up, while Xave was trying to

“Wake up,” a male voice rang in Leonard’s ears. 
He opened his eyes and was greeted by Mardo’s eager face. Leonard sat

He had been sleeping on one of the mattresses on the second floor of

The red car that Xave drove around a street corner purred like an old cat 
and acted just as stubbornly. He probably could have asked Walter, Isela 
or Petar for a new car, or hell, even a limousine along with a driver in a 
white suit and a trunk full of DVDs he could watch while hanging out on 
the leather seats – but, he had always been humble and this red coughing 
garbage on wheels was the first self-acquired car he had bought years ago. 
There was nostalgia hanging alongside the loose cloth off the seats and an 
air of adolescence among the smell that reminded him of the good old 
days of his trashy apartment; a stench that he wouldn’t dare to investigate 
further (but he had been able to trace it back to the dark depths below the 
driver’s seat). 
His destination was hell; actually, the first stage out of two. He felt hot 
and yanked on his tie, only to straighten it again. 
The words he was going to use in a few minutes to console the parents 
of the boy Fretz, whom he, from a certain sensationalist angle, had killed, 
hadn’t been invented yet. A throng of writers couldn’t come up with fitting 
words in a thousand years, Xave realized, as he approached a tall 
apartment building and parked opposite to it. 
A bad idea, he noticed. As soon as he got out of the car, reporters and 
protesters raced towards him from across the street. He marched through 
them, his tie waving in the storm of questions. 
“Mr. Stern, what will you say to Fretz’s parents? Do you think words 
will revive him?” a man yelled at him sarcastically, carrying a sign that 
read, “Travel INSTANT – to hell”. 
I’m going to paint a pentagram right at the center of their living room and 
chant a long forgotten song right into their faces for starters, Xave thought, but 
kept his eyes fixed on the door of the building and his mouth shut.  
“Are you sad?” a reporter with a dictating machine asked. 
I’m shedding manly tears, Xave thought. 
“What does the death of Fretz mean for all other instant travellers?”  a 
woman in a blue coat asked. 

 
180 
Xave looked at her out of the side of his eye and thought: We’re gonna 
get you all, what else? 
Almost at the entrance. 
“What would your parents think?” someone shrieked. 
Xave turned around and before he could actually fire back with a 
remark a wrapped package of instant noodles slapped his face and 
dropped to his feet. Then it rained a bunch of them. He shielded himself 
with his hands, ducked and dashed towards the entrance. 
The flood of protesters and reporters split apart and two policewomen 
helped Xave arrive with his scalp intact. The two uniformed women kept 
the angry mob away from the two large glass doors, while a third police 
officer opened one of them and lead Xave inside. 
“Mr. Stern,” the officer greeted him in a dark voice. “I hope you have 
prepared a world-changing Nobel prize winning speech, because if not, 
you will probably return home in four pieces.” 
“I’ve actually already arrived in four pieces, that’s how prepared I am,” 
Xave said, sternly. 
They moved up a narrow staircase, two or three floors, passing another 
two people in blue, until the officer gestured at an inconspicuous door and 
stood aside, nodding at Xave and knocking twice with the back of his left 
hand. 
The door opened slightly and a devastated female face appeared in the 
crack. 
“Mr. Stern here to visit you, Mrs. Hamer.” 
The door shut with such a force that Xave felt the vibration in his feet, 
and a cloud of dust exploded from the frame. 
The officer and Xave looked at each other, while a distant sob was 
heard behind the door. 
“Mrs. Hamer,” the uniformed man repeated, hitting the wood with the 
back of a finger again, four times; gently at first, louder at the end.  
They waited an eternity in awkward silence, listening to muted sniffs 
and coughs. The officer looked at his wristwatch every once in a while, but 
never into Xave’s eyes. 
Even that guy seems to think I’m a killer, Xave thought, feeling blood 
dripping from his hands, which turned out to be sweat. 
“Sure is stuffy in here,” Xave noted, looking around, noticing an 
elevator with an “out of order” sign. 
“It’s an old building,” the dark voice told Xave. “Built right after the 
second world war.” 
 
181 
“Ah,” Xave said, rocking back and forth on his heels and toes, hands 
behind his back. 
There was noise coming from the door to the left, probably people 
trying to hear what was going on. 
The door in front of Xave opened and a man one head taller than him 
stepped outside. He was wearing casual clothes, and when he turned to 
look at Xave, his long brown hair flowed over his shoulders like tiny little 
snakes. 
“Mr. Hamer, this is-” the officer began, but a huge hand stopped his 
introduction. Mr. Hamer’s stare never let go of Xave. 
“Our sincere condolences, Mr. Hamer,” Xave said, after a thousand 
little processes in his head had come up with a million other sentences and 
agreed to only one. 
The man said nothing, looked at the officer, went back inside like a 
slow giant and the door shut softly. 
Xave stared at the door, then on the ground for half a minute, turned on 
his heel and approached the stairs. 
The officer held him back on his right arm and his calm, yet chillingly 
direct tone caught Xave slightly off guard. “You have a fine invention at 
your disposal, I sincerely hope that you realize your responsibilities and 
didn’t know beforehand that it could and would hurt people the way it 
already did.” 
Xave swallowed, feeling his arm pulsate where the officer was holding 
him - then he was let go. The officer went past Xave and led the way down 
the stairs. 
Xave’s mind rebelled against the idea of being responsible for the boy’s 
death; his ego didn’t even begin to support that idea. He knew Walter well 
enough; the scientist was a perfectionist, errors happened only because 
there was a zero point one percent chance of them happening. It hadn’t 
been their fault that the teenagers had died, not in the least. 
They reached the entrance and the officer opened one of the two doors. 
Xave stopped and stared at the policeman. 
“They will kill me if I go out there alone,” Xave said. 
“Would that be my fault?” the man said, his buttons on his blue shirt 
reflecting neon light. His face was that of a father, trying to teach his stupid 
kid a life lesson. 
“Technically, yes, since you could have prevented it,” Xave replied. 
“Exactly,” the officer said with satisfaction, and walked outside. 
 
182 
He was just playing with me, Xave thought and hurried after him, passing 
the two policewomen, wading through the roaring manifestation of 
random insults. He felt two dozen eyes fixated on his neck, trying to break 
it. The policeman carved a safe tunnel out of the mass of raging bodies 
until they reached Xave’s car. 
For a second Xave saw blood dripping off the car’s sides, but it was just 
his mind trying to instill guilt. 
Not even my mind’s on my side. 
“Let the man drive,” the policeman said loudly. The mob’s yelling 
quieted down a bit and they retreated a meter or two. 
Xave thanked the policeman, got into his car and left the scene.  
One more to go, he reminded himself. 
The image of Fretz’s father flashed in front of his eyes, a stoic giant, 
with a blunt weapon that inflicted remorse instead of wounds and broken 
bones. 
“Well, fuck you too, Mr. Hamer!” he shouted at the windshield, both of 
his hands wringing the steering wheel. “Oh, you’re not saying anything to 
me, are you? I’m not worthy of an answer, am I? Well, tough shit, guess 
who doesn’t give a fuck!” 
He hit the wheel in the center once, his car honked in agreement. 
That felt better. 
The other parents lived in Mienz as well, fortunately, just ten minutes 
away, this time in a small house that already looked sad by itself. There 
were two camera teams and reporters of different news channels waiting 
for their prey and probably summarizing “the events so far”. More people 
with strong vocal chords and fists that didn’t seem to be affected by 
gravity had gathered behind the cameras, waving more signs: 
“No more INSTANT deaths!” 
“Return to hell – this INSTANT!” 
“Travel INSTANT, kill your infant!” 
Xave parked his car, took a deep breath, got out, and as he began 
walking towards the house, he was immediately surrounded by the 
inquisitive multi-headed monster. 
“Are you going to resign?” 
“Did you ask God before plotting against him?” 
“Will you compensate the family monetarily?” 
“Why isn’t Walter here himself?” 
“How many more are you going to kill?” 
“Is this just a game to you?” 
 
183 
Xave exploded into anger and turned around so furiously, that his 
followers were shocked for a moment. He breathed in and set out to say 
something, waited for a bit, then – before he could say something stupid – 
a microphone poked his jaw and deformed his face into what must have 
been a funny grimace to his onlookers. 
He swallowed his hasty response – and continued his march to the 
house, where three policemen waited for him. 
Before Xave could say anything, the door to the house sprung open and 
an old, but surprisingly fit man welcomed him. “Ah, welcome, Xave! 
Please, come in, come in.” 
Xave rubbed his jaw, nodded at the uniforms, and went inside. The old 
man grabbed his hand and shook it. “How nice of you to visit us, we’re all 
waiting in the living room.” 
He left a clueless Xave standing in the entrance area, so motionless that 
he felt like a statue. At least nobody could have held it against a statue for 
not having the right words to apologize for a murder he hadn’t committed. 
And, by the way, brain, it wasn’t murder, it was involuntary suicide, Xave 
retorted in his head. 
“Onwards,” Xave whispered to himself and forced his right leg to start 
his death march. 
The interior of the living room was quite lovely and warm. The 
fireplace sung a crackling song. There were paintings of various people on 
the walls and a dozen lit candles around a picture of the deceased Brittany 
on a small table next to an ancient sewing machine. A black hi -fi system 
tower was quietly pushing some kind of Rock music through two 
speakers. Four people looked at Xave as he entered; he assumed them to be 
parents and grandparents. 
They all smiled. 
Xave hesitated. There was something eerie about the four faces, the 
death of their loved one hanging in the air like a veiled ghost. 
“Please, take a seat,” the younger woman said, probably Brittany’s 
mother. 
Xave sat down across all four of them on a heavy arm chair, the black 
leather creaking under his butt as he slowly lowered his body. 
He decided to go first. “Let me begin by saying that you have our 
deepest sympathies.” 
“Thank you,” they all said almost in unison. 
“Oh, Mr. Stern,” the old woman said. “Don’t trouble yourself with 
finding the right words.” 
 
184 
Xave’s reply got stuck in his throat and once again he resembled a 
marble statue. 
“Our Brittany has left us on her own choice,” the father said. “We know 
she’s happy now.” 
The mother smiled broadly and almost giggled. 
“I… We…” Xave stammered, trying to get a foot on solid ground. 
“We’re happy, happier now than we were before,” the old man said. He 
was sitting on the armrest of the sofa in front of Xave, on which his wife 
was sitting. The second sofa, near the fireplace, was occupied by the 
younger couple. 
“But…” Xave leaned forward to try and get a hold of the situation. 
“You have our full support,” the mother said, gleefully. “Please do not 
hesitate to fully inspect Brittany to help the cause and improve the world.”  
“You want money?” Xave blurted out his best guess. Was that the 
reason for their excitement and indifference? Because they awaited a hunk 
of change? 
“No!” they all said, one waving hands, others shaking heads. 
“You misunderstand,” said the grandmother. “We don’t see death as 
something bad.” 
“You don’t,” Xave said. 
“We don’t understand why people are so upset about death,” the father 
continued. “Life is preparation, death is the journey.” 
“You can have money,” Xave said. He felt like an absolute idiot, but he 
forgot his good words at the door. 
The grandfather laughed. “It’s not about money,” he said and slapped 
his thigh. “We don’t feel sadness, we are happy.” 
Xave’s mind returned to work and he seemed to get it. “In that case, let 
me at least assure you that Brittany will be most important in our future 
research. She will play a major role in improving instant travelling for the 
rest of the world.” 
“Thank you,” the mother said, her hands folded. 
There’s a tear, Xave thought. But not a sad one. 
“That’s all we wanted to hear,” the grandmother said with a shivering 
voice. “Oh my, where are my manners? Do you want coffee? Something to 
eat, perhaps?” 
Xave rose from his seat. “It has been a pleasure to meet such an idyllic 
family, but I’m afraid my time is up and must be spent elsewhere. Fires are 
burning back in the company, you understand,” he said, smiling and 
flashing his teeth. 
They all stood and shook his hand one after another. 
 
185 
“Absolutely,” the father said. 
“Please take care,” were the mother’s words. 
“Let us know when you have any news,” the grandfather said. 
And finally, the grandmother dragged Xave down to her level and 
spoke into his right ear: “Brittany would have wanted this as well, you 
must know. She would’ve been very proud, as proud as we all are right 
now.” 
Xave straightened his back again and bowed slightly. “We will do our 
best to honour Brittany and her family.” 
With that, he left the house and his smile broke apart. He didn’t try to 
comprehend what his senses had just endured. Instead, he faced the 
yelling people outside, almost welcoming them and their logical ways. 
Two of the officers helped him to his car this time, and as he put the foot 
on the gas pedal he waved at everyone, smiling. 
 
186 
 
 
 
The INSTANT tent in Vowkinn was surrounded by annoyed travellers, 
who had obviously already become dependent on the quick and free 
transportation. 
Leonard approached the crowd from the left side of the pedestrian 
path. There was only one guard keeping the mob at bay, telling them to 
remain calm. 
No police, no press. 
They are all focusing on Mienz because of the teenager tragedy, just as we 
hoped, Leonard realized. 
“The instant travelling service will return to regular working conditions

I need you to calm down, turn around and spend the day somewhere 
else!” 
Leonard moved closer and heard various complaints. Someone was 
about to miss an important meeting, a woman said her family was on the 
other side, yadda, yadda. As if there were no other means of transportation 
anymore, Leonard thought. 
Bellward, Timony, and Mardo arrived at the scene from all sides and 
joined the crowd. 
Timony, resembling a stick figure with a dark blue hat, vanished 
completely, while Bellward pushed people away as if they were blades of 
grass, with Mardo following in his path. 
They were all wearing improvised INSTANT guard uniform replicas. 
Fortunately for them, Verayne had a special yearning for stealing clothes 
(and commanding others to do so). On the second floor of the thieves ’

and costumes. Half of the available clothes had been red - her favourite 
color, but they had found some blue ones as well. Cheap chains to wear 
from shoulder to shoulder had completed the trick. However, only Timony 
had a fitting hat. 

 
187 
Bellward looked incredibly funny, Leonard had to admit. He hoped

Bellward’s body by her husband Chawlee and the young Marla the night 
before. It had been a priceless situation, but hopefully with a prize in sight.  
Leonard had to push aside his distracting thoughts and concentrate on

So far, everything went according to the plan they had come up with

Someone else is coming? Leonard thought and he felt a rush of anxiety. 
“We’re here to take the booth for repairs,” Bellward said. 
They had suggested he should talk for obvious reasons. 
Not waiting for a response from the flabbergasted guard, the four

The air was thick in the tent, Leonard realized, probably because there

shining quite eagerly. 
“Wait,” the real guard said and made an attempt to turn away and pick

“Quick,” Leonard said through his teeth to his three companions.  
For a moment he couldn’t believe he was already on a job together with 
more or less a bunch of strangers, but the past few days had been too 
reassuring, too comfortable and - dare he think - fun. Almost like a fam-

Leonard stepped forward and leaned against the glass booth. To his

Timony giggled. 
Leonard stepped forward. “Somebody please call the ambulance - we

They made an effort to walk faster, even Bellward, who continually

we are, I’d say.” He grabbed a small neat pile of money and held up the 
valuable pieces of paper. 
Bellward breathed heavily and wiped his forehead. “Good. I ’m not 
gonna carry this thing another foot.” 
 
196 
 
 
 
While Kristella and Walter made their way to the 8 p.m. press conference, 
Rick was working like a maniac on the seventh floor of the INSTANT 
hotel. Blue light shone outside through the three large bullet-proof 
windows into the night, sent out by the eight heavy CRT monitors 
standing across three desks with their backs against a wall. 
Three of the monitors were set to display three different sectors of the 
INSTANT cable network. They overlapped at the edges, but this setup 
eradicated the need for scrolling. All yellow blinking lights across the map 
indicated dysfunctional booths, and green lines represented cables, the red 
line between Taresville and Illsburg blinked dangerously. 
The fix that Walter had uploaded onto a server on the network was 
supposed to go out to all booths now, which, according to Walter himself, 
shouldn’t be a problem thanks to the tool Walter had programmed. 
Rick followed the overly complicated instructions to send out the 
software patch to all booths remotely. He flipped through ten pages, 
clicking and typing something every now and then, and repeated the 
process for every booth. 
This wasn’t his kind of work. Even though he started to memorize 
certain workflows, he never really warmed up to computers. 
Someone has to do it, he reassured himself. 
He had to stop halfway through when he tried to update the booth at 
Vowkinn. It wouldn’t respond. He finished the other ones shortly before 9 
o’clock, pushed himself away on his office chair to a table on the other side 
of the room and before he could pick up the receiver to call the Vowkinn 
guard - the phone rang. 
He stuck the receiver to his ear. “Yeah?” 
“Hey, Rick,” the Vowkinn guard said. 
“Oh good, I was about to call you,” Rick said. 
“I just woke up in the hospital an hour ago, they wouldn’t let me call 
you earlier.” 

 
197 
“Hospital?” Rick stood up and stretched, suppressing a hearty moan. 
He glanced at the monitors, out of habit. 
“Yeah, some other guards visited me today while I was keeping the 
people away. The bigger one grabbed the booth and shoved it in my face. I 
lost two teeth and the doc says I have a slight concussion.” The voice 
sounded aggressive. 
“You mean, the booth’s gone?” Rick asked, now looking out of the 
window. Ever since the windows had gotten replaced by bulletproof ones 
he had felt a lot safer. There had been a discussion to even remove the 
windows completely and insert bricks instead, but Xave had been against 
it. “Never show them we’re afraid,” he had said. “We’re already afraid 
enough by not letting Walter out of the house.” 
“Yes,” the guard confirmed. “Jesus, I still can’t believe he just picked it 
up, ripping it out of the ground like the goddamn Hulk.” 
“I’ll mention that to the police in a few minutes when I’m at the 
Taresville INSTANT. Do you remember their faces? Any names maybe?” 
“Faces – maybe,” the guard said hesitantly. 
“I’ll call you again later.” 
“Sure, I’ll be here all night.” 
“Good, talk to you later then,” Rick said, bundled the curly telephone 
cable in his left hand, hung up the receiver and dropped the cable next to  
the phone. 
He checked the monitors again. There were two keyboards lying in 
front of the screens and he typed a few commands on both of them, 
finishing up his patch work, rebooting the complete network. 
He rose straight up, inhaled and exhaled, looking at his work. 
“There,” he said to himself. “Now off to the main node.” 
He and Walter had organically started calling the Taresville INSTANT 
tent “the main node” because it was the heart of the network; you could 
travel to all available booths from there. 
He picked up his blue coat and turned to the right, where a booth stood 
in the corner, right next to the door. Both ways led out of the room, and 
Rick couldn’t shake that strange feeling when looking at both exits; they 
seemed so similar and yet so different. 
He activated the booth from a console next to it and checked one of the 
monitors from afar, squinting a little. “Seems to work,” he mumbled, 
moved inside, vanished and stepped out of the maintenance booth in 
Taresville’s main station. 
 
198 
The silent humming of the computers got replaced by loud, impatient 
voices of press people trying to squeeze answers out of the poor guards, 
and customers who demanded to travel this instant, and the sound of cars 
passing by on wet pavement outside of the INSTANT tent. 
“Hey Rick,” Rae in her blue uniform greeted him. “Boy, those guys 
have been giving us a pretty rough time the past few hours.” 
“Yeah, it sounds pretty nasty,” Rick said, turning around, taking in his 
new surroundings. Flashing blue and red light highlighted the dozens of 
heads and umbrellas in the night outside. “The police are still here?”  
“Yep,” she nodded. “Guess they either found something or died from 
the stench.” 
Rick looked right at her. “I’ve updated all booths remotely, check and 
see if they work and then resume normal activity. Don’t worry about 
Vowkinn for now.” 
“Alright, I’ll get right to it,” Rae said and jumped to the next booth’s 
console. 
Rick fought his way through the sea of hands, umbrellas and 
microphones, and walked around the tent, evading three parked police 
cars. The entrance to the sewers was surrounded by one giant lamp, police 
lines, and two policemen. 
“Hello, Mr. Lorand,” one of them said. “The chief inspector wants to 
talk to you.” 
“Alright,” Rick said and climbed down into the sewers. 
There were identical little spotlights placed every five meters in the 
corridor, lighting up the rubbery surface of the black, thick and heavy 
INSTANT cables. He heard voices echoing from further down the path and 
he followed them. When he stepped into the actual sewers, he stopped for 
a moment to stare at all the lights the police had mounted on the walls, as 
if Christmas had come early to the underworld. 
A policeman nodded at him while speaking through his radio. 
Bernand would have a field day down here, Rick thought. 
After a minute or two he reached the scene of the caved in ceiling and 
the destroyed cable. There were a handful of uniformed men and women 
looking at the smallest details to recover any clues they might have missed. 
Lots of little numbered cards marked their previously found oddities; a 
scratch on one of the fat INSTANT cords, a dent in the wall, a crack in the 
ceiling, and footprints on the ground next to the flowing water. 
Somebody shot photos in regular intervals. Two people were talking 
with each other, another uniformed woman spoke commands into her 
radio while straightening her fancy peaked hat. 
 
199 
The chief inspector stood in the middle of all this, holding a bunch of 
papers in his right hand. Rick’s first impression formed a fifty-year-old, 
grumpy man, married with kids, beer-loving, as well as way too much into 
strictness in his rules and surroundings. The chief also appeared to have 
had red hair once. 
“Chief inspector,” Rick said, shaking his hand. “I heard you were 
looking for me. I’m Rick Lorand.” 
“Ah,” the inspector said. “Chief inspector Reklur, at your service. And 
yes, I’ve been looking for you.” 
“How’s the investigation doing?” Rick asked, trying to sound like the 
issue at hand was life-threateningly serious. 
“Just fine, just fine,” the chief said, a broad grin on his chubby face. 
“How’s the work on the network coming along?” 
“Everything’s patched up,” Rick said. “But the booth in Vowkinn was 
probably stolen.” 
“Really?” Reklur asked. 
“Well, the guard said something along the lines of fake guards coming 
in, uprooting the booth and knocking him out with it.” 
Reklur’s face was a mixture of wonder and incredulity. “I’ll send 
someone there to check it out.” 
“Thank you,” Rick said. “Do we already know how that happened?” 
He pointed at the wall of debris, blocking the tunnel behind Reklur’s back. 
“It wasn’t an earthquake or any other natural cause, we know that 
now,” Reklur explained, crossing his arms. “There were two or three 
people here, placing explosives. Some nearby residents actually he ard a 
blast, and all of their approximate times match up. We’re still trying to find 
someone who saw anyone entering the sewers, but no luck so far. The 
cables are being watched by some of my guys, it must have happened on a 
shift change or toilet break or whatever.” 
“Or whatever?” Rick repeated, instead of holding it in. 
“That’s the theory of that idiot over there.” He pointed a thumb at a 
hardworking man behind him. “I think, it could have also been an inside 
job, some of our guys,” he said and nudged an elbow in Rick’s side. 
“That’s a lot more exciting, isn’t it?” 
Rick ignored Reklur’s weird euphoria. “Is it possible to get rid of all the 
bricks and dirt and everything? We’d really like to resurrect our 
connection.” 
Reklur sighed. “We don’t know yet, there’s still a lot we can do here to 
find traces of the intruders. A satanic spell or some voodoo trick we 
haven’t thought of yet.” 
 
200 
“Are you taking this seriously?” Rick said, knowingly trying to insult 
the man, his eyes ablaze. 
“Why yes,” Reklur defended himself. “If I’m like this, you can be sure I 
am taking it super serious, squared.” He patted Rick on his back and 
grinned. “You need more humour in your life, or else you’ll turn into the 
asshole you’re trying not to be.” 
Rick didn’t know whether to laugh or sink his right fist into Reklur’s 
left cheek. 
“This isn’t a murder scene,” Reklur continued. “Else you’d be hearing 
even more jokes. This is how I work.” 
“Fair enough,” Rick said, giving up. “Let us know when you know 
more.” 
“When?” Reklur asked. 
Rick was about to leave and had to face Reklur again. “When you know 
more?” 
“When’s that?” the chief inspector asked. 
“Soon, I hope,” Rick answered. 
Reklur produced a little black appointment book out of his coat, clicked 
a pen and began to write something down. “Let… Rick… know… soon.” 
He shut the book with a bang, and hid both book and pen in his coat. 
“Consider it done,” he said, mockingly. 
Rick left Reklur standing there without another word. As he left the 
scene, he heard a hearty chuckle behind his back. 
 
201 
 
 
 
After four hours of sleep, Kristella slammed the screaming alarm clock 
next to her finely crafted bed. 
There was a foul taste in her mouth, but she hesitated comparing it to 
that of a dead rat. Her eyes opened up against their will, her body moved 
even though it weighed a ton and she yawned and stretched even though 
she’d rather fall back and keep on sleeping for at least two years.  
She stood up, not knowing why. At 7 a.m., the sun on the horizon was 
aggressively shooting sun rays at her through the unbreakable windows, 
tickling the skin. 
The bathroom awaited her with a heated floor and she felt a warming 
feeling crawl up from her toes over her back to her neck and she stood still 
for a minute, swaying back and forth. 
The mirror told her she wasn’t wearing anything. A look over her 
shoulder revealed that her clothes had somehow dropped to the floor in a 
circle around her bed, right after the press had finally had enough.  
Walter had decided to answer every question, even duplicates. Kristella 
still couldn’t believe that - although he had constantly contradicted himself 
- he had continued to be able to form words without stumbling over them. 
Kristella had merely coordinated the interview, pointing at raised hands.  
She was already brushing her teeth before she realized it and in her 
head she went through the storm of words they had endured a few short 
hours ago. 
In the end, they had handled the situation well enough. And by 
answering every question without exception, they had shown patience and 
willingness to participate in a dialog with the public. 
She spat out white foam and continued to brush. 
Whom was she kidding, it was probably a sign that they didn’t take 
their work seriously and had all the time in the world to linger around and 
repeat the same answers for several hours. 
Several hours! 

 
202 
The tooth brush slipped past a back tooth and bruised the inner cheek. 
She pulled it out, moaned angrily, spat again, put away the brush and 
rinsed her mouth. 
Then she stepped into the shower. 
Whatever, right? In the end, they were still the company who had made 
travelling from one end to another possible without much of an 
interruption; they had eradicated the journey and provided access to the 
destination immediately. How cool was that? 
What’s a few missteps, such as, you know, dead people? Who’d give a 
crap in a week, right? 
Thirty minutes later, with a layer of makeup on her face to hide the 
dark shadows below her eyes, and casual clothes that fit nicely, she was on 
her way into the dining hall, where Walter was already sitting and staring 
at the empty table, waiting. 
“Let me guess,” Kristella said while taking a seat opposite to him, her 
chair creaking as she moved it. “You haven’t slept at all.” 
Walter looked up in slow motion. His brain seemed to function at 
normal speed – which compared to mere mortals was still fast – but the 
reaction time of his body had set a new low record. 
As he spoke, his mouth appeared to form words after they had been 
spoken, his tongue floating up and down like a whale. “Sleep… You keep 
mentioning that word.” 
“It’s an ancient invention, actually,” Kristella said. “You should look it 
up. It rivals the awesomeness of instant travelling.” 
Walter guffawed. 
Xave, in his usual salesman attire, and Rick, once again wearing his 
dark blue INSTANT uniform, arrived in the hall and sat down next to 
Walter and Kristella. 
“Good morning,” Xave greeted. 
“Hey,” Rick said. 
“Morning!” Kristella cheered. 
Walter waved, two seconds too late. 
Xave looked at the scientist. “Oh no,” he said. 
“Yep,” Kristella replied, nodding. “Once again a stranger to sleep. Says 
he’s never heard of it.” 
“You know I can’t repair you, right?” Rick said. 
A waiter appeared with four plates seemingly stuck to his arms. “Good 
morning everyone, here’s your daily dose of heart-warming breakfast.” 
 
203 
One plate landed in front of every sitting person, and the smell of 
scrambled eggs and roasted ham seduced their stomachs. The waiter 
disappeared again where he had come from. 
They started eating one after another, except Walter, who made picking 
up his fork a life time effort. 
“You gotta lie down,” Kristella said, munching, pointing with her fork.  
“She’s right, Walter,” Xave agreed. 
Walter turned to Xave like a robot low on energy and a message ran 
from his lips: “I’ve almost fixed Jeremy.” 
“I slept in the same room tonight,” Xave replied. “His life is reduced to 
a wheeze every five seconds.” 
“Shouldn’t we call some relative or something?” Kristella asked. 
“I’m not even sure Jeremy’s his real name,” Xave said. “But you’re 
right, I’ll contact someone he’s related to.” He swallowed, chopped off 
another piece of egg and hovered his filled fork in front of his face. “When 
he’s up again.” He shoved it into his mouth. 
Kristella raised her eyebrows, but before she was able to criticize him, 
her mind had already worked out the reason. “We’ll let the world know 
when it can’t sue us,” she said. 
The biting, swallowing and chewing of the three men were the only 
response. 
Walter had just finished his first bite and continued with the second 
part of his food adventure, with the fork already on its way back to the 
plate. He said: “I can fix him. Almost there.” 
Kristella felt like watching a badly dubbed foreign movie. “How? I 
hope your plan involves at least an hour of sleep. You can’t go on like 
this!” 
Rick finished his plate first and leaned back, wiping his mouth. “She’s 
right.” 
Walter made an annoyed face and continued eating in silence.  
Xave sighed and changed the topic. “I made new friends yesterday.”  
Kristella welcomed the change and chomped on another piece of ham 
with a hearty crust. “Really?” 
“Yep, the family of the girl were real eager to hear that she’s, as they 
said, in a happier place,” Xave told, pushing leftover pieces of eggs to one 
corner and digging it all up with one swipe. “So I guess that’s a new kind 
of service we’re offering. Travel INSTANT, kill your infant! At least, that’s 
what a sign suggested by one happy bystander.” 
Rick chuckled, but in a sad way. 
 
204 
“What about the other family?” Kristella asked, picking up the last egg 
crumb. 
“They weren’t inviting me with open arms, to be frank,” Xave said. 
“You should’ve heard me talk to myself in the car afterwards. Actually, 
scratch that. You would all stop looking at me.” 
All eyes were on Walter now, who was wrestling the god of food.  
“What about the conference?” Xave asked. 
“Well,” Kristella said, gesturing at Walter. “They drained all his blood.”  
Xave patted Walter on his back and grinned. 
“Oh yeah, I already told Xave about it,” Rick began. “Chief inspector 
Reklur is working on the case with the broken cable and the theft of the 
Vowkinn booth.” 
Walter spit out half his hard-earned ham. “Theft?” 
“Yep, it’s gone,” Rick said. 
Walter’s chin hung to the floor, letting Kristella see what he had already 
carefully chewed on. 
Then the scientist suddenly woke up. “What?! How… How is that 
possible? Weren’t they drilled to the ground?” 
“We were planning on doing that,” Rick explained. “But we assured 
ourselves that the connections were thick and strong enough to make any 
attempts at stealing them impossible. Remember?” 
Walter let his fork fall on his plate and hid his face in his palms. 
“How big of a deal is this?” Kristella asked. “Nobody’s going to be able 
to do anything with it, right?” 
Walter spread his fingers and two dark eyes peered at her through 
them. “A nobody might not be able to understand it, no.” He removed his 
hands, but somehow, a shadow remained in his eyes. “There could always 
be competition, though; other people, trying to race against us.”  
“There was this company that tried to compete,” Xave said. “But the y 
were just full of crap.” 
“So, you think they returned to steal our tech to produce it 
themselves?” Rick asked. 
“Possible,” Xave said. “You say this Reklur guy is handling both 
cases?” 
Rick nodded. “Yes.” 
“Any mention to the press?” Kristella asked. 
 
205 
Xave, once again, let out a sigh. “Our technology kills people and we ’re 
losing that technology to others we don’t even know. That sounds like a 
headline in the making.” He shook his head and continued before anybody 
could say anything. “Don’t say a word,” he said, pointing at Kristella. 
“They will write about people not being able to travel from or to Vowkinn 
at the moment, there will be rumours, some witness reports, but we won ’t

“If you find someone who’s okay with being imprisoned for eternity, go

“Don’t think you’re the only one being okay with that,” Xave said. “I’ll

“Are we still going to look at the teenager corpses?” Rick asked. 
“I have to,” Walter answered, his eyes shutting down again. “I’m 
obligated to inspect them, even if just out of respect. They died because of 
me.” 
“Nuh-uh,” Xave intervened. “They died because they were stupid. 
We’ve established that.” 
Walter ignored him, dozing off. “But first I need to fix Jeremy.”  
“What do I do except the usual stuff, like answering calls and not 
telling anyone anything?” Kristella asked, with her hand raised at shoulder 
height. 
Xave seemed to know just the right thing. “Ring up Petar and Isela, see 
if you can sustain their financial support for a while longer. We’re not sure 
yet how much payment people are going to accept for using the best way 
to travel. Maybe ask them for suggestions. They’ll like that.” 
Walter opened his eyes to stare at his cold food. “God damn it. I got 
another idea.”

After leaving the van to its own fate in the suburbs of Vowkinn, Bellward 
Leonard, Mardo, and Timony walked the rest of the way to their hideout. 
As they passed a giant green statue standing next to an ancient building 
surrounded by mighty pillars, Timony hugged himself tightly and said: 
“We’re gonna get caught, I can feel it in my fucking bones.” 
“Keep your pessimistic crap to yourself,” Bellward bellowed. 
They were marching in a single file on the narrow sidewalk. Cars shot 
by in the early morning sun. 
Leonard tucked on his blue outfit. “They may remember our faces,” he 
dared to mention. 
Bellward, who was walking in front of Leonard, shrugged with his 
mountainous shoulders. “It’s not like the world’s fond of my face, 
anyhow.” 
Carrying the treasure box wrapped in parts of his uniform, Mardo said: 
“With this money we can lay low for a while, I think.” 
“Ya think?” Timony asked, looking nervously left and right. “Why 
didn’t you think before we executed your plan?” 
“You were the one who was so eager to just do it, remember?” Mardo 
shot back. 
“Well shit, ain’t you the clever one now,” Timony said. 
“I’ll tell Verayne to sew your lips together when we’re home,” Bellward 
said. “That’ll teach ya.” 
“At least I’m speaking my mind,” Timony countered. 
“Well, your mind’s full of shit.” Bellward pointed to his right. “This 
way.” 
Everyone followed. 
They passed dozens of houses, built right next to each other, most not 
being more than five storeys high. For another half an hour they saw 
nothing but streets, pedestrians, cars, taxis, busses and tramways.  
“To think all these things could disappear,” Leonard said, more or less 
to himself. 

 
207 
“It’s kinda neat, right?” Mardo added from behind him. “And we held 
the technology in our hands!” 
Leonard looked at a handful of people flocking into an overloaded bus, 
which groaned with exhaustion.

surroundings, just as Bellward announced: “Here we are.” 
They entered the hideout through the secret entrance and greeted 
Verayne, Chawlee and Marla.

“You guys are in the news,” Chawlee said, with a slightly worried

“Really?” Mardo asked.

Bellward crossed his arms, flexing his muscles. “He wouldn’t stop 
yapping.”

Mardo handed him the shiny box. “There’s money inside. It’s about

“What?” Mardo asked, his face shocked and sad. “But this is the perfect

“Yeah, the fuck, man?” Timony disagreed. Bellward slapped the back

“The news report said that they set a certain Detective Reklur on the

“Oh god, that asshat?” Timony shouted. 
“Unsurprisingly, we did strike a nerve,” Chawlee said. 
Bellward shook his head and Mardo sat down. 
“Leonard,” Chawlee said, addressing the man with the long hair. 
“Yes?” Leonard answered. 
Chawlee counted a small fragment of the money he was holding in his

fact, I believe it’s simply better for you.” 
 
208 
Leonard understood fairly well. “No worries,” he said. Although they 
had been like a family to him, he knew there was a reason not to get too

“You were a part of this, so that money is yours anyway,” Chawlee

Chawlee nodded. “Now that you’re here, we shouldn’t waste more 
time.” 
“We’re leaving already?” Mardo asked, beside himself. 
Poor guy’s probably thinking it’s his fault, Leonard thought. 
“In that case,” Leonard intervened, “I shall depart right away.” 
Mardo stared at Leonard with an open mouth. 
Leonard shook everybody’s hand, except Marla’s who he waved at from 
afar. “It was a pleasure being a part of your family for the last few days. I 
will hold the memories dear.” 
Chawlee patted Leonard’s shoulder. “If we had known you a little 
longer, I could trust you more. I really hope you understand.” 
“I do,” Leonard reassured. “Trust is not easily won.” 
“Maybe we’ll meet again,” Mardo said, now standing. 
Leonard grinned, holding up his cash, gently waving it in the air.

Against all good advice he’d heard the past few hours, Walter hadn’t slept 
one bit. 
He was sitting on the ground of his suite, surrounded by piles of paper. 
His fingernails were black from the dozens of pencils he had used, half of 
which were resting behind him, reduced to mere stumps. There was sweat 
on his forehead, so much in fact that it looked like he had just stepped out 
of a shower. His teeth were showing, creaking against each other, his chin 
was moving left and right like a saw trying to cut a tree. He wrote so 
quickly, his hand was a blur. 
Nothing he wrote lasted long, for all the numbers and symbols the 
pencil would emit were thrown away within seconds. 
Walter yelled out loud. He rubbed his temple with both of his hands, 
smearing the black pencil color all over his cheeks. 
It just doesn’t work. There is no logical solution. 
An old notebook Walter had written when he was sixteen years old sat 
next to him, opened up to a random page that showed sketches of a device 
almost identical to the ones that were currently serving hundreds of people 
and their needs to get to their destinations as quickly as possible, as 
instantly as possible. 
Hundreds of people. 
And if fate would have its sadistic ways, they were all travelling to hell. 
Walter flung through his wall of papers that he had built, jumped to his 
feet, and with a scream he jumped to his right and attacked the bullet -
proof window with his pencil. 
The wooden writing tool burst at its tip. 
Stars at the bottom of the night - the lights of the living city - filled 
Walter’s eyes. His right hand stood still in the air with the pencil at the 
window’s heart. 
Walter looked down. 
The black knob in the dead rat’s head stared at him reproachfully. 
“Not enough time,” Walter said through his teeth. 

 
210 
Violently, he grabbed the rodent with his bare hand, letting the pencil 
drop to where the rat had been lying, and bolted towards the phone, 
twitching his fingers to enter Xave’s room number. 
“Pick up,” he yelled, almost hysterically. 
“What,” Xave answered, after Walter’s universe had collapsed. 
“Get Jeremy down to floor five,” Walter commanded. “Now.” 
On the way out Walter managed to throw away the receiver and pick 
up his notebook filled with loose papers in one motion with his left hand. 
He stormed into the hallway and called for the elevator by squeezing the 
life out of the button. 
He danced on his feet, the rat’s fur tickling his hand. Infuriated, he 
tucked the old book under his right armpit and punched the elevator 
button with his left knuckles. Five times, six times, until blood appeared 
from underneath his pale skin. 
The elevator graciously arrived. 
Walter practically was inside the lift before the doors were open the 
tiniest bit, and pushing buttons again. 
“Down, down, down,” the scientist’s mouth spat. “I don’t have time for 
this.” 
As the doors of the elevator closed in an awful, sleep inducing speed, 
Walter had to restrict his anger enough to not squeeze the rat’s corpse too 
much. 
The elevator music almost lulled Walter to sleep. He staggered, then 
adrenaline shot back into his bloodstream and his eyelids ripped open 
again, revealing eyes that were showing the world what being tired 
actually meant. 
Walter wasn’t on his feet anymore, he was flying. He wasn’t conscious, 
he was somewhere else, controlling his body remotely. 
“Oh god, who invented this,” Walter exclaimed and kicked the metal 
door in front of him, which now reflected a misshapen face of the scientist.  
The elevator happily and patiently announced its arrival on the fifth 
floor with the noise of a bell and tried to open its doors, but only the one 
that hadn’t been kicked succeeded to slide aside. 
Walter leaped out, the tail of the rat flailing away from his grip and two 
loose papers fleeing from the book in a high arch. 
Floor number five, just like number six, was reserved for building 
booths. Walls of previous suites had been taken down; now the floor was 
simply a large area with supporting pillars here and there. 
 
211 
Ghostly glass tubes, fat cables, and heaps of electronics were lying 
about, hardly illuminated by a handful of lamps on the ground and spare 
lights on the ceiling. 
Walter found two booths which he had already prepared for different 
experiments; updates to the core ability of instant travelling. He had to 
make a few adjustments now, according to his calculations. 
Not many. 
In fact, so few it was almost bafflingly simple. Yet again, simple was 
rarely convincing to a complex mind, and changing one minor variable 
could always result in a devastating chain reaction. 
The elevator arrived again, almost without Walter noticing it. 
Xave stumbled outside, carrying Jeremy around his back with his arms 
under Jeremy’s thighs. 
“Walter,” Xave said, out of breath, coming closer to the scientist. “I ’m 
feeling sick. I’ve only slept like an hour. How come you haven’t vomited 
all over the place already?” 
Walter, already at work, fighting cables and wires and conductors and 
resistors, just shook his head. “Put him down here.” 
Xave gasped for air as he let Jeremy down to the ground as gently as 
possible. Jeremy hit the ground with a thud and wheezed. 
The salesman looked at the Trekkie. “What have we done to that poor 
bastard.” 
Jeremy replied by wringing in agony and coughing blood. Xave looked 
away. 
“Step back,” Walter announced. 
This better work, Walter thought. I hope I was right. 
He grabbed the rat and threw it into the right booth, where it hit the 
back of the glass and slid down to the base. 
“Close it,” Walter shouted at Xave, pointing a finger. “And the other 
one too.” 
Xave did as he was told, stepping over a puddle of Jeremy’s bodily 
fluids. 
Walter fiddled around with cables, a spark appeared between his 
fingers; thankfully at this point he didn’t feel anything anymore. Finally, 
the electronic thing he had assembled was pushed underneath the booth 
with the rat in it until it clicked into place. A provisory console next to it 
with cables protruding left and right, was victim to Walter’s fingers as they 
hacked away at it like vultures. 
“Do you even know what you’re typing without a screen?” Xave 
commented. 
 
212 
Walter jotted down a key, like God putting a finger down on Earth. 
“Jesus,” Xave said, holding both of his hands in front of his face, as a 
sudden light appeared from the rat’s glass coffin. Hiding behind his hands 
seemed enough at first, but then he looked away entirely. 
Walter, however, glared at his light. It was no match for him. 
The second booth flared up, the noise almost unbearable. 
Xave didn’t know whether to shut his ears or eyes or just run away. 
“Walter!” he screamed, trying to shoot words against the booth’s aural 
attacks. “What are you doing?” 
If he hadn’t known better, Xave surely felt the ground shaking and 
falling apart. As he tumbled backwards and landed on his back – the world 
around him went dark and silent. 
He opened his eyes and watched the pattern on the ceiling. For a 
minute, he thought he had seen stars. 
Walter appeared in his vision, stepping in front of a light and appearing 
only as a black shadow. Xave blinked at him. 
“It worked,” the scientist said, offering a hand to help Xave stand up. “I 
can save Jeremy now.” 
Xave pulled himself up with Walter’s help and dusted off his suit. “I 
like the sound of that, Walter,” he said and shook his head rigorously, 
trying to regain his right ear’s hearing ability. “But maybe you better wait 
until I’m out of the building before you hit that key again.” 
Walter chuckled. “There’s no time for that.” 
Xave realized what Walter meant when he saw Jeremy again. “How is 
he still alive?” 
Walter sighed and gestured Xave to grab Jeremy’s feet, while the 
scientist pulled up the man’s arms. Together they squeezed the dying 
human into the booth, which had previously contained the rat. 
“Where’s the rodent?” Xave asked, stuffing Jeremy’s foot inside and 
closing the booth. 
“There,” Walter said, pointing over his shoulder. 
Xave looked at the ground and saw a rat with its intestines spilled 
around it. 
Actual, normal intestines. 
Walter fed the console with input and before Xave could make a run for 
it – he hit that key again. 
“Oh god,” Xave said, but he was prepared this time. He stuffed his 
head in his coat and waited for the firework spectacle to be over.  
 
213 
Walter examined Jeremy for the whole process through the blinding 
light, as if gazing at the center of the sun, straining his eyeballs until tears 
dropped from his cheeks. 
At the height of the noise and light show, Jeremy’s body disappeared 
and was reassembled in the other booth. 
Calmness returned and Walter opened the booth containing Jeremy, 
who fell to his knees. The man inhaled, seemingly tasting air for the first 
time ever, then he stood up, tripped over his own feet, vomited and passed 
out. 
Walter turned to Xave and grinned with relief. 
 
214 
 
 
 
Kristella wasn’t of the sort to care much for food; nowadays she ate to stop 
her stomach from interrupting unannounced meetings with angry grow ls. 
Sitting in her suite, she chopped a triangular piece of dark chocolate 
cake into bite-sized pieces and moved a forkful towards her mouth. 
To the left of her small plate, she went over a small pile of papers, filled 
with various facts of Walter’s and Xave’s new ideas to bring in some 
money. 
Xave had just barged in, telling her, with an exhausted face, that 
Walter’s experiment had worked, and Kristella was to go ahead with the 
plan. 
It was almost midnight; Isela had assured her that she would still be in 
her office until at least 2 a.m. and that she wouldn’t have to wait. 
Her left hand drew a quick thin line at the bottom of a table of numbers 
and Kristella stood up, leaving the rest of the cake behind. 
She took the elevator – the incredibly slow elevator – up to floor 
number seven, where Rick was probably still working his butt off, while 
yearning to be with his family. 
As she went down the hallway towards the control room, her mother ’s 
voice in her head resounded: “What’s with you and your inability to have 
relationships? It’s not that hard, look at your father! Even he managed to 
help me make you.” 
“Yeah, yeah,” Kristella mumbled to herself, or rather to her mother. 
There was no need to force herself into some random guy’s arms, just to 
sustain the growth of human population; she always assumed the world 
could just as well go on without her help. 
“Knock, knock,” she said, while hitting the door with her fine -boned 
knuckles twice. 
“Yeah,” Rick responded from inside. 
“Hey,” Kristella said while entering the room, papers under her right 
arm. 
“What’s up?” 

 
215 
“Going to visit Isela.” 
Rick was resting his feet in front of one of the monitors, reading a book.

focus. 
Kristella gestured at the booth. “Is it okay if I…” 
“Oh yeah, sure, hang on.” Rick shut the book and flung it on the table. 
“Go on in, I’ll send you over.” 
Kristella stepped inside the booth; Rick closed the lid with his right 
hand while typing away on a small console with his left. 
He looked tired, Kristella realized. “How’s your son?” she asked, her 
voice echoing loudly around her. Rick probably wasn’t hearing her clear 
enough. 
“He’s fine,” Rick said, looked up at her, drew up one corner of his 
mouth and sent Kristella away with the push of a button. 
Being torn apart and pieced together didn’t sound very romantic and 
certainly felt weird, like a needle stuck underneath your skin, being turned 
left and right. It felt wrong. The darkness that lasted maybe a second, 
maybe a day still seemed otherworldly. She used to wonder if people who 
had hopped on a train for the first time had ever felt the same, the limits of 
speed being challenged more and more. Or the first human to take flight, 
had they also felt like breaking the laws of nature? 
A large group of people appeared in front of her, the city hall of 
Taresville in the background. A guard opened the booth and helped her 
outside. 
“Good evening, Ms. Attenburg,” the guard greeted her. She simply 
nodded with a smile in return and continued to make her way through the 
waiting travellers. 
She crossed the street, went up the stairs, looking at the two statues that 
pointed randomly at the night sky and walked inside. 
Through the maze in the city hall, she arrived at Isela’s office in the 
right wing of the building. 
The obligatory twin bodyguards stood outside, left and right, looking at 
her curiously. They both recognized her, the left one opened the door and 
let Kristella in. 
Isela was on the phone, speaking gibberish in another language. One of

while the other one was sitting at a smaller table in the adjacent room, as 
Kristella witnessed through the heavy wooden door that stood ope n. 
 
216 
Isela saw Kristella and gestured her to sit down. The mayoress’ makeup 
was thick, her green eyes accented by long fake eyelashes, her silver 
wristwatch dangling off her writing hand. 
Six signatures later, the secretary left the room, sitting down opposi te to 
the other assistant on her own even smaller table. 
Kristella noticed herself fidgeting around on her soft seat and forced

She straightened her posture, her leather chair imitating a throne.  
“Thank you for having me at this late hour, Mrs. Mantorry,” Kristella

“Indeed, indeed, we all know time is money and time stops for no one,”

Kristella knew better than to chuckle in response. “Actually, yes,

Walter would probably kill himself right now, Kristella thought.

Isela’s phone rang and one of her secretaries in the other room took the

As he saw Jeremy being hauled onto his bed by two of the bodyguards, 
Xave finally admitted to himself that travelling appalled him. 
The notion of being torn into pieces sounded terrifying. He trusted 
Walter, he knew the device worked, but only to a certain degree. 
Something felt entirely wrong, unnatural. 
For a few hours Xave watched Jeremy on his bed while doing some 
paperwork, mundane preparations for upcoming meetings with several 
(sometimes self-declared) important higher-ups that got wind of the scent 
of instant travelling. The smell of opportunity, money, fame; finally getting 
that entry into the history books. Hopping aboard the bandwagon as long 
as it’s popular. 
Like those god-damn Tamagotchis every kid has to have nowadays. 
Walter was in bed, at least that’s where Xave had ordered him to go to

Kristella was still out to talk to Isela, although Xave realized that she

222
