After two days of sleeping below a staircase in a dark alley and aimlessly 
wandering around, Leonard decided to go back home; a town called St. 
Frank. 
He still possessed 550 Schilling, and now that he knew where to go, he 
was glad he hadn’t spent it all on some random bed and breakfast hotel. 
The funny thing was that he had grown accustomed to Vowkinn and he 
remembered one of his rules: Don’t stay in one place for too long. The fact that 
it had been a week suddenly scared him. 
Vowkinn was quite different in size compared to other towns in 
Lostarichi. Since Leonard had been moving around, he calmed himself 
down with the thought that he hadn’t stayed in one place anyhow. 
And yet – his body had built up a base level of awareness. 
He walked through crowded streets, enjoying the freedom of not 
having to think quickly to acquire money for something to eat. He had 
bought small rations, mostly crispbread and cookies, and had filled his 
plastic bottle with fresh water at a nearby fountain. All of it was stored 
away in his coat pockets. 
The train station that would let him travel back home was still half an 
hour away and he enjoyed the fading autumn sun on his skin. 
He couldn’t remember which kind of potential he had seen in those 
instant travelling devices. Maybe some sort of quick escape? Not possible, 
too heavily monitored, too crowded, too distinct. Might as well leave a 
thick trail of money after robbing the bank. 
St. Frank would do him good. Not that his parents were still alive, or 
anyone else of his non-existent family, but he enjoyed the nostalgic feelings 
it provided him. 
His long hair rode the wind, his coat dancing along. He evaded people 
left and right; people talking to each other, people in groups with a dozen 
bags in their hands, children and their parents, pupils and students of 
various sizes, and beggars. 

 
226 
Normally he would’ve given away some money to people of his kind – 
well, maybe some of them weren’t his kind– but he already felt too 
vulnerable, as if highlighted by a spotlight in the crowd. 
The more he thought about it, the quicker his feet moved. 
At first he stopped for red lights blocking the way, but the closer he got 
to the train station, the more simply he dismissed the signals. 
He had to go home. 
A quarter of an hour later, he approached the train station; a giant old 
hall, surrounded, or rather entangled, by modern steel and glass buildings. 
He wanted to feel relief, but his brain knew better and told him to press on 
and actually wait with the celebrations until his behind was sitting firmly 
in one of the train cars. 
He entered the hall through the sliding doors. The loud noise of cars 
was replaced by a wide flood of distant voices of at least a hundred people. 
There was a large clock on the wall to the right, approximately three 
meters tall. There were two dozen shops, monitors hanging from walls, at 
least ten ticket stations spread out throughout the area, several information 
booths, and more beggars. 
A female voice announced arrivals and departures, while Leonard 
stepped in front of a ticket machine. 
He awkwardly pressed a button and readied his money – when 
suddenly, somebody touched his right shoulder. 
“Mr. Garrett,” a voice said. “Are you leaving us already?” 
Leonard turned around in shock – somebody knew where he was and 
who he was. 
He saw a heavy man in a suit and coat towering in front of him, maybe 
fifty years old; there was something in his eyes – determination. 
“Who are you?” Leonard asked. 
“Chief inspector Reklur, at your service. Or disservice, however you 
want to look at it.” 
Leonard’s mind shattered into a thousand pieces; time stood still. The 
clock on the wall moved its hands in slow motion, producing damped 
clicking noises. 
“How…” 
“Easy,” said Reklur, producing a small book. “I’ve been following you 
and your gang for the past few days. We saw you guys on video, did you 
know there are security cameras in front of the city hall? Crazy, right? In 
what world do we live in, for god’s sake! It’s a fucking police state! Ever 
since some guy shot at Walter and his big reveal party in front of the city 
hall, everybody’s taking things way too seriously around here.” 
 
227 
“I…” 
“Not to mention all the cameras in front of all the INSTANT booths, 
you remember, right? People waving at each other through those small 
TVs? Family entertainment!” Reklur said, his book, some sort of calendar 
book or diary, attached to his hand as if it were biting him. “We know you, 
Leonard. You’re a small fish, you do no harm. Sure, you steal every now 
and then, but in all honesty you’re like a paper cut to a giant. You’re 
actually doing something good for society! Everything you steal is being 
put directly back into the market, the economy’s thankful for that, believe 
me.” 
Leonard’s feet were rooted into place. The presence of the man in front 
of him had knocked his senses out cold. 
“So yes, we have a recorded history of your criminal past. You are a 
criminal; you understand that word, right? A derivation of crime?” Reklur 
continued. 
Leonard admitted nothing, although he had never thought of himself as 
a Samaritan, that’s for sure. 
“Saying nothing actually means you agree, I like that.” Reklur shifted 
his weight from one leg to the other. 
“I have a right to remain silent,” Leonard countered. 
“And I have a huge mountain of evidence in my backyard, there’s 
nothing anyone can do about that. Unless you hire a lawyer who sets that 
mountain on fire.” Reklur patted Leonard’s right shoulder. “So,” he 
continued. “I noticed you guys split up. What happened? I haven’t figured 
it out yet. Did they throw you out? Haven’t you paid your rent?” 
“I don’t know what you mean,” Leonard mumbled. 
“Of course you don’t, very clever,” Reklur said, laughing evilly. 
Some people around them already began to walk a little slower and 
stare. Leonard felt heat rising up in his body, his cheeks flaming hot.  
“You’re coming with me,” Reklur commanded, a triumphant and 
knowing smile between his puffed up cheeks. “I’m not going to hurt you 
or arrest you – as long as you cooperate.” 
“Cooperate?” 
“Yeah, you know, play along, play nice, play dead, roll over. The whole 
shebang. I want to know everything about your gang and what you were 
doing after you stole the teleportation device.” 
“I could outrun you,” Leonard said with conviction. 
“You can, but I will find you again,” Reklur assured him. “I won’t be as 
nice the next time, I can tell you that much right away.” 
 
228 
Leonard sighed and surrendered. “There’s not much to tell either way. 
They’re not my gang, we parted ways, and we sold the INSTANT booth.”  
“Well, aren’t you the talkative type,” Reklur cheered. “Sold to whom?” 
“They called him Einstein,” Leonard said. “He lived in some kind of 
underground laboratory below a field.” 
“Why are you telling me this?” Reklur asked, visibly amused. “Do you 
want to endanger your friends?” 
Leonard hesitated. “They’re not my friends. In my eyes, they were 
business partners for a short period of time. I tagged along just to see any 
possible opportunities, beneficial only to myself.” 
“I like that, only think for yourself, rat out anyone who’s getting too 
close to you,” Reklur summarized. “They were getting too close, right?” 
Leonard looked left and right. He had let himself go too much, 
enjoyment had gotten too big. Yes, he had actually enjoyed the little 
excursion, but deep down inside he had always had doubts. 
“Can you show me where they sold the booth?” Reklur asked, quickly 
evading a running tourist with two suitcases. “Watch it, asshole!” Reklur 
called after the hurrying man. 
“It was outside the city, I have got no clue whatsoever,” Leonard said. 
He wasn’t sure where the truth would get him, but the honesty train had 
departed and wouldn’t simply stop now. 
“Huh,” Reklur said, impatiently slapping his calendar notebook thing 
with the back of his hand. 
The life of a dozen strangers passed them both every few moments 
while Reklur and Leonard kept standing still. Time moved on without 
them, it seemed. 
“Whatever,” Reklur said. “We don’t need you, you’re a trout anyhow.” 
He wrote something down, ripped off the note, handed that to Leonard 
and pointed at the gates to various departing trains. “You better leave 
now. If you ever recall anything else, dial this number and share the 
painful memories.” 
“What makes you think I’ll keep that piece of paper after you’re out of 
my sight?” Leonard asked. 
“You may never know when you need something to wipe your ass 
with.” 
“How romantic.” 
“At least I’m letting you go, my love,” Reklur said, squeezing Leonard’s 
cheek and then striking it hard. “And that is so you won’t ever forget me.” 
Reklur left the station with wide steps. 
 
229 
Leonard crushed the piece of paper in his fist, threw it away - then he 
picked it back up, bought his ticket and went to one of the trains leading to 
St. Frank. 
He sat down next to an old woman and rubbed the spot where Reklur 
had hit him. 
 
230 
 
 
 
Walter woke up from a seemingly endless nightmare: The door to his suite  
had been unhinged, and creatures, or rather things had been watching him 
sleep. He had seen Kristella getting cut so deep, that only fragments of her 
body had remained, yet somehow, with one eye and parts of her head and 
mouth, she had still been able to look at him and speak nonsensical words, 
resulting in a horrifying sound of a throat with a gaping hole and a face 
with only one half of a cheek. 
But now, Walter was awake – for the first time in a while. He rubbed 
his eyes – the skin on his hands dry – and stood up from his bed. A look 
outside the bulletproof window told him it was day, the digital clock 
affirming that by gleefully displaying 16:17 in a glowy red. 
A moment for himself. He took a deep breath, looked at the chaos 
around him and decided to pull himself together and clean up the mess. 
He picked up a trash bin as large as his upper body, started sorting 
through papers, broken pencils and unwashed clothes, and threw away 
half of the items he found away. His furious garbage collection hurricane 
reached the dead rat – Walter hesitated, then threw it into the dark plastic 
abyss, where it landed with a damp knock in a bed of highly scientific 
papers. 
He froze for a second and looked after the rodent. 
Xave barged into the room. “The glorious scientist, as I live and 
breathe!” 
As always, Xave was wearing his suit, Walter observed, with a green tie 
today. 
“What are you doing?” Xave asked, standing on several papers on the 
ground, marking them with his shoes. 
“Getting rid of bad ideas,” Walter said simply. He still felt tired, but it 
was a comfortable fatigue that one only experiences when sleeping too 
long after days of testing the limits of one’s body. He grabbed the rat and 
put it back on the table. 
Xave probably saw him do just that, as he said: “Nostalgic, are we?” 

 
231 
“It’s not the end it deserves, I’ll try to keep it somewhere else,” Walter 
replied. 
“A proper funeral, maybe?” Xave said, adding a cough that was 
probably meant to be a laughter. 
Walter wasn’t in the mood to fight Xave’s strange attempts at humour. 
He looked at him and smiled, slightly. “Even better, I’ll nail it to the wall 
with a syringe.” 
Xave raised one eyebrow, then his eyeballs rolled a full circle. “You 
almost got me with that,” he said. 
Walter continued throwing now insignificant notes into the trash; at 
this point you could already see the floor again. It felt good to just throw 
things away and get rid of some of the weight. He almost wished he 
could’ve opened one of the windows, but after remembering the weather 
report forecasting a hail of bullets at all times, he embraced that inability 
again. 
“It’s been five days since the death of the teenagers,” Xave said. 
“Ah yes, the fun-loving, experimental, lustful adolescents.” Walter 
kicked a chair aside and picked up a disposable pack of bleached and 
pressed trees. 
“Indeed. So you remember, that’s good! Anyway, I pulled a few strings 
and you can look at them anytime,” Xave said. 
Walter turned around. “Thank you, though I’d rather not leave the-” 
Xave held up a hand. “No need to leave, they’re right here.” 
“Where?” 
“In front of your door.” 
“How…” 
“Well,” Xave said, walking to Walter’s bed and resting his body on it. 
“Today’s their funeral.” 
“That doesn’t explain how they’re able to visit us, them being the dead 
bundle they are.” Walter put aside the trash bin, which, now weighing at 
least twenty kilos, met the floor with a loud thump, sending a few 
surrounding stray papers into the air. 
“Oh yes, it does! They’re here because their families and friends believe 
their bodies were burnt and cast into urns.” 
Walter looked at the ground, thinking. “You tricked them?” 
“No, nobody’s been tricked,” Xave said, unbuttoning his jacket. “A 
small sum of money exchanged owners, a simple act some would call 
economic revitalization.” 
“So, these people aren’t burying their kids’ ashes.” Walter scratched his 
chin. “What are they burying?” 
 
232 
“You’re a scientist, Walter. One of the best, if not the best I have had the 
privilege of knowing. You of all people should know that everything and 
everyone is simply made of stardust. Ash equals ash. Stardust equals 
stardust. Besides, funerals are just an act, a play, a ceremony, a habit, a 
tradition; truth is simply spun into a shiny cloth, wrapping the death of 
people in roses, so their relatives may bid goodbye.” 
“You’re distasteful. You’re the one clouding everyone’s judgment with 
your truths.” 
“Correct! I’m a salesman, not a soul’s man. And yet, my methods aren’t 
harming anyone.” 
“Especially not yourself.” Walter sighed. 
“My, my,” Xave said. “I thought you had a good night’s sleep, and this 
is the Walter I get from letting you replenish your health? Besides, you 
have reasons to celebrate. Did you forget what you invented when you 
fixed Jeremy?” 
Walter decided to evade the brewing argument. “How’s he?” 
“He lost his faith in your invention, but he’s alive and he can re-watch 
all the movies he saw in the past two years, I’d say that’s a neat trade-off.” 
Xave grinned cockily. 
For a moment, Walter had decided to smash Xave’s teeth with the trash 
bin, but his aggression made way for curiosity. “What happens when I 
step outside of this room, will I stumble over a pile of bodies or did you 
arrange them neatly in the shape of a heart?” 
“They’re in a metal box, Walter, I’m not that heartless. You said they 
don’t decay, so there’s no cooling equipment or anything fancy like that. 
Oh yeah, they’re pretty naked.” 
“They’re not separate though, are they?” Something deep down inside 
Walter wanted to see his invention’s deeds as close to reality as possible. 
“No – I made the deal just as the guy in the morgue pulled out his 
chainsaw.” 
Walter caught himself being a part of a discussion he had never 
dreamed of participating and literally stepped back with one foot. “What 
are we to do with them after my inspection?” 
“They’re pretty stiff, might as well put them up against a wall, or use 
them as your personal clothes stand.” 
Walter looked Xave in his eyes and for a split second he was almost 
terrified of the man resting on his bed. 
The salesman shook his head, laughed and swung up from the bed, 
opened the door and ordered both of Walter’s bodyguards to carry the 
metal case inside Walter’s suite. 
 
233 
They dropped the giant shining casket as big as both bodyguards in 
front of Walter’s feet. 
“Have fun,” Xave said, waving his hand. 
“You’re staying here,” Walter insisted. 
“I have more important things to do than watch two teenagers cuddle 
up for eternity,” Xave said. 
“You visited their parents, you might as well look at them yourself.”  
“I already saw them, as I said, they’re naked.” 
“I’m sure you didn’t look at them, though.” Walter opened the two locks 
with distinct clicks. 
Xave nervously looked at the bodyguards and said: “Fine. You can wait 
outside, that saves at least someone from becoming traumatized. And you 
better forget this ever happened!” Both meat shields returned to their 
stations. 
Once the door to Walter’s suite shut tight – Walter opened the cold 
metal door. 
A cloud of vile air attacked Walter’s nose; the stench of urine, feces, and 
weeks-old sweat punched him into the stomach like a foul invisible 
creature. He swallowed hard, his whole body revolting against the simple 
idea to just run away. 
Xave just stood there with his tie held up to his nostrils. 
It was a surreal sight, almost as if an artist had lived through the 
weirdest dream and sculptured a still image out of living flesh. 
Several parts of the male teen were visible, but busted up by the female 
body, as if the girl had entered the boy and left an impression on the boy’s 
back and a hole on the whole front. The head of the girl looked through th e 
shoulder blades, even comfortably so, seemingly asleep and content. The 
boy’s face was normal at the top, but it went apart from the nose down, 
like a zipper, the tongue split up into two lanes, drawing lines over the 
girl’s back; his teeth building milestones along the way. 
Their feet were a tangled mess; a mass of bones with too many toes. 
Their fingers stuck out randomly like arrows that had been shot at their 
meaty hulls from within. Something was curled up along an out of place 
limb sticking out from her knee. Her back was split apart, with his legs 
protruding below; remainders of pieces of arms, elbows and thighs veiled 
everything in a grotesque shadow. 
Walter let the casket lid fall shut with a bang. 
 
234 
 
 
 
“No, mum,” Kristella said into her phone, leaning back in her cheap office 
chair behind the desk filled to the brim with notes about the whole 
INSTANT travelling business: lists and cards of important people, random 
trivia about travelling (for those cases where she was interviewed on the 
phone about how things work), a stack of current newspapers, a heavy 
book about the history of transportation (in case of arguments about 
efficiency or why people fear rapid change) and several glass bottles of 
water, one empty, the others full. 
“Everything’s alright, mum, you can use the INSTANT again now,” she 
said, sighing. “You don’t need a car anymore, I tell you, the few minutes 
you have to walk to the station are good for you anyway, remember?”  
She browsed through the newspapers with a bored face. 
“If you want to visit other places further away you can always borrow a 
car from Rick, he says he’d happily lend you one.” 
Usually, there was at least one article in each newspaper referencing 
Walter’s company in one way or another – travelling instantly had become 
commonplace for some, and there used to be a time when Kristella had 
been surprised by that. 
Now she was just bored, although observant enough to catch any 
irregularities. 
“I’ll probably get more money next month, I haven’t talked to Walter 
yet, we’re running a bit short on cash, as you know.” She distanced herself 
from the speaker as her mother coughed loudly into it, then pressed it 
against her ear again – which was getting hotter by the second. “There are 
plans, yes – no, the company’s not going anywhere. People love to go 
places instantly, hell, you do, that’s saying something.” 
She read a couple of lines, skimmed through freshly printed pages, 
grabbed the next paper and looked at page two. An interview with – 
“Jeremy?” she said aloud in disbelief. 
A few more words later, she told her mother: “I gotta hang up, 
something has come up. I’ll call you later. Love you!” 

 
235 
She grabbed the one page, stormed outside of her suite and called for 
the elevator by punching the button, ignoring the two guards that looked 
past her in a strange authoritarian way. 
Xave should be in the building, prepping up more business talks after 
finally convincing Walter to sign the deal with the production company. 
He should be the first to know, although she had never been completely 
sure of whom to send irritating news first. 
A few minutes later she finally stepped out on floor ten and knocked on 
Xave’s door. 
“Come in, if you dare,” his voice resounded. 
Kristella rolled her eyes and entered the suite. “Jeremy’s leaked our 
new feature.” 
Xave was upside down on his hands, his feet supporting his body 
against a wall, his body naked except for blue underpants with white 
vertical stripes. 
He looked skinny; it caught her by surprise that this was her only 
thought. 
In a swift motion Xave lowered his legs and sprung back into a normal 
standing position, his head red as blood. “Already?” he said, reaching for 
the loose page in Kristella’s hand, quickly snatching it away and 
immediately starting to read. 
“What an ass,” Xave remarked after being half-way through. 
“Why are you in your underpants?” Kristella asked. 
“I would’ve been naked if I had locked the door,” he replied, still 
reading. “I thought he was done with beaming,” he imitated Jeremy’s voice. 
“And now he’s trumpeting his experiences out into the world. But hey, I 
expected as much.” He returned the piece of paper back to Kristella. “It ’s 
always attention that people crave and never fail to succumb to.” He 
turned around and looked for his trousers. 
“Why were you upside down just a minute ago?” Kristella asked. 
“The world looks different that way, it is more apparent to me in that 
state what it needs,” the salesman explained mystically. 
“And?” she pressed on. 
“Right now, it needs us,” he said, stuffing one leg inside his trousers, 
followed by the other. 
“That’s a bit big headed, don’t you think?” 
“A big head is always in good fortune,” Xave sang. “Now – about what 
we’re going to do about Jeremy. We need to make it look like we were 
first, even though we weren’t.” 
 
236 
“You mean, the first ones to release the news about how we’re able to 
let people jump back into their previous bodies?” 
“Right,” he said, put on his shirt, flung his loose tie around his neck 
and started working on the knot. “We need to re-launch our product, re-
define our image, jump-start the overall idea. It’s not just about instant 
travelling anymore, that’s already old.” 
Kristella checked the interview again and started to read aloud. 
“Jeremy, are you saying you lost two years of your life? – My mother 
helped me reconstruct bits and pieces, but it’s all a complete blank for me.” 
She looked at Xave again. 
“What of it?” he asked, bending forward to reach for his shiny black 
boots. 
“People don’t need to be blanked out, do they?” 
“Some do, in fact, I saw something yesterday I could do without 
remembering,” he said, sighing. “We need to turn this around and make it 
work for us. It’s good news! Here’s a device that lets you start again if 
memories keep dragging you down.” 
“We’re therapists now?” 
“Careful, Kristella, your negativity has potential to drag me down.” 
“I’m giving you the outsider’s point of view.” 
Xave swung his jacket on his back and magically ended up with both 
arms in it. “Travelling in and of itself is therapy, we’re already dealing 
with deep psychological aspects of humanity. We all need to be 
somewhere sometimes, and it has to be fast. It’s relieving to know that the

new standard of minimum minutes required. It’s a public service of stress 
relief.” 
“Not according to this,” Kristella pointed out, raising the paper into the 
air. “According to this, we’re monsters, trying to alter the world when it 
doesn’t need altering.” 
“That’s the first sign that it indeed does need altering,” Xave countered, 
buttoning his sleeves and checking his father’s watch in his chest pocket. 
“We’re going into an aggressive stance, we’ll re-launch our transportation 
device on a set date with a slick redesign, a new logo, a new slogan – a new 
name. And we’re adding a specific sound to instant travelling, an audio 
logo, so to speak. I have a few ideas already drawn up and ready to go. 
This interview might hamper our image, but we can build something new 
from its ashes, something that shuts people up about Jeremy and the 
teenagers.” 
 
237 
“Speaking of which, I’ve had people call me and ask about rumours

“It’s a lie, Walter only saw some photos. So did I,” Xave said, looking

She looked at the interview again and paused. Then she said: “Okay, I ’ll

Fully dressed and ready to go, Xave said with conviction: “A week

Four Years Later 
 
The crowd went wild. The air was thick with nicotine smoke and artificial 
fog; it reeked of hundreds of sweaty and drunk people, some of them 
throwing their hair around as if possessed by demons, whipping invisible 
angels. 
Glaring and flickering lights shot at the stage, where three thin and 
proud entertainers played bass, drums and electric guitar, all of them 
summoning the Lord of Hell himself by roaring - to a non-fan - 
indistinguishable lyrics into microphones that were tied to stands, like 
victims about to be sacrificed. 
Arms were moved to the rhythm (if there was one), index finger and 
pinkie extended. The loudness of the chaos erupting from two meter tall 
speakers was drilling holes into unprotected ear drums and massaging 
several hearts with murderous and uncontrolled bass. 
Amidst the mass of hands and heads that repeatedly crashed against 
the stage like an army ramming the front door of a castle, there were four 
isles of peace and calm. Heray was one of them, a package of bulging 
muscle, resisting the throng of bodies like a rock, arms crossed, his long 
hair in a tight, aggressive pony tail. His eyes were like searchlights 
watching prisoners. 
The current song - if you could call it that - changed pace, and a scream 
undermined the wailing guitar, the random notes of the bass and the 
whirlwind of the drummer. The sea of random body parts at the foot of the 
three musicians escalated. Plastic cups were thrown against innocent 
heads, fists met chins, and eager mouths started chanting “encore, encore”, 
even though the music - the noise - hadn’t ended yet. 
Heray sprang to life. With the ease of a child playing with puppets he 
forced his way towards two wranglers that were about to beat each other  
senseless. It looked kind of funny how Heray was just using a single hand 
to move dancing and twitching persons to the side, one after another, as if 
parting the sea. 

 
239 
The raging storm around him didn’t matter in the least as he grabbed 
two stick figures at their collars, dragged them through the crowd to the 
exit and threw them out of the hall into an alley like trash bags. He wanted 
to turn and walk back inside, when he heard one of the bags speak. 
“Do you even know who I am?” 
The slowly closing door fell into lock and it was silent. Heray turned 
around. It was a long motion, a mountain uprooting itself and facing the 
other way. His hair seemed to tighten up around his head, his black 
clothes turned even darker, his arms flexed. 
Two teenagers, probably eighteen or nineteen, one standing, the other 
one lying in the dirt, were looking him straight into his eyes. 
Heray spoke, his voice a distant rolling thunder, deep enough to make 
his appearance ever the more sinister: “No.” 
The standing teenager, daring to raise his finger against Heray, 
involuntarily took a step back, blinked and then opened his mouth again: 
“The guy who’s singing? Yeah, that’s my dad.” 
Heray cracked his knuckles. “Sure. Spit another lie, and you won’t have 
the mouth for more.” 
The walkie-talkie on his belt cracked and hissed, and a colleague talked 
to him. “Heray, you dumbass!” 
Heray took it up to his mouth. “What’s up?” he answered, calm and 
casual. 
“You just kicked the singer’s son out of the concert! I don’t believe it!” 
his superior blared. 
“He was clearly fighting somebody and not following house rules,” 
Heray defended himself. 
“We could’ve used the income tonight, and you blew it,” came the 
response. 
Both teenagers were standing in front of him now, the singer’s son 
playfully pushing the other kid to the side with an evil giggle. 
“I don’t understand,” Heray said, still eyeing the young ones like a 
laser beam cannon, ready to fire. 
“We’ll probably have to use double of tonight’s cash on lawyers just to 
be able to say one word at court! This singer’s a freaking ass, and you 
know it,” his boss yelled, not knowing the son was listening. He probably 
didn’t care either way. 
“I’ll get him back inside,” Heray offered. 
“You do that, but you stay out, you’re fired!” 
 
240 
Heray rolled his eyes and sighed. It was the sigh of a giant, bending 
trees as he exhaled. He crushed the walkie-talkie into pieces in his balled 
up fist and let the parts fall out of his hand like a shredded butterfly. 
“Haha, you lost your job,” the son of the screamer proclaimed and 
broke into a little dance. 
Heray hit the teenager’s face with his fist; he wasn’t using full force, just 
a tap that sent the young man against the wall. 
The other teenager ran away, stumbling over several trashcans and his 
own feet. 
It wasn’t the first job Heray had lost, and not even the first to lose over 
something so trivial. He wasn’t sad or disappointed; he had just lost one 
more bit of faith in humanity, but there was a lot left of it. 
He disregarded the crying boy and walked out of the alley. He decided 
to go home and take a nap. The world would offer him another chance as 
soon as he woke up. 
It was a warm summer night; people were strolling past him. There was 
an INSTANT around the corner that would lead him straight home. The 
street he was walking on had been turned into a pedestrian area since a lot 
of people had sold their cars in the past few years, with the INSTANT 
center being built right in the middle of the pavement, as if trying to put a 
stop to traffic. 
He remembered long lines the first few months, but not any more. You 
could pretty much use a booth, well, instantly. No more waiting. He 
admired the company for what they had done. The air smelled cleaner - 
even if it was just imagination - and many destinations weren’t out of 
reach any longer, neither geographically nor financially. You could be 
everywhere, right now.  
Well, not exactly everywhere, but as far as Heray knew they were 
working on that. 
He entered the INSTANT building. It was almost like a temple, with 
mighty pillars outside and a church-like interior. There were high, white 
walls, decorated with black tubes that went upwards, met at the center of 
the ceiling, led back down to spread apart again and connect to dozens of 
beaming booths standing in a majestic circle. 
People were arriving and leaving constantly, even at night. 
There were guards, jokingly called Instances or The Blues. They were 
just making sure nobody would try anything funny. No other work 
required. Just standing there, being not more than security cameras with a 
pulse. 
 
241 
“Good evening,” one of the Instances said, a young woman with a 
charming smile. 
“Ma’am,” Heray said and entered a booth. 
To his luck, their redesign had made it possible for him to use instant 
travelling as well. He was a bear of a man and had to squeeze into the 
booths prior to the rebuilding; something he had not been fond of.  
Now, it was comfortable. He could even turn around and stand 
normally. 
Heray entered his destination on a user friendly on-screen keyboard, 
stepped inside, let the lid close automatically, watched as the computer 
made sense of his words and - disappeared. 
As soon as his body had rematerialized, he coughed three times and 
stumbled out of the booth. He inhaled deeply, craving fresh air as if he 
hadn’t breathed in a year. 
This was new to Heray. 
And this also wasn’t the location he had typed into that damn 
computer. 
It wasn’t even an INSTANT location. He was inside a room. 
He craned his neck left and right, setting his bones straight, and started 
making sense of his surroundings. 
There were two long tables set against the wall, with a dozen monitors 
standing atop and a handful of keyboards and mice in front of the bright 
displays. 
There were three empty chairs, cupboards filled with files and loose 
papers, and a large window. 
Heray stepped closer and glanced outside into the night. 
“Wow,” he said as he realized he was high above the ground, the city - 
a city - lying there, filled with a few small random lights of different 
purposes. 
A door opened behind him and two INSTANT guards walked in, 
talking casually until they saw the mountain of a man standing where one 
wouldn’t expect. 
The left one with broad shoulders drew his black stick and yelled: 
“Intruder!” 
He charged at Heray, who - even though the surprise had petrified him 
for a split second - evaded and let the guard’s head meet the window, 
which to Heray’s surprise didn’t break. 
The second guard had already jumped in the air to try and tackle Heray 
from behind by climbing his back. 
 
242 
There was a grotesque image of Heray having a blue monkey around 
his neck for no apparent reason. Heray used one hand to get him off and 
let gravity deal with the rest by drawing him to the ground. 
“What is going on he-” another person marching into the room said. He 
was wearing blue as well, but regular pants and no stupid hat. 
“Are you in charge here?” Heray asked. 
The man looked flabbergasted and stood still. “Did you just take out 
my two guards?” 
“Guards?” Heray asked in disbelief. “You must be joking.” 
“I’m calling the police,” the man said and grabbed a receiver. 
“I just lost my job, stepped into an INSTANT booth and it spat me out

conjuring dark clouds on the ceiling. 
“What do you mean? Nobody can access this booth except me and 
those who are authorized.” 
Heray raised his arms. “I’m telling the truth, man, I entered this room 
and those guys attacked me for no reason.” 
“Well, you aren’t supposed to be here, that might be reason enough for

sure what to do. “What’s your name?” 
“Heray Peer.” 
“Let me check the logs and see if you’re right.” The man replaced the 
receiver, checked screens and entered a couple dozen phrases Heray 
couldn’t make out from his corner. The two guards were still lying on the 
ground, unconscious for whatever reason. 
The man stood upright again and sighed. “You just caught something 
Walter would call a nasty bug.” 
“Excuse me?” Heray asked. 
“You encountered a new error.” 
“Is that good?” Heray was unsure where the situation was headed. 
“I’m wondering why it hasn’t happened earlier,” the man said. Then he

headquarters.” 
Heray shook his hand instinctively. “Uh,” he coughed. “It’s nice to 
meet you, I suppose.” He scratched his head where the pony tail 
originated. “Another thing I should probably mention,” Heray added. “I 
had a really nasty cough when I appeared here and I was struggling for

Rick nodded. “Definitely not normal.” 
Heray looked around. “I should return home.” 
 
243 
“Wait,” Rick said. “You mentioned that you just lost a job.” 
“That’s right,” Heray admitted.

“I see,” Rick said and looked at the two guards on the ground. “Looks

Thin fingers buried their nails deep into the black, soft wall of earth.  
Leonard’s face was strained to a grimace, loose long hair dangling into 
his view. He had probably been able to construct something better to keep 
his food stashed away and cooled at the same time, but this seemed like 
the one idea that wouldn’t arouse suspicion of anyone trying to steal from 
him. 
He was kneeling on the stone floor of the cellar with a lonely candle 
keeping him company in the dark, its glimmer shyly dancing on the red 
stone walls, up towards the curved ceiling. 
Leonard’s claws struck silver; with great effort he dragged the cooling 
bag out of the earth, wiped off the dirt with three loud strokes across the 
shimmering surface and opened it. 
There were three bottles of water, canned meat and vegetables, 
wrapped candy and chocolate bars. 
It was Leonard’s emergency stash. He’d been hungry for two full days; 
it was time. 
He closed the hole in the wall as quickly as possible with the dirt on the 
ground, and the end result looked consequently rushed. His mother had 
taught him to hide food underground, a technique useful during the world 
war, to keep food out of the hungry hands of trespassing soldiers. But she 
wouldn’t have been happy to know about his current state. 
Once he stepped out of the cellar into the small room - one of the four 
rooms of the small house - he ripped open the canned meat using the pull-
tab with his black fingernails, grabbed a piece and took a greedy bite. He 
tore open the vegetables, swallowed their insides, gnawed on tiny 
conserved carrots and washed it down with still and cool water out of a 
plastic bottle that cracked in Leonard’s grip. The water tasted funny, but he 
didn’t care; it was better than the water of the nearby river. 

 
245 
One can only eat so much meat without bread before feeling sick, and 
the thief’s mind warned him just in time. He stowed everything back in the 
bag - which he’d actually bought in a supermarket four years earlier with 
Reklur’s money - and sat down in the kitchen on an old wooden chair. 
The kitchen was empty. Every cupboard, the table at the wall, the 
worktop - everything was uninhabited, forgotten, left alone. Leonard felt 
right at home. 
The sun high up in the cloudy sky was making admirable attempts to 
cheer Leonard up through the broken window. Even the rain that fell five 
minutes later could neither wash away his gloom nor pour money into his 
soiled hands. 
Hands that shone with animal fat, fingers that were covered with layers 
of filth like gloves, long nails that sometimes bled under the cracks. 
Leonard formed two fists without strength - and stood up, taking the 
bag with him outside. 
The house, a mass of stone that somehow resembled a reminder of  
civilization, was standing on top of a hill, surrounded by a dozen trees - 
yet not enough to call it a forest. There were three towns in three different 
directions. Across the four years, Leonard had taken great care not to make 
a big fuss in any of them. Only once a week, he would appear in one of the 
locations, cycling through various less crowded areas. 
Before stealing, he would always pay attention to the possible reactions 
of his deeds. You don’t steal money from a woman that can hardly take 
care of her two kids. You’ve got to target the ones that load up their 
shopping carts to the rim; those that barely shrug when they realize 
something’s missing. Leonard wouldn’t have made it to forty-three 
without knowing how and when to keep a low profile. 
Once again he looked at his fingernails. At this rate he’d have to bite 
them to survive. Whatever he was making, it definitely wasn’t a living. 
St. Frank was lying calmly to the south of the hill. Leonard followed a 
beaten path down the grassy slope, the bag hitting his right leg on the way 
down. He passed the heritage-protected sacred sanctuary, a building out of 
the 14th century, almost as tall as the house he lived in, and almost as 
curvy in its supposedly sturdy outline. From here on out it was flat land 
with fields of sunflowers that Leonard traversed. He would take a few sips 
of water every twenty steps or so to keep himself going, watching the sun 
lower itself down to the ground behind heavy clouds. The village drew 
closer, and when Leonard finally reached it, he stopped beneath two 
intertwined trees to take a breather. 
 
246 
The shadows stretched out on the ground like grotesque stick figures as 
the sun beamed her last smile for the day. People were passing Leonard, 
who was wearing wet jeans and a sticky brown t-shirt. He hoped nobody 
would notice his hands, which he hadn’t cleaned up to save his precious 
water and the next river was the opposite direction. He would ’ve been the 
perfect villager, if not for his hands, face and soaked hair. 
He quickly recalled the last few times he had been here and went 
through possible locations for today. The town hall, the rehabilitation 
center, the main square - if you could call it that - the railroad bridge, the 
two supermarkets, the post office, any of the residential places. 
Maybe he would just walk down the main street leading through St. 
Frank today and wait for the right situations to arise. 
In that moment he noticed a suspicious amount of people walking 
towards the main square, which was basically a statue and pretty stone s 
forming a ground to walk on, with elegant green benches at the sides.  
Or so Leonard remembered. 
He followed a group of primary school children and their two teachers 
to the heart of St. Frank. 
Instead of a statue, there was a building now, almost appearing to be a 
shrine, with graceful pillars surrounding it and carrying a pointy roof. It 
was huge compared to the houses nearby, using up most of the space.  
Leonard noticed the festive decorations thrown across the street lamps, 
and men in suits and women in fancy dresses idling near the entrance of 
the new facility that looked so out of place. Above the black opening that 
led inside, Leonard saw a rectangular shape, veiled in a blue, silky cloth.  
There was a podium, complete with a microphone and a tiny plastic 
bottle of mineral water, waiting for someone or something. A curious and 
murmuring crowd gathered in front of it, until finally a person appeared 
out of the corner of Leonard’s eye, approaching the pedestal; a man in an 
expensive, tight suit, with his tie waving in the wind, and his heavy, 
confident steps echoing off the nearby house fronts. He climbed behind the 
microphone, tapped it, opened the bottle to drink quickly, and, while 
leaning forward and resting his upper body with both hands on the 
podium, began his speech. 
Leonard folded his arms and listened from across the street in the 
shadow of a weeping willow. 
“My name,” the man with the wild tie announced, his voice booming 
through invisible speakers, “is Xave Stern, I am the co-founder of the 
INSTANT company and I’m here today, well, for obvious reasons.” He 
waved a hand and hinted at the monstrosity behind him. 
 
247 
Someone laughed. 
“I’m proud to be standing here today, to be able to improve lives, to 
reach out to more individuals than ever before, to connect even more 
people that long to be closer to each other, or take on a job far away, or get 
inspired to visit places that they have never even dared to dream of 
visiting.” 
A dramatic pause followed. The crowd shifted weights impatiently.  
“This,” Xave Stern continued, “is an opportunity I love to share with all 
of you. I am, of course, talking about INSTANT travelling.” 
With that, the cloth magically fell to the ground, revealing the company 
logo in big white letters, shining brightly in the falling dusk. Xave Stern 
stepped aside, two INSTANT guards appeared out of the building’s 
entrance and gestured at the applauding people to come and try out the 
new attraction in town. 
Leonard wiped a finger across his nose. The future had caught up with 
him. 
 
248 
 
 
 
It wasn’t Friday, yet Xave was standing in front of his parents’ grave, one 
hand in the pocket of his black trousers, the other holding his father’s 
watch. Its chain dangled between Xave’s fingers, playfully nudged by the 
wind, and its hands had stopped showing the current time. 
His Friday tradition had shifted since his and Walter’s ever growing 
level of paranoia had exceeded a certain limit. Unlike Walter, Xave hadn ’t 
gone entirely crazy yet, but he was still afraid that people – the wrong kind 
– would recognize him and make him pay. 
For what? 
Even chief inspector Reklur couldn’t answer that. There had been hints 
of envy of several big players in transportation, but nobody had made an 
actual public appearance or statement of any kind regarding INSTANT ’s 
disrupting success. 
Sure, some higher-ups had mocked the very idea, but ultimately 
stopped doing so when it had made them look close-minded in the eyes of 
the public. 
Also, there had been no more shootings or other assassination attempts 
since that last time. 
But frankly, that had made it even worse. You can’t fight the unknown, 
you can just try to ignore it or hide as best as you can. 
“What do you think, father? Am I just talking to myself again? Maybe I 
shouldn’t bother coming here anymore. You’re long gone anyway, right?” 
Xave said to the roses living off his parents’ decaying remains. “Yeah. I’m 
sorry too.” 
He exited the cemetery and went back to his car. 
“Hey,” a female voice said from behind Xave. 
He turned around. A woman, roughly forty years old, was approaching 
him. 
“Hey, I know you,” she said, the white plastic bag in her left hand 
hissing along with every step. “You’re the guy from the INSTANT firm, 
right?” 

 
249 
Xave tensed up, but tried to play it down. “Yes, the name is Xave Stern. 
And you are?” He extended a hand – which got ignored. 
“You know, your little invention might have brought people happiness, 
but to me it seems you don’t actually trust your fancy new transportation 
system,” she spouted, pointing at Xave’s car. 
“Now,” Xave said, raising both arms as if held at gunpoint. “INSTANT 
travelling is the safest way to get from point A to point B, and we have 
statistics to back up my claim.” 
“You probably asked ten people,” she scoffed. 
“We didn’t ask anyone, we compared our failure rate to all the other 
means of transportation, and we clearly are the safest solution,” he 
explained, now standing akimbo. 
“Why don’t you use it? There’s an INSTANT going off fifteen minutes 
from here.” 
The truth burnt inside Xave like a demon; he swallowed as hard as he 
could to extinguish it. “I have to visit areas that haven’t been connected to 
our INSTANT network, I’m a salesman, and this is my living room on 
wheels.” 
The women nodded slowly in disbelief, rolled her eyes and left.  
Xave wanted to say more, but instead he just got into his car and drove 
to the INSTANT headquarters.

Walter woke up to the sound of his own screaming. He had seen darkness 
lurking outside his windows, something had been approaching, he had felt 
it and tried to shoot it through the bulletproof glass with arrows. There 
had been his mother, whom he had tried to save. All he had been able to

“Walter, are you there?” Xave’s damp voice was heard behind the door,

Kristella stepped out of the booth. 
She was standing next to a cliff, the waves of the sea breaking against it, 
the wind blowing through her hair and bending the grass to the sides of 
the beaten path. 
The booth was surrounded by several other tourists, some of them 
attending the merchant stands a few meters away from the main attraction, 
called Cabo da Roca. 
Sayve appeared in the booth and joined Kristella, embracing her on

“It’s beautiful!” Kristella exclaimed. 
Sayve sighed. “I’ve got to return to work.”

He bowed down to her face and kissed her cheek. 
They’d been together for almost three months. He was a nice guy,

254 
The INSTANT there was a small tent with a lonely transportation 
device in it and a thick cable leading off from behind it far into the 
mainland. People were constantly arriving and leaving. Thankfully, the 
sound it made was very quiet, otherwise it would have disturbed the 
peaceful place. 
After waiting a minute, it was Sayve’s turn to enter the booth. He typed

She stood up, opened a small notebook in her right hand and checked

Sayve was a great guy, no question about it. But was love meant to be

Hundreds of people were seemingly aimlessly walking around.

She remembered the discussions three years ago that had made it

How ironic was it that the idea had come from her head? A man

There had been a bunch more like these, and some funny ones. The

Now there were so many people using and used to instant travelling, it

they are and remain perfectly content. I imagine there will be a lot mor e 
commuters than ever before, but the meaning of that word will be 
outdated soon. You just go to work; where you go to work doesn ’t matter 
anymore.” She paused and looked at passing people outside the café’s 
windows. “And personally, I think that’s beautiful. It’s liberating.” 
 
258 
“It’s scary,” Sayve admitted. 
“How old are you? Eighty? Are you saying you can’t handle change? 
Why can’t you see the positive side of things? Don’t you see that it’s 
exciting, a landmark in history, and you’re there to experience it?” 
Sayve grimaced. “All I’m saying is, you’re changing the world, and you 
don’t see it from the perspective of the common people.” 
“The common people?” Kristella raised her voice. “Which category do I

Sayve shuffled his feet under the table. “You’re a celebrity.” 
Wonderful, Kristella thought. 
“You’re jealous,” she stated. “Is that it? Do you want to trade places?” 
“No.” 
“And for your information, I’m not a celebrity, I’m a spokesperson for a 
successful company. I’m a face people can refer to when talking about 
instant travelling.” 
“People regularly recognize you on the streets,” Sayve noted. 
“And then what? Do I give autographs? Do I pose for a group shot, 
leaning forward and showing my cleavage for fan service?” 
Sayve seemed to try to remember. “I don’t know, I guess not. You do 
get letters and postcards as well, though.” 
“Including death threats, right,” Kristella shot back. She noticed other 
guests looking at them and consciously tried to wind down a bit by 
swallowing the last sip of orange juice. 
For a moment she imagined herself sitting in the same café thirty years 
from now, having the same discussion with an old and bitter version of 
Sayve. 
“Screw this,” she said, grabbed her backpack, pulled a fifty Schilling 
note out of her right jeans pocket, threw it in front of a blankly staring

Walter’s phone rang. Again. Again and again. 
The scientist exhaled, a cloud of smoke riding along with his breath. He 
was lying in bed, fully clothed in black jeans, white shirt and dirty  gray 
socks, that had probably been white a long time ago. 
He got up. The phone was too persistent. Better to answer it than trying 
to think while it continued to annoy him. 
“Yes,” Walter said into the plastic receiver with the cord dangling from 
it, his right hand killing the cigarette amidst black spots on the wooden 
table. 
“Walter,” the new assistant Samir Zewail said. “Wireless travelling!”  
“Yes,” Walter repeated and rubbed his eyes. “Yes, yes.” 
“It works!” the voice on the other end of the receiver said with a thick 
accent. 
“How?” Walter asked, bored. Bored of everything. 
“It’s easy,” Samir continued enthusiastically. 
“You said that the last dozen times.” 
“But it really is! Listen to me,” Samir began explaining. “We’re already 
using molecules to rebuild the body they belonged to. But – we can use the 
same principle to use said molecules on a different template!” 
“Not all bodies are made of the exact same amount of molecules. Not 
even clones are. It doesn’t work that way,” Walter said, with almost no 
alteration in the tone of his voice, like a bagpipe. 
“But what if,” Samir made a dramatic pause. Walter yawned. “What if 
we had a large pool of available molecules?” Samir asked triumphantly.  
Walter rolled his eyes. “We don’t have a large pool of molecules, unless 
we throw a bunch of people in a blender.” 
Walter’s eyes opened up. That was it. That was what Samir was leading 
up to. A pool of molecules. 
“Exactly,” Samir said. “Nonono, I don’t mean to kill people, but what if 
we use bodies that are already dead?” 

 
260 
Walter stood up and retraced his beard towards his chin with his left

“Awesome, right?” Samir rejoiced. “Is that the correct word to use

“Unethical,” Walter corrected him and sat back down. “Nobody will 
allow us to do that.” 
“Think of it like organ donors,” Samir said. “People could always just 
give their bodies away for the greater good.” 
Walter looked at the night sky outside his window. Why did he even 
care? His work was already done. The world was already in peril because 
of him. 
“Here’s the best part,” Samir concluded. “Once you send a person 
away, the body will be stored in the local pool. So, in theory once you

never need to be replenished.” 
“What about clothes and other non-organic objects? Say, people with

“A shared pool,” Samir evaded Walter’s shot with the enthusiasm of a 
child in front of a toy store. “If any molecules are missing, some pools will 
be connected and draw in needed molecules.” Samir shrugged. “If no 
matching molecules are found, just display an error message before 
sending the man with the backpack.” 
Walter gave in. “That’s a great idea, Samir,” he congratulated his

anyway?” 
“Oh, yes, I’m just too excited, you know how it is.” 
Walter looked around. Everything around him screamed pathetic. “I

and kids. Go back to Alexandria. Tomorrow is another day.” 
“Alright, thank you, Walter. And thanks for believing in me.” 
Kristella suddenly barged into Walter’s room. She had tears in her eyes. 
“Be safe on your travel back home, Samir,” Walter said and hung up. 
He didn’t know what he should be embarrassed for first. Luckily, most 
of his suite was shrouded in mystery, only the small lamp next to the bed 
dared to break through the darkness, and light from the hallway shone i n 
from behind the shivering woman. 
“Kristella?” Walter asked in disbelief. 
He hadn’t seen her in a long time. Months that had turned into years. 
How long had he been leading this reclusive life? 
She ran towards him and - 
 
261 
Walter almost died as she put her arms around him, squeezed the air 
out of his lungs, and dug her wet face into his right shoulder. She began to 
sob, then she cried loudly. 
He couldn’t move, he didn’t dare to move. The last time a woman had 
come close to him was… a century ago. In another life altogether, it 
seemed. He just stood there like a statue. 
The situation was almost too awkward. The last shower had been too 
far back, the place was a mess. At least he wore something. 
Kristella calmed down, let go of the scientist and wiped her face a cross 
her left hand. “I’m sorry,” she whispered. 
Walter watched Kristella slowly come to her senses and realize her 
surroundings. 
“You… look like crap,” Kristella said with a sweet undertone, still on 
the brink of crying. She took a look around. “Your room looks like crap.” 
Then she looked at him with a sudden firm expression, took his hand, 
dragged him to the bathroom at the other end of the suite, turned on the 
lights inside, left Walter standing there and shut the door. 
“You’re not coming out before I can see your face again and you smell 
like roses,” Kristella said through the closed door. 
Walter blinked at his own reflection in the mirror, while Kristella 
started to move around furniture and apparently throw stuff around in his 
suite. 
“What are you doing?” Walter asked in a loud voice. There was no 
answer. 
He undressed and began to shave.

Rick parked his Ford near the INSTANT station in Monoura. He, his wife 
and his son got out, Rick locked up, and they gathered together in front of 
the hood of the car. 
They could have walked instead, but Roanne usually drove to work as 
she wasn’t very keen on travelling, so she would take the car later. 
It was 7 a.m., some people looked like zombies as they approached the 
station. There were a bunch of school kids too, with bags on their backs 
almost as big as their whole bodies and displaying way too many colors.  
“Alright, son,” Rick said, offering Bernand his hand. “Let’s get you 
your bracelet.” 
It had been Bernand’s greatest wish since half a year ago when the 
Trimond company had announced that it was safe for kids to “save 
themselves” and apply for the Time Machine service. Most parents of 
Bernand’s friends had been on the fence about it, so he was still among the 
first. And it gave Rick the chance to create a memorable fourteenth 
birthday for his son. 
It was also free for company employees - a nice bonus. 
Rick regretted not taking the day off, but somebody had been 
vandalizing INSTANT equipment all over Europe, and the other two 
colleagues that he had trained weren’t capable of dealing with the 
situation. His wife had to go to work as well, and Bernand wasn’t 
supposed to stay away from school, so there wasn’t really a lot of incentive 
anyway. Besides, Bernand would have a field day in school with his new 
bracelet, he’d be the talk of the day in his classroom. 
And Rick knew that Bernand enjoyed attention far too much. 
The little family approached the INSTANT station. It all had begun 
here, and where four years ago there had been a little tent, there was now a 
small shrine, sheltering a dozen booths, sending happy travellers up to five 
thousand kilometers away into all directions in mere seconds. 

 
263 
Bernand was visibly excited. They had had a little fight the day before 
over something trivial, but it was all forgotten now. His eyes glowed, he 
walked with a spring in his step and he fired questions at Rick and 
Roanne. 
“Can I be fourteen forever now, dad?” 
 “Yes, and no,” Rick said, amused. “It’s like saving a game on your 
Nintendo. You can always come back to it, but you won’t have any 
memories of what you did after saving.” 
“It’s called PlayStation,” Bernand corrected him. “Will it hurt, though?” 
“No,” Rick said. “It’s just like travelling. But instead of coming out 
somewhere else, you stay where you are. You go in, there’ll be a flash of 
light, and that’s it.” 
Bernand had been using it for the past year to get to school. It was 
much cheaper and naturally quicker than the train and bus he usually had 
to take. 
“Do I have to wear the bracelet all the time from now on?” Bernand 
continued. 
“Yes,” Rick said. “You should. You get a new saved state of yourself 
every time you use it from now on.” 
“Is it all stored on the bracelet?” 
“Yup.” 
“Would it fit on my memory card, too?” 
Rick laughed. “No, it’s actually being saved in several strings of DNA, 
it’s like a very long staircase with stuff placed on it. The constellation of 
your molecules is a lot of information.” 
Rick caught himself almost sounding like Walter. How had he 
transformed from a mechanic into somebody who worked with computers 
most of the time? 
“What if I lose it? Can somebody else become me?” Bernand’s mouth 
opened with awe. 
“No,” Rick said. “Don’t worry about it.” 
Roanne sighed, she was nervous. 
“It’s going to be fine,” Rick assured her. There had been a long and 
thorough discussion about Bernand’s birthday gift, but Bernand himself 
had won the argument. 
As usual, Rick thought. 
They were standing in line now. Rick knew better than to take 
advantage of his job and cause unrest among the travellers. When it was 
their turn, Hannah greeted all three of them. 
“Let’s get him his bracelet, Hannah,” Rick said to the woman in blue.  
 
264 
“Oh, right,” she said. “I remember you telling me about it. Here we go,

Bernand stepped inside the booth, and Hannah pushed a button. The 
ring on the bottom of the booth turned bright blue and a ringing sound 
was heard three times. It had become a sort of tradition that bystanders

Bernand was looking at him with a bright mood. 
Bernand was anxious, but extremely happy, having a broad grin on his

Rick smiled at him and nudged Roanne. She couldn’t help but smile in 
return. 
There were still people arriving through the other eleven booths, but 
everyone else stood still. 
“Are you ready?” Hannah asked Bernand. 
He nodded twice and gave her the thumbs up. 
Hannah pushed a button and a light show began. There were four 
lamps that had been placed on the ceiling just for occasions like this, and 
they were shining on Bernand now. The lid closed automatically and 
Bernand looked upwards and downwards, checking his feet. 
The blue circle below him turned brighter and brighter until it 
appeared white, the sound of the turbines got louder and louder - and

Somebody started to applaud and someone else whistled loudly, but

The lid of the booth opened, and the bracelet appeared inside a square 
hole in the console in front of Hannah. She had been ready to take it and 
put it around Bernand’s arm, but instead she looked at it as if it were a 
materialized ghost. 
Roanne slowly turned her head to her husband, frightening him with 
her pale face.

Xave was fed up. Every time, every day, he had to get up, ring up

Leonard woke up when the siren of a police car blared through his 
nightmares. 
He had seen a white and orange cat, looking for food, finally finding a 
tarantula and as soon as the cat let its teeth sink into the insect, it had 
resulted in a ghastly cracking and tearing sound, like plucking grass from 
the ground. 
It was early in the morning, seeing as the paper container he had slept 
behind was still covered with morning dew. Fog crawled through the 
alley, seemingly searching for survivors of the night. 
The noise of the wailing police car disappeared in the distance and 
Leonard stood up on wobbly legs. 
It wasn’t cold; the thief was just weak, not having eaten properly in 
days. His emergency stash was gone. And paranoia had finally gott en to 
him. He wasn’t a thief any longer. He was…  
Nothing, he realized. 
Sometimes he would recall the burning on his cheek where Reklur had 
hit him. His eyes seemed everywhere, following his every step. 
He had been indifferent to his illegal deeds, though, right? 
I’m a trout, Leonard remembered. I’m unworthy of being chased. 
And yet here he was, stunned and unable to act out his favourite 
forbidden dance of following people, swimming along with them, 
foreseeing their slightest movements, finding treasures at the bottom of 
their pockets, retreating with a bow. 
In the past four years he had stolen less and less directly off living 
bodies. More often he would simply rob stores, find a rare unlocked car in 
the night with candy inside, visit homeless shelters when able to ignore the 
humiliation, or even rummage in the trash of the cities when the night 
veiled him in his familiar shadow. 
Not today… 
Leonard made plans to do something about his sorry ass. There had to 
be a way to get back on top of things. 

 
269 
He dusted himself off and walked out of the alley. A handful of people 
were looking at him and then retreating their gazes as if they hadn’t seen 
him. Leonard ignored them. He promised himself that this would be the 
day things changed. Maybe he could actually work in the homeless shelter. 
Surely there was something he could do. 
He crossed the street, and the last things he heard were screeching tires 
and a cursing driver. 
 
He woke up when his head hit the window inside of a car. His hair left 
a greasy smudge right in the reflection of his face. 
“Leonard, you’re awake!” the driver cheered, looking at Leonard on the 
backseat through the rear-view mirror.

passenger seat. “Hello,” she greeted him with her usual expression-free 
face. 
She looked a little older, Leonard realized through his blurry vision. 
Maybe twenty-five. Bellward still was the same living cupboard he 
remembered, his left arm resting on the door, his short curly hair dancing 
in the airstream flowing through the open window. 
“How…” Leonard began. He looked around and made sense of his 
surroundings: a car with four doors, a pretty spacious trunk, black fabric 
covering all five seats, and a futuristic dashboard. 
“Did you hit me with your car?” Leonard asked, touching the back of 
his head. 
“Yes,” Bellward admitted. “It seemed reasonable back then. You know, 
hitting people in the face is kind of my thing.” 
Marla coughed. 
“I’m sorry,” Bellward said. “I didn’t see you, and I’m sure you didn’t 
see me either.” 
Marla said in her quiet voice without turning around: “He saw you, but 
instead of hitting the brakes, he spit out a vile word that is still hanging in 
the air. Oh, and then he did jump on the brakes when he recognized you. It 
was a little too late.” 
“Well observed,” Bellward said. “Let’s hope nobody calls you to the

270

And a strong headache.

had cared for her? Had her ex lowered her expectations in men that much?  
She ran aimlessly through the streets for an hour, gradually slowing 
down to a normal walk. She noticed an old church, kept intact neatly, 
surrounded by modern buildings that seemed to turn their backs on the 
church. 
She thought of talking to a certain someone up there, but even that was 
a man, apparently, so why bother. 
A few minutes later she strolled by a group of children, led by one 
grown-up teacher and trailed by another at the end. They looked and 
smiled at her. “Hello,” one boy yelled. 
Were they recognizing her? Kristella hadn’t been on the news for a 
while, delegating that job to Calem. 
Kristella’s cheeks still had dried tears on them, yet she smiled. “Hello,” 
she said back. 
Then she stumbled; once again a slimy fluid forced itself out of her 
mouth onto the ground. The floor was spinning and she felt like she was 
falling off the Earth. 
As the kids screamed and yelled, Kristella hit the pavement and passed 
out.

A few minutes later, Xave was sitting in his car, listening to the radio. A 
nostalgic song played, embracing Xave and filling his head with thoughts 
from his past. It was afternoon; the streets were almost empty in the city. 
Rush hour would occur soon, but it wasn’t that big of a deal anymore. 
Once the high buildings were behind him and grass and trees

“Kristella Attenburg, main spokesperson of the Trimond company, was 
found unconscious today and is now in an undisclosed hospital,

Xave turned it off and opened the window to blast his face with fresh 
air. 
This is a serious allegation, travelling isn’t killing anyone, Xave thought. 
Sure, early adopters might show some side effects, but what’s a little cough? 
Jeremy had been a special case, but Kristella? No. Walter had assured 
him that things worked. 
Xave sighed. 
Of course, that wasn’t right; Walter had told him at every corner of this 
journey that things hadn’t been ready. All that suffering happened because

On the top of a hill, in the middle of a forest, Xave kicked the brakes 
and brought his beloved car to a halt at the side of the road. The car’s tires 
screeched like a whinnying horse. 
Xave pulled up the handbrake with too much muscle, jumped out of his 
car and shut the door behind him so hard, its windows vibrated.  
The forest was deep. As he took twenty steps into the woods, he could 
hear the leaves of the trees rustling and birds singing in the distance.  
The idyllic scenery hung on Xave’s shoulders, and as the seconds 
passed, the rustling became the whispers of a thousand souls, chanting 
about him being responsible for having sent them to hell. 
Xave took out his gun and fired six bullets into a tree.

deformed trunk.

consciously, the burnt smell filling his lungs.

Leonard awoke to a hammering noise and people cheering. 
He stood up from his bed and brushed his hair back with his hands; he 
had been given a room by the townspeople, right after they had eaten.  
It was a strange feeling: There was actual food in his stomach – a huge 
difference compared to the candy bars Bellward had offered him during 
the trip – and he had slept comfortably. Again, it was like waking up from 
a nightmare. Only to realize that reality might turn out even worse.  
He approached the only window and pushed aside the curtains. Right 
in front of Leonard there were parts of a dismantled cable in a puddle of 
dark and hardened earth-coloured ooze. Right where Leonard had 
remembered the tall steel tower was a crowd of people and several 
beheaded black INSTANT snakes, spewing the same liquid onto a heap of 
metal junk. 
Standing right in the middle of the mayhem, Bellward held the giant 
circular tool into the air. 
The crowd loudly approved of his victory gesture. 
Leonard recognized Marla standing to the sides, her arms crossed and 
her head lowered. She seemed distant and worried. 
With a sigh, Leonard closed the curtains and turned around. He took 
off his clothes and stepped into the shower. It took him almost half an hour 
of submitting to hot water to feel clean for the first time in years. He felt 
reborn. He felt relaxed. 
But nevertheless, something wasn’t right. He had stumbled upon 
Bellward and Marla, both following an eerie purpose and telling Leonard 
to stay right in the middle of a war of technological beliefs. He still 
remembered the first time he had heard people mention instant travelling 
when riding the train. There had been progress in the air, a hint of 
excitement and fear. 
He put on his old ragged clothes. Hanging them near the window 
during the night hadn’t helped much to get rid of the smell. But for once 
his body wasn’t drenched in his own dried sweat.

The slippery brain started to crawl towards Kristella. It was talking in a 
hundred different voices, using words that didn’t make any sense in the 
order they were uttered. 
As Kristella started to run, the brain began to chase her by taking on 
shapes of persons Kristella had met in her life. Every step it flickered and

But then the brain also shuffled through objects, it was funny, yet
