doorstep, all I have to do is let them in.” 
“Perfect,” Xave said. “Third, announce that we’re working on 
improving our technology, we’re rebuilding, we’re improving; and we’ll 
release a new product soon: Mass Transportation.” 
“Didn’t we just have a relaunch?” Calem asked. 
“Nevermind that, we need to be loud about our will to better ourselves, 
we’re not stationary, we’re always moving. That new product should get 
people excited.” 
“I reckon at the moment people are more excited to see their loved ones 
again in the next few weeks,” Calem said sarcastically. 
“Exactly, they can’t wait for us to be back up again,” Xave said with 
excitement. “Now that we’re gone, they’ll cherish us even more. People 
always miss the things they don’t have. And when they realize that we 
have even more to offer as soon as instant travelling works again - well, 
who can say no to that.” 
“They’ll kill me with questions about the missing persons,” Calem 
complained. 
“I know,” Xave said. “I’m in a meeting with the mayors right now, they 
all agree that it’s best to let the police handle those.” 
“You’re meeting with the mayors?” 
“Yes, I told them I had to call you right away. I’m using the phone 
they’ve installed in this room.” 
Calem heard someone starting a chant. She looked and saw Heray 
shaking his head at the yelling person outside; a familiar face. “What was 
the guy’s name who lost his memory because he was the first beamed 
man?” 
“Uh, Jeremy, why?” Xave asked. 
“He’s here too, leading some kind of choir of hysteria,” Calem 
described the scene in front of the building. The glass door creaked from 
the people leaning against it. “We’ve already called the police again ten 
minutes ago, they should be here soon. I don’t know why they even bother 
to leave.” 
“Alright,” Xave said distractedly. “The inspector is here, talk to you 
later.” 
They said their goodbyes and hung up. 
 
 
310 
Inspector Reklur entered the meeting room first, wearing the usual coat 
and hat. A middle-aged woman followed, her appearance strict and 
official. Finally, two uniformed men walked in, the last one closing the 
door. The first of those men was carrying a tripod, the second, a flip chart. 
They assembled the two pieces and all four persons faced the audience.  
Around a large, round table Xave was sitting between known faces; 
there were Isela Mantorry, Sam Steward, Clause Florentia, Tyra Omand 
and a dozen other very important persons Xave had met throughout the 
years. The air was thick with authority; there was so much power in this 
room one could almost taste it. 
And get high on it, Xave thought. 
The salesman, now free from the phone cord, welcomed the new 
guests. “Inspector Reklur, thank you for coming.” 
Every person on the table nodded at them. 
“If I may,” Reklur began, removing his hat and placing it on the table in 
front of him, at a spot where nobody was sitting. “I want to introduce my 
colleague, Madame Eulalie Leclair, one of the best detectives from Paris.” 
Eulalie bowed. 
“Thank you for coming,” Isela greeted her. 
“Two of my personal guards are with us as well,” Reklur said, pointing 
at the two policemen, standing side by side next to the flip chart. 
Xave could see Reklur biting his lip. Was he holding back? 
“Anyway,” Reklur continued, facing the empty first page on the tripod. 
“We’re going to tell you a little story now, but keep in mind, this is top 
secret.” 
A mayor grinned, visibly excited. 
Reklur stepped aside, gesturing the detective. “Madam Leclair, please.” 
She kept a straight face, turned over the first page and started talking 
with a French accent: “Three months ago, Monsieur Reklur and I crossed 
paths during an investigation concerning the ongoing acts of vandalism on 
Trimond property.” 
The page behind her showed a map of Europe. Red dots had been 
drawn all over the countries, and Xave assumed those were reported 
incidents. 
“As you can see here,” Eulalie continued, her hand hovering over the 
continent. “The destructions happened everywhere, with increasingly 
lower pauses in-between. We were neither able to discern a distinctive 
strategy to their actions, nor catch a glimpse of their identity.” 
 
311 
Sam interrupted her, his second chin wobbling: “We’ve heard witness 
reports of a man with a vacuum cleaner strapped to his back, apparently 
he just shot holes into the cables next to a post office and drove off. Would 
that help?” 
“We’ll get to that,” Reklur countered. “Just enjoy the show for now.”  
Eulalie’s mouth sharpened, then she carried on. “Monsieur Reklur and I 
shared our findings, but it still left us clueless. However,” she paused, 
turning the page and letting it hang behind the chart with a dwarfish 
thunder. “We both found this.” 
Xave leaned forward and took a closer look at the paper next to Eulalie. 
He saw a symbol of some sort, a clean circle, but with a small triangular 
dent at 12 o’clock - an arrow pointing towards the center. 
“Repeatedly, we’d stumble upon a sort of logo. It was prominent in all 
death threat letters and spray painted on walls or cables where they 
destroyed cables and facilities.” 
“That’s correct,” Isela noted. “We saw it on the letters we all received.”  
There were affirming voices from the other mayors. 
“That was our first clue,” Reklur chimed in. “But, that wasn’t all. May 
I?” 
“Bien sûr,” Eulalie acknowledged. 
“You see,” Reklur said, puffing himself up, turning the page on the 
chart. The new image showed a detailed drawing of the round device with 
a backpack wrapped in cables. “Four years ago, one of the teleportation 
devices got stolen. A group of people sold it to a man nicknamed Einstein, 
who allegedly used the technology to construct said vacuum cleaner, only 
it doesn’t clean vacuums, it scrambles whatever it has targeted into dust, 
or, should I say, atoms, and what your witness forgot to tell you,” he faced 
Sam and pointed with his finger. “It shoots the stuff out behind its 
wielder.” He returned to a proud position. “I suggested calling it an Atom 
Disorder Device, or ADD in short, but nobody cared.” 
Eulalie took over. “We knew that by catching individuals we’d get 
information, but these tidbits only gave us names, and no location. So, we 
decided to lead our own undercover group.” 
She licked her finger and dragged the page up and away by its edge.  
Xave held his chin and focused. On the chart there were around thirty 
photos of individuals he’d never seen before. 
“In total, we tasked thirty-one people to infiltrate the organization,” 
Eulalie elaborated, guiding a hand over the photographs. “Some had 
already been on the inside, receiving anonymous messages on what to 
destroy, others owed us favors or were looking at police pardons.”  
 
312 
Reklur cleared his throat. “Imagine that: They all had heard about 
Einstein, but nobody had seen him, except for a group of p eople I 
personally knew. They guided me to a location, at which they had 
previously exchanged the stolen beaming device for a portion of Einstein ’s 
money. It was a grass field right in the middle of nowhere, but there were 
no traces left of the man.” 
Clause clicked his lighter and burnt the head of a fresh cigar, inhaling 
its emanating cloud. 
“Do you presume that Einstein is dead?” Isela asked. 
“For all we know, that’s a possibility,” Eulalie pondered. “What we do 
know for certain is that all devices must have originated from Einstein, 
which means that whoever was in charge ordered duplicates in high 
amounts.” 
“And here’s what we found out so far,” Reklur proclaimed with 
enthusiasm. He told one of the policeman to turn back the pages to the 
strange logo. “They have a name,” Reklur said, hitting the paper with his 
flat hand. “Neo-Stalactites,” he announced with delight. 
“Neo-Luddites,“ Eulalie corrected him. 
“Exactly,” Reklur mumbled and moved on. “They’re a group of people 
who just can’t handle that the world is moving on and constantly 
changing.” 
The two policemen looked at Reklur, but kept their mouths shut and 
their hands behind their backs. 
Reklur crossed his arms. “Our theory is that they ordered several of 
these devices to destroy technology with its own poison; sort of as a 
message soaked in irony.” 
Xave shifted his weight and put out a doubting hand, palm upwards, as 
if he were begging for more details. “That’s their whole agenda? What’s 
with the shooter? And the guy who attacked us back in the storeroo m?” 
Eulalie sighed and replied: “They usually act on their own, but share 
information with each other. However, the recent acts of vandalism are in 
fact coordinated, but not by a single person. They set up meetings and 
agree to execute a suggested plan.” 
Tyra broke her silence and put a foot down. “You are admitting that 
you inflicted major damage in a public transport service, causing accidents 
and even deaths?” 
Reklur shook his head. “I’m having none of that. This is a critical part of 
our investigation to find the head of the snake. If holes in the cables cause 
problems, Mister Stern should be your contact person.” 
 
313 
“We talked through it before you arrived,” Xave countered. “We just 
shut everything off and we’re actively dealing with our affected 
customers.” 
“Well, there you have it,” Reklur said. 
Tyra recovered, but not before sending off another remark: “It might 
still have consequences, depending on the results of your experiment.” 
Reklur was about to shout back, but Eulalie put a hand in front of him 
and shook her head. 
“We’re doing the right thing, and here’s why,” Eulalie concluded. “We 
have gathered intel about a bigger target, an event which will give us a 
huge lead. We have conceived a plan to send out a police force to strike 
accordingly. However, we must remain as silent as possible and not blow 
our group’s cover.” 
The smoking mayor put out the cigar in the tray before him. “It ’s going 
to be tough keeping this under wraps,” he said with a raspy voice. 
“It’s definitely going to get irregular,” Reklur summed up. “But I’m 
positive, we’ll rid the world of the threat once and for all.” 
Reklur gestured at both policemen, and they started disassembling the 
chart. 
“Wait, we still have questions!” Xave intervened. “When will you have 
more information to share? When can we turn our network back on?” 
Eulalie looked at Reklur, then at Xave and said: “We don’t know yet. 
Please excuse us, we have to continue our investigation. There’s nothing 
more to share right now.” 
“When will you contact us again?” Isela wondered. 
“I suppose,” Reklur responded, picking up his hat. “Right before 
everything blows up in our faces. Good day, ladies and gentlemen.” 
 
314 
 
 
 
Heray and Calem were sitting across each other in the Trimond HQ 
restaurant. Nobody else was there this late in the evening, other than the 
policewoman idling at a privacy-respecting distance. 
“Want me to fire some random questions at you?” Heray asked, the 
muscles in his arms flexing while cutting a piece of meat on his plate. It 
could’ve been a blunt wooden knife and the sheer force would’ve split his 
food apart like butter. 
“No, I think I’m covered,” Calem answered, crunching on a green salad 
leaf as big as her mouth, and fishing for a small piece of baked chicken 
with her fork. 
“Have you done this before?” Heray asked in-between swallowing and 
chomping. 
“Eating in a restaurant, being with a man?” Calem asked in return. She 
took a quick glance at the policewoman and took a sip of her water.  
Heray smiled, but made a face that said: You know what I mean. 
Calem sighed. “Not really, I mean I talked to crowds before. I reckon 
that’s not going to be a normal crowd though.” 
“Well, you’re a beautiful lady, they have enough prejudice to treat you 
as one,” Heray said, trying to cheer her up and, yes, bluntly attempt to 
flirt. 
She scoffed. “My appearance will be my downfall, surely. Either they’ll 
treat me like a star, or they’ll treat me like I’m the dumbest person on the 
planet who’s just here because of her looks.” 
“You can’t give them a choice, you pick the way you want to be treated, 
and you play the part,” Heray concluded. 
“Alright, going to wear my super intelligent backup plan glasses then.” 
Calem put both fork and knife parallel to each other on her plate and 
shoved it to the edge of the table. She continued to lick her teeth with her 
tongue. “I was starving.” 

 
315 
“Same,” Heray said, digging for tomatoes in his salad bowl. “I had 
completely forgotten until my stomach was louder than the voices in front 
of the door.” 
The radio on the shoulder of the policewoman cracked and spit o ut 
indistinct words. 
“Trouble?” Heray asked her over his shoulder. 
The policewoman shook her head. “Not yet.” 
They had arranged five rows of folding chairs in the entrance hall, right 
in front of Calem’s desk; they’d hold the conference there. Heray had told 
the police that he and Calem would require a quick break before the show.  
After Heray had finished eating, the waiter took care of the dishes. 
They ordered coffee and enjoyed the silence for another ten minutes.  
“Take your time, okay?” Heray told her. 
“I can take care of myself, white knight,” Calem said. 
“I was just-” 
“Let’s go.” Calem stood up and went past the policewoman, leaving a 
half-empty cup behind. 
The audience had already been in place when Heray arrived in the 
entrance hall. All five rows of seats were taken, some photographers were 
standing and shooting. Jeremy and a dozen of his followers were gathered 
near the entrance, with three police bodies between them and the press.  
Everyone seemed to be chatting, but it was mostly inaudible to Heray.  
He wiped his hand over his neat ponytailed hair, stood next to Calem and 
crossed his arms, puffing up his appearance. 
Calem nodded. “I’ll take your questions now; please raise your hands.” 
Three news cameras started rolling. 
Calem pointed at one such raised hand, the owner of which - a woman - 
started talking immediately: “We have several reports of missing persons. 
How many people are stuck in the cables, like the son of Rick Lorand?”  
Calem smiled. “What happened to Rick’s son was an unprecedented 
tragic incident that is still under investigation. No further similar accidents 
have happened. We have no missing persons due to travelling; but we 
know that people are missing because they couldn’t travel back home and 
haven’t found a way to reach out to their friends and relatives yet. As you 
know, we started an initiative at all INSTANT stations to help out as best 
as we can.” 
“By handing out coffee and cookies?” the same woman asked 
sarcastically. 
 
316 
“Coffee and cookies do not in fact solve anything, it’s meant to be a 
gesture of goodwill to our customers while we’re working on the actual 
solution together, namely - getting people home safe and sound.” 
So far so good, Heray thought without moving an inch, doing his best to 
intimidate everyone in front of him. 
Calem pointed at a different hand. 
“Is Mr. Rick Lorand still with the company?” a well-dressed man asked. 
“Yes, he is still in charge of system security.” 
“How much longer will you put up with him? Clearly he’s the one 
responsible for everything.” the man continued. 
“He is indeed responsible for one of the safest means of transportation, 
if not the absolute safest, I see no reason not to put up with him. In fact, 
he’s not the one perforating INSTANT cables, he and his team are the ones 
who’re patching them up as we speak,” Calem answered calmly and shot 
her finger at another waving arm. 
But the person didn’t give up: “How can you rely on a man who has 
just lost his son and whose colleague lies in a hospital?” 
“Finding his son is top priority for him, which includes making 
travelling as secure as possible. He’s one-hundred percent focused on his 
task, I can assure you. As for Ms. Attenburg, I know she’ll recover,” Calem 
answered, her finger still targeting the next reporter. 
Heray saw Jeremy and his group getting nervous. 
“The doctors say, they don’t know what happened to her,” someone 
yelled out. “Are you sure it’s not in any way related to her being one of the 
first to use your machine?” 
Calem stretched out her hands. “Not going to answer that, you get to 
ask your question when it’s your turn.” 
So they got the doctors to talk, Heray thought, suppressing a frown. 
“Ms. Attenburg is going to die, isn’t she?” another woman asked 
through the forest of raised hands. 
Calem pointed at a different body and waited for an authorized 
question. 
“How long will INSTANT be down?” the male reporter voice asked.  
“We have to investigate every meter manually to make sure the cables 
are in best condition before we can resume business,” Calem answered. “I 
can give no time frame at this point.” 
“Who’s going to pay for that?” someone shouted. “You guys have no 
money, right?” 
Calem hesitated. “We have investors-” 
“You’re toying with government money.” 
 
317 
Calem shoved the glasses up the bridge of her nose and snapped: “I’m 
not your scapegoat, I’m here to provide the answers I’ve been given. I’m 
not here so you can have your way with me and spill blood for blood ’s 
sake. You will behave, raise your hands, ask in a civilized tone and I will 
respond as best as I can. We’re all humans, we have a problem, and I’m 
here to help you.” 
Half the press stared at her, others scribbled hectically on their 
notepads. 
Heray rearranged his tie and noticed Jeremy had turned into a statue.  
One brave hand rose up and Calem acknowledged its existence with a 
nod. 
The reporter asked: “There are rumours of slightly disarranged limbs 
after instant travelling and apparently doctors had to tell patients to refrain 
from using teleportation for up to two weeks to heal. What can you tell us 
about that?” 
“We don’t comment on rumours,” Calem dodged. 
Next hand rose up and its owner was allowed to speak: “I realize these 
are rumours, but I’m sure the general public would like some form of 
reassurance. Is INSTANT travelling safe?” 
“I appreciate that you want information on that topic but I’m not the 
appropriate person to answer technical questions. All I know is that we 
have been given regulations from several nations; there are conditions our 
system has to meet, we have scheduled routine tests and maintenance. 
Suffice to say, we are the safest way to travel.” Calem swallowed. 
“Technical difficulties such as the damages we’re dealing with right now 
are an unforeseen human intervention.” 
Jeremy spoke up: “Instant travelling is an unforeseen human 
intervention on nature.” He marched three steps towards Calem, the three 
policemen gently held him back at his arms. “These people are trying to 
right your wrongs out there. Humans weren’t made to be taken apart and 
reassembled like robots; how do you know we’re not already dead, devoid 
of our souls, the minute we’re blown up? We’re using machines that we 
don’t understand. It’s not natural. It’s not meant to be. I’m the prime 
example.” 
Heray tensed up and was ready to jump in front of Calem, but then 
Jeremy just shook his head, freed himself from the police gloves and left 
the building, followed by his confused disciples. 
 
318 
Calem recovered and aimed to take control of the situation. 
“Nevertheless, statistics support the safety of INSTANT travelling – we 
offer the safest means of transportation. Our plans to rebuild and 
strengthen our cables with Kevlar coating should show you how serious

The room went silent. 
Calem continued: “This is also where I’d like to announce that we will 
soon release a new service – mass transportation. It will allow our 
customers to save even more time.” 
A reporter unfroze. “I’m sorry, what? People are missing out there,

products? Getting creative with new ways to kill us?” 
Other voices chimed in and supported his negative tone. 
“No, no-” Calem said, holding up her hands in defence. 
The same reporter apologized sarcastically: “I’m sorry, but that’s how I 
heard it.” 
Calem raised her voice. “This is not a murder game to us! Yes, we’re in 
a shitty situation, but we also have content customers, whom we owe a 
positive stance and an optimistic outlook. We can’t just be all doom and 
gloom.” 
That was it. People jumped from their seats and threw curse words at 
her. 
“Can’t be all doom and gloom, huh? Gotta cover up the stink, throw 
some flowers on it,” someone shouted in disgust. 
Calem blinked, then she shot a look at Heray. 
“Enough for today,” Heray said. He opened his giant arms and slowly 
cleared the entrance hall like a snowplough. “Good night everyone,” he 
said. “Show’s over,” he repeated a few times. 
 
319 
 
 
 
The smoke clouded the tavern like the early morning mist hovering above 
the fields outside. 
Leonard slowly drank his coffee, cherishing every drop for its warmth 
and the stimulating effect it had on his body. He took another bite from his 
breakfast - white bread with butter and honey - and washed it down with 
more coffee. 
People around him were enjoying their morning meals just as well; the 
tavern was small and could only serve a dozen people at a time. The two 
tables were fully occupied, so were the five seats at the bar. Almost 
everyone was watching the TV screen as it proclaimed the news.  
Apparently the entire Trimond network had been shut down the night 
before. They showed excerpts from a press conference with a woman 
Leonard didn’t recognize; he thought there had used to be a different 
spokesperson. 
Apparently the damage to the cables had brought down the whole net, 
they had reached a point where the risk of casualties had been simply too 
high. 
The reporter narrating and connecting the loose pieces of information 
announced future plans of the Trimond company, namely a complete 
overhaul of the cable structures with a strong Kevlar coating and mass 
transport. They showed images of the aftermath of said announcement, 
reporters shouting, the camera man losing control and shaking the image, 
followed by more protests outside of the Trimond building. Police had 
cleared the scene peacefully, but Leonard was sure that hadn’t been the 
end of it. 
Leonard looked at Bellward, who was tilting a jug of cocoa against his 
mouth. He remembered Bellward’s round doomsday device; the turning 
crank that had activated it before making that loud noise and evaporating 
anything it had touched into dust behind Bellward’s large back. Leonard 
felt certain that a different coating wouldn’t have helped keeping the 
cables intact. 

 
320 
The people at the table next to them started applauding when the 
broadcast displayed a dozen different videos and photos of marred , 
leaking cables. 
“That should show them,” an elder woman said. 
“Awesome job, man,” a muscular man on a bar stool bellowed; he was 
just within reach to hit Bellward on his shoulder. 
Unable to catch his breath, the reporter continued the coverage: 
Apparently just an hour ago the Trimond company had started to cover up 
their INSTANT stations and hand out coffee and cookies for people that 
had arrived to commute to their workplace or were stranded there and 
needed a way to get home. 
“Good job, Bellward,” Leonard said. 
Bellward caught Leonard’s sarcasm. “You think I did this? I just 
punched a dozen holes. Do you realize how big that network is?”  
“You still had a hand in it, I mean you became a local hero because you 
slayed the biggest farmer threat – a colossal cable scarecrow.” Leonard had 
made the conscious choice to say it just so only Bellward could hear it.  
Bellward glared at Leonard. “You’re damn right, I had a hand in it. I’m 
not even sorry.” 
He wiped his mouth with his right arm and stood up to leave the 
tavern. 
Marla, who was sitting right next to Leonard, finished her coffee. She 
hadn’t eaten anything; her white plate was clean. Leaning forward, she 
whispered to Leonard: “I know he enjoys this all a little too much, but 
please believe me when I say, we have a plan. And we’ve got to stick to it.” 
“What kind of plan?” Leonard asked. Suspicious ears twitched around 
him. 
Marla shook her head and looked around, as if to say, “Not here.” 
Just when the smoke and the tension got unbearable, Bellward returned 
to them and said: “We’re leaving.” 
The town folks were displeased. 
“Already? Why?” a young farmer asked. 
“It’s not over yet, we’re bringing the fight to them,” Bellward said. 
Leonard noticed a hint of pride. 
Marla seemed relieved. “Finally,” she said and stood up, taking 
Leonard outside with her, right after Bellward. 
As they sat down in their car, the assumed leader approached the 
driver’s window. 
“Do you need help? We could tag along,” he offered, still holding a cup 
of something hot. 
 
321 
Bellward refused. “Nope, we gotta do this on our own, thanks though. 
I’ll check back once I’m done.” 
The leader shrugged and moved back into the tavern. The crowd that 
had built up around them lost interest and dispersed. 
“Not the farewell celebration you expected?” Leonard said from the 
backseat. 
Bellward turned around, his seat creaking, shooting a fierce look at 
Leonard. “You should be glad I can’t just kick your ass out,” he said. 
“What’s holding you back?” Leonard asked curiously. 
Bellward retreated, started the car and jumped on the gas pedal. “None 
of your business.” 
Leonard thought to just exit the moving car, but the town inhabitants 
were just a bit too unsettling. Besides - for some reason he felt like he had 
to come along, even if just to protect Marla from Bellward, who seemed to 
slowly lose patience and mind over a matter which Leonard hadn’t quite 
figured out yet. 
He realized Marla wasn’t beautiful, but even though, or exactly because 
of that, he had begun to feel... closer. Feeling protective sent old memories 
back into Leonard’s vision. Was it just a seemingly long forgotten pattern 
that had snuck up on him? Was his mind trying to relive the routines of a 
couple? 
Leonard looked at Marla’s face from the side as she once again checked 
out the list, the final item reading “INSTANT HQ”. 
“What are we going to do?” Leonard decided to ask, if only to break the 
awkward silence. 
“You’ll see,” Bellward replied aggressively. Leonard could hear 
Bellward’s grip tighten around the steering wheel. 
Marla hid behind strands of her low hanging hair. 
“Look,” Leonard said, trying to make amends for his earlier biting 
remarks. “You have your reasons as to why I have to accompany you and 
why you can’t reveal your ultimate goal, I realize that, and by now it’s no 
matter if you tell me or not – I simply want you to know I’m on your side.” 
“On whose? Mine or Marla’s?” Bellward shot back. 
Marla’s, of course, Leonard thought. “Both,” he said. It was no lie. He 
had known the early version of Bellward and he had come to enjoy his 
company; so much so that he now worried that he might go down a path 
he wouldn’t be able to retreat from. 
“Just promise me you’re not doing something you’re going to regret,” 
Leonard said, trying to settle on that point at least. 
 
322 
Bellward caught Leonard’s look in the back mirror. After a moment 
Bellward replied: “We’re not evil, if that’s what you mean.” 
“You seemed to enjoy destroying cables,” Leonard added. 
“I had to let off some steam,” Bellward defended himself. “I can’t tell 
you, I’ve been told not to tell you. It’s probably better I just shut up again.” 
Marla looked back at Leonard. There was a trace of a distant smile 
around her mouth. 
Half an hour later on the highway they passed a truck loaded with a 
giant connector piece with a Trimond logo plastered all over it. The  truck 
had two dozen small tires and took up two of the three lanes. Two smaller 
cars with orange blinking warning lights accompanied it front and back to 
keep other cars from getting too close. 
Two more of the same truck and warning car constellations followed. 
The carried pieces were gigantic compared to Bellward’s car and for a 
second Leonard felt like swimming alongside a whale family. A vision 
showed him a glimpse into what would happen if the pieces came loose 
and rolled over the street at their current speed. 
Marla had turned on the radio, which kept bringing up the “ongoing 
maintenance of INSTANT stations” between songs of the 80s. The news 
lady had also mentioned the controversy of the newly announced product 
of mass transportation and had summarized people’s opinions as “bad 
taste” in light of all the stranded and missing people. 
Fifteen minutes later they crossed the city’s borders, and another fifteen 
minutes passed until they arrived a block away from the Trimond HQ.  
The commotion in front of the converted hotel blocked all traffic. 
Leonard estimated that there were a hundred people protesting, some of 
which were sitting on parked cars. Police did their job and kept them at a 
safe distance from the HQ, but Leonard easily imagined people throwing 
rocks at the windows. 
Stationed policemen and women were redirecting traffic as best as they 
could.  
Bellward turned left before being seen and took another turn right 
afterwards. 
“Should be here,” he said, slowing down the car and pointing at a 
garage door at the base of a seven-story apartment building. 
Marla reached for a tiny remote control and pressed one of the two 
buttons. 
The garage door creaked and folded upwards; Bellward drove through 
and into the inner courtyard, a small place in-between several of the 
apartment blocks. 
 
323 
A dozen shady people eyed their car suspiciously. Bellward stopped 
and got out, signalling Marla and Leonard to stay inside. 
“There you are,” a man said as he came closer. 
Bellward shut the door. 
Leonard recognized the device on the man’s back: It was the same 
vaporizing gun Bellward had used. He looked at the rest of the figures 
through the car’s windshield – all of them carried the same weapons. 
Hoping he would see the same startled emotion on Marla’s face, he 
looked at her – but instead, there was the dreaded realization of known 
future events coming true. 
She knew, Leonard repeated in his head. Everything. 
“Why is it you couldn’t tell me any of this?” Leonard whispered 
through the silence of the car and over the muffled voices from outside. 
“We were never sure which information you were given access to,” 
Marla replied in a low voice. 
“What does that mean? Who’s giving you orders?” 
Marla turned to look Leonard straight in his eyes. “Reklur.” 
That took some time to comprehend. “Are you saying, he’s 
orchestrating an attack on Trimond property?” 
“No,” Marla said, nervously eyeing Bellward from the side of her eyes.  
Before she could continue, Bellward turned around and opened the 
door on his side. 
“Let’s go,” he said. 
Marla frowned at him and shook her head. 
Bellward shrugged and got his vacuum-cleaner shaped tool from the 
trunk. 
 
324 
 
 
 
“We have to move,” Heray decided, looking at the growing crowd outside 
of the Trimond building. 
“Where to?” Calem asked. She was sitting in the front row of the chairs 
they had assembled for the press earlier that day. “Why do we have to 
move? Can’t they just arrest everyone?” 
Heray knew she wasn’t serious, but he forgot to acknowledge her 
remark with a smirk. He remembered audiences at rock concerts, 
especially the moments where he had known when a fight would start. 
Usually, there had been a kind of spark in the air, an ever-intensifying 
cloud of aggression, on the brink of eruption. 
“Walter was supposed to be here by now,” Heray said. 
“The road’s blocked, nobody gets close by car, and I’m not sure he 
would try going through that nice group of onlookers,” Calem theorized.  
“And he can’t teleport here either,” Heray added. 
Calem looked at him incredulously. “First of all, it’s travelled,” she 
joked. “Second, Walter doesn’t travel.” 
“Right, I keep forgetting, that’s a bit odd, isn’t it?” 
Calem’s phone rang. “Again?” 
“You had your sixty seconds break, what more do you want?” This 
time he did smirk. “Stay seated, I’ll get it,” he said, feeling guilty. 
He moved around the counter and picked up the receiver. “Trimond 
HQ, this is Heray Peer speaking. What can I do-” 
A breathless voice shouted in Heray’s ear: “Get out of the building, 
move, now!” 
Used to pranks, Heray got a hold of his initial adrenaline rush and 
asked: “Who am I talking to?” 
“Inspector Reklur, now get the f-” 
A loud exploding noise destroyed the glass front of the entrance hall. 
People started screaming and running in all directions. 
“I said, get the fuck out of there,” Reklur finished yelling and hung up. 

 
325 
Calem had run for cover behind the counter almost instantly. Her eyes 
were wide, but she didn’t panic. 
For another long second Heray observed the scene on the street outside. 
Police tried to control the situation and to locate where the explosion had 
originated from. Two officers were frantically roaring into their radios. 
From afar, Heray couldn’t figure out if anyone had been hurt. He let the 
receiver land on the table, grabbed Calem’s arm and rushed with her to the 
elevator. 
The elevator doors opened as Heray was about to punch the button. 
Standing inside was no one other than Rick. 
“What’s happening?” he asked. “I just wanted to check up on you two 
and now I’m hearing bombs go off or something.” 
Heray pushed Calem inside and followed. As he pressed the button for 
one of the parking garage sub floors, he inquired: “Anyone else still in the 
building?” 
Rick, who looked like a vampire to Heray, shook his head. “We sent 
everyone out for repairs or covering up our shit after shutting everything 
down.” His eyes drifted off. 
Heray was taken aback by Rick’s cursing, but remained silent. 
“What about the cooking staff?” Calem asked. 
Rick shrugged. “Some insane person ordered coffee and cookies for 
everyone. Not that our cooks provide everything, but, you know.” 
Can’t argue with his choice of words, Heray thought. 
The elevator arrived and the doors opened. 
A group of gloomy strangers stared at Heray, Rick and Calem. In the 
foreground, there were a large, muscular man, a woman hiding behind 
him and a slender male figure in a worn out coat. The big one held a large 
circular plate and aimed it at Heray. 
One of the men from the back, all of whom wielded the same strange 
device, shouted: “Get rid of them.” 
The explosion – it had been a diversion, Heray realized in horror. These 
people had entered the building forcefully through holes in the ceiling of 
the parking floor, leading to the street behind the Trimond HQ building. 
How did they- 
A whistle rang through everyone’s ears; uniformed rescuers jumped 
through the same holes and landed gracefully on their feet, immediately 
drawing weapons. 
 
326 
The garage area felt crowded all of a sudden. Just as Heray had made 
sense of the situation, the big fellow in front of him stepped inside the 
elevator and pushed Heray back by pressing the large round device 
against Heray’s chest with incredible force. Calem gasped, tripped and fell 
backwards into the elevator while Rick had swiftly evaded and was now 
standing outside. 
The young woman and the slender man were seemingly surprised, and 
before they could follow their apparent leader – the elevator doors closed. 
“Halt,” a policeman shouted, the loud echo through the garage hall 
dying between the closing elevator doors. 
Somebody must have called for it, Heray realized – then he tensed up his 
right arm into a ball of raw muscle force and jerked the round device away 
from his body. While his opponent hadn’t seen it coming, he also hadn’t 
lost grip of it, but the force threw him off balance. 
Heray followed his action with a right foot kick to the man’s stomach, 
pushing him against the closed doors. The elevator bounced violently.  
Calem covered her head and remained seated on the ground; there was 
enough room for maybe ten people, but the two fighting colossi turned the 
whole space into a tight arena. 
“I don’t want to hurt you or your girlfriend here,” the big man 
proclaimed while regaining his balance. “I’m just here to destroy your 
equipment.” 
“How kind,” Heray replied. “I don’t want to hurt you either, I’m just 
here to protect said equipment.” 
“Seems like we’re at an impasse,” the bulky man assessed. 
“Wrong,” Heray said. “Only you are.” He sent his right balled up fist 
flying through the air like a wrecking ball, but his opponent raised his 
device just in time to deflect the attack with a loud clanking noise. Seeing it 
coming half-way, Heray had already prepared his left hook to move 
around the round shape to hit his opponent in the right side. 
The man roared, but not because of pain, just increasing anger. With 
Heray still in position for his left hook, the man raised his knee to Heray’s 
chin, hitting hard. 
Heray was used to that kind of encounter from a fist, but a knee was a 
whole other level of pain. 
Then Heray’s face met with the cold round metallic shape. The push 
made him stumble, fall. Calem watched him wide-eyed as he hit the back 
of his head. How much more could Heray take? 
“Just let me do my job,” the stranger requested. 
 
327 
Calem shot up and slapped the man hard on his temple. Once, twice, 
and a third time – before the man wiped her away with his right arm. 
Heray used the opening to kick the round device upwards with his foot 
so hard that it finally escaped its owner’s hand and crashed into the light 
on the ceiling. One of the two fluorescent light bulbs shattered and rained 
down on the three passengers. The elevator groaned and creaked but still 
continued his way up. 
The man tried to catch his device with both hands – but instead, a sharp 
gasping sound escaped his mouth; the device crashed to the floor and 
began to lead a life of its own, while smoke was pouring out of the man’s 
backpack. 
Heray sprang to his feet and as he heard a loud whirring noise he 
jumped to the side just in time. 
A split second later dust shot out behind the stranger and a large hole 
appeared half on the ground and half on the wall where Heray had just 
been leaning against. Cables dangled in the elevator shaft and lights flew 
by in regular intervals. The sounds of the moving elevator were heard loud 
and clear now. 
Every one of the passengers coughed vehemently; Heray forced one of 
his eyes open and once more tried to smash the man’s face in. His fist 
cracked against the jaw, but it didn’t leave much of an impression. This 
time he didn’t see the retaliation coming in the cloud – and a massive boot 
tore skin down through his jeans on his right shin. 
Heray cursed; which was a rare reaction. Meanwhile Calem had 
recovered and attempted to throw several punches and kicks near the 
giant’s private area. 
The elevator ground to a halt just as the man was about to smack 
Calem’s skull – and the doors opened. 
“Put your hands up in the air,” an old voice instructed with authority.  
The towering man with the smoking backpack turned around and said: 
“Inspector Reklur.” 
Heray jumped at him from behind and, by hanging onto the 
contraption, they both fell like two dinosaurs, the man’s face digging into 
the floor. 
“Watch out, Heray,” Reklur said, holstering his gun. “This man just 
saved your life.” 
Calem ran past Reklur, her dress and hair full of dust. A policeman 
held a hand out to her and walked her outside of the building, where 
several emergency lights illuminated the house on the opposite side of the 
Trimond HQ. 
 
328 
Heray slowly stood up and let the man go. “He wanted to destroy 
company equipment, not to mention our faces,” Heray said confused.  
“Yeah, yeah,” Reklur said, waving a hand. “He just didn’t want to blow 
his cover. All’s fine now. Right, Bellward?” 
The man on the ground sighed. 
 
329 
 
 
 
Xave’s right leg fidgeted, while Walter was blowing smoke into the air. 
They were sitting on a comfy couch in Walter’s suite, the bullet proof 
windows darkened with drapes that had gone out of style ten years ago. 
There was a policeman leaning against the door, reading a car magazine.  
The salesman and the scientist stared at the TV’s images of the

Xave’s fingers mindlessly played with his father’s golden watch. 
“So,” Xave broke the silence.

“People like us just fine,” Xave countered, still looking at the TV. His

“They wanted to show us how bad this technology really is,” Walter

330 
Walter shook his head and leaned forward to kill the glowing cigarette

“How long we’re going to be stuck in here,” Walter said. 
They had brought Walter in with a police escort after finding him on

Rick, Calem and Heray had been taken to a hospital, mostly for routine

The phone in the room didn’t work, which made Xave even more

“I need to make some calls,” he mumbled. 
“I need to tend to my mistakes,” Walter joined in. “I already know what 
I have to do once I can get back to work.” 
“Yeah? That sounds great,” Xave said. To his own surprise there was no

Walter turned to him. “They’re not small fish.” 
“Well, look at the ocean we’re drowning in right now, yeah, they are

Samir was in Egypt right now, but Xave had asked to send policemen

Trimond company; Xave had been forced to talk way too much for his  
liking to get the mayors and other higher ups to do that, instead of just 
throwing everyone in one “prison” for their own safety. 
“I imagined all people that are stranded right now or in danger because 
of me, and I saw them all screaming, then I caught a glimpse of Kristella in 
hospital, the cold steel bed and the sterile white surroundings. And I 
realized that even though a thousand hearts are louder than her single 
heart...” Walter paused and looked away. “I can still only hear hers.”  
Xave slapped the scientist. “You are not falling in love with an 
employee, you fool. Do you even know what love is? Have your parents 
taught you that in their absence?” 
 
331 
Walter looked smitten. 
“You’re a scientist, Walter,” Xave stated. “You’re not here to make

Don’t you see the hundreds of distant relationships you’ve made possible? 
You don’t have time for love, you’re here so others can get busy, but you 
can’t, that’s a fact.” Xave grit his teeth. “And neither do I.” 
They sat in silence for a minute, listening to the policeman behind them

“My parents love me,” Walter said.

The scientist moved back from the demeaning gesture. Xave didn’t

Xave’s thoughts burned inside. Why blow so much potential away for 
an earthly relationship when you could affect all of humanity?  
“Were you ever in love, Xave?” 
“I thought I was,” Xave responded. “I ran after a girl for months, and

“Why?” 
Xave shrugged. He remembered the classroom and how the girl had

Xave had lowered his voice enough for Walter to hear.

332 
“I tell you the most disgusting thing in the world, and you still don ’t

Reklur had vaguely explained the situation to Xave a few hours ago,

The TV showed an interview with Jeremy, whose sentences only

333 
 
 
 
“Good morning,” a deep voice said. It belonged to a huge man sitting in a

shared the room and was still lying in his own bed at the opposite wall.

The mechanic poured himself some water out of a glass jug into a

334

It took only a quick memory of Rick embracing his baby and the smile

people who had bombed and infiltrated the INSTANT headquarters. Many 
of them would not talk and rather be sentenced to a lifetime of 
imprisonment. 
One in particular had been grazed by bomb fragments and turned out 
to be - more or less - talkative while being hooked to all sorts of cables in a 
hospital bed, his face a distant reminder of a former human tool for 
displaying emotions. 
After putting that person’s clues together with a dozen of additional 
hints, Reklur had been able to track down several meeting points all over 
Europe and successfully raid those with the assistance of local police. In 
the end, they had found the group responsible for bombing the Trimond 
HQ, yet failed to discover who had shot at Walter and Kristella years ago, 
attributing it to a single person acting on their own. Though, more 
importantly, they arrested one influential person, who helped pinpoint the 
location of the man behind all the weapons of destruction; the last piece of 
Reklur’s puzzle. 
He straightened his collar and moved towards the entrance door in a 
small building next to a turning and shifting head of an extracting 
machine. 
Reklur coughed into his fist, and knocked on the door twice with the 
other hand. 
“Room service,” he yelled. 
He waited a minute, then gestured the police squad that had built up 
behind him. They stormed the door and marched inside. 

 
356 
With pistols drawn, Reklur and his uniformed men went down a long 
and narrow set of stairs. It went from bright to almost pitch black, and 
Reklur’s squad turned on three flashlights. There was a shimmer of light 
behind another door with a logo on it. 
Reklur grinned, noticing the circle with the dent and the arrow facing 
the center. He didn’t have any patience left, so he just ordered his men: 
“Open it.” 
They tore the door down and went inside. 
Einstein looked up from a ball of cables on a long desk, spanning the 
entire room he was in. There were two more open doors, and the rooms 
behind them were filled with electronic parts of all sizes, including a series 
of instant travelling booths in various states of disarray. 
“Reklur,” Einstein said, calm and with a smile, like a grandfather seeing 
his grandson. 
Reklur’s group surrounded Einstein and waited for commands. 
“Let me do the honors, gentlemen,” Reklur said, squeezing through 
two of the policemen, producing a set of handcuffs. 
“That won’t be necessary, it was difficult enough to find me,” Einstein 
insisted, bowing deeply, almost like a losing chess king figure. 
“I had suspected you for years,” Reklur said, spinning the cuffs around 
his finger. “It had your signature style written all over it.” 
“You flatter me too much,” Einstein laughed, his tired Adam’s apple 
wiggling up and down. 
“Why didn’t you work with us instead?” Reklur asked, with a hint of 
sadness. 
“I worked with nobody,” Einstein explained with a raspy voice, 
scratching the gray stubble on his cheek. “Some people paid me for what I 
love to do. Their ulterior motive was none of my business.” 
“You sure loved them, what with their emblem on your door,” Reklur 
countered. 
“It’s true that they thought I was part of their equipment, their pocket 
scientist,” Einstein remarked, catching air. “I let them move me around, 
under the condition that they would provide me with parts I needed for 
my own experiments.” 
“You’re saying you trusted them, and in turn, they took control of 
you?” Reklur summarized. “I don’t buy it, you’re a mad scientist, you saw 
an opportunity to get ahold of illegal materials to play around with.”  
“Illegal materials? Mad scientist?” Einstein guffawed. “My word, 
whatever do you think I have been working on?” 
 
357 
“Wireless teleportation, perhaps? More guns that shoot holes into 
things?” Reklur suggested. “You scientists are all the same.” 
Einstein raised an eyebrow. “My, my,” he said, slowly shaking his 
head. “Your generalizations still don’t disappoint. Say, does your 
appointment book still talk to you?” 
Reklur held up both his arms. “Don’t listen to this old man, take him 
outside.” He closed in on Einstein and put an arm around him. “We’ve got 
a special surprise for our special guest.” 
They went upstairs and as soon as Einstein spotted the motorcade, he 
said: “Reklur, old friend, do me one favor.” 
“Yes?” Reklur answered, opening the motorcade’s door and holding it 
open. 
Einstein crawled inside and took a seat. He leaned out and told Reklur: 
“Now that you found me, please, seek help. Get your head examined.”  
The inspector laughed in a forced manner, closed the door and looked 
at the policemen around him. He couldn’t figure out whether they all 
agreed. 
 
358 
 
 
Six Years Later 
 
Beaming with Delight 
 
An exclusive interview with Walter Trimond, by Editor-in-chief 
Madeleine Long. 
 
Even though it had been one of my first jobs, I clearly remember the 
day I entered the noble Trimond family house twelve years ago. The 
building I’m stepping into now is far from humble; the skyscraper - 
labelled TRIMOND with ominous white letters at the top - stands tall on 
the edge of Vowkinn, reflecting the sun on all sides like a polished 
monolith. 
The receptionist welcomes me and, after I tell her who I am and that 
I’m being expected, I’m offered a choice between an elevator, an INSTANT 
booth or the old-fashioned staircase to reach the top. 
I, of course, take the INSTANT. If it took five minutes to the top with an 
elevator, that’d be almost as long as teleporting from New York to Paris, as 
the notorious billboard adverts inform us. 
After arriving instantly at the top, I am joined by Walter and we sit 
down in a lounge. The scientist hasn’t aged a lot since meeting him last, 
although I can discern a few lines of wisdom around his eyes. 
 
Madeleine Long: It’s a pleasure to meet you again. 
Walter Trimond: I’m delighted myself; it seems we met just yesterday, 
doesn’t it? 
Madeleine: Absolutely. But I feel old all of a sudden. 
Walter: Probably because Nirvana is slowly being categorized as oldie.  
Madeleine: (laughs) A lot has happened since yesterday. In fact, you’re 
celebrating your ten year anniversary. 

 
359 
Walter: Yes, we have ongoing discounts for all of our services and are 
preparing for an announcement. It will be revealed tonight. (Editor’s note: 
They announced wireless travel - see front page, but he wouldn’t tell me just yet, 
despite the fact that this interview would go live after the event.) 
Madeleine: Care to spoil it for my readers? 
Walter: I can’t, Xave [Stern] is probably watching me right now. He has 
very strict rules, but that’s why everything he does succeeds. 
Madeleine: Is it the long rumoured wireless travelling? 
Walter: (laughs) I really shouldn’t say. 
Madeleine: There have been reports in relation to wireless travelling 
that the Trimond company is building cemeteries. 
Walter: We’re always looking at offering more services. 
Madeleine: Instant funerals? 
Walter: That’s a great idea, I’ll talk to Xave about that. 
Madeleine: Hypothetically speaking, if we were able to travel 
wirelessly, when would you expect us to travel to the moon? 
Walter: As you probably know, our cables are an obstacle to a handful 
of people, so our main focus would be on getting rid of them. 
Madeleine: So we’re not beaming to the moon? 
Walter: (laughs) You’re still using that word? But yes, we’ve conducted 
lots of research, the results of which shall be revealed in time. 
Madeleine: You’ll put NASA out of their jobs. 
Walter: We’re not astronauts; if it ever came to space travelling, we’d 
need their - and every other space agency’s - help to build interstellar 
INSTANT stations. 
Madeleine: Bringing it back down to Earth again, according to a poll on 
our website, you are putting hundreds of people out of work right now. 
Your invention has caused ripple effects that are felt in all kinds of 
transportation industries. 
Walter: Xave and his sales team regularly board planes, call taxis, and 
sometimes even ride trains on their business trips. I can tell you from 
Xave’s observations that none of these businesses are going anywhere. You 
can use the INSTANT to travel from city to city, but we’re just now 
pushing forward with regional connections, and, as you may have heard, 
personal booths for home use were presented at last year’s CES. I imagine 
the latter will have the biggest impact, but trains didn’t go extinct because 
of cars or planes. There’s room for everyone. There’s even a group of 
people who claim INSTANT travelling has negative effects on your body; 
as long as people like that exist, everything else will. 
Madeleine: You believe in co-existence? 
 
360 
Walter: Absolutely, and I can confirm those are also Xave’s intentions. 
Madeleine: Does instant travelling have a negative effect on our bodies? 
Walter: No, we’ve proven throughout the years that there are no side 
effects whatsoever, even doctors approve. 
Madeleine: I heard some wild stories; people going to doctors and 
joining the Jeremy Instant Travelling Care because their limbs felt like 
coming off after commuting to work using the INSTANT every day. They 
claim that mass teleportation equals mass suicide. 
Walter: Those are negative voices, of course they stand out due to their 
loudness, but I promise you, the positive effects instant travelling has on 
people’s lives vastly outweigh any psychosomatic pain. 
Madeleine: You’re calling them crazy? 
Walter: On the contrary, but just because my shoulder feels stiff, I don’t 
tell everyone that sleeping in a bed is making me sick. There are usually 
other reasons - an open window, stress, or sore muscles. 
Madeleine: It’s definitely not due to your product, then. 
Walter: Millions use it every day; if we received a million complaints, I 
would be concerned. 
Madeleine: Do you use it yourself? 
Walter: Yes, I do. 
Madeleine: Are you sure? Sources claim that you and Mr. Stern have 
never been witnessed travelling instantly. How are you getting around on 
business trips if not by your own invention? 
Walter: I usually don’t comment on rumours, especially from 
anonymous sources, but suffice to say, I don’t do business trips. I’m a 
scientist through and through; my home is my castle. Xave, as I said 
earlier, also travels by other means of transportation, not only to check out 
what the competition is doing, but to be a fair sport. He doesn’t want 
anyone to go out of business. He thinks it brightens his public image when 
he is seen travelling using different ways. Besides, he has met a lot of 
business partners that way. 
Madeleine: That is something romantic that is lost on instant travelling, 
I believe. If you remove the journey, you can’t sit down and drink a cup of 
coffee with a stranger for a few hours. 
Walter: Well, despite mass transportation, our lines are still getting 
longer. I’d say people chat there while waiting to travel. 
Madeleine: Any future plans to circumvent that? Or are chatting people 
good for business? 
Walter: Our announcement may help out immensely, we’ll see. We’re 
constantly seeking ways to improve. More cables aren’t always the answer. 
 
361 
Madeleine: Let’s tackle some of the philosophical ramifications. Popular 
physicist John Clauser recently said that he wouldn’t step into your 
machine, the question about whether you are still the original person after 
being teleported scares him. What’s your take on that? 
Walter: I like that man a lot, but I honestly believe he’s just jealous. Of 
course, the person on the other side is you. You wouldn’t be able to know 
otherwise. 
Madeleine: That is a dangerous thing to say. You’re saying, you don’t 
remember dying and not waking up, and thus we conclude that the person 
on the other side is the original, when in fact something must have 
happened. 
Walter: All I’m saying is - are you using instant travelling? 
Madeleine: Sure, all the time. I beamed up here. 
Walter: You don’t care, do you? It’s fast, painless, convenient. 
Madeleine: People care what happens to their bodies. 
Walter: We can keep discussing the philosophy behind travelling for 
eternity. My point is: You want to go from A to B as fast as possible, we 
provide the solution, even with health benefits. I have nothing more to say 
on that topic. 
Madeleine: Very well. As of two years ago, your system reaches far into 
the world outside of Europe. What has taken so long? 
Walter: Back when we connected the United Kingdom about seven 
years ago, we faced the difficulties of putting cables underwater. We ’ve 
learned a lot from it and began coating our cables with Kevlar, as well  as a 
polyethylene and steel reinforcement, to connect to the rest of the world. 
Cost was a big factor in our delay, we paid several hundred millions of US 
dollars. Figuring out a deal with Kevlar and where best to put the cables 
proved quite a challenge to us, but as you said, now we’re worldwide. It 
feels good. 
Madeleine: Are you going to end world hunger? 
Walter: That is the hot topic in our meetings right now. We’ve already 
improved humanity’s health with the addition of medical tools based on 
our instant travelling technology, as well as our premium saving service, 
but world hunger is a different, political matter. 
Madeleine: I heard some mumblings about a deal with Egypt for a 
specialized traffic line down to South Africa. 
 
362 
Walter: There was in fact a deal on the table, Xave can probably tell you 
more details about that. We would’ve required 3.3 billion in US dollars to 
build a centralized hub in the Qattara Depression. There was a company 
that suggested 200 hydrogen bombs to construct the necessary canal for 
less than half that price. The government declined, naturally. 
Madeleine: So, what will you do to feed everyone? 
Walter: In theory, we can create anything we want at remote places, as 
long as there are all the necessary materials available. The problem is 
getting everything there first. It still requires an incredible amount of 
effort. Even with wireless, you’d still have to have something stored to 
construct food. We would like to make a difference, but we’re not there 
yet. 
Madeleine: Won’t people just flock to countries where there is enough 
food? 
Walter: There are limits as to how many people can enter other 
countries. In some, people have to be checked before they can exit the 
INSTANT area, sometimes they get held up for several days. We’ve hit a 
handful of political roadblocks, as you can probably imagine; Cuba was 
especially interesting. 
Madeleine: How come? 
Walter: Because of the different currencies and the way they treat 
foreigners. There’s an interesting story, actually. Xave is easily sunburnt 
and our meetings in Cuba took place with the sun glaring down on us. He 
entered a heavily guarded store and was greeted by the shopkeeper inside. 
It took a while to convey what Xave wanted to buy, the shopkeeper took 
us to a special glass case, opened it with a key, and presented Xave a bottle 
of sun lotion, as if it were the most precious thing. 
Madeleine: But it was just sun lotion, right? 
Walter: It was probably the cheapest sun lotion one can get, quality 
wise, and it cost a lot. (laughs) 
Madeleine: Speaking of costing a lot, here is one thing I keep hearing 
about your premium service. 
Walter: Is it that expensive? 
Madeleine: I’m talking cost in terms of addiction. Did you know some 
people get addicted to reloading themselves? 
Walter: What makes you think they are addicted? 
Madeleine: They use it daily to avoid ageing and having to eat at all. 
Since their memories reset every time, they keep elaborate journals, 
sometimes even in video form. Will you ever implement certain 
restrictions? 
 
363 
Walter: We’re offering a service, and they are our paying customers. It 
is their right to do what they want. 
Madeleine: I imagine a future in which nobody can play the piano 
anymore, because they keep resetting their brains. 
Walter: I wouldn’t worry about that, there will always be piano players. 
Besides, maybe we will be able to add memories, just like we can modify 
human bodies. 
Madeleine: I just had a vision, people stop giving birth to babies and 
just push a button to summon their perfect child. 
Walter: Every disruptive piece of technology has brought change and it 
has shown time and time again that we can all adapt, no matter what. New 
generations will get used to it quickly, old generations will fade away.  
Madeleine: You believe I’m afraid of change? 
Walter: I think you are one of the first to embrace it due to your 
insightful visions. 
Madeleine: (laughs) I’m offering my services, if you ever want to know 
what the future brings. 
Walter: No offense, but I’d say Xave is years ahead of all of us. 
Madeleine: And that’s good, that makes him more valuable. How are 
you two getting along after all these years? 
Walter: He is still the driving force behind the company, I can’t imagine 
having a different business partner. 
Madeleine: Business partner? You two look like a happy coupl e every 
time I see you on TV. 
Walter: You can’t befriend salesmen, can you? They’re only after your 
money. (laughs) Besides, my wife Kristella [Attenburg] would heavily 
object. 
Madeleine: Fair enough. Walter, this final question is going to sting a 
bit, I hope you’re not squeamish. 
Walter: I hate needles, but go on. 
Madeleine: Rumours are abuzz about Bernand Lorand, son of your 
chief mechanic Rick Lorand. The last official word was that you found him 
and since then he’s been in therapy. But nobody actually sees him, and as 
the years go by, people start to wonder. So, here I am, asking you: Is 
Bernand alright? 
 
364 
Walter: There definitely have been photos circulating, paparazzi are 
always present whenever his parents take him outside. He’s doing great, 
actually. He’s getting private lessons, and we try our hardest to shield him 
from the public eye, as to not corrupt his young mind with bad external 
influence of an always hungry audience. It was the decision of his parents 
to handle the situation that way. In short, he’s fine. I see him regularly 
myself. 
Madeleine: Is he okay with how things turned out for him and his 
family? 
Walter: He’s very proud, that’s all I know. 
Madeleine: As he should be. Walter, it’s been a great pleasure talking to 
you again. 
Walter: Likewise, this has been very educating. We will have to repeat 
this. 
Madeleine: I agree. 
 
We wrap up the interview, shake hands and say our goodbyes. I take 
the INSTANT down, wondering if I have ever used my premium 
membership to restore myself without remembering. It would show up in 
my past activities, wouldn’t it? Is it possible to intentionally travel “under 
the radar” and prevent my future self from knowing? 
By writing this I will make sure to remember this journey, and maybe 
ask Walter these questions next time. 
 
365 
 
 
 
Xave’s gaze wandered down to his feet; he felt a soothing breeze combing 
through his hair, and saw the wind playing with his tie as if it were a leash.  
“Here we are, father,” he said, leaning forward and touching the 
double slant headstone of his parents. Looking slightly to the right, he 
added: “And mother.” 
He let go of the moss-riddled stone and reached inside his chest pocket. 
The watch was cold to his touch as he pulled it out, its chain dangling. 
With the slightest click, he opened it and was greeted by a photo of 
Jeffrey and Dorothy Stern, smiling. 
Xave smiled back; warm, then bitterly. 
His trousers fluttered like a flag as he crouched down. He firmly closed 
the watch and put it to rest between dried up marigolds, right in front of 
the aging marble that bore his family’s name. Dirt in his eyes, he gathered 
two fistfuls of earth, and buried the glittering timepiece underneath. 
Leftover soil escaped from his fingers and drifted away with leaves flying 
by. 
“I officially make you a part of it,” Xave declared. “Feel proud, if you 
want.” 
He stood up, turned around and walked to a man standing behind him, 
waiting with pen and documents. Xave signed three times and waved a 
group of workers with shovels, idling in front of a monstrous excavator.  
“Alright,” he announced, while walking back to his car. “All yours.” 
And that’s graveyard number ten. 

 
366 
 
 
 
One of the four elevators arrived on the floor labeled as negative three. It 
was a restricted, employee-only area, which could only be accessed with 
the right key. There was a low hum filling the hallway, and lights were 
sparse. 
The doors started sliding apart and Roanne walked out as soon as the 
gap was wide enough. Rick followed, holding Bernand by his hand.  
Roanne led the way to the third door on the left and knocked twice. 
No answer. 
Rick nodded, checked if Bernand was okay to stand alone, pulled out 
his key chain from his jeans pocket, searched for the right key and 
unlocked the door. 
As soon as he opened the door, the hum got twice as loud. 
There were electronic boxes stacked up in two long rows, old and new 
travelling devices neatly placed in the third row, and a bunch of computers 
on several tables standing in-between the rows, sometimes connected to 
machines with multiple cables. 
Roanne took Bernand by his hand and followed Rick inside. 
“I don’t like this place,” Bernand mumbled. He looked down on the 
floor, his eyes barely open. 
Rick sighed. The medication was wearing off sooner today. 
Roanne rubbed Bernand’s hand soothingly. “It will be fine. You’ll see.” 
One of the newer travelling booths activated itself and spew out a 
person with long hair and a cleanly shaven face, wearing black jeans and a 
white shirt. 
“Ah,” Samir said, stepping away from the booth. “There you are. I was 
just checking out one of the new cemeteries. I’m really nervous. You know. 
Wireless!” 
He walked towards a computer and hacked at a few keys, ending the 
procedure by passionately hitting the enter key. 

 
367 
“I wish you would join us again as we release this product, Rick,”

“Right, let me take a look at you, Bernand,” Samir said, clapping his

“He’s been stable,” Rick reported flatly.

“A thousand, licking their hide, a million, praising the Sade,” Bernand

368 
Samir came back with a strange old device in both hands, it looked like

Rick’s head started to hurt. 
“You see,” Samir said, putting his hands on his hips, addressing

I’m afraid.” 
Bernand looked at him. “We can keep a secret, but my time is running 
short, I-” His head fell forward. 
Samir summoned an empathic look on his face. “I can try to do the

“Isn’t that what you did last time?” Roanne asked, bewildered. “You 
didn’t exactly fix him.” 
Samir shook his head. “Up until now, I kept refining the method of 
bringing back your son. This would be a different approach. You must 
understand: Xave told me to do everything possible to make the man 
normal again.” He looked Rick straight in his eyes. “Even if it meant 
restoring someone else’s mind inside.” 
Bernand collapsed into Roanne’s arms; Rick jumped to supported her 
and told Samir to get a chair. 
When Bernand was resting safely, Samir continued: “Working on 
wireless has led to new discoveries and I called you here today because I 
am confident that I can help you out this way. I can try and restore a mind 
that is, in the very least, similar to your son’s; similar to Bernand.” 
Roanne slid a hand through her hair and breathed slowly in and out. 
She looked at her husband for a few seconds, the humming sound 
straining their nerves. 
Then, they both turned to the scientist and nodded in agreement.  
 
369 
 
 
 
“Thank you,” Leonard said, holding the ceramic bowl with warm soup in 
his left hand, accepting the spoon with his right. “Are you sure I shouldn ’t 
wait? He’ll be home any minute.” 
Marla shook her head. “It’s alright.” 
“Move along, old man,” Bellward jested, almost causing Leonard to 
trip. 
“I’ll let you know when I’m above fifty, then you can call me that,” 
Leonard said, walking outside of the house and sitting down on one of the 
chairs around a small bonfire. 
He took a spoon full of nettle soup and, as the taste engulfed his

Five years ago, they had all worked together to repair and rebuild. He 
could barely believe that it was the same house he had used to live in, all 
alone, clawing stored food out of the earth of the basement. 
Inspector Reklur had aided them in actually receiving ownership of the

for trespassing and, as he put it, “snoring on stolen property.” 
“Come now, what’s with the sentimental face?” Verayne said, carrying 
a bowl, a cup of water and a spoon between her fingers. She sat down next 
to Leonard, letting out a sigh as she dropped. “I tell you what, these bones 
aren’t getting any younger.” 
Leonard swallowed. “So, if I’m old, and I’m forty-five, what are you?” 
“Fifty and soon stone cold,” Chawlee giggled, joining his wife on a

Verayne jabbed Chawlee’s sides with an overly shocked face. 
“Unless she finishes the whole pot of this delicious soup,” Chawlee 
said, slurping on the side of his bowl. 
Timony arrived outside as well, and after picking up what Chawlee

“It’s Timony’s recipe,” Marla said from the kitchen. 

 
370 
“Oh, I forgot,” Chawlee chuckled. “Everyone’s a cook in this 
household.” 
Marla soon sat down by the fire as well, carrying her own bowl, which 
looked different than all of the others. 
Five minutes later, Mardo finally appeared. They saw him coming up 
the road, that led him up the hill straight to the house. 
Everyone greeted him. 
“You guys started without me? Again?” he said with a big grin on his

“Night’s still fucking young,” Timony mumbled. 
Chawlee threw his spoon at him. 
“Spoon fight!” Bellward yelled and jumped from his chair, wielding his

“That’s not legal, is it?” Leonard asked. 
Mardo shrugged. “It’s money, I don’t second-guess. I’m just happy to 
be a part of this family and that I can contribute without resorting to my 
old skills.” 
 
371 
Leonard nodded. “Still, if it ever gets too much for you, you know you 
don’t have to do it. We will always find a way to get by.” 
“I know,” Mardo said, his eyes reflecting the bonfire. “That’s why I 
brought you with me.” He patted Leonard’s shoulder and started moving 
inside. 
 
372 
 
 
 
“This sounds weird,” Kristella complained, shuffling the papers in her 
hand. 
She was wearing a long black dress, and her hair was freshly styled by 
her favourite hairdresser. Around her neck was a light, INSTANT blue 
coloured scarf, and she kept tugging at it to correct its position. 
Calem was sitting to her right; the driver of their INSTANT car made a 
turn and she held onto the grip on the door next to her. 
Even to Kristella it was ironic that the company owned a variety of cars 
for moving their VIPs around, but which options did one have if one 
wanted to go places where they were just building new travelling booths? 
Go by foot? The press loved to spin jokes on that topic whenever they got 
the chance, never offering up a better solution themselves. 
“This is not good at all,” Kristella said, handing a piece of paper back to 
Calem. “I know Xave means well; the wording is just super strange.” 
Calem took the paper and read through the speech that was supposed 
to open up the event revealing wireless technology at the company’s new 
cemetery, which was the only one that had been built from the ground up 
to fit the INSTANT style. 
On the rest of the stack of papers, Kristella found variations of the same 
speech. None of them were any better. 
“Why did he not trust me to write my own speech this time?” Kristella 
grumbled. 
“Everything has to be just right for this, I guess,” Calem answered, 
keeping her eyes on the words in front of her. “It does sound off, doesn ’t 
it?” she finally agreed. 
Kristella reached out with her right hand. Calem instantly knew what 
she wanted, took a pen out of her purse and handed it to Kristella. 
“He doesn’t know the crowd as much as I do,” Kristella said, fiercely 
striking through several words, holding the papers against her lap.  
Calem grinned. “He probably expected you to react like that, coercing 
you into writing something even better.” 

 
373 
Kristella sighed. “Probably. Even so, this won’t do and I have to change 
it.” She shook her head. “It sounds like a sermon. I’m not sure we’re 
supposed to behave like that as a tech company.” 
The car went over three speed bumps as they sneaked their way 
through a small village at the foot of a hill. Kristella struggled to keep her 
pen straight. 
“We’ll be there in ten minutes,” the driver announced. “Do you want 
me to take an extra turn somewhere?” 
“I got it,” Kristella said. “Ten minutes is enough.” 
“We still have a few hours for rehearsal and preparation, no?” Calem 
reminded her. “I believe we’re the first ones to arrive, anyway.” 
Kristella wrote words and formed new sentences, and without any 
interruption to her scribbling, she said: “We’re not the first. Heray already 
drove Walter and Xave there. Xave wanted to show the cemetery to Walter 
in private to get his opinion.” 
“What’s that supposed to mean?” Calem asked. “In private?” 
“I’m sure Walter will tell me later on,” Kristella said, slashing another 
sentence. 
“They do have their own thing going on,” Calem said, looking at the 
trees outside, where there used to be houses a minute ago. “Don ’t you 
agree? That there is something between them that you can’t quite 
understand? A bond of some sort?” 
Kristella looked at her. “They’re partners in crime,” she said, deliberately 
rolling her eyes. “They just found something they’re both passionate 
about, I guess.” 
“No dark secrets?” Calem dug further. 
“Just two boys building dangerous sand castles,” Kristella said, 
finishing her speech. 
 
374 
 
 
 
“What do you think?” Xave asked, as he and Walter approached a giant 
black tent, spanning the size of a large family house. 
The surrounding old trees blocked the sun, casting a dark shadow on 
both of them. 
“It’s black,” Walter commented. 
Xave chuckled. “Let’s get inside.” He went closer and raised a cloth 
functioning as a curtain. “We’ll be right back,” Xave shouted back to 
Heray, who was waiting a few meters away by the car. 
The scientist and the salesman disappeared in the tent. Walter 
immediately noticed that there was a hole above them instead of the 
ceiling; the area was simply encapsulated by the longest curtain in the 
world hanging off a steel skeleton. 
“What the hell?” Walter remarked, as he noticed the contents that the 
curtains had kept hidden. 
“Ta-da,” Xave sung, presenting the building behind him with raised 
arms. 
They were standing on a patch of grass with glowy blue tombstones in 
regular intervals, showing fictional names on stylish displays. 
“Man Without Body, George Fly Guy, Atlan Tick, Professor 
Challenger?” Walter read aloud. 
“Those are names to test the displays,” Xave responded impatiently. 
“What do you think of this?” He once again gestured at the building 
behind him. 
It was a chapel-like structure with a hint of gothic architecture, mixed 
with emphasized borders of blue LED lines and a bright “INSTANT” logo 
at the top where one would expect a wooden Christian cross.  
“Which member of this company is a god?” Walter enquired. 
“Well, judging by what you do in your lab...” Xave shrugged and 
grinned. “I already know my place, it’s time you knew yours.” 
“Naturally, you built this without asking me first,” Walter said, 
examining the building closer and opening the sliding door. 

 
375 
He saw the clean interior design, complete with tables and chairs for 
funeral services. There wasn’t much decoration, but the windows were - of 
course - tinted blue. 
“I can’t imagine anyone using this,” Walter concluded. 
“You know the price of regular funerals, right? This is basically free,” 
Xave argued. “Nothing beats free - even with an asterisk.” 
“You’re deliberately competing on funeral prices?” 
“I see families struggling to come up with enough money to lay their 
loved ones to rest. I’m offering a service to these people, giving them a 
chance to be useful to the community, to humanity.” Xave said, touching 
Walter’s shoulder. “Wireless travelling, Walter. We’re making it happen. 
People donating their bodies are making it happen. And I’m telling you 
something: INSTANT travelling already has a religious following. Why 
not commit at least a little bit?” 
Walter could have sworn that Xave purposely tested Walter’s limits at 
least once a week. 
The scientist sighed. “You didn’t bring me out here to hear my consent, 
did you?” 
“I don’t hear your rejection, that’s enough for me,” Xave said. 
“You don’t hear rejections in general, you have a filter for that in your 
head. Rejection doesn’t reach you,” Walter explained. “And you don’t 
care.” 
“I care about what’s important to the company,” Xave retaliated. 
Walter nodded. “I know. Nevermind.” 
Usually, in moments like these Walter would have been furious; this 
time he felt nothing. He assessed the situation: It was a cemetery owned by 
his company. His brain filed it away and dreamed up something pleasant.  
“You know,” Walter said, after having a thought. “What if I killed 
myself and I’d be the first to use our own INSTANT funeral service?”  
Xave guffawed. “We both know that would be the first time you ’d use 
INSTANT anything.” 
For a moment Walter wasn’t sure if anybody had heard Xave and 
looked around. 
“Oh, did I spook you?” Xave said. “Nobody’s here, don’t worry.” 
“We rarely talk about it openly, it threw me off guard,” Walter 
admitted. 
“I wouldn’t use it either. Hell, teleportation has been creeping me out 
ever since I saw you testing it on a rat,” Xave said. 
Walter held his finger against his lips. “Shhh!” 
 
376 
“I don’t know how I pulled it off, but somehow I made people forget 
that it affects their bodies,” Xave rambled on. “I mean, I know, sometimes I 
just don’t get why people actually accepted it.” 
“You’re making me nervous,” Walter said. “Do you not care whether 
the walls catch the words you’re blurting out?” 
“Walter,” Xave scolded him. “No walls, just cloth.” 
“Are you two done?” Heray asked. “Remember the conference you 
wanted to hold in a few hours right where you stand? More people will 
arrive soon.” 
Walter turned around with a pale face; Xave remained calm. 
“Thanks,” Xave said. “By the way, what do you think, Heray? Do you 
like it?” 
“Looks fine to me,” Heray said, taking in his blue surroundings. “By 
the way, I already knew about you both not using you-know-what. Don’t 
worry about it, I’ve kept worse band secrets on smaller salary.” 
Walter saw Xave staring at Heray and calculating wildly. “Good man,” 
Xave said. “I’m glad you like it.” 
Heray nodded and left the tent. 
Lowering his voice, Walter addressed Xave with his concerns. 
“Repeatedly and more often in recent months, I stop and wonder where 
this is all going to end. Don’t you ever ask yourself that question?” 
At first Xave looked as if choosing words from his usual repertoire, but 
something tilted his mood. For a while he drifted off and stared at the 
grass below his feet.
